Received 
      7th April 2022
    First published on 10th November 2022AbstractHalf a century after the synthesis of the first subporphyrinoid, the study of tripyrrole and trisoindole porphyrin analogues constitutes a fervent and rapidly expanding research area. The outstanding structural, electronic and optical features of these cone-shaped aromatic macrocycles render them attractive candidates for a wide variety of applications, ranging from optoelectronics to biomedicine. To tune their properties and exploit their functionalities, the development of novel methodologies for the synthesis and post-functionalization of these contracted porphyrinoids, as well as a deep understanding of their supramolecular organization and their implementation into multicomponent systems of increasing complexity are of paramount importance. Herein, a review of the most recent advances in the fundamentals and applications of subporphyrinoids is presented, which comprehensively cover the last decade of discoveries. The final aim is to highlight the chemical versatility and intriguing physicochemical features of subporphyrinoids, while providing an updated overview of their most promising applications.
Giulia Lavarda
Giulia Lavarda studied Chemistry at the Università degli Studi di Padova (Italy), where she obtained her BSc in 2012 and her MSc in 2014. Then she moved to the Universidad Autónoma de Madrid (Spain), where she completed her PhD in Organic Chemistry under the supervision of Prof. T. Torres focusing on the synthesis and study of novel subphthalocyanine-based systems. During this period, she realized predoctoral stays in the laboratory of Prof. A. Osuka at Kyoto University (Japan), working on the preparation of metalated subporphyrin complexes, and in the laboratory of Prof. D. M. Guldi at Friedrich Alexander University Erlangen-Nürnberg (Germany), where she studied the photophysics of porphyrinoid-containing multicomponent systems. Currently, she is Marie Skłodowska-Curie postdoctoral researcher in Prof. E. W. Meijer's group at the Eindhoven University of Technology (The Netherlands), where she investigates the fundamentals and applications of supramolecular polymers.
Jorge Labella
Jorge Labella studied Chemistry at the Universidad de Alcalá, where he obtained his BSc with honours in 2017. Afterwards, he moved to the Universidad Autónoma de Madrid to complete his MSc in organic chemistry (honours) in 2018. He is currently working on his PhD in Organic Chemistry under the supervision of Prof. Tomás Torres, funded by a FPU scholarship. During this period, he has realized a predoctoral stay in the laboratory of Prof. Md. K. Nazeeruddin at École Polytechnique Fédérale de Lausanne (Switzerland) to work on the fabrication of perovskite solar cells. Currently, he is realizing a second predoctoral stay in the laboratory of Prof. Jonathan Sessler at the University of Texas at Austin (United States), where he is working on expanded porphyrins. His research is mainly focused on the preparation of novel SubPcs, and their application in photovoltaics, chirality, and supramolecular chemistry.
M. Victoria Martínez-Díaz
M. Victoria Martínez-Díaz, Full Professor of Organic Chemistry at Autónoma University of Madrid, obtained her PhD in Chemistry from this university under the supervision of Prof. Tomás Torres working on Supramolecular Chemistry, on the selective recognition of chiral ammonium ions mediated by heterocycle-containing receptors. After a year postdoc at CEA Saclay (France), addressing the organization of amphiphilic functional molecules at the air–water interface using the Langmuir–Blodgett (LB) technique, she joined for two years the group of Sir Prof. Fraser J Stoddart at the School of Chemistry of the University of Birmingham (UK). Her current research interest is focused on the synthesis and organization of phthalocyanines and subphthalocyanines-based systems with potential application on organic photovoltaics and nanomedicine.
M. Salomé Rodríguez-Morgade
M. Salomé Rodríguez-Morgade is a Full Professor at the Universidad Autónoma de Madrid and a Senior Researcher at the Institute for Advanced Research in Chemical Sciences (IAdChem) at UAM/Spain. Since 1999, she is integrated in the Nanoscience and Molecular Materials' group at UAM. Her scientific activity is concerned with the synthesis and study of molecular materials based on phthalocyanines and other azaporphyrins. Her current research interests are focused on the design and development of phthalocyanines and new porphyrinoids such as subporphyrazines and analogues for their application as photosensitizers in photodynamic therapy (PDT) and molecular photovoltaics.
Atsuhiro Osuka
Atsuhiro Osuka started an academic career at the Department of Chemistry of Ehime University as an assistant professor. In 1984, he moved to the Department of Chemistry of Kyoto University, where he became a professor of chemistry in 1996. In 2022, he moved to Hunan Normal University as a distinguished professor. His research interests cover development of porphyrin-related compounds with novel structures, electronic systems, and functions.
Tomás Torres
Tomás Torres is Head of the Institute for Advanced Research in Chemical Sciences (IAdChem) and Full Professor of Organic Chemistry at the Autónoma University of Madrid (UAM) and Associated Senior Scientist at the IMDEA Nanoscience. In addition to various aspects of synthetic and supramolecular chemistry, his current research interests include the preparation and study of optical properties of organic functional materials. His group, that presently consists of twenty-five researchers, is currently exploring several areas of basic research and applications of phthalocyanines, porphyrins and carbon nanostructures (fullerenes, carbon nanotubes, graphene), including organic and hybrid solar cells, and photodynamic therapy, with a focus on nanotechnology.
1. Introduction
Subporphyrinoids – comprising subporphyrins (SubPs),1–5 subporphyrazines (SubPzs),1,6 benzosubporphyrins (BzSubPs)1,4,5,7 and subphthalocyanines (SubPcs)1,8–10 as the four cornerstones – are curved, aromatic macrocycles that constitute a class of extremely versatile and attractive compounds. By virtue of their relatively recent discovery2,3,6,7,10 and their outstanding physicochemical properties, the study of these contracted porphyrinoids represents a still young but rapidly expanding research area. Subporphyrinoids differ from other porphyrin analogues in the size of the macrocycle, which is composed of three pyrrole or isoindole units, and exclusively coordinate a boron atom within their binding core (Fig. 1). The boron tetrahedral coordination imposes to these 14 π-electron compounds a characteristic cone-shaped geometry, rendering subporphyrinoids one of the few known examples of non-planar aromatic molecules. As a result, subporphyrinoids exhibit peculiar structural, optical and electronic features which make them intriguing candidates for a wide variety of applications ranging from optoelectronics to biomedicine.
 Fig. 1  Chemical structure of the four main classes of subporphyrinoids. 
A comprehensive review on subporphyrinoids was presented by some of us in 2014.1 Since then, an impressive number of publications has been reported dealing with the synthesis, study and application of these fascinating derivatives, which highlight the huge interest of the scientific community in this fervent field. Remarkable progress has been made in the chemistry of SubPcs, which represent the first discovered macrocycles in the family of subporphyrinoids. Novel strategies have been presented for the derivatization of the preformed SubPc macrocycle. Crucial advances have been made in understanding the organization features of SubPcs, and a plethora of SubPcs as dimeric, oligomeric and polymeric systems has been described. The ground- and excited-state interactions of SubPc-based multicomponent systems have been thoroughly investigated, laying the foundations for their implementation in solar energy conversion schemes. Important steps forward have also been made in the application of SubPcs in the biomedical area. In addition, substantial progress has been done in the synthesis and reactivity of the younger SubPs, SubPzs and BzSubPs congeners, and several new analogues have been reported.
Herein, we present a review of the most recent advances in the chemistry of all these contracted porphyrinoids. The first part of the review is dedicated to SubPcs, which constitute by far the most investigated subporphyrinoids. We will first discuss the synthetic procedures for the preparation of SubPcs, which trimeric structure is invariably assembled through boron-templated cyclotrimerization of phthalonitrile derivatives. In addition, we aim at providing an overview of the reactivity of these macrocycles, with a special emphasis on the methodologies for the post-functionalization of the axial and peripheral positions. Then, we will describe the supramolecular organization of SubPcs in the crystalline solid state, as well as in thin films, on surfaces, in solution and in the mesophase. We will next examine all-SubPc as well as hybrid, SubPc-containing multicomponent systems. In this connection, we will give a special focus to the photophysics of donor–acceptor systems based on SubPcs. Finally, we will describe the applications of these subporphyrinoids, with a particular emphasis on their exploitation as molecular materials in organic photovoltaics.
The second part of this review is devoted to SubPs, BzSubPs, SubPzs, as well as their structurally related analogues. In this case, a great assortment of synthetic procedures and starting materials to assemble the very different structures will be described. We will particularly emphasize on the youngest SubPs, since they are by far the subporphyrinoids for which a wider variety of synthetic procedures and post-functionalization methods has been developed. Comparisons between the physicochemical properties of SubPs and those of their better-studied SubPc relatives will be made. We will also describe recent advances in the synthesis of SubP-based multicomponent systems and initial studies on their technological applications, an aspect that remains in its infancy. A comprehensive overview of the chemistry of the SubPz and the subnaphthalocyanine (SubNc) families is also included. Reported for the first time more than 25 years ago, SubNcs have been the object of recent interest for their application in solar technologies.
In summary, the final aim of this review is to bring out the outstanding chemical, structural, optical and electronic features of subporphyrinoids, while providing an updated view of the most promising applications of these fascinating macrocycles.
2. Subphthalocyanines
SubPcs are aromatic macrocycles composed of three N-fused 1,3-diiminoisoindole units arranged around a boron atom, which also bears an axial substituent. The 14 π-electron conjugated system of these derivatives is mainly located in the inner ring, whereas the isoindolic benzenes virtually preserve their electronic structure. Hereafter, the XnSubPc–Y notation will be employed to refer to SubPc species, where X and n indicate the nature and the number of the substituents linked to the isoindolic benzene rings, respectively, and Y stands for the apical ligand (Fig. 2). In the presence of peripheral substituents different from hydrogen atoms, the latter will not be indicated.
 Fig. 2  Generic chemical structure of a SubPc derivative. 
2.1 Synthesis of subphthalocyanines

2.1.1 Synthetic approaches for subphthalocyanine synthesis. 
The main outcomes on SubPc synthesis published during the first four decades after their discovery10 have been reviewed by some of us in previous works.1,8 Here, general considerations on SubPc preparation are reported and the major results presented since our last review on SubPcs and other contracted porphyrinoids are discussed.1
SubPcs are synthesized through cyclotrimerization reaction of one (or more) phthalonitrile derivative(s) in the presence of a boron-based Lewis acid at high temperatures (generally, above 140 °C) in high-boiling aromatic solvents. To date, no reports have been published on the synthesis of “free-base” SubPcs or SubPc derivatives featuring a central atom different from boron, which preparation constitutes one of the foremost challenges in SubPc chemistry. However, the possibility to incorporate central atoms different from boron within the SubPc core has been widely investigated through theoretical calculations, as will be described in Section 2.3.9.
BCl3 constitutes undoubtedly the most employed boron reagents in SubPc synthesis. Although to a lesser extent, BBr3 is also quite commonly used for the preparation of SubPc derivatives. Upon reaction of these Lewis acids with a starting phthalonitrile, the corresponding SubPc-Cl and SubPc-Br species are formed, respectively. The reason why BCl3 constitutes by far the most used boron halide in SubPc synthesis despite its lower reactivity in comparison with BBr3 lies in the higher stability of axially chlorinated SubPcs with respect to the corresponding brominated analogues.
Some significant improvements have been introduced in the synthetic methodology for the preparation of SubPc-Cl derivatives since their serendipitous discovery in 1972. For three decades after their first preparation, SubPcs have been mainly synthesised by addition of an excess of BCl3 – as a condensed liquid or as a solution in low-boiling solvents – to the starting phthalonitrile dissolved in a high-boiling solvent.11 In 2003, a highly efficient method for the preparation of axially chlorinated SubPcs involving the use of p-xylene solutions of BCl3 was presented by Torres, Schucmann and co-workers.12 The general protocol presented in this work consists in the addition of 1 equiv. of BCl3 from a 1 M solution in p-xylene to the starting phthalonitrile under argon atmosphere. The reaction mixture is then refluxed for a time depending on the nature of the phthalonitrile precursor (Scheme 1). This approach presents several advantages over the use of condensed BCl3. First of all, the use of a BCl3 solution allows to control the stoichiometry of the reagents, thus minimizing side reactions related to the use of large excesses of a strong Lewis acid. In particular, p-xylene is a very good solvent for precursor phthalonitriles and has a sufficiently high boiling point to allow for its use in SubPc synthesis. Moreover, this solvent can efficiently reduce the chlorine released during the preparation of the SubPc core (please refer to Section 2.1.4 for further details on the mechanism of SubPc formation), preventing the formation of peripherally chlorinated SubPc by-products. Finally, the set-up of the reaction is easier.
 Scheme 1  General synthesis of SubPc-Cl derivatives. 
This approach represents nowadays the most employed methodology for SubPc synthesis. Nevertheless, it is worth to mention that minor variations have been introduced in this procedure in some cases. A possible modification consists in employing a 1 M solution of BCl3 in p-xylene in the presence of a co-solvent (e.g. chlorobenzene,13o-dichlorobenzene,14,15 or 1-chloronaphthalene16).11 SubPcs synthesis has also been carried out by addition of a 1 M solution of BCl3 in heptanes17–19 or dichloromethane20–27 to a solution of the starting phthalonitrile in a high-boiling solvent such as p-xylene,22,24 1-chloronaphthalene,18o-dichlorobenzene,17,19,21,23,25,26 1,2,4-trichlorobenzene20 or dimethyl sulfoxide.11,27 Microwave irradiation has also been employed for SubPc preparation.28,29 In this case, a heptane solution of BCl3 was used in the presence of 1-chloronaphthalene or o-dichlorobenzene as the co-solvent.
As mentioned above, the preparation of axially brominated SubPcs is less reported than that of SubPc-Cl derivative due to the lower stability of the former, which may lead the hydrolysis of the initially formed halogenated SubPc to the corresponding SubPc-OH species during the synthesis and work-up procedures. Nevertheless, it should be kept in mind that the higher reactivity of the B–Br bond may represent an advantage in axial ligand exchange reactions (see Section 2.2.1). For the preparation of SubPc-Br derivatives, BBr3 is commonly added as a neat liquid to a solution or suspension of the precursor phthalonitrile in a high-boiling solvent such as, for example, chlorobenzene,30o-dichlorobenzene,23 1-chloronaphthalene,31 or toluene/bromobenzene mixtures.11
Notably, the attempted synthesis of Cl6SubPc-Br 1 from 4,5-dichlorophthalonitrile and BBr3 in chlorobenzene was found to lead to the formation of a mixture of axially brominated and chlorinated Cl6SubPc derivatives 1 and 2, as reported by Bender and co-workers (Scheme 2).30 In contrast, cyclotrimerization of 3,4,5,6-tetrachlorophthalonitrile in the same reaction conditions proceeded smoothly, affording solely the axially brominated Cl12SubPc derivative. The authors indicated the solvent rather than the starting phthalonitrile as the possible source of chloride, as no evidence of peripheral bromination was found in the formed macrocycles. A similar halogen exchange was observed a few years later by the same group during cyclotrimerization of 4,5-difluorophthalonitrile and 3,4,5,6-tetrafluorophthalonitrile in the presence of BCl3 to form the corresponding SubPc-Cl derivatives (Scheme 3).32 In this case, the concomitant formation of the axially fluorinated conjugates 5 and 6 was observed along with the formation of the target SubPc-Cl derivatives 3 and 4, which was ascribed to the degradation of the precursor phthalonitriles. In light of this findings, the decomposition of the chlorinated phthalonitrile should be consider as a possible rationale in the formation of Cl6SubPc-Cl from 4,5-dichlorophthalonitrile and BBr3.30
 Scheme 2  Cyclotrimerization of 4,5-dichlorophthalonitrile in the reaction conditions reported by Bender and co-workers (i = BBr3, chlorobenzene, 100 °C, 4 h).30 
 Scheme 3  Cyclotrimerization of 4,5-difluorophthalonitrile and 3,4,5,6-tetrafluorophthalonitrile in the reaction conditions reported by Bender and co-workers (i = BCl3 (1.0 M in heptane), o-dichlorobenzene, 180 °C, 26 h).32 


2.1.2 Isomerism in subphthalocyanine synthesis. 
2.1.2.1 Regioisomerism in subphthalocyanine synthesis. 
Cyclotrimerization of non-C2v-symmetric phthalonitriles lead to the formation of a mixture of C1-symmetric and C3-symmetric SubPc constitutional isomers, which separation is commonly carried out by column chromatography on silica gel.33,34 For meta-substituted SubPcs, these regioisomers are obtained in a statistical 1:3 C3:C1 ratio. On the contrary, in the case of ortho-substituted SubPc derivatives, steric and electronic effects play a significant role in the regioselectivity of the cyclotrimerization reaction due to the proximity of the peripheral substituents to the SubPc core. As an example, 1.5:1, 1.75:1 and 1:9 C3:C1 ratios have been observed in the presence of peripheral nitro, iodo and propylsulfonyl groups, respectively.33 Notably, cyclotrimerization of 3-nitro-5-t-butylphthalonitrile afforded a mixture of C3 and C1 regioisomers in a 9:1 ratio, which substantially differs from the 1.5:1 ratio found in the synthesis of the ortho-(NO2)3SubPc derivative.31 In 2015, the regioselective synthesis of a C3-symmetric ortho-pentafluorosulfanylphenyl-substituted SubPc (7, Fig. 3) was reported.35 The selectivity observed in the cyclotrimerization reaction of the corresponding phthalonitrile was attributed by the authors to the steric hindrance of the SF5 group. Interestingly, the preparation of the trifluoromethylphenyl-substituted analogue lead to the obtention of a mixture of both the C3 and C1 regioisomers.
 Fig. 3  Chemical structure of C3-symmetric SubPc derivative 7 obtained from the regioselective cyclotrimerization reaction of the corresponding phthalonitrile precursor.35 
2.1.2.2 Stereoisomerism in subphthalocyanine synthesis. 
Due to their cone-shaped geometry, SubPcs are intrinsically non-centrosymmetric. As a consequence, each of the C1- and C3-symmetric constitutional isomers obtained from cyclotrimerization of phthalonitriles lacking C2v symmetry is inherently chiral and is obtained as a racemic mixture of two enantiomers (i.e. M and P for the C3 regioisomer, MPP and MMP for the C1 regioisomer, Fig. 4).
 Fig. 4  Possible isomers of a trisubstituted SubPc featuring a meta functionalization pattern. 
The first report on the optical resolution of racemic SubPcs was published in 2000 by Claessens and Torres.34 In this work, the enantiomers of both C3- and C1-symmetric meta-substituted I3SubPc-Cl were separated by analytical chiral HPLC. Two years later, Kobayashi et al. reported the semipreparative resolution of the C3 regioisomer of a SubPc prepared from cyclotrimerization of 3-nitro-5-t-butylphthalonitrile, which allowed the measure of the first circular dichroism (CD) spectra of enantiopure SubPc species.31 Almost a decade later, the optical resolution of a C3-symmetric and a C1-symmetric 1,2-SubNc derivative by chiral HPLC was presented by Shimizu and co-workers.36 Remarkably, single-crystal X-ray diffraction analysis of the isolated enantiomers allowed to establish a correlation between CD spectra and absolute configuration of optically pure SubNc species. The enantiomers of a C3-symmetric SubPc-F bearing three peripheral arylamide groups were also separated by means of analytical chiral HPLC, and the self-assembly properties in solution of the racemic and the enantiopure species were investigated (see Section 2.4.4.1 for further details).37,38 Very recently, the solid-state organization of racemic and enantiopure C1- and C3-symmetric SubPc derivatives bearing fluorine as substituent at both the axial and peripheral positions of the macrocycle was investigated by Miyajima and co-workers (see Section 2.4.1.1).39
An alternative approach for the resolution of inherently chiral SubPcs consist in the derivatization of a racemic SubPc with an enantiopure chiral auxiliary, followed by separation of the resulting diastereomers and removal of the resolving agent from the isolated diastereomeric derivatives. In this connection, a high degree of diastereoselectivity has been observed during the oxidation of an asymmetric SubPc derivative bearing an ortho thioether substituent to the corresponding sulfoxide derivative, which resulted from the intrinsic chirality of the SubPc core.40 As a matter of fact, a 95:5 diastereomeric mixture was obtained in the investigated conditions, and the two diastereomers could be separated by column chromatography. Remarkably, the oxidation of the meta-substituted analogue did not show any selectivity as it led to the formation of a 1:1 mixture of diastereomers, which could only be separated by HPLC.
Regarding the introduction of chiral moieties through derivatization of either the axial or the peripheral positions of the SubPc macrocycle, the synthesis of a couple of SubPc dimers bearing either a racemic BINOL or an enantiopure (R)-(+)-BINOL bridge was presented in 2003 by Kobayashi, Durfee and co-workers.41 Notably, CD measurements on the optically active derivative (8, Fig. 5) evidenced an induced CD on the SubPc generated by the chiral binaphthyl unit. More than a decade later, two chiral SubPcs bearing three peripheral (R)- or (S)-1,1′-bi(2-naphthoxy) moieties (9R/S, Fig. 5) were reported.20,42 Also in this case, an CD signal in the SubPc absorption range induced by the chiral ligands was observed.
 Fig. 5  Structure of the optically active BINOL-bridged SubPc dimer and the chiral binaphthoxy-functionalized SubPcs reported by Kobayashi, Durfee, Jiang and co-workers.20,41 
Recently, the preparation of peripherally perhydrogenated and perfluorinated SubPc derivatives bearing a 1,1,4,4-tetracyanobuta-1,3-diene (TCBD)–aniline moiety at the axial position of the macrocycle (10 and 11, Fig. 6) was reported by Guldi, Torres, Bottari and co-workers.43 Due to the restricted rotation around the central bond of the TCBD unit and the quasi-orthogonal arrangement of the two dicyanovinyl halves, each of these conjugates is formed as a racemic mixture of two atropisomers, which were successfully separated by chiral HPLC. Notably, single-crystal X-ray diffraction analysis of the isolated enantiomers allowed to unambiguously assign their absolute configuration. Moreover, the racemization process was thoroughly investigated by experimental and theoretical studies.44 CD measurements either in the presence or in absence of stimuli (i.e. light and molecular oxygen) showed that in both derivatives enantiomer conversion is boosted upon illumination of oxygen-free solutions, whereas in absence of light or in the presence of oxygen no racemization take place. DFT calculations confirmed the exceptionally high thermal stability of the enantiopure species and evidenced a substantial lowering of the energy barrier of isomerization (i.e. 15 kcal mol−1 and 21 kcal mol−1 approximately for 10 and 11, respectively) upon excitation. Notably, similar energy barrier values were found upon computing racemization from the S1 and the T1 excited states. These findings, along with the well-known ability of molecular oxygen as triplet-state quencher, support a peculiar light-triggered racemization mechanism enabled by triplet-state photogeneration, which could be rationalized on the basis of structural and kinetic considerations. Interestingly, the racemization behaviour of these TCBD-substituted SubPc derivatives substantially differs from that of a SubP analogue bearing a TCBD–aniline unit in the axial position (see Section 6.4.5).45 For the latter, a thermally induced racemization was observed. Two SubPcs functionalized at their peripheral or peripheral and axial positions with three or four TCBD–aniline moieties, respectively, (12 and 13, Fig. 6) have also been prepared.46 In these derivatives, the presence of multiple axially chiral TCBD moieties along with the C3 symmetry of the peripheral functionalization pattern and the cone-shaped geometry of the SubPc macrocycle results in the formation of several stereoisomers. Surprisingly, quite sharp 1H-NMR spectra were registered at room temperature, due to the rapid interconversion between isomers on the NMR timescale. Such interconversion is significantly slowed down at low temperatures, as evidenced by the increasing of multiplicity, the broadening and shifting of the proton signals.
 Fig. 6  Chemical structure of the SubPc–TCBD–aniline conjugates investigated by Guldi, Torres, Bottari and co-workers. 


2.1.3 Synthesis of low-symmetry subphthalocyanines. 
Unsymmetrically substituted SubPcs can be prepared through cross-condensation reaction of different phthalonitrile precursors. In this way, a mixture of SubPc derivatives differing in the nature, the number and the position of the ring substituents is obtained. Due to the low tendency of SubPcs towards aggregation in solution, the separation of these products in generally achieved through common chromatographic techniques, in contrast to what observed in phthalocyanine (Pc) chemistry. Nevertheless, solubility issues or intermolecular interactions between side chains may prevent the separation of all the formed compounds.
Both steric factors and relative reactivities of the starting phthalonitriles toward the boron reagent play a role in the selectivity of the reaction, although a clear tendency cannot be defined due to the scarceness of reports on this topic. In general, the selectivity of the reaction can be partially controlled tuning the stoichiometry of the reaction in terms of molar ratio between the starting phthalonitrile species. During the last years, the synthesis of several unsymmetrical SubPc derivatives has been reported, including the preparation of low-symmetry SubPcs bearing peripheral ortho-substituents.14,17,47–58


2.1.4 Mechanism of subphthalocyanine formation. 
The first computational investigation on the formation mechanism of H12SubPc-Cl from phthalonitrile in the presence of BCl3 was reported in 2007 by Torres and co-workers.59 In this work, a two-phase mechanism was proposed in which oligomerization of the dicyanobenzene unit to build the macrocycle framework is followed by formation of the final SubPc valence system through a reductive step. In the first stage, Lewis acid activation of the starting dinitrile by BCl3 was deemed to trigger the formation of an intermediate species, from which an open trimer is obtained through successive additions. A ring-closing step was claimed to complete the formation of the macrocyclic framework. In this phase, the importance of the central boron atom as template able to ensure the spatial arrangement required for cyclization was underlined, along with the role of BCl3 as activating agent through formation of the initial phthalonitrile–BCl3 adduct. In the second stage, reduction of the system via BCl3-catalyzed concerted elimination of Cl2 from the close-ring oxidized precursor was argued to afford the final SubPc valence system.60 A decade after this pivotal study, the crucial catalytic role of BCl3 was clearly unveiled by an in-depth DFT study on SubPcs’ formation mechanism, in which a twenty-three-step reaction pathway was disclosed.61 As a matter of fact, BCl3 was argued to act as chloride-transfer shuttle in a number of elementary reactions involved in the formation of the SubPc macrocycle, thus reducing the activation barrier of the process. Moreover, the importance of the boron halide as activating and templating agent was confirmed. In particular, two molecules of boron halide were claimed to be involved in the initial activation of the starting phthalonitrile, each one coordinating one of the cyano groups of the precursor species (Fig. 7).
 Fig. 7  Energy profile for the first steps of SubPc formation mechanism. Reproduced with permission from ref. 61. Copyright 2017 Published by Elsevier B.V. 

2.2 Reactivity of subphthalocyanines
Over the years, SubPcs have proved to constitute a class of extremely versatile compounds in terms of chemical modification of the macrocyclic system. Traditionally, SubPc reactivity has been classified in three main categories, depending on the reactive center and the nature of the target product: (a) axial reactivity, (b) peripheral reactivity and (c) ring-expansion reactions.1 In axial and peripheral reactivity, the reactive center consists in the axial B–Y bond and the functional groups linked to the isoindolic benzene rings, respectively. Both maintain the structure of the macrocycle, and result in the formation of functionalized SubPcs. On the other hand, ring expansion reactions, in which the reactive center is constituted by the imine-type SubPc core, determine the loss of the SubPc macrocyclic framework affording low-symmetry Pcs.
Although the preparation of asymmetric Pcs has constituted one of the first applications of SubPcs, the interest in the chemical modification of these macrocycles has been shifted almost completely over the decades to the functionalization of the SubPc axial and peripheral positions. As a matter of fact, the restrictions imposed by the use of boron reagents in SubPc synthesis in terms of variety of both axial and peripheral substituents prompted the development of numerous strategies for the post-functionalization of the preformed macrocycle. Importantly, tailoring the molecular structure of SubPcs permits to tune their physical, photophysical and electrochemical features (see Section 2.3 for further details).62 Moreover, the introduction of proper functionalities allows the control of the organization features of these macrocycles, besides enabling the preparation of dimeric, oligomeric or polymeric SubPc-based structures, as well as the integration of SubPcs in multicomponent systems (see Sections 2.4–2.6). Thus, investigation on novel approaches in SubPc axial and peripheral reactivity is of paramount importance in SubPc chemistry.
Besides these reactivity modes, Sessler, Guldi, Torres and co-workers presented in 2012 a novel type of reactivity in which the SubPc acts as π-ligand towards the arenophilic Ru(II) in the presence of [Cp*Ru(MeCN)3]PF6 leading to the formation of ruthenoarene π-complexes of SubPcs.63 Direct functionalization of either the axial phenol moiety or one of the isoindolic benzene rings of the SubPc precursor was found to take place. Regarding the π-metallocene complexation of the curved SubPc surface, coordination of either the endo or the exo π faces of the SubPc was observed, with a higher reactivity towards the convex π surface.
An additional type of reactivity consists in the coordination or protonation of the meso-nitrogen atoms of the SubPc ring in the presence of Lewis acids or Brønsted acids, respectively.64 Evidence of complexation of the SubPc macrocycle in the presence of boron or aluminum halides as well as H2SO4 has been reported by the groups of Bender and Torres.1,65,66 Recently, the reversible protonation of the azomethine bridge of the SubPc macrocycle in the presence of organic Brønsted acids was studied by Decréau and co-workers through UV-vis, NMR and IR spectroscopic assays.67 In particular, the number of protonated sites was shown to depend on the pKa of the acid employed as well as on the peripheral substitution pattern of the SubPc, which was argued to regulate the basicity of the meso-nitrogen atoms.

2.2.1 Axial reactivity. 
Substitution of the axial ligand represents a recurrent method for the functionalization of the SubPc macrocyclic system. The reason why axial reactivity has been thoroughly employed for the derivatization of SubPcs rely on a combination of different factors. First, the reaction protocols for axial substitution reactions are generally simple. Second, modification of the axial ligand permits to preserve the electronic properties of the macrocycle, which are mainly determined by the ring substituents. Third, functionalization of the apical position allows to avoid the synthesis of asymmetrical SubPc. Moreover, the introduction of proper axial functionalities can improve the solubility and stability of SubPc derivatives.
As a result of the peculiar coordination of the central boron atom, modification of the axial substituent involves reactions that are specific of SubPcs chemistry. In particular, the low reactivity of the tetracoordinated boron atom constitute a major drawback for axial functionalization. Thus, harsh conditions are generally required, which may lead to the decomposition of the macrocyclic system. Nevertheless, efficient methodologies have been developed for axial substitution reactions, which are based on the generation of activated SubPc intermediates that are susceptible to react with a variety of nucleophiles under mild reaction conditions (Scheme 4).
 Scheme 4  Axial substitution reaction in SubPcs via an activated SubPc-OTf intermediate68 (left) and via activation with AlCl3 (right).65,87 
A one-pot, two-step procedure for the replacement of the axial ligand in SubPc-Cl derivatives was presented by Guilleme, González-Rodríguez and Torres in 2011 (Scheme 4).68 In this protocol, a highly electrophilic SubPc species with a weakly coordinating axial triflate anion is prepared in situ by reaction of an axially chlorinated SubPc derivative with AgOTf or Me3SiOTf. Subsequent reaction of the SubPc-OTf intermediate with a nucleophile in the presence of a hindered tertiary amine (i.e. DIPEA) affords the corresponding axially-substituted SubPc species. The scope of this reaction comprises alkyl and aryl alcohols, carboxylic acids, alkyl thiols, thiophenols, amines, imidazole and pyrrole, as well as aliphatic and aromatic carbon nucleophiles in the form of magnesium bromide derivatives. During the last decade, this methodology has been widely employed for the preparation of axially functionalized SubPc derivatives.57,69–84
A couple of SubPc-OTf species have been isolated, and their crystal structures reported.85,86 The reactivity of the hydrogenated SubPc-OTf species was further studied by Fu and Wang.86 Treatment of the triflate derivative with a bulky base such as lithium bis(trimethylsilyl)amide (LiHMDS) in the presence of ethers, amides or ketones was found to promote the cleavage of the C–O and CO bond in such substrates (Scheme 5). The authors proposed as active species in the C–O and CO bond cleavage the highly reactive ion pair (SubPc)B+N(TMS)2−, which is supposed to be formed upon treatment of SubPc-OTf with LiHMDS. Such species is likely to lead in absence of substrate to the formation of the quenched Lewis adduct (SubPc)BN(TMS)2 and of (SubPc)BMe through Si–C cleavage, which were obtained in a 1:1 approximate ratio as revealed by 1H-NMR and ESI-MS measurements. As a matter of fact, the X-ray crystal structure of (SubPc)BMe was reported, and the ionic (SubPc)B(OCHNMe2)+OTf− species, which results from treatment of the transient ion pair with DMF, was also isolated and crystallized. Anyway, no direct evidence of the formation of the (SubPc)B+N(TMS)2− ion pair was reported.
 Scheme 5  Reactivity of H12SubPc-OTF towards ethers, amides and ketones in the presence of LiHMDS.86 
In another axial substitution protocol presented by Morse and Bender in 2012, the generation of an activated SubPc species is accomplished upon treatment of the SubPc-Cl or SubPc-Br precursor with AlCl3 (Scheme 4).66 In these conditions, a complex is formed in which the aluminum interacts with the axial halogen and meso nitrogen atoms of the macrocycle, which confers to the SubPc solution a characteristic deep blue color. Addition of the nucleophile and subsequent quenching of the resulting complex with a Lewis base such as pyridine afford the corresponding substituted SubPc. Through this methodology, oxygen-, sulphur- and nitrogen-based aromatic nucleophiles could be introduced at the apical position of the SubPc macrocycle.
Activation by AlCl3 was exploited some years later by Nielsen and co-workers for the axial ligand exchange reaction of SubPc-Cl with TMS-protected alkynes to afford axially alkynyl-substituted SubPcs (Scheme 4).87 This protocol allows for the use of milder reaction conditions (i.e. room temperature and absence of basic reagents) in comparison with the ones commonly employed for the axial functionalization of SubPcs with alkyne nucleophile (vide infra). Axial substitution was carried out through this methodology in the presence of alkynes bearing electron poor or electron rich aryl group, TMS-protected triple bonds, ferrocenyl moieties or azulenyl groups. Allyl-TMS was also found to react with SubPc-Cl under the same conditions, affording the corresponding SubPc featuring a B–C(sp3) bond (Scheme 6). In addition, a SubPc–Fc conjugate in which the Fc unit was directly anchored to the central boron through one of the cyclopentadienyl carbon atoms was prepared following the same procedure, with a significant improvement in yield with respect to a previously reported procedure involving the use of lithiating agents.88 In the attempt to obtain the axially substituted aryl-SubPc through formation of a direct B–C(sp2) bond by reaction of SubPc-Cl and 1,2,3-trimethoxybenzene under the same conditions, the corresponding boronic ester was formed instead, although in low amount, upon demethylation of central methoxy group and substitution of the axial halogen by the oxygen atom.87 The protocol presented by Nielsen has been exploited over the years for the preparation of intriguing SubPc derivatives and SubPc-containing multicomponent systems.56,58,75,89,90 Among them, it is worth to mention the multi-step preparation of a series of SubPc-based acetylenic scaffolds upon exploiting the connection of the central boron atom of two SubPc units to the geminal alkynyl position of a tetraethynylethene moiety, which acts as bridging unit (see Section 2.5.2.3 for further details).75
 Scheme 6  Reactions of H12SubPc-Cl with allyltrimethylsilane, ferrocene and 1,2,3-trimethoxybenzene nucleophiles in the presence of AlCl3.87 
Besides the approaches which involve the generation of intermediate activated species, several works have been published in which axial substitution in SubPcs is performed by direct reaction with specific nucleophiles. In general, harsher conditions are employed in these reactions. A summary of the older publications dealing with axial reactivity have been already reported by some of us.1 Here, the most common procedures employed for these axial substitution reactions are described, along with some recent examples.
2.2.1.1 Formation of B–O bonds. 
The replacement of the B–Y bond with a B–O bond represents by far the most common approach for axial functionalization of SubPcs. In particular, aryl alcohols constitute undoubtedly the most employed oxygen nucleophiles, as their reactions with SubPcs are generally simple and efficient. Moreover, the introduction of a phenol moiety in the axial position is known to improve the solubility and the stability of the SubPc conjugates. In this context, a report was presented in 2012 by Bender and co-workers in which the solubility of SubPcs in organic solvents was significantly tuned upon varying the number and position of methyl groups in the axial aryloxy moiety.91 As a matter of fact, 3-methylphenoxy- and 3,4-dimethylphenoxy-substituted SubPcs were found to be two orders of magnitude more soluble than 4-methylphenoxy- and phenoxy-substituted SubPcs in chloroform. The dramatic improvement of SubPc solubility in the presence of a meta methyl group on the aryloxy axial moiety was ascribed by the authors to symmetry factors.
Axial substitution of the axial chlorine atom with phenols is commonly carried out by refluxing the starting SubPc-Cl with the corresponding alcohol in a high-boiling point aromatic solvent such as toluene,46–48,50,53–56,58,70,73,76,91–117 1:1 toluene/THF mixture,118 chlorobenzene,17,119 dichlorobenzene (DCB)29,30 and o-xylene.120 Interestingly, reaction of SubPc-Cl, SubPc-Br and mesylated SubPcs with phenols in DMF was found to afford the corresponding formate derivative in addition to the target phenoxy conjugate and the hydrolyzed SubPc-OH product (vide infra).121 Axial substitution with phenols can be performed by a one-pot two-step procedure without purification of the chlorinated precursor, by adding the aryl alcohol and the solvent directly to the crude cyclotrimerization product.17,47,48,50,53,54,56,58,92,97,100,102,105,106,111,114,118,120 In some cases, a base such as 1,8-diazabicycloundec-7-ene (DBU),92,110,118 triethylamine,47,53,70,73,99,109 pyridine103,113 or DIPEA76 is added to neutralized the generated HCl. The use of microwave irradiation for axial substitution with hydroxy-pyridine in o-DCB has also been reported.29 Tin(IV) chloride has been used as activating agent in the same solvent at 60 °C.122
An in-depth investigation on the mechanism of the axial ligand exchange reaction between SubPc-Cl derivatives and phenols was reported in 2014 by Torres, Corral, González-Rodríguez and co-workers.123 In this work, a bimolecular σ-bond metathesis mechanism was postulated, in which the phenol assists in weakening the B–Cl bond by proton coordination concomitantly with the formation of the B–O bond. The peculiar chemical environment of the tetracoordinated boron atom in SubPc derivatives justifies such an intriguing mechanism, not common in the chemistry of boron derivatives. Considering the proposed mechanism, a number of experimental and theoretical findings could be rationalized. As a matter of fact, kinetic measurements showed that at low conversions a bimolecular second-order rate equation reliably fits the experimental data. DFT calculations confirmed the predicted individual rate orders, and revealed a double-well reaction profile involving a four-centered bimolecular transition state of the rate-controlling step in which two bonds (B–X and O–H) undergo dissociation and other two bonds (B–O and H–X) undergo formation in a concerted mechanism (Fig. 8). In addition, the effect of several parameters such as the nature of the SubPc axial and peripheral substituents, the electronic features of the phenol and the characteristics of the solvent on the rate of axial substitution were investigated. The axial ligand exchange reaction rate was found to increase upon changing the axial halogen atom in the order Br > Cl > F and in the presence of electron-donating groups at the SubPc periphery. Moreover, faster axial substitution reactions were detected in the presence of phenols bearing an electron-withdrawing group at the para position. Regarding solvent effects, increasing polarity resulted in an enhancement of the reaction rate, while the use of coordinating solvents led to slower reactions.
 Fig. 8  Double-well reaction profile postulated from DFT calculations for the substitution reaction between phenol and SubPc-Cl. Reproduced with permission from ref. 123. Copyright 2014 American Chemical Society. 
Based on these results, González-Rodríguez, Torres and co-workers presented in 2015 a novel methodology for axial substitution in SubPc-Cl derivatives based on reaction with TMS-protected nucleophiles, which allows for the efficient introduction of aryloxy substituents at the apical position of the macrocycle (Scheme 7).85 In these conditions, a volatile by-product (i.e. Me3SiCl) is generated, which pushes the equilibrium to the right and simplify the purification of the target SubPc derivatives. Moreover, the TMS functionality decreases the nucleophilicity of the substrate, reducing the generation of side products from SubPc decomposition. Similarly to what observed for the reaction of SubPc-Cl with phenols, in this one-step reaction a bimolecular four-centered transition state is formed, in which the electrophilic TMS functionality plays the role of the phenol proton assisting B–Cl bond dissociation through coordination of the halophilic Si center to the leaving chlorine atom concomitantly with the formation of the new B–Nu bond. As a result, a concerted rupture of the B–Cl and Si–O bonds and formation of the B–O and Si–Cl bonds is observed. Surprisingly, in the case of TMS-protected phenol, DFT calculations revealed an activation barrier higher than in the presence of the unprotected alcohol. Such a finding can be rationalized on the basis of the steric hindrance of the TMS moiety and is responsible for the smaller reaction rates observed in axial substitution reactions with TMS-protected phenol derivatives. On the other hand, in the case of aliphatic alcohols no reaction and/or decomposition or the macrocycle was observed.
 Scheme 7  Reaction between SubPc-Cl derivatives and TMS-nucleophiles to afford axially substituted SubPcs.85 
Several dimeric, oligomeric and polymeric systems such as the representative examples shown in Fig. 9 have been prepared by exploiting the axial ligand exchange reaction of SubPc-Cl with aryl alcohols featuring more than one OH group or with moieties bearing several phenolic functionalities. The synthetic strategies employed for their preparation as well as the main spectroscopic features of these derivatives will be discussed in Section 2.5.2.
 Fig. 9  Chemical structure of the SubPc-based systems 24–30 investigated by Shibata and co-workers. 
When the aryl alcohol is linked to a chromophoric unit – such as a BODIPY,98 an azaBODIPY,99,109 a perylenediimide,124 a Ps,14,125,126 a Pcs,76 a pentacene dimer,82 a diazobenzene24,127 or a dithienylethene moiety22 – a multicomponent system is obtained upon axial substitution of the SubPc moiety (see Section 2.6).
Axial substitution in SubPc dimers,128 SubPc fused dimers,48 as well as in SubPc-containing fused hybrid systems129 is realized under similar conditions to that employed in monomeric SubPcs.
Despite the higher reactivity of SubPc-Br precursors in axial ligand exchange reactions,123 axial substitution with aryl alcohols from axially brominated SubPcs is much less common than from chlorinated SubPc species, due to the drawbacks associated with the lower stability of the B–Br bond such as the formation of hydrolyzed SubPc species.96,111,121,122,130–133
Axial ligand exchange with alkyl alcohols proceeds in general less efficiently than with phenols due to the lower acidity of the hydroxylic proton, in line with the postulated mechanism for axial substitution proposed by Coral, González Rodríguez and co-workers.123
Substitution of the axial chlorine ligand in the presence of alkyl alcohols is normally carried out in boiling toluene, with reaction times varying from 24 and 72 hours.134–140 The use of higher boiling solvents such as o-DCB15 or the addition of a base to neutralize the generated HCl (e.g. K2CO3),136 have also been reported.
Surprisingly, axially 4-bromobutoxy-substituted SubPc has been detected as by-product in the reaction of H12SubPc-Br with aryl Grignard reagents in refluxing THF, as a result of the ring opening of the solvent.141 Such kind of reactivity was further investigated by Bender and co-workers extending the scope to different cyclic ether solvents bearing endocyclic C–O bonds.142 As a matter of fact, cleavage of one of the endocyclic C–O bond of the solvent and subsequent addition to the SubPc unit to afford the corresponding ring opened products 32–34 was observed in the presence of THF, 1,4-dioxane and γ-butyrolactone (Scheme 8). A σ-bond metathesis mechanism was proposed for this reaction, in line with the findings of the group of Torres.123 It is interesting to observe that in THF, C–O bond cleavage is not observed in absence of the Grignard reagent. On the other hand, in the presence of acyclic ethers (e.g. diethyl ether) or cyclic solvents featuring different heteroatoms (e.g. N-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone, morpholine) no conversion, partial hydrolysis or decomposition of the macrocycle was observed.
 Scheme 8  Reaction of SubPc-Br with solvents bearing an endocyclic C–O bond to yield ring opened products.141,142 
Replacement of the apical bromine atom with 4-hydroxy-TEMPO has been exploited to prepare the SubPc precursor for the selective functionalization of polystyrene and poly (n-butyl acrylate) ω-chain end (see Section 2.5.2.4 for further details).143,144 Reaction of SubPc-Br with carboxylic acid functionalities has also been employed by Bender and co-workers for the preparation of SubPc-containing polymers upon refluxing of the SubPc and the polymeric material in high boiling solvents.145
Axial substitution with polar aprotic solvents was investigated by Bender after detecting the formation of formate-substituted SubPc as by-product in the reaction of SubPc-Br with phenol in DMF.121 As a matter of fact, upon heating the brominated SubPc precursor at 100 °C in the presence of dimethyl amide-containing solvents such as DMF and DMAc, the formation of the corresponding SubPc-formate and SubPc-acetate derivatives (35 and 36) was observed (Scheme 9). Axial substitution with DMF and DMAc was found to occur also from the axially chloro-substituted and mesylate-substituted derivative, even if in smaller amount due to the lower reactivity of these precursors with respect to SubPc-Br. On the other hand, the attempts of axial substitution reaction in non-amide containing solvents such as acetone and N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone led to the hydrolysis or the degradation of the SubPc precursor, respectively.
 Scheme 9  Reaction of SubPc-Br with DMF and DMAc to yield formate- and acetate-substituted SubPcs.121 
SubPc-OH species have been synthesized upon heating under reflux a suspension of the starting axially halogenated SubPc in water,146 pyridine/water,147 DMSO/pyridine/water,130,148 acetonitrile/water149 or acetone/water121 mixtures. A silanoxy SubPc derivative was prepared by Bender and co-workers from the hydroxy-substituted precursor upon reaction with the corresponding chlorosilane in o-DCB.150 μ-Oxo SubPc homo- and heterodimers have been prepared by self-condensation of SubPc-OH derivatives or through reaction of SubPc-OH derivatives with axially halogenated SubPcs (for further details see Section 2.5.2.1).151–153
2.2.1.2 Formation of B–N bonds. 
The introduction of N-based ligand at the apical position of the SubPcs macrocycle is commonly accomplished through activation of the SubPc precursor (vide supra).65,68 As an exception, the direct reaction of phthalimides with SubPc-Cl in refluxing dichlorobenzene for prolonged reaction times (i.e. 90–100 hours) was reported in 2011 by Bender and co-workers.154 Recently, García and co-workers reported the formation of B–N bonds between encapsulated SubPcs and the amino functionalities of the host MIL-101(Cr)-NH2 metal–organic framework (MOF) upon refluxing the suspension containing the active MOF and the SubPc-Cl precursor in toluene during 48 hours.155 A novel SubPc-azido derivative was obtained from the reaction of a SubPc-Cl precursor with Me3SiN3 in refluxing toluene.85 Surprisingly, the product turned out to be perfectly stable, even if the reactivity of the axial azido group was found to be different from the expected probably due to the electronic and steric influence of the SubPc macrocycle.
2.2.1.3 Formation of B–C bonds. 
Formation of B–C bonds has been also widely employed as a strategy for the axial derivatization of SubPc chromophores. A common approach for the introduction of ethynyl functionalities in the axial position of the macrocycle involves the reaction of a SubPc-Cl precursor with a Grignard reagent which is formed in situ upon treatment of a terminal alkyne with EtMgBr, as reported in the pivotal works of Ziessel and co-workers (Scheme 10).156 In this protocol, both the formation of the Grignard derivative of the acetylenic precursor and the replacement of the axial chloride ligand are carried out in THF at 60 °C. By reacting their Grignard-derivatives with axially chlorinated SubPcs, ethynyl-ferrocene,88 ethynyl-aniline43,46 and ethynyl-pyrene157 moieties have been successfully introduced at the apical position of the macrocycle. For the introduction of ethynyl-ferrocene, the reaction with the starting SubPc-Cl was carried out at room temperature.88
 Scheme 10  Preparation of ethynylaryl-substituted SubPcs from reaction of SubPc-Cl and Grignard reagents.156 
The direct synthesis of a SubPc–Fc dyad featuring a direct bond between the boron and a carbon atom of one of the two cyclopentadienyl rings has also reported.88 In this case, the chloro precursor was reacted at room temperature with ferrocenyl lithium, which was prepared by treatment of bromoferrocene with BuLi in THF at −78 °C.
The synthesis of aryl-functionalized SubPcs by axial substitution reaction of the precursor SubPc-Br with aryl moieties bearing a hydrogen, methyl, methoxy, phenyl or fluoro substituent at the para position of the ring was reported in 2015 by Bender and co-workers.141 In this work, the replacement of the axial bromine atom was carried out upon refluxing in THF the SubPc precursor with aryl magnesium bromide reagents. The formation of SubPc-4-bromobutoxy as side product was proposed as a rationale for the low yields observed in these reaction, and was deemed to prevent the formation of the target products in the presence of tri- and penta-fluorinated aryl precursors.
Remarkably, a nitrile functionality could be introduced for the first time at the axial position of the SubPc macrocycle through reaction of axially chlorinated SubPcs with Me3SiCN, as reported by Guilleme et al.85 On the other hand, reaction of SubPc-Cl precursors with the TMS derivatives of other carbon nucleophiles following the same procedure was found to led to the decomposition of the macrocycle. Recently, the formation of a SubPc-CN derivative upon reaction of SubPc-Cl with cyanide in the presence of cryptand[2.2.2] has also been inferred.158
2.2.1.4 Formation of B–F, B–Cl and B–Br bonds. 
The formation of boron-halogen bonds through ligand exchange from a different halogenated SubPc derivative or by axial substitution of SubPc-OAr precursors constitutes another common reaction of SubPcs. Axially fluorinated SubPcs have been prepared from the corresponding SubPc-Cl, SubPc-Br and SubPc-OAr precursors by treatment with a large excess of BF3·OEt2.49,65,102,159,160 SubPc-F derivatives can also be prepared from the chlorinated precursors in the presence of AgBF4.13 On the other hand, chloro-substituted SubPcs have been obtained from SubPc-OH, SubPc-OPh or SubPc-Br derivatives in the presence of BCl3, AlCl366 or p-toluenesulfonyl chloride (TsCl).161
Unexpected axial halide exchange reactions have been observed during SubPcs synthesis by Bender and co-workers.30,32 In the attempt to synthetize Cl6SubPc-Br from 4,5-dichlorophthalonitrile in the presence of BBr3, the concomitant formation of the axially chlorinated conjugate was observed (Scheme 2).30 The authors suggested the solvent rather than the phthalonitrile precursor as the source of chloride, as no peripherally brominated products were detected. A similar halogen exchange was observed a few years later during the synthesis of F6SubPc-Cl and F12SubPc-Cl from 4,5-difluorophthalonitrile and 3,4,5,6-tetrafluorophthalonitrile in the presence of BCl3.32 In this case, a mixture of axially fluorinated and chlorinated SubPcs was obtained (Scheme 3). The degradation of the precursor phthalonitrile was inferred as a rationale for this experimental finding. The reaction of the resulting SubPcs mixture with BF3·OEt2 by the established halide exchange protocol,65 which involves the use of 25 equiv. of boron halide, did not afford a solid product. Instead, completion of the fluoride exchange was accomplished by diminishing the equivalents of BF3·Et2O to 10. For what concerns work-up procedures, short-path distillation of the excess of boron reagent was found to be the best method to obtain SubPc-F derivatives in high yield and purity.32
2.2.1.5 Formation of B–H bonds. 
The preparation of SubPc-hydride derivatives has been recently accomplished by Torres, Martínez-Díaz and co-workers.162 Such derivatives were prepared employing DIBAL-H as hydride donor which, due to its empty π-orbital, is able to coordinate the apical chlorine atom thus assisting the axial exchange. Importantly, these derivatives were found to be effective reagents for the hydroboration of aldehydes in the presence of catalytic amounts of a carbonyl activator (e.g., TMSOTf) or a hydride scavenger (e.g., Ph3C[B(C6F5)4]).
2.2.1.6 Formation of B–B bonds. 
The only example of B–B bond formation in SubPcs was reported by Torres and co-workers in 2007 and consists in the preparation of a SubPc dimer via reductive Wurtz coupling of SubPc-Cl.163
2.2.1.7 Reactions on the axial substituent not involving the B–Y bond. 
Besides reactions of axial substitution at the boron atom, another class of reactions can be distinguished which result in the chemical modification of the axial substituent but not involve the apical B–Y linkage. While the former are specific of SubPc chemistry, the latter are conventional reactions. Hereafter, an overview of the reaction on the axial substituents not involving the B–Y bond is reported.
The reaction between SubPcs bearing axial hydroquinone and phloroglucinol functionalities with SubPc-Cl precursors94 or the corresponding activated triflate derivatives70,73 has been exploited for the preparation of dimeric or trimeric SubPc species (25–30, Fig. 9). Axial alkyl alcohol substituents have been employed to replace the axial chlorine atom in SubPc-Cl derivatives leading to the formation of SubPc-stoppered rotaxanes, even if in very low yields.79 Axially hydroquinone-substituted SubPcs have also been employed in nucleophilic substitution reactions.108 Prato reaction involving the axial hydroxybenzaldehyde group of SubPc precursors89,93,104,105,107,164–166 constitute a common approach for the obtention of fullerene-containing multicomponent systems based on SubPcs. Treatment with sodium chlorite in the presence of sulfamic acid as hypochlorite scavenger has been employed for the oxidation of the axial hydroxybenzaldehyde group to the carboxyl derivative.47,117 Reduction of the axial nitrophenoxy group with molecular hydrogen,97,100 alkylation of the axial aminophenoxy ligand with propanesultone to afford the corresponding alkylsulfonate species97 and quaternization of tertiary amines in the axial moiety with methyl iodide134 have also been reported, as well as acylation with protected acids using EDC and NHS as coupling agents100 or reaction with succinic anhydride.108 The amino functionality of the apical aminophenoxy group has been used as nucleophile with triflate-activated SubPcs to form nitrogen-bridged SubPc dimers.74 Reaction of SubPc-TEMPO at the ω-chain end of polystyrene and poly(n-butyl acrylate) produced by nitroxide mediate polymerization has also been performed.143 Upon probing nucleophilic addition or hydrolysis of the axial cyano functionality as well as reduction of either cyano or azide moieties directly linked to the boron atom of SubPcs no reaction or decomposition of the macrocycle have been observed.85 Copper(I)-catalyzed azide-alkyne cycloaddition reactions (CuAAC) have been carried out from axially azido-substituted SubPcs85,138 and ethynyl-substituted SubPcs.72,136,138–140,167,168 SubPcs with terminal alkyne substituent have been employed in Pd-catalyzed oxidative Glaser–Hay coupling,72,75,138 modified Cadiot–Chodkiewicz coupling72,75,90 and Sonogashira coupling57,58,72,75,90 reactions. Sonogashira cross-coupling reactions from the apical bromophenoxy group169 and Suzuki cross-coupling either from iodophenoxy or borylated phenylacetylene with boronic acid and halogenated derivatives170,171 have also been carried out. Recently, cycloadditon–retroelectrocyclization (CA–RE) reaction of axial ethynyl-aniline,43,46 ethynylferrocene and butadyineferrocene56 ligands with tetracyanoethylene to afford TCBD–aniline (10 and 11, Fig. 6), TCBD–ferrocene and ethynyl–TCBD–ferrocene SubPc derivatives have been reported by the groups of Torres and Nielsen.


2.2.2 Peripheral reactivity. 
Unlike axial substituents, which usually have no or very little impact on the electronic characteristics of the SubPc macrocycle, peripheral substituents can produce strong perturbations on the system which are reflected on the spectroscopic and electrochemical properties of SubPc derivatives. The harsh conditions required for SubPc synthesis limit the functional groups that can be introduced in the starting phthalonitrile, since many functionalities are incompatible with the boron reagents employed. As a matter of fact, the peripheral groups that can be introduced directly through SubPc synthesis are restricted to halogen atoms, nitro groups, amines, alkyl groups, ethers, thioethers or sulphones. For this reason, chemical modification of the peripheral substituent of the SubPc preformed ring is of paramount importance.
Whereas axial ligand exchange reactions are exclusive of SubPc chemistry, peripheral reactivity involves common organic reactions. Anyway, it is worth to mention that due to the chemical instability of SubPcs, the experimental conditions need to be carefully selected in order to prevent the decomposition of the SubPc macrocycle. As a matter of fact, in presence of strong nucleophile the macrocyclic ring is often destroyed.
Several protocols have been employed for the derivatization of the SubPc peripheral benzene rings including metal-catalyzed cross-coupling reactions (i.e. Sonogashira, Suzuki, Stille, Hartwig–Buchwald and borylation reactions), alkylation of pyridine groups, acylation of hydroxy derivatives, deprotection reactions of ester and ether functionalities, oxidation of sulfides, reduction of triple bonds, and cyclopropanation reactions to link fullerene moieties, which have been previously reviewed from some of us.1 Here, the publications dealing with SubPc peripheral reactivity which are subsequent to our previous review on contracted poprhyrinoids1 are summarized.11 Sonogashira coupling reactions of peripherally iodo-substituted SubPc precursors in the presence of ethynyl-pyridine,92 propargylic acid,47 ethynyl-benzoic acid,47,50 4-(7-ethynyl-2,1,3-benzothiadiazol-4-yl) ethynyl-benzoic acid,55 tris(dodecyloxy)phenylacetylene,102 ethynyl-thiophene derivatives,171 ethynyltrimethylsilane and (triisopropylsilyl)acetylene,75 a tetraethynylethene derivative,53 1,2-diethynyl benzene,172 ethynyl-N,N-dimethylaniline,46,55 trimethylsilyl-protected ethynylferrocene and butadyineferrocene,56 ethynyl perylenediimide precursors,173 ethynylated dihydroazulene,57 and a bis(ethynyl)boron-functionalized SubPc dimer58 have been reported, as well as the coupling between 1,4-diethynyl benzene and the peripherally bromo-substituted SubPc precursors.169 Suzuki reactions of pyreneboronic acid and phenylboronic acid tethered pyrene with iodophenoxy-SubPcs,164 of ferroceneboronic acid111 and aminophenylboronic acid pinacol ester174 with iodo-SubPcs, of thiophene boronic acid pinacol ester derivatives with bromo-SubPcs,116 and of bromo terpyridine with borylated SubPc species15 have also been published. Other publications reported the Heck coupling between vinyl-pyridine and iodo-substituted SubPc precursors,118 the Stille coupling between trimethyl (4-pyridyl) tin and iodo-SubPc derivatives,113 the Hartwig–Buchwald reaction of tri(dodecyloxy)benzamide with iodo-substituted SubPc precursors37 and of phenothiazine with iodophenoxy-93 and iodo-substituted SubPc species.104 Borylation reaction of iodo-SubPcs with bis(pinacolato)diboron15 and Knoevenagel condensation on thiophene-carbaldehyde substituted SubPcs116 have also been reported.
Despite the sensitiveness of SubPcs to the cyanide anion, an efficient method for the preparation of peripherally cyano-substituted SubPcs (37–47, Fig. 10) from the corresponding iodo-precursors based on a mild palladium-mediated coupling reaction in DMF in the presence of zinc cyanide has been reported by the group of Torres.48,49,54 In this approach, microwave irradiation was found to be fundamental to obtain a complete conversion in short reaction times, limiting SubPc decomposition. Cyanation of peripherally hydrogenated SubPcs or SubPc functionalized with peripheral electron-rich groups was found to provide better yields than cyanation of SubPcs bearing peripheral electron-withdrawing substituents. This behaviour was attributed to the ring-opening side reaction of the SubPc macrocycle subsequent to the nucleophilic attack of the cyanide anion on SubPcs substituted with electron-poor groups.54
 Fig. 10  Chemical structure of cyanated-SubPcs 37–47.48,49,54 
Methylation of pyridine118 and sulfides18 with methyl iodine have been employed for the preparation of cationic SubPcs. Cycloaddition–retroelectrocyclization (CA–RE) reaction with tetracyanoethylene of ethynyl-aniline SubPc precursors to obtain the corresponding TCBD–aniline derivatives (10 and 11, Fig. 6)46,55 and of ethynylferrocene- and butadiyneferrocene-substituted precursors to form the corresponding TCBD–ferrocene and aniline-TCBD–ferrocene conjugates56 have been recently exploited by the groups of Torres and Nielsen. Deprotection of silyl ether groups in the presence of CsF in acetone and subsequent reaction of the hydroxy-substituted SubPc with 1,4-benzene diboronic acid have also been reported.120


2.2.3 Ring-expansion reactions. 
The preparation of unsymmetrically substituted Pcs via ring-opening reaction of SubPc derivatives was first reported by Kobayashi et al. in 1990 (Scheme 11).175 In the attempt to synthesize a SubPc derivative according to the procedure published by Meller and Ossko,10 the authors decided to trace the reaction and found that it always yielded a blue compound as byproduct, which was later identified as a Pc. This work triggered the interest of the porphyrinoids community on the chemistry of the three-isoindole-based SubPc macrocycle, constituting the practical start of subporphyrinoids chemistry.
 Scheme 11  General reaction scheme for ring-expansion reactions. 
Many efforts have been dedicated to the study of this type of reactivity after its discovery. As a result, different reaction conditions (e.g. the nature of the SubPc and the diiminoisoindoline/phthalonitrile precursors, solvent and temperature) have been explored, and the factors affecting the efficiency of the reaction have been identified.1 Although the use of SubPcs as building blocks for the synthesis of non-symmetrical Pcs has constituted during several years the main application of these contracted macrocycles, during the last couple of decades the attention toward this class of reactions have diminished substantially. However, a few reports on SubPc ring-expansion reactions have been published during the last years.11,176 In 2015, the first chiral unsymmetrical Pc prepared through SubPc ring-expansion reaction was synthetized via reaction of achiral H12SubPc-OPh and an optically active binaphthyl-functionalized diiminoisoindoline.177 In the same year, novel Pcs bearing six aryl-thioether groups were prepared from ring-expansion reaction of peripherally-substituted SubPcs and diiminoisoindoline.16 Pcs functionalized with aryl alcohols were also prepared from unsubstituted SubPc-Cl and the corresponding phthalonitrile precursors.28

2.3 Characterization of subphthalocyanines

2.3.1 Single-crystal X-ray diffraction. 
In contrast to Pcs, which are characterized by a flat or nearly flat structure, SubPcs feature a non-planar, cone-shaped geometry, as confirmed by single-crystal X-ray diffraction analysis. The nature of the axial and peripheral substituents does not practically affect the position of the nitrogen and carbon atoms of the ring, with the exception of slight variations of the bond lengths and bond angles at the level of the Y(Ni)3 tetrahedron, where Y indicates the apical ligand and Ni refers to the isoindolic nitrogen atoms.1 As a general trend, the B–Y bond length increases descending along the halogen series, while it does not practically depend on the peripheral substitution pattern of the macrocycle. The bond angles involving the central boron atom, which are slightly deformed with respect to that of an ideal tetrahedron, also feature small variations upon varying the nature of the axial ligand.
A comprehensive study of the crystal structures of axially and peripherally halogenated SubPcs has been reported in 2014 by Bender and co-workers.30 As expected, the axial B–Y bond length was found to be almost independent on the nature of the peripheral substituents, while it was observed to be greater in the axially brominated derivatives with respect to their chlorinated analogues. The axial B–O bond length in SubPcs axially substituted with a pentafluorophenoxy ligand also showed minimal variations upon modification of the peripheral substituents. In all the investigated structures, the distances between the boron atom and the planes defined by the inner N atoms and the outer N atoms showed negligible variations. On the other hand, the distance between the boron atom and the plane of the six outer C atoms showed higher perturbations. This evidence was ascribed to π–π and halogen–π interactions in the crystal systems. A similar effect was observed in SubPcs axially functionalized with hydroxy-phenoxy moieties, in which distorted B–O–C angles were detected as a consequence of H-bonding interactions.130 The sensitiveness to strong intermolecular interactions of the angle between the apical phenoxy ligand relative and the SubPc macrocycle had previously been observed in the presence of strong halogen bonds.178
In 2016, Bender and co-workers redetermined the crystal structure of the prototypical H12SubPc-Cl derivative 20, which was originally obtained by solvent crystallization methods,179 using crystals grown through slow train sublimation at different temperatures.180 The use of an improved train sublimation apparatus enabling low temperature ramping rates allowed to obtain relatively large (i.e. up to 0.8 mm) and high-quality single crystals. Moreover, the exploitation of the train sublimation technique for the obtention of crystal is of particular interest in view of the potential applications of SubPcs as molecular materials, as it well approximates the vacuum deposition conditions normally employed for the preparation of organic electronic devices. The obtained results confirmed that previously obtained by solution-growth methods,179,181 although the precision of the geometric parameters was significantly improved, and lower R values were determined. In particular, intramolecular metrics were found to be highly consistent with the reported data. The small differences in the unit-cell parameters were therefore ascribed to differences in the intermolecular distances.
The structural parameters of a series of SubPc X-ray structures have been previously reviewed by some of us.1 Several novel crystal structures of SubPc derivatives have been published since then, which are reported in ref. 17, 19, 30, 32, 43, 56, 57, 79, 85–88, 91, 96, 97, 108, 111, 114, 121, 130, 132, 141, 143, 145, 165, 172, 180 and 182–185. Selected data from X-ray diffraction analysis of some of these crystal structures are reported in Table 1. Among them, it is worth to mention that of perfluorinated and perhydrogenated SubPc-OTf derivatives 14 and 54 (Scheme 5), which constitute strategic activated species in axial substitution reactions (see Section 2.2.1).85,86 In 2017, the resolution of the crystal structure of isolated enantiomers of H12SubPc and F12SubPc species functionalized at the apical position of the macrocycle with an axially chiral TCBD–aniline moiety (10 and 11, Fig. 6) was reported by Torres and co-workers.43 Remarkably, the X-ray crystal structure of a SubPc cation having a weakly coordinating carborane anion has also been reported, showing the tendency of the central boron to adopt a planar geometry, as expected for a tricoordinated B atom.186 The crystal structures of some covalently linked SubPc dimers72,75,79,127,128,151,185 and fused SubPc-containing hybrid dimers129,187,188 have also been reported.

2.3.2 Mass spectrometry. 
SubPcs generally afford very clean mass spectra in which the major peak corresponds to the molecular ion. The peak originating from the loss of the axial ligand is also quite commonly observed.


2.3.3 NMR spectroscopy. 
Unlike Pcs, which 1H-NMR spectra are generally broad due to aggregation issues, SubPcs do not tend to form aggregates in solution. Therefore, their 1H-NMR spectra are usually characterized by sharp peaks which chemical shifts do not depend on the concentration of the sample. Due to the diatropic ring current generated by the 14 π-electron aromatic system, the signals of the protons of the isoindolic benzene rings appear at low fields (generally between 7.8 and 9.9 ppm), while the signals of the protons of the axial substituent are shifted upfield with respect to that of the corresponding free ligand. As an example, the axial phenyl group in H12SubPc–Ph gives rise to three signals at 6.63, 6.51 and 5.37 ppm, being the latter the one corresponding to the closest proton to the SubPc core, whereas the signals of the ortho and meta protons of the macrocyclic framework appear at 8.78–8.76 and 7.81–7.79 ppm, respectively.68 The axial hydroxy substituent yields a broad signal at −0.58 ppm, while a methoxy group linked to the boron atom gives a signal at 1.50 ppm.68
The effect of the diatropic ring current of the macrocycle on the ring substituents allows to differentiate the signals of the protons located “inside” or “outside” the bowl-shaped SubPc framework. For peripherally binaphthyloxy-substituted SubPc 9 (Fig. 5) two sets of peaks were observed for the protons of the ring substituents, as a consequence of the location of the naphthalene rings at both the endo and the exo sides of the macrocycle.20 A similar effect was detected in the low-temperature NMR spectrum of a SubPc peripherally substituted with six 2′,6′-di-iso-propylphenoxy moieties.19 In the case of SubPc π-complexes featuring a Cp*Ru+ unit coordinated to either the concave or the convex face of the macrocycle (63–66, Fig. 12), a notable diatropic effect on the Cp* moiety was observed in the case of the endo-coordinated compound, while an exceptionally small shift was detected for the exo-coordinated complex.63
Peripheral substituents influence the chemical shifts of the isoindolic benzene protons in a predictable way, according to their electronic nature. On the contrary, the effect of the axial ligand is not as clear, although differences in the chemical shifts of the isoindolic protons of SubPcs axially substituted with different moieties suggest the existence of a communication between the aromatic macrocycle and the axial substituent.68 As a matter of fact, the chemical shifts of the aromatic protons of the SubPc macrocycle follow the order δH (YB = Br) > δH (YB = O) > δH (YB = Cl), where represents the atom of the axial substituent directly linked to the boron atom. Notably, the variation of the axial ligand from an alkoxy (YB = O) to a thioalkoxy (YB = S) or an amino (YB = N) moiety does not influence in an appreciable way the chemical shift of the peaks corresponding to the benzo protons.68 Upon addition of acids, a downfield shift of the signals corresponding to the SubPc benzene rings was reported by Decréau and co-workers.67 The protonation of the azomethine bridges was proposed as a possible rationale, although peaks corresponding to iminium-like protonated bridges were not identified.
The central boron generally gives rise to a sharp singlet located between −13 and −20 ppm in the corresponding 11B–NMR spectra, in accordance with the expected chemical shift for a tetrasubstituted boron atom. In the 11B–NMR spectra of SubPcs axially substituted with a fluorine atom, a doublet (J ≈ 30 Hz) is observed as a consequence of the B–F coupling.159 Both peripheral and axial substituents have an appreciable influence on the chemical shift of the peak corresponding to the boron atom. As an example, in the peripherally perfluorinated SubPc-Cl derivative the boron signal is upfield by approximately 0.4 ppm with respect to the corresponding hydrogenated analogue.159 Regarding axial substitution, the boron signal appears at 13.3, −13.8, −14.3, −15.1 and −15.1 ppm, respectively, in thio-, chloro-, fluoro-, aryloxy- and amino-substituted SubPcs.68,159 Although the origin of these small variations is not clear, the trend reflects the different extent of π back-donation from the axial ligand to the boron atom. In SubPcs featuring a B–C bond, the boron signal is generally located between −15 and −18 ppm.68,156 In particular, the chemical shift is related to the hybridation of the carbon linked to the boron atom, being the order δ (Csp3) > δ (Csp2) > δ (Csp).68
In 19F-NMR spectra of SubPc-F derivatives, a quartet centered between −160 and −155 ppm is originated by the axial fluorine substituent due to the coupling with the boron atom.49,67,102,159


2.3.4 IR spectroscopy. 
Infrared (IR) spectra of SubPcs are dominated by the bands arising from the bending and the stretching of the C–C and the C–N bonds of the macrocycle.
