The assignation of the stretching band corresponding to the B–Y bond was first made possible by substituting the central boron atom with the 10B isotope and monitoring the changes in the IR spectra.189 For SubPcs bearing an axial halogen atom, a decrease of the B–Y stretching wavenumber is observed descending along the series, along with the decreasing electronegativity of the substituent. Values of 1063, 960 and 622 cm−1 were reported, respectively, for the B–F, B–Cl and B–Br stretching in peripherally perhydrogenated SubPc derivatives. Concerning SubPcs axially substituted with alkyloxy or aryloxy ligands, the B–O stretching originates a band at 1119 cm−1 for Y = OtBu, 1107 cm−1 for Y = OiPr, 1052 cm−1 for Y = OPh and 1067 cm−1 for Y = O-p-C6H4–OH in the corresponding H12-SubPc species. In the presence of a thiophenoxy axial substituent, a band at 962 cm−1 is observed for the B–S stretching. Such a decrease (90 cm−1) in the B–Y stretching wavelength when shifting from oxygen to sulfur is in line with the trend observed within the halogen series. On the other hand, in the presence of axial amino substituents, B–N stretching bands between 1060 and 1107 cm−1 could be observed.68 In the infrared spectrum of the H12SubPc derivative axially substituted with an acetate group (Y = OCOCH3), bands at 1702 and 1277 cm−1 were found for the ν(C–O) and the ν(CO) stretching, respectively.189 The difference between ν(CO) and ν(C–O) wavenumbers is in line with the monodentate ligand character of the apical substituent.189 In the presence of TFA and its deuterated analogue, new features were observed in the IR of SubPc-Cl derivatives (i.e. H12SubPc-Cl and (NO2)3SubPc-Cl). The appearance of the N–H and N–D stretching bands of the iminium species formed upon protonation and deuteration of the SubPc azomethine bridges was suggested by the authors as a possible rationale for this experimental finding.67


2.3.5 UV-vis absorption spectroscopy. 
The absorption spectra of SubPcs are dominated by two intense bands in the 260–370 nm region and the 460–580 nm region. The absorption at lower wavelengths (i.e. Soret band or B band) originates from the S0 → S2 transition, while the absorption at higher wavelengths (i.e. Q band) is attributable to the S0 → S1 transition. Shoulders of the Q band associated to vibronic transitions are also commonly observed. Although the overall shape of the UV-vis spectra of SubPcs is similar to that of Pc derivatives, both the B and the Q band are blue-shifted with respect to the corresponding Pc transitions as a consequence of the smaller π-conjugated system (i.e. 14 π-electrons in SubPcs vs. 18 π-electrons in Pcs). The molar extinction coefficients (ε) of the main bands are lower for SubPc than for Pcs, being the ε value of the SubPc Q band of the order of 5–6 × 104 dm3 mol−1 cm−1. Such a difference in the absorption coefficient of the Q band could be related to the non-planar geometry of the SubPc macrocycle. With some exceptions,37,38 concentration effects on the position and shape of the absorption bands are commonly absent, confirming the low tendency of SubPcs towards aggregation in solution. The effect of SubPc substituents on the absorption characteristics of these macrocycles greatly depends on the functionalization site. A review on the impact of the macrocycle functionalization pattern on the optical properties of SubPcs has been recently reported by Dowds and Nielsen.62 In general, axial ligands do not affect or have a very small effect on the absorption maxima (see for example ref. 1, 32, 110, 122, 132 and 141). On the other hand, peripheral substituents have appreciable effects on the position of the absorption bands. As a matter of fact, in the presence of both electron-donor (see for example ref. 16, 19, 20, 23, 35, 46, 47, 50, 51, 55, 105 and 118) and electron-withdrawing (see for example ref. 29, 32, 50, 52, 80, 114 and 122) peripheral substituents able to slightly extend the π-conjugation of the macrocycle, the Q band is commonly shifted to higher wavelengths with respect to the perhydrogenated SubPc derivative. When the macrocycle is substituted with π-donor atoms (e.g. oxygen, nitrogen or sulfur), as in the case of ether-, amino- or thioether-substituted SubPcs, this effect is generally stronger. In this case, the shift to the red of the Q band increases with the π-donating characteristics of the atom linked to the macrocycle and the number of π-donor peripheral substituents. Furthermore, in the presence of alkoxy, sulfonyl, thioether or amino substituents, additional bands in the 400–500 nm region originating from n → π transitions are observed. A red shift of the Q band is observed also in SubPc analogues (e.g. SubNcs) characterized by an extended conjugated system and in SubPc fused dimers (see Sections 2.5.1 and 4.2).
An extraordinarily red-shifted absorption (Δλ = 87–94 nm with respect to H12SubPc-Cl) was reported by Bender and co-workers for SubPcs substituted with six catechol moieties (e.g.51, Fig. 11), which was rationalized by the authors as the result of the formation of an extended pseudo-π-electron system, rather than as simple substitution effect.17 Recently, the effect of peripheral cyano groups on the absorption characteristics of SubPcs has been investigated by Martínez Díaz, Seki, Cheyns, Torres and co-workers.48,49,54 Intriguingly, the electronic properties of the SubPc macrocycle were found to depend on the number and relative position of the cyano substituents. As a matter of fact, in unsymmetrical ortho-dicyano and bis-ortho-dicyano SubPcs 37–46 (Fig. 10) a remarkable bathochromic shift (15–29 nm) of the absorption maxima was observed along with an increase of the extinction coefficients (with values in the order of 8–10 × 104 dm3 mol−1 cm−1) in comparison with the precursor halogenated derivatives. These variations can be rationalized of the basis of the extension of the π-conjugation of the system. Surprisingly, the UV-vis spectrum of symmetrical tricyano-SubPc 47 does not present any change in the position of the absorption maxima with respect to the halogenated precursor, although higher ε values can be observed.
 Fig. 11  Chemical structure of some selected SubPcs which structural data from X-ray diffraction analysis are reported in Table 1. 
 Fig. 12  Chemical structure of SubPc π-complexes 63–66 reported by Sessler, Guldi, Torres and co-workers.63 
In the presence of organic acids, a new high-wavelength absorption bands can be observed in the UV-vis spectra of SubPc derivatives (e.g. at 610, 631 and 665 nm for H12SubPc-Cl) along with a decrease of the intensity of the SubPc Q absorption, probably as a consequence of the protonation of the azomethine bridges.64,67
In SubPc-based multicomponent systems, ligand-centered transitions or other photophysical events (e.g. ground-state charge transfer processes or other photophysical events (exciplex formation) can take place, leading to peculiar features in the absorption spectra of these conjugates. A detailed description of these systems is provided in Section 2.6.
In the solid state, similar absorption spectra are observed to those obtained in solution. In general, a redshift of the UV-vis maxima along with a broadening of the Q band in observed in SubPc films.25,71,101,110,116,118,173,190 The electroabsorption spectra of Pc-doped SubPcs in PMMA films have also been measured.191
Recently, the absorption properties of different SubPc light harvesters coupled to metallic optical cavities have also been investigated.192,193 In the case of a TCBD-substituted derivative, different coupling regimes of the two characteristic electronic transitions (i.e., the Q band transition and the CT transition) were observed as a direct consequence of their different oscillator strength, resulting in very different spectral and directional light-harvesting features.192


2.3.6 CD spectroscopy. 
Circular dichroism (CD) measurements of enantiopure SubPc derivatives have also been reported. In 2002, Kobayashi and co-workers published the first CD spectra of enantiomerically pure SubPcs.31 In the investigated C3-symmetric derivatives, peripheral substitution with nitro and t-butyl groups was aimed to increase the intensity of the CD signal and the solubility of the compounds, respectively. In 2003, the synthesis of an optically active SubPc dimer featuring a chiral (R)-(+)-BINOL moiety as linker (8, Fig. 5) was presented.41 CD measurements on this derivative evidenced an induced CD on the SubPc by the binaphthyl unit. Similarly, in the CD spectra of a couple of chiral SubPcs bearing three peripheral (R)-2,2′-binaphthyloxy and (S)-2,2′-binaphthyloxy moieties, respectively (9R/S, Fig. 5), the detected signals in the 260–600 nm region were attributed to induced CD generated by the chiral ligands.20 In 2011, the correlation between CD spectra and absolute configuration was unambiguously established for the isolated enantiomers of a C3-symmetric and a C1-symmetric 1,2-SubNc derivatives (see Scheme 31 in Section 4.1) on the basis of single-crystal X-ray diffraction analysis.36
Recently, Torres and co-workers reported the synthesis and optical resolution of axially chiral SubPcs bearing a TCBD–aniline moiety at the apical position of the macrocycle (10 and 11, Fig. 6).43 As a consequence of the hindered rotation around the central C–C bond of the TCBD framework, these conjugates are formed as racemic mixtures of two enantiomers, which were separated by chiral HPLC. The CD spectra of the isolated atropisomers confirmed their enantiomeric relationship. The resolution of the single-crystal X-ray structure of the enantiopure species allowed to unambiguously correlate for the first time in SubPc-based and TCBD-based chemistry the CD spectra and absolute configuration of chiral SubPc derivatives. Moreover, CD measurements at different temperature over time in the presence or absence of stimuli (i.e. light, molecular oxygen) were performed on enantiopure samples of 10 and 11 in order to shed light on the racemization behavior of the investigated derivatives and determine the activation parameters for the racemization process.44 These studies, along with theoretical calculations, revealed a peculiar light-triggered enantiomer conversion mechanism enabled by triplet-state photogeneration, which can be rationalized on the basis of energetic, structural and kinetic considerations. The thermal configurational stability of the isolated enantiomers of 10 and 11 is in contrast with what reported in literature for a C60-TCBD–aniline analogue and what observed for a SubP derivative functionalized at the axial position with a TCBD–aniline unit, for which a thermally induced racemization was observed.45,194 As such, The peculiar racemization behavior of the investigated SubPc–TCBD–aniline derivatives render them intriguing photoresponsive systems for potential applications in the fast-growing field of light-controllable molecular switches.
Finally, CD measurements have also been employed to investigate the solution self-assembly of C3-symmetric SubPcs to form non-centrosymmetric columnar assemblies or dimeric structures.37,38 The results of these studies are discussed in detail in Section 2.4.4.1.


2.3.7 Excited states. 
Emission spectra of SubPcs are commonly the mirror image of the absorption Q-band. Low-symmetry substituted SubPcs, which usually present a split Q-band, may represent an exception to this rule.195,196 SubPc emission maxima are generally located between 570 and 620 nm, and are shifted accordingly to the absorption Q-band maxima as a function of the substitution pattern of the SubPc macrocycle.1 In general, very small Stoke shifts (around 10–15 nm) are observed, along with unusually narrow widths at half of the maximum intensity. Such spectral features suggest an only slight geometric distortion between the ground state and the singlet excited state as well as a narrow density of states of the HOMO and LUMO levels. Normally, modulation of solvent polarity does not lead to systematic changes in the position of the absorption and emission maxima, indicating a negligible variation of the dipole moment between the ground state and the singlet excited state. Singlet excited state energies of approximately 2.1–2.0 eV are generally calculated for SubPc derivatives from the intersection between the absorption Q band and the emission band, which are consistent with the HOMO–LUMO gaps calculated from cyclic voltammetry studies. Anyway, lower values have been observed in specific cases (e.g. in the presence of diphenylamino substituents).197 In general, these values are higher in comparison with those observed for Pcs (around 1.7 eV) and similar to those observed for porphyrins (approximately 2.0 eV). SubPcs are normally characterized by fluorescence quantum yields (φF) of 0.2–0.5, and fluorescence lifetimes (τs) of the order of a few nanometers, although higher φF values can be observed in some cases.1 In general, low φF values can be rationalized on the basis of fast intersystem crossing processes in heavy-atom containing SubPc derivatives or considering aggregation issues (e.g. in water). In addition, photoinduced energy transfer and electron transfer events in SubPc-based multicomponent systems can result in the quenching of the SubPc emission (refer to Section 2.6 for further details).
On the other hand, triplet excited state energies of approximately 1.45 eV are commonly calculated based on phosphorescence emission maxima, which are located in the near-infrared region. Thus, singlet–triplet energy gaps of around 0.65 eV are normally observed for SubPc derivatives, somewhat bigger than those calculated for phthalocyanines and porphyrins (0.5–0.6 eV and 0.6 eV, respectively).1 Phosphorescence emission of SubPcs can be enhanced adding a heavy atom provider (e.g. ethyl iodide) at low temperatures.195 Singlet oxygen measurements represent an alternative tool to indirectly estimate the lower limit for triplet quantum yields. A thoroughly study of the triplet excited state features of SubPcs based on time-resolved electron paramagnetic resonance has also been published.198
Recently, aggregation induced emission enhancement has been observed in a SubPc derivative decorated at the axial position with a tetraphenylethylene moiety, which was ascribed to a fluorescence resonance energy transfer process from the excited apical moiety to the macrocyclic core (Fig. 13).119 On the other hand, the functionalization of the SubPc chromophore with tetraarylethylene unit at the periphery did not lead to any aggregation induced emission phenomenon.
 Fig. 13  Chemical structure of the tetraphenylethylene-substituted SubPcs investigated by Luo and co-workers.119 
The transient absorption characteristics of different SubPcs have also been reported (see for example ref. 1, 43, 46, 48, 58, 76, 78, 81, 88, 89, 92, 93, 99, 104, 105, 107, 109, 111, 126, 164–166, 170, 184, 188 and 199). In transient absorption spectra, singlet excited states of SubPcs are typically characterized by a strong bleaching in the Q band region and distinctive signatures in the 400–700 nm region. Decay dynamics are monoexponential, with rates on the order of 109 s−1. SubPcs triplet excited states also give rise to characteristic transient features. Besides through the formation of new features, intersystem crossing in SubPcs can also be monitored through the disappearance of the singlet signatures in the transient absorption spectra. In general, time constants for singlet decay and triplet growth processes are similar. Commonly, triplet lifetimes (τt) of approximately 80–150 μs are calculated from decay dynamics.
The absorption features of radical anion and cation SubPc species have also been studied by means of pulse radiolytic reduction and oxidation measurements, allowing for the assignation of the corresponding features in transient absorption measurements.43,165,166,197,200,201


2.3.8 Voltammetry. 
Both the first oxidative and reductive events of SubPcs are one electron processes. While reduction is often (quasi-)reversible on the cyclic voltammetry scale, oxidation gives rise to less stable species. Upon scanning at more positive or negative potentials, several redox events can typically be observed. These events, which in some cases are multi-electronic, are usually irreversible or quasi-reversible.197,200,202 Such irreversibility is generally ascribed to chemical transformations or deposition of the redox species on the electrodes. Electropolymerization on the working electrode has been reported for certain axially substituted SubPcs.135,137 A reversible oxidation has been reported for asymmetric SubPcs peripherally substituted with catechol moieties (Fig. 14).17 Moreover, two reversible reduction events were detected for some of these derivatives. The enhanced electrochemical stability of these compounds was explained on the basis of an increased delocalization of charge throughout a pseudo π-extended system or a partial charge separation between the oxygen-containing and the halogen-containing halves of the bowl, which was evidenced by DFT calculations.
 Fig. 14  Chemical structure of the asymmetric SubPc derivatives bearing peripheral catechol substituents investigated by Bender and co-workers.17 
For SubPc derivatives incorporating a carboxylic acid as peripheral substituent, a shoulder-like pre-peak was observed in the reduction scan at a relatively high SubPc concentration (0.5 mM), which was ascribed by the authors to a follow-up self-protonation reaction of the radical anion by the neutral substrate.55
The peripheral substitution pattern of the macrocycle influences the position of the SubPcs redox peaks in a predictable way. Generally, the first reductive event is observed between −1.58 and −0.82 V, while the first oxidative event is detected between +1.15 and 0.37 V employing Fc/Fc+ as internal ref. 197 and 200. A significantly low oxidation potential (0.10 V) has been observed in a hexaphenyl-substituted SubPc-Br derivative,23 probably as a consequence of the electron-donating features of the peripheral moieties, while a potential of 0.398 V was determined for a SubPc-OH bearing six peripheral alkyl chains.203,204 On the other hand, the nature of the axial ligand influences to a much lesser extent the values of the first oxidative and reductive half-wave potentials. Nevertheless, the axial substituent affects in a considerable way the reversibility of the redox events. As an example, in axially phenoxy-substituted SubPcs the reversibility of the redox processes is higher than in chloro-substituted SubPc, probably due to a better stabilization of the charged species in the former.200
An extensive study on the electrochemical properties of a series of SubPc derivatives with different axial and peripheral substituents has been recently reported by Bender, Kadish and co-workers.122 This work evidenced that the reversibility of the redox processes is influenced both by the nature of the axial and peripheral substituents and by the dipole moment of the boron-to-axial ligand bond. Two years later, the effect of fluorine versus chlorine as axial and/or peripheral substituents on the electrochemical properties of the SubPc macrocycle was investigated in detail by Bender, Gross and co-workers.205 This study pointed out that as a consequence of the stronger σ-withdrawing and π-donating effect of fluorides, the two halogens originate similar effects on the reduction potentials of SubPcs. Moreover, fluorides were found to stabilize the reduced species better than chlorides. The electrochemical study of a series of SubPcs functionalized with fluorophenoxy axial substituents and differing for the number and position of fluorine atoms in the apical moiety (72–78, Fig. 15) revealed that the presence of a fluorine substituent at the meta position of the apical aryl unit varies the half-wave reduction potential by 0.07 V, allowing to fine-tune the electronic properties of the macrocycle.132 In axially aryl-substituted SubPcs, the presence of a substituent different from hydrogen at the para position of the aryl moiety was found to decrease the reversibility of the second reduction process.141 On the other hand, the presence of peripheral cyano groups (Fig. 10) was evidenced to have a stabilizing effect, leading to up to three reversible or quasi-reversible reduction waves, in addition to give rise to easier reduction phenomena.54 Recently, the redox properties of different SubPc derivatives containing axial ferrocenyl substituents have also been investigated.80,83,206,207
 Fig. 15  Chemical structure of the SubPcs axially substituted with fluorophenoxy substituents investigated by Bender and co-workers.132 
The impact of different experimental variables such as the nature of solvents, internal standards and electrolytes, the type of working electrodes and the scan rate on the electrochemical behavior of various SubPc derivatives was systematically investigated by Bender and co-workers, who established a standard operating procedure for data acquisition.208
The redox gap of SubPc is larger than that of Pcs, being the former of about 2.1 eV and the latter of approximately 1.7 eV.1 Several techniques have been employed for the estimation of the absolute HOMO and LUMO energy levels of SubPcs, such as cyclic voltammetry in solution or photoelectron spectroscopy onto highly ordered pyrolytic graphite (HOPG) substrates.209,210 A mathematical model has been presented by Bender and co-workers for the prediction of the frontier orbital energy levels in SubPcs from semiempirical or DFT computational data, which was experimentally validated through cyclic voltammetry, absorption spectroscopy and ultraviolet photoelectron techniques for a set of selected SubPc derivatives.210 This study confirmed that the nature of the axial substituent does not affect to a large extent the HOMO and LUMO energy levels, as opposed to what observed for peripheral substituents. In particular, depending on the different peripheral substitution pattern, the HOMO level is commonly situated between −3.3 and −4.7 eV, while the LUMO value can varies between −6.8 and −5.3 eV.


2.3.9 Theoretical calculations. 
Geometry, electronic structure, electrochemical and spectroscopic features of SubPcs have been thoroughly investigated by theoretical calculations.211–233 A detailed study on a series of three SubPc-Cl derivatives bearing different peripheral substituents (i.e. perhydrogenated, trinitro- and tri-t-butyl SubPc) at Hartree–Fock (HF) and density functional theory (DFT) levels using STO-3G and 6-31G basis sets has been reported by Ferro et al.211 The calculated geometries of the unsubstituted derivative were found to be in accordance with the experimental data from X-ray diffraction studies, and no substantial changes were detected with respect to the substituted derivatives. Subtle variations were observed in the B–Cl distance. As a matter of fact, a lengthening or shortening of the distance was detected in the presence of electron-donor or electron-withdrawing substituents, respectively. The study evidenced a close similarity to the experimental values of the calculated dipole moments, which main contribution was shown to correspond to the direction of the axial B–Y bond. Some of the bond constituting the SubPc structure (e.g. B–Cl and B–Ni) were found to be moderately polar, while the electronic density distribution of the macrocycle was observed to not be influenced by the peripheral substitutions. The calculations reproduced the breakdown of the Gouterman's four-orbital model in SubPcs, although a minor difference between HOMO and HOMO−1 was obtained by DFT method. The nodal pattern obtained at semiempirical level was confirmed by HF theoretical calculations, which included a significant contribution of the chlorine atom to the HOMO−1 and an important presence of the substituent in HOMO−1 and LUMO+1. Both DFT and HF calculations confirmed the high π character of the frontier orbitals, from which it is possible to assume the aromatic nature of the macrocycle.
A theoretical study on redox processes in SubPcs was carried out in 2003 through a full geometry optimization at B3LYP/3-21G level.213 This study evidenced that the cone-shaped geometry of the macrocycle is maintained in the charged derivatives even upon donation or subtraction of two electron to/from the SubPc unit. The B–Cl bond was found to suffer the major geometric variation upon electron exchange processes, playing a valuable role in releasing the extra charge from the SubPc ring. In particular, this bond was found to exhibits length variation upon oxidation (shortening) or reduction (lengthening) of the SubPc macrocycle. On the other hand, the electronic density distribution over the entire ionic SubPc species was observed to be almost unchanged in comparison with the neutral species.
The spectral features of SubPcs have been computed by means of TDDFT calculations, which evidenced that the nature of the peripheral substituent affects lo a large extent the absorption profile.214 Calculations showed that the effect of peripheral substituents is different depending on their position (i.e. whether both substituents are located at the same side or different sides of the molecular cone). The computed spectral characteristics were found to agree with the experimental data, especially when solvent effects were included in the model.
In 2017, a TDDFT study on the structural and optical properties of SubPcs was reported.215 In this work, both the ground-state and excited-state properties of SubPcs were investigated using ab initio approaches accounting for environmental effects. The structural parameters and NMR features of SubPcs were reproduced using DFT(PBE0) as level of theory. Moreover, the calculations demonstrated the strong aromatic character of the SubPcs macrocycle, which was quantified and ascribed to their bowl topology and their formal carbocationic nature. Finally, the spectral features (including the emission properties) of SubPcs were investigated. The 0–0 energies were calculated, and were shown to match experimental data with a very low mean absolute deviation (<0.1 eV). The vibronic couplings were calculated ant the origin of the shapes of the absorption and emission bands in terms of vibrational contributions was unravelled.
A year later, the absorption spectra of H12SubPc-Cl in different solvents were calculated using generalized Kohn-Sham density functionals with a non-equilibrium solvation model.216 In particular, the performances of BP86, B3LYP, LC-ωPBE, wB97XD and CAM-B3LYP functionals were compared, and the effect of the inclusion of a first solvation shell was investigated.
Recently, the effect of the axial halogen ligand on the electronic spectra of SubPc derivative in solution and in the solid state was investigated through DFT/TDDFT calculations.217 The computed frontier molecular orbital energies and the simulated spectra were found to match well with the experimental data in solution. Moreover, two configurations (convex-to-convex and concave-to-concave) were modelled for SubPc dimers in the solid state, based on the experimental organization in thin films. For SubPc dimers in the solid state, the computed absorption wavelengths in the visible region were found to increase going down along the halogen series, while the absorption strength was shown to follow an opposite trend. Calculations suggested that that the convex-to-convex configurations of dimers could be the main contributors to the UV-vis spectra of axially halogenated SubPcs in thin films.
Substitution of the central boron atom has also been investigated through theoretical calculations. The structure and stability of SubPc-Cl derivatives in which the central boron had been replaced by aluminium, gallium, carbon, silicon, germanium or magnesium have been studied at the B3LYP/6-31+G*//B3LYP/6-31G* level by Yang and co-workers.218,219 Calculations showed that the stability of derivatives bearing Al, Ga, Si and Ge as central atom is lower than that of boron SubPc due to the steric issues. In the presence of Al, Ga, Si and Ge a more pronounced pyramidal geometry was calculated. On the other hand, the introduction of C as central atom provided the required angle for a regular tetrahedron. A stronger aromaticity was determined for boron SubPcs with respect to Al and Ga analogues on the basis of calculated NICS values. The same conclusions were reached in a publication from Milletti at al., in which the factors responsible for the structural differences among the four SubPc derivatives were analyzed.220
In 2012, a series of free base, boron, aluminium and gallium SubPc structures were studied using calculations at the B3LYP/6-311++G(2d,p) level.221 Metal–ligand bonds, which were investigated using AIM and ELF methods, were found to be intermediate between pure covalent and pure ionic bonds. Moreover, it was suggested that the initial step for the decomposition of SubPcs is the perpendicular attack to the carbon atom close to the meso nitrogen.
Recently, predictive simulations were reported for a series of SubPcs in which the central boron and the axial chlorine atoms had been substituted with different trivalent atoms (i.e. aluminium, gallium, indium, scandium and yttrium) and halogens (i.e. fluorine, bromine, and iodine), respectively.222 The structure, electronic properties and optical properties of these derivatives were calculated both in the gas phase and in their predicted crystalline solid phase. The degree of openness of the cone was found to depend on the nature of the central atom, and the molecular dipole (pointing from the electronegative halogen toward the trivalent atom) was shown to vary from 1 to 6 D. In the bulk crystalline phase, no net polarization was found due to the alternating arrangement of molecular dipoles. Moreover, the static permittivity of the derivatives was found to increase within the halogen series.
In 2012, Yang investigated the borenium and aluminium cations generated by the loss of the axial ligand in the corresponding SubPc derivatives.223 This investigation showed that in the borenium cation, singles states lie well below triplet states, similarly to what observed in most macrocycles without transition metals. In the triplet state, the unpaired electrons delocalized in the conjugated ring, and extra stability is supplied by delocalization. In contrast, in the aluminium cation the triplet states are stabilized through metal-centered and ligand-centered separation of unpaired electrons. The longer Al–N bond length and the smaller N–Al–N bond angle in the triplet state with respect with those in the singlet state are a consequence of the metal-centered unpaired electron, while the ligand-centered unpaired electron leads to the odd number of π electrons in the macrocycle in the triplet state. Accordingly, a notable energy relaxation after the first vertical singlet-to-triplet transition was suggested by TDDFT studies. For both the singlet borenium cation and the singlet aluminium cation, the NICS values at ring centers were found to be negative, indicating the aromatic nature of the rings. On the other hand, the NICS values at the inner rings were found to be positive (which indicate the antiaromatic nature of these rings) and the NICS values at outer rings were close to zero (as the unpaired spin density mainly concentrates in the inner part of the macrocycle) for the triplet borenium cation. Nevertheless, the whole macrocycle is apparently antiaromatic. On the contrary, a single aromatic or antiaromatic nature was not supported by NICS values for the triplet aluminium cation.
The spectral features of SubPcs featuring Al and Ga as central atoms were investigated in 2013 by TDDFT calculations, which revealed bathochromically shifted absorptions with respect to the boron derivatives.224
In 2016, the structure and photochemical properties of beryllium SubPcs complexes were investigated for the first time using TDDFT calculations.225 Such compounds were shown to be thermodynamically stable in the gas phase. The nature of the bonding between the cation and the macrocycle was found to be slightly different from boron SubPc, as revealed by NBO and AIM analyses. On the other hand, the absorption spectra, which were calculated at the BPW91/6-31+G(d)//BPW91/6-31G(d) level of theory, were found to be similar to those of the boron-containing analogues.
In the same year, the self-assembling properties of beryllium SubPcs have been investigated by means of DFT calculations by Yáñez and co-workers.226 In this work, free dimers of Be SubPc and their complexes with water and pyridine were computed at the Wb97X-D/6-331+G(d,p) level of theory. Interestingly, this study revealed the presence of Be–N intermolecular interactions, suggesting that these species are likely to undergo self-assembly.
Recently, a theoretical study on the electronic spectra of a series of SubPc-Cl derivatives bearing Fe, Co, Ni, Cu and Zn as central atoms was reported.227 The optimized results evidenced that these structures may be chemically stable in the gas phase and condensed phase, with an order of stability Fe > Cu > Ni > Co > Zn predicted on the basis of the calculated binding energy. Anyway, all the structures were found to be much less stable than boron-containing SubPcs. The investigated compounds were found to be more non-planar that the corresponding boron derivatives, and were shown to have smaller dipole moments. Moreover, the HOMO–LUMO energy gap in SubPc presenting Co, Ni and Cu as central atoms was determined to be smaller than that of the boron analogue. The simulated absorption wavelength in the visible region for zinc- and nickel-containing SubPcs is red-shifted in comparison to the corresponding boron-SubPc, while in the case of the iron-substituted structure a noticeably stronger absorption strength was found.

2.4 Organization of subphthalocyanines

2.4.1 Organization in the solid state. 
How the molecules arrange in the solid state is relevant to the formation of thin films and charge transport within an organic electronic device. Engineering the crystallization of SubPcs should facilitate a control over the electronic properties influencing the performance of the device. Considering the aromatic nature of SubPcs, the major intermolecular forces accounting for their organization in the solid state are mainly driven by π–π interactions between the isoindole subunits of neighbor macrocycles.234 These interactions can occur through the two different faces of the SubPc bowl, that is, either through the convex (the face where the axial substituent is located) or through the concave face (the opposite to the axial ligand). In this way, three different arrangements are possible: (a) the concave–concave, (b) the convex–convex, and (c) the convex–concave (also called head-to-tail organization).
The crystal structure of perhydrogenated SubPc-Cl 20, first reported in 1974 and confirmed later by Bender, is dominated by π–π interactions between adjacent molecules through their πPyrrole and πPh fragments forming ribbons.65,179,180 Each SubPc-Cl molecule participates in two concave–concave π–π interactions between rings, which are separated by 3.70 Å (Fig. 16a). In addition, two convex–convex π–π interactions with the aromatic rings of neighboring SubPcs of a different row are observed as well as one halogen–π interaction between the B–Cl unit and the peripheral benzene ring of a neighboring molecule. Other unsubstituted SubPc-Halo derivatives (Y = F, Br) are isostructural in the solid state,65 crystallizing in well-defined rows into a concave–concave arrangement.
 Fig. 16  A section of the unit cells of different SubPc-Cl derivatives: (a) H12SubPc-Cl 20, (b) F12SubPc-Cl 4, (c) Cl6SubPc-Cl 2, and (d) Cl12SubPc-Cl 48. Reproduced with permission from ref. 30. Copyright 2014 American Chemical Society. 
When bearing an aromatic aryloxy or aryl axial ligand, other arrangements became possible, namely, the SubPc bowls can also interact through the axial aromatic ligand in the concave face (concave-to-ligand, Fig. 17a). However, single crystals of most SubPcs axially functionalized with p-substituted aryloxy groups show organization motifs in which dimeric association of macrocycles through their concave faces predominates (Fig. 17b).235 The different arrangements of SubPcs in the solid state (mainly unsubstituted SubPcs bearing different aryloxy and aryl axial ligands) whose X-ray structures were reported up to 2013 have been previously reviewed by some of us.1 Since then, novel structures of unsubstituted SubPcs bearing other axial ligands have been reported, accounting for the contribution of π–Br,236 π–π,91,96,132 C–H⋯π,141 and hydrogen bonding130 interactions that involve the axial ligand and the macrocycle to direct the formation of one-dimensional ribbons or 2D-networks.
 Fig. 17  (a) Organization of SubPcs through concave-to-axial ligand interactions. (b) Front and side views of the crystal structure of SubPc 72 forming dimers (in orange), which are also interacting with neighbouring SubPcs shown in violet. Adapted with permission from ref. 235. 
2.4.1.1 Organization of peripherally halogenated subphthalocyanines. 
Due to the increasing interest in peripherally halogenated SubPcs for molecular electronics (that will be discussed in Section 2.7.1.2.2), it is important to expand the understanding of the solid-state organization of these SubPc derivatives and correlate it with properties such as charge-carrier mobilities.237 Here, we will focus on the discussion of the main solid-state motifs found in crystals of peripherally halogenated SubPcs, and the influence of both the type and number of peripheral halogens and that of the axial substituent.
Different organizations have been found in crystals obtained by train sublimation of peripherally chlorinated SubPc-Cl species. Similar to the perhydrogenated derivative 20, perchlorinated Cl12SubPc-Cl 48 arranges in the solid state forming pairs of molecules through a concave–concave π–π interaction between the πPyrrole and πPh rings of the SubPc's periphery at a separation distance of 3.80 Å (Fig. 16d).30 Association in pairs is further driven by two C–Cl⋯πPyrrole interactions between the terminal chlorine atoms and πPyrrole units of the neighboring SubPc involved in the dimer with a close interaction of 3.17 Å and 3.12 Å. Cl⋯Cl interactions are also present between the axial chlorine and two bay chlorine atoms on the periphery of a neighboring SubPc with a separation distance of 3.5 Å and 3.4 Å.
On the contrary, peripherally hexachlorinated Cl6SubPc-Cl forms slip-stacked columns (concave–convex) in which molecules are tilted in alternating directions relative to the b-axis with the axial B–Cl bond pointing towards the aromatic ring of the adjacent molecule (Fig. 16c).30 This arrangement is additionally stabilized by an intercolumn C–Cl⋯πPh interaction between a peripheral C–Cl bond and a neighboring SubPc peripheral benzene ring at a separation distance of 3.5 Å. The corresponding peripherally chlorinated SubPcs with bromine as axial substituent produced crystal structures nearly identical to their respective SubPc-Cl counterparts.30
A similar organization in the solid-state is presented by perfluorinated F12SubPcs with different axial substituents. F12SubPc-Y derivatives (Y = F,159 Cl,266 Br238) arrange in one-dimensional columns in which each SubPc unit is offset and angled in a bowl-to-ligand packing, so that the axial halogen atom is located in the concave face of the neighboring SubPc, underneath the boron atom with an intermolecular Y⋯B distance smaller than the sum of the van der Waals radii. In the F12SubPc-Cl derivative, the axial chloride interacts with a pyrrolic nitrogen (B–Cl⋯Np), as well as with one of three inner pyrrole rings (B–Cl⋯πPyrrole) of a neighboring SubPc unit at a distance of 3.3 Å (Fig. 16b). In the case of the F12SubPc-F derivative, the axial fluoride of one SubPc unit interacts with two of the three inner aromatic five-membered rings of the neighboring SubPc. A C–F⋯N interaction is also observed between a terminal fluorine atom of one SubPc and the imine nitrogen of the neighbor at a distance of 2.9 Å, producing a dense packed structure. Furthermore, the same number of up and down columns exist in the crystals.
A clear influence of the axial substituent on the solid-state arrangement was found in F6SubPc-Y derivatives 3 and 5 (compare Fig. 18A and B). Molecules of F6SubPc-F 5 are arranged in a concave-to-axial ligand way, similar to the packing of F12SubPc-Y species discussed above, where the axial fluoride of one SubPc unit fits within the concave side of a neighboring F6SubPc-F (Fig. 18B).32 The peripheral benzene rings of F6SubPc-F arrange one above the other, at a separation distance of 4.4 Å. Two B–F⋯πPyrrole interactions between the axial fluoride and the aromatic five-membered rings of a neighboring SubPc at a distance ranging from 2.9 Å and 3.1 Å are also observed, along with peripheral F–F interactions between neighboring SubPcs with separation distances of 2.63 Å and 2.61 Å. In contrast, a different organization motif was found for F6SubPc-Cl 3, which is arranged in a concave–concave fashion by π–π interactions between the peripheral isoindole fragments of the two neighboring SubPc units, separated by a distance of 4.03 Å (Fig. 18A). An intermolecular halogen-nitrogen interaction, specifically between one of the terminal fluorines and the pyrrole nitrogen of the neighbor SubPc unit with a distance of 3.0 Å, is also observed. No peripheral fluorine-fluorine interactions are noted in this case.
 Fig. 18  Molecular packing of peripherally hexafluorinated SubPcs: (A) F6SubPc-Cl 3 and (B) F6SubPc-F 5. Adapted with permission from ref. 32. 
Overall, peripherally fluorinated SubPcs, namely F12SubPc-F, F6SubPc-F and F12SubPc-Cl, arrange in a concave-to-axial halide motif (Fig. 16b and 18B). F6SubPc-Cl, on the other hand, exhibits a distorted concave–concave head-to-tail arrangement with only a few π–π interactions.
In summary, the crystal packing of halo-SubPcs is generally not affected by changing the axial chlorine substituent by bromine. However, the axial fluorine significantly enhances a columnar-type type solid state arrangement with intercolumn connections through B–F⋯π interactions. Changes in the nature and number of peripheral halogen substituents also have an impact on the solid-state organization. Peripheral chlorination enhances π–π interactions, while peripheral fluorination enables a column-like arrangement. The number of peripheral chlorine substituents influences more the solid-state arrangement than the number of peripheral fluorine atoms.
As peripherally halogenated SubPcs are π-electron deficient molecules, the introduction of axial π-electron donor aryloxy groups favors π-stacking interactions with shorter distances between the axial substituent and the concave face of the macrocycle.96
The organization of SubPcs in columnar assemblies is experiencing an enormous interest as potential ferroelectric and bulk photovoltaic properties are typically developed in polar organic materials. Due to their polar B–Y bond, when SubPcs organize in columnar assemblies, a macroscopic polarization along their longer axis forming polar assemblies is developed, due to the sum of the individual molecular dipoles. However, as mentioned above when discussing the solid-state organization of SubPcs bearing axial fluorine atoms, the columns are arrayed in a more electrostatically stable, antiparallel orientation, thus cancelling the macrodipoles, and giving rise to non-polar crystals. New rational approaches need to be developed to selectively stabilize parallel orientations in columnar assemblies. Very recently, Miyajima and co-workers have developed a strategy based on spin frustration to form SubPc-based polar assemblies, by inducing a trigonal lattice of the columnar organization using C3-sym bowl-shaped π-systems based on SubPcs.39 For this study, in addition to α-F6SubPc (79) and β-F6SubPc (5), enantiomerically pure C3-α-F3SubPc (80), C1-α-F3SubPc (81), C3-β-F3SubPc (82) C1-β-F3SubPc (83) were used. All of them formed one-dimensional columnar assemblies with SubPcs piled up. α-F6SubPc formed crystals with a trigonal lattice with all SubPcs oriented in the same direction in the crystal by forming a 3D-hydrogen bonding network between fluorine and hydrogen atoms (Fig. 19). The six fluorine and six hydrogen atoms of α-F6SubPc-F form H-bonds with SubPcs located obliquely up and down, respectively, resulting in stagger-like arrangements of macrocycles. Similarly, the other α-F3SubPcs, regardless of the number of fluorine atoms (3 or 6), molecular symmetry (C1 or C3) and chirality (racemic or chiral) formed the same polar crystals. In contrast, all the SubPcs having fluorine atoms at β-positions, such as β-F6SubPc, formed nonpolar assemblies with antiparallel orientation of the SubPc columns due to stronger F–F interactions. Interestingly, it was noticed that β-substituted SubPc-F derivatives possess superior bowl stacking ability than the α-substituted analogues, which results in antiparallel packing. Indeed, remarkable differences in the π–π distances between the SubPcs when comparing the polar (3.74 Å) and non-polar (3.53 Å) assemblies may account for this behavior that can be explained considering the differences in the bowl depths. Therefore, the formation of polar assemblies with C3-sym bowl-shaped molecules based on SubPcs can be induced by enhancing the C3-sym intermolecular interaction and reducing the bowl-stacking nature.
 Fig. 19  Top: Structure of the SubPc-F derivatives tested by Miyajima to form polar assemblies in the solid state. Bottom: Top and side views of the polar columnar assemblies formed by α-F6SubPc 79. Adapted with permission from ref. 39. Copyright 2020 Wiley-VCH GmbH. 
2.4.1.2 Co-crystallization of subphthalocyanines. 
Crystal polymorphs of SubPcs that show inclusion of aromatic solvent molecules (toluene, DCB) within the cavity of the macrocycle have been reported.11,19,185 Single-crystal XRD analysis of chiral peripherally trihalogenated SubPcs, namely β-F3SubPc-Cl, α-F3SubPc-Cl, β-Cl3SubPc-Cl and α-Cl3SubPc-Cl, showed that these derivatives co-crystallized as mixtures of the C1 and C3 regioisomers forming solid-state mixed alloyed compositions.239
More interestingly, the concave face of SubPcs is perfectly adapted to the spherical shape of fullerenes and this results in several reported SubPc/C60 co-crystals,11,111,129,172,184,240 which formation is driven by effective π–π-interactions between the components and in which, typically, a single C60 molecule is embraced by two SubPcs. For a detailed discussion about the formation of SubPc/fullerene complexes in solution we refer the reader to Section 2.5.4.2.
Based on the ability of the SubPc macrocycle of encapsulating fullerenes, a SubPc-based COF meant to act as an electron donating scaffold, able to dock electron-acceptor fullerene C60 in the pockets, has been designed as next generation co-crystalline OPV devices.241 The high internal surface area of the SubPc-based COF, combined with a directional charge transport via columns of acceptor molecules, could overcome the competition between the D–A surface area and long-range order in current OPV architectures.


2.4.2 Organization in thin films. 
The morphology and microstructure of organic thin films determine their optoelectronic properties and device performance.242,243 Generally, thin films with long-range ordered structures, such as single crystalline thin films formed by high vacuum vapor deposition,190,244,245 are desired for device applications, considering their higher charge carrier mobility and longer exciton diffusion length. On the contrary, producing long-range ordered organic thin films via solution processing is a difficult task. The latter has been addressed by introducing post-treatments – such as thermal246 and solvent annealing – on disordered organic thin films, to introduce molecular reorganization and ordering degree of the molecules. It is also important to study whether the solid-state arrangement determined by XRD of single crystals is kept when molecules are deposited as thin films.247
Despite the easy formation of uniform, grain-boundary-free amorphous thin films via simple solution processing of SubPcs, these materials possess relatively low charge carrier mobility, on the order of 10−6 cm2 V−1 s−1. This is much lower than the typical value for vapor deposited crystalline organic thin films (10−3cm2 V−1 s−1), limiting the exciton diffusion length, and resulting in OPVs with low power-conversion efficiencies. Neither thermal nor solvent annealing post-treatments proved very effective to tune the morphology of solution processed SubPc films until Lin and Ma developed a post-treatment approach capable of introducing molecular ordering (or crystallization) in solution processed organic semiconductor thin films, through a thermal imprint process (or thermal annealing under mechanical gauge pressure).183 In particular, they have shown that large area phase change from amorphous to crystalline morphology for solution processed 2-allylphenoxySubPc (84) thin films can be realized by thermal imprint with a flat mold. Indeed, the XRD spectra of the thin films upon thermal imprint at 90 °C for 12 hours displayed very clear peaks associated with high degree of ordered molecular stacking. The morphological change induced in the films by thermal imprint results in enhanced electronic properties, in particular the hole mobility that increases from 1 × 10−7 to 2 × 10−3 cm2 V−1 s−1. It was also crucial that 2-allylphenoxy substituted SubPc 84 crystallize forming columnar stacks of macrocycles through which charge carriers can move.
Solution processed thin films of trialkoxylbenzyloxyl-substituted SubPcs 85 and 86 (Fig. 20), spontaneously adopt a highly crystalline lamellar structure in the solid state, which is driven by strong core–core interaction for the peripherally perfluorinated derivatives 85. Within the hydrogenated series 86, decreasing the length of the alkyl side chain (R2 = C6H13) provides the same crystalline behavior by increasing the core–core intermolecular interactions and weakening the van der Waals forces between the alkyl chains.71 As a result of the long-range order, stronger intermolecular electronic coupling between neighboring SubPcs is allowed in these crystalline films, and therefore, more efficient homomolecular energy transfer and larger exciton diffusion lengths have been observed.
 Fig. 20  Molecular structure of SubPcs 84–86 whose solution processed thin films have shown molecular ordering. 
Thin films of Cl6SubPc-Cl (2) are amorphous when thermally evaporated in vacuum on many functional unheated surfaces, such as glass, glass/ITO, untreated silicon (111) wafer, ZnSe, independently on the deposition rate.248 However, they show some diffraction features, attributed to an increasing molecular ordering, upon annealing on the silicon surface heating at a rate of <30 °C min−1 to 150 °C for 10–15 min under continuous Ar flow, followed by a slow cooling down to room temperature.249 More interestingly, annealing of a Cl6SubPc-Cl (2) layer deposited on a CuI surface leads to both appearance of a well-defined ordering of molecules and texturing of the halide underlayer. The role of the CuI is not only to be a growth surface but also a donor component of the hybrid p-CuI/n-Cl6SubPc-Cl (2) heterojunction, which shows improved performance in comparison with the non-structured HHJ device.250 In addition, structural and optical spectroscopic analysis of SubPc 20 deposited as thin films has been performed to accurately estimate the bandgap energy and the emission peaks, which are slightly affected by annealing.251


2.4.3 Organization on surfaces. 
In electronic devices, molecules are in contact with metal surfaces. Therefore, understanding the interactions taking place in such metal–organic semiconductor interfaces is crucial for improving device performance. For that, firstly, it is important to study the adsorption process, then the growth mechanism, and finally the characterization of the ordered molecular arrays formed on the metal substrates, often inferred by spectroscopic techniques.
Different metal/molecule forces, such as dipole interaction, charge transfer, and van der Waals bonding, occur with similar magnitude, making quite unpredictable the resulting thin film structure upon molecular adsorption on different types of metal substrates. Substrate chemical identity plays the most relevant effect on the absorption process. Secondly, the structural diversity of organic semiconductors may be considered.
For instance, dipolar non-planar semiconductors such as SubPc-Cl derivatives tend to flatten when deposited on metal surfaces. Moreover, their cone-shape results in different adsorbate configurations that have been classified as “Cl-up” (with the axial chlorine atom facing vacuum), or “Cl-down” (with chlorine atom pointing towards the substrate). These two configurations differ on the conductance at the molecular center, producing two different STM contrasts.
Although the first SubPc-Cl deposition was described by Yanagi in 2000 on a Si(111) substrate,252 most of the work has been performed on Cu, Au and Ag metal surfaces to yield highly ordered 2D-arrays. From these studies, it can be concluded that the growth of SubPc monolayers is an epitaxial process that respects both the symmetry and the main geometric characteristics of the underlying substrate.
2.4.3.1 Chloro-subphthalocyanine on Cu(111) surfaces. 
Studies reported by Monti in 2016253 have shown that at low coverage, only two types of contrasts are observed by low-temperature STM, independently on the tip–surface voltage (from −2.5 V to +0.5 V). Molecules of type I exhibit a bright center while for type II the conductance increasing in the molecular center is much less pronounced (Fig. 21a). When the two most widely proposed configurations, that is, molecules with chlorine atoms protruding toward vacuum (“Cl-up”, Fig. 21b), and molecules with chlorine atoms oriented toward the substrate (“Cl-down”, Fig. 21b) were simulated by DFT, the results indicated that Cl-up configuration results in the most stable adsorbate geometry, with an adsorption energy of −3.56 eV, and a simulated STM image that is remarkably similar to those observed for type I molecules. Moreover, the simulations revealed that the proposed Cl-down configuration does not form readily on the surface, showing a small adsorption energy of only −0.60 eV. Intriguingly, the simulated STM images in Fig. 21 for the Cl-down configuration look profoundly different from either the type I or the type II molecules observed in the experimental images, suggesting a different nature of the type II configuration. In fact, results were interpreted as a surface-catalysed dechlorination, yielding “DeCl-up” molecules. Simulated DeCl-up molecules show a height increase in the center of the molecule, which is in good agreement with the experimentally determined constant current images for adsorbate type II. It is worthy to point out that no evidence for dechlorination prior to adsorption was found. Indeed, dechlorination of SubPc-Cl is reported to occur in solution, in particular, when nucleophilic solvents and SubPcs bearing peripheral electron-donating groups are employed, because they lead to the formation of a more stable borenium cation. In this case, the Cu(111) can play the same role, acting as an electron donating pool that partially stabilizes the borenium cation formed by dechlorination of the SubPc-Cl molecule.
 Fig. 21  (a) Experimental constant-current STM images showing two different contrasts for molecular adsorbates, classified as type I (high) and type II (low) depending on the conductance in the molecular center; (VS = 0.5 V; It = 150 pA; T = 5 K). (b) Schematic and atomistic representations based on DFT calculations and simulated STM images (VS = −2 V) of different adsorption configurations: (i) Cl-up, (ii) Cl-down, (iii) DeCl-down, and (iv) DeCl-up. Adapted with permission from ref. 253. 
Both species adsorb preferentially at hcp (hexagonally close-packed) hollow sites of the Cu(111) surface, in order to maximise interactions between the N atoms of the SubPc ligand and the Cu atoms in the first surface layer (Fig. 22). The Cl-up molecules self-assemble on the surface to form two different types of hcp islands, while the DeCl-up molecules remain isolated unless stabilized in a bilayer structure with Cl-down or DeCl-down molecules. Computational studies show that the most favourable adsorption geometry places the axial Cl-B ligand above one of the two hollow sites (hcp and face-centered cubic (fcc) hollow).
 Fig. 22  (a) STM images of 0.50 MLE SubPc-Cl on Cu(111) prepared at 205 K showing the coexistence of different ordered islands formed from Cl-up adsorbates. (i) Hexagonally closed-packed islands with detail and unit cell dimensions (close-up red box in right panel). Imaging conditions: VS = −2.5 V; It = 50 pA, 5 K. (ii) Low-density Cl-up islands with close-up detail and unit cell dimensions in right panel. Imaging conditions: VS = −2.0 V; It = 50 pA, 5 K. (b) STM image of 0.50 MLE SubPc-Cl on Cu(111) for a film prepared at 205 K (VS = −2 V; It = 50 pA, 5 K) showing (i) a bilayer structure from DeCl-up molecules. (ii) Profile of height difference along dashed red line in part (i). (iii) Unit cells of wetting layer DeCl-up and second layer Cl-down molecules. (iv) Side-on cartoon view of bilayer structure. Adapted with permission from ref. 253. 
The consequences of strong coupling between polar adsorbates such as SubPc-Cl and Cu(111) on the self-assembly at the surface have also been studied. The combination of photoelectron spectroscopy (UPS) and LT-STM can help to understand these interfaces. UPS measures the work function changes of a system upon surface/molecule interactions. Monti has observed that SubPc-Cl undergoes a significant charge transfer upon adsorption on Cu(111) since a newly filled f-LUMO located at the Fermi level was observed.250 This charge transfer state is interface specific and related to the “Cl-up” orientation of the molecule on the surface. The same author has also studied the origin of the strong electronic coupling between SubPc-Cl and Cu(111), which seems to arise from a planarization of the macrocycle upon adsorption on the surface, driven by strong of van der Waals interactions.254 Under these conditions, the macrocycle loses it peculiar cone-shape geometry and recovers a 94% of planarity, becoming a disk with trigonal pyramidal feature. Moreover, the electronic structure of SubPc-Cl become metallic due to the strong hybridization between molecule and metal states occurring in conjunction with electron transfer from the substrate to adsorbate. In fact, the strong-coupling regime can be reached only provided that the molecule significantly changes its shape, which is shown to be a consequence of van der Waals forces pulling it toward the surface.
2.4.3.2 Chloro-subphthalocyanine on Ag(100) surfaces. 
Individual SubPc-Cl molecules have been clearly visualized by STM on Ag(100) surfaces.255 As in Cu metal surfaces, some SubPc molecules have bright protrusions at their center due to the presence of their axial B–Cl bonds, and were assigned to a Cl-up binding configuration, with the three isoindole moieties bound to the substrate. In contrast, molecules without a bright center are assumed to adopt the Cl-down binding configuration with the molecule's axial chorine atom pointing toward the substrate.
According to Jiang's studies, these two configurations are roughly equivalent in their prevalence, in line with the small difference of 0.09 eV between these two binding configurations on their calculated adsorption energies, i.e., Eup = 0.19 eV vs. Edown = 0.10 eV. DFT simulation identified the interstices of the topmost layer of the Ag(100) substrate as the preferred binding sites for SubPc. The highly dense SubPc layer on Ag(100) can be viewed as stacked chains of molecules mechanically interlocked (Fig. 23), resulting in a zipper-like pattern, different than the honeycomb-like patterns reported before on Ag(111)256 and Au(111).257 Moreover, the SubPc molecules seem to be slightly tilted with respect to the Ag(100) substrate, making the isoindole lobes between adjacent chains closer to the substrate than those interlocked along the same chain. SubPc molecules within the “zipper” pattern are seen to be aligned with the lattice direction of the Ag(100) single crystal. The interactions between either individual molecules or SubPc islands with the underlying Ag(100) are relatively weak, as concluded from Tip-enhanced Raman Spectroscopy (TERS) and SERS experiments. TERS has been shown to be an effective tool for interfacial characterization of nanoscale molecular systems, since small shifts of a vibrational peak are due to strong molecule–substrate coupling.258 The vibrational spectra exhibit no discernible differences in peak position and/or intensity between the Cl-up and Cl-down binding configurations. The B–Cl bond-stretching mode should be much more susceptible to frequency shift than others, due to the plausibly strong coupling between the Ag(100) substrate and the highly polarizable chlorine atom of SubPc regardless of its binding configuration. Moreover, the intermolecular interactions between SubPc molecules within an island on the Ag(100) substrate are also relatively weak. Otherwise, the strong couplings between SubPc molecules would have notably deviated their vibrational frequencies from those for an isolated molecule. Both molecule–substrate and molecule–molecule interactions are rather weak for the self-assembled SubPc layer on Ag(100).
 Fig. 23  STM image showing submonolayer coverage of molecular islands of SubPc exhibiting different configurations (Cl-up or Cl-down) on an Ag(100) substrate (STM imaging conditions: V = +1.0 V, I = 150 pA). Adapted with permission from ref. 255. Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society. 


2.4.4 Organization in columnar assemblies. 
Supramolecular columnar assemblies of π-conjugated disc-like molecules are suitable materials for optoelectronics because they are hold by π–π interactions between molecular components, that facilitates the contact between their π-surfaces and, therefore, the efficient exciton and charge transport along the column axis. In particular, discotic molecules forming spontaneous columnar liquid crystals have been extensively studied. In those assemblies van der Waals forces between the aliphatic chains contribute to stabilizing the organized columnar mesophase, in addition to π-stacking. SubPcs are peculiar molecules with bowl-shape geometry, always bearing an axial substituent. Therefore, their organization in columnar liquid crystalline phases requires SubPcs bearing small axial ligands (typically fluorine) which can stack in a head-to-tail fashion. The resulting columnar assemblies present some additional features, that is, they are non-centrosymmetric due to the intrinsic C3 symmetry of the components. In addition, these assemblies are polar, if we consider that all the polar B–Cl or B–F bonds may be oriented in the same direction within the column. The non-centrosymmetric head to-tail or convex-to-concave columnar organization, with a permanent axial dipole moment observed for SubPcs, induces a macroscopic polarization that can result in electric-field-responsive polar materials.259
2.4.4.1 Columnar assemblies in solution. 
Nonplanar molecules such as SubPcs are reluctant toward aggregation, especially if they bear bulky substituents at the axial position. This has been so far the basis of their applications in organic optoelectronics. Nevertheless, the aggregation of bowl-shaped polar molecules in columnar stacks that can interact with electric fields is quite challenging, since they can generate polarly ordered materials exhibiting switchable (i.e., ferroelectric) or permanent polarization, as well as anisotropic charge transport.
SubPc 87a (Fig. 24), bearing peripheral primary amide substituents and a bulky tert-butylphenoxy axial substituent, is fully dissociated in H-bonding competing solvents such as dioxane, but it undergoes a concave–concave dimerization process in non-polar solvents such as toluene or methylcyclohexane (MCH) (Fig. 24a).38 The supramolecular scenario changes radically in SubPc 87b bearing a very small axial fluorine ligand, forming robust SubPc-based columnar nanostructures in strongly non-polar solvents (MCH, heptane or dodecane) by a convex–concave supramolecular polymerization (Fig. 24).37 In that case, the weakly bound SubPc stacks are further stabilized by three lateral H-bonds between peripheral arylamides. The presence of a strongly polar axial B–F bond additionally reinforces convex–concave association due to dipole–dipole interactions. Detailed aggregation studies were carried out by monitoring UV-vis absorption, emission, and CD spectral changes as a function of temperature, concentration and solvent nature. Whereas both 87a and 87b show a Q band absorption at 580 nm as monomers, Fig. 24b and c show the different spectral changes experienced by both monomers as a function of the volume fraction of dioxane (χD) in a MCH solution (3.2 × 10−5 M, T = 20 °C). Fig. 24c clearly shows a critical solvent composition in which polymerization of SubPc 87b is abruptly triggered, with a clear isosbestic point at 528 nm. The self-assembly mechanism was analyzed by the cooperative nucleation-elongation model developed by Meijer and co-workers, inferring first the formation of a dimer (nucleation) that then grows via a cooperative mechanism by successive SubPc stacking. The main thermodynamic parameters, both for the nucleation (dimer formation): ΔH0nucl = −11.7 kJ mol−1, and Knucl = 7.1 × 103 M−1, as well as for the elongation process: ΔH0e = −56.1 kJ mol−1, ΔS0 = −55.0 J mol−1 K, Ke = 4.2 × 105 M−1, and Te = 344.9 K, ΔG0 = −38.0 kJ mol−1 and σ = 0.027 are in agreement with this proposal. In addition, SubPc 87b is a C3-symmetric chiral molecule, whose enantiomers (P and M) can be easily separated by HPLC. Aggregation studies were carried out for each isolated enantiomer by UV-vis absorption, emission, and CD spectroscopies, indicating the formation of homochiral non-centrosymmetric columns (Fig. 25). Specifically, compound 87b displayed blue-shifted absorption (Q band at 518 nm whereas the monomer absorbs at 580 nm), a significantly quenched emission, and a remarkably different CD spectrum upon aggregation, which is a mirror image for each enantiomer. In addition, hydrogen bonding between the exocyclic amide groups was confirmed by 1H-NMR and FTIR spectroscopy. Interestingly, important differences were observed when comparing the absorption changes at 580 nm as a function of temperature for the racemic mixture of 87b and that of its P or M enantiomers, indicating that the racemic stacks are substantially more stable than the homochiral stacks obtained from the individual enantiomers. These results can only be explained considering a clear preference for heterochiral head-to-tail binding in the self-assembly of racemic 87b. Quantum chemical calculation studies additionally shed light to understand the cooperative character and the chiral self-sorting for this self-association process. It was found that the optimal peripheral amide conformation in enantiomer P of SubPc 87b leading to the most stable homochiral right-handed (P) helical stack, is the one named anti-in, with the carbonyl dipole aligned antiparallel to the axial SubPc B–F bond, and in orientation with respect to the isoindole ring, in order to both maximize the intermolecular H-bonding and minimise the net dipole moment of the assembly. In the stacks, neighbouring molecules are separated by 4.12 Å and rotated by 23.08°, establishing that 16 molecules are necessary to complete one helical pitch. In addition, The Fδ−⋯Bδ+ contacts between adjacent molecules are calculated to measure 2.78 Å, which are significantly shorter than the sum of the van der Waals radii of boron (1.92 Å) and fluorine (1.47 Å), that evidencing the contribution of the strong dipolar interaction between B–F dipoles along the stacks. As shown in Fig. 26, as the columnar stack grows, the H-bonding network strengthens and the aggregate becomes more stable, suggesting a cooperative character to the self-assembly of SubPc 87bP in non-polar solvents. Moreover, these studies reproduced the experimental CD of a right-handed trimer made of three P enantiomers of SubPc 87b with an anti-in conformation. Theoretical calculations also helped to understand the reason why pure enantiomers (M and P) self-associate with itself forming homochiral columns, whereas an alternate stacking of P and M enantiomers is favoured from racemic samples. The formation of a helical heterochiral supramolecular polymer was found to be energetically favored against a homochiral polymer to maximize core-to-core contacts of head-to-tail heterochiral stacks, thus in agreement with the self-discrimination process experimentally observed.
 Fig. 24  (a) Structure of SubPcs 87a and 87b and models of the different assemblies formed in toluene and MCH. Changes in the (b) 87a and (c) 87b Q-band absorption spectra as a function of the volume fraction of dioxane (χD) in MCH (3.2 × 10−5 M, T = 20 °C). Arrows indicate spectral changes upon molar fraction of dioxane increase. (d) Fitting of denaturation curve of 87b at 519 nm to the extended nucleation-elongation model. Adapted with permission from ref. 38. Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society. 
 Fig. 25  (a) Structure of the P and M enantiomers of SubPc 87b and (b) models of the columnar assemblies formed. (c) Circular dichroism spectral changes of SubPc 87b (P and M) as a function of temperature ((T = 368–268 K; [] = 3.9× 10−6 M) in MCH solutions. (d) Absorption changes as a function of temperature in MCH solutions of enantiomerically pure SubPc 87bP (left) and racemic 87b (right). Adapted with permission from ref. 38. Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society. 
 Fig. 26  (a) Amide conformations that lead to triple intermolecular hydrogen bonding in the P helical stacks. syn and anti orientations are defined as depending on whether the amide carbonyl dipole is aligned parallel or antiparallel, respectively, to the axial B–F bond. In and out orientations describe the position of the carbonyl oxygen (red atom) with respect to the isoindole ring. (b) Calculated stabilization energy per monomer unit (ΔEmon,n). (c) Side views of the helical homochiral (left) and heterochiral (right) optimized hexamers calculated at the GFN2-xTB level of theory. Adapted with permission from ref. 38. Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society. 
Very recently, a SubPc-F peripherally substituted with nine tetra(ethylene glycol) chains (three per isoindole unit) has been reported by Torres, García-Iglesias and co-workers. This novel amphiphilic derivative can self-assemble in water forming spherical nanoparticles with a hydrodynamic diameter of 154 nm, as determined by DLS.260
2.4.4.2 Liquid crystalline columnar assemblies. 
Liquid crystalline behaviour of SubPcs bearing at their peripheral positions six long alkyl chains ranging from 10 to 18 carbon atoms was reported years ago, provided that they contain a relatively small axial chlorine substituent.261 Transition from mesophase to isotropic liquid occurred at ca. 90 °C. More recently, Guilleme et al. have reported a more sophisticated design of a trisubstituted SubPc (88b, Fig. 27a) with a C3 symmetry, bearing a small fluorine axial group, which form columnar LCs.102 According to DSC thermograms, once melted to the isotropic liquid (I), the mesophase appears on cooling at 89.2 °C, and maintains stable at room temperature for several weeks. Semiempirical calculations showed that aggregation within a column is not only driven by dispersion or van der Waals forces, but also by strong F⋯B dipolar interactions between adjacent SubPcs. Therefore, the axial, highly electronegative fluorine atom was key to obtain a stable head-to-tail non-centrosymmetric columnar stacking with a strong axial dipole (Fig. 27b). A second harmonic generation (SHG) signal was observed in the mesophase when the material was cooled down from the isotropic liquid under a DC field. This SHG response was preserved after turning off the field and maintained at room temperature for several weeks without appreciable degradation, indicating that the material has a macroscopic polar axis along the direction of the field. However, SHG interferometry showed that the liquid crystal sample is permanently polarized once formed, but it is not electrically invertible. In other words, the mesophase is not ferroelectric but pyroelectric. The only way to invert the polarization of the material is to heat back to the isotropic phase, and cool down again by applying an electric field in the opposite sense (Fig. 27c).
 Fig. 27  (a) Molecular structure of liquid crystalline SubPc 88. (b) Head-to-tail SubPc 88b stacking leading to a polar columnar assembly. (c) Schematic representation of the polarization behavior of a LC film of SubPc 88b. Arrows represent groups of molecular dipoles which assemble in columns in the mesophase. The polarization is developed on cooling the material from the isotropic liquid under a DC electric field. Within the mesophase, the polar order remains unaltered after the electric field is switched off and cannot be reversed. Adapted with permission from ref. 102. 
Very recently, the mesogenic properties regioisomeric C1/C3 mixtures of trisubstituted SubPc-F derivatives incorporating either none- (88a) mono- (88c) or bis(thienyl) (88d) units in the peripheral arms have been reported, showing the formation of polar parallel dimers in the case of the thienyl derivatives, that pile up into polar liquid crystalline columnar phases, as confirmed by X-ray scattering, dielectric spectroscopy, second harmonic generation, alignment, and conductivity studies.262
Although SubPc 87 also exhibits a thermotropic LC behaviour, the high viscosity of the mesophase and the high transition temperature to the isotropic liquid (i.e. around 250 °C) make its processing difficult and prevent possible applications. Therefore, authors tried to keep the columnar order of the mesophase while adding a nonpolar solvent that would be incorporated within the columns, hence weakening the intermolecular forces between SubPcs. Indeed, a 10 wt% (3.4 × 10−2 M) SubPc 87 solution in dodecane formed amphitropic columnar LCs that are stable at room temperature.263 POM and X-ray diffraction studies indicated the formation of a columnar nematic NCol lyotropic mesophase which, as other nematic mesophases, has shown an electro-optic response.
A polar switching was confirmed by texture observations and SHG methods. When an electric field of 10 V μm−1 was applied, a black texture could be observed between crossed polarizers, which was retained for at least several minutes when the electric field was turned off. Moreover, a molecular reorientation occurred when voltage was inverted, reappearing the brown texture. In line with these results, strong SHG signals were observed when the sample was subjected to an electric field, which saturate for fields in the range of 10 V mm−1, indicating that the material has a macroscopic polar axis along the field direction. A remanent polarization (Pr) of ca. 0.13 mC cm−2 was calculated. To further confirm the switching process, SHG interferometry experiments were carried out, showing that positive and negative fields induced opposite polarities in the sample. Therefore, this material was considered as the first nematic columnar ferroelectric phase. Taking into account that bowl inversion of SubPc 87 within the columns is not likely to occur, authors have speculated that the SubPc columns might disassemble into shorter columns that could move from up to down and invert their macrodipoles upon field application (Fig. 27).
More recently, the problem of the high temperature required to melt the mesophase of SubPc 87 was circumvented by applying an external field of ca. 30 V μm−1 at 130–140 °C, for about 20 min, which resulted in a high degree of homeotropic alignment. Using this alignment procedure, Kemerink et al. developed the first organic material in which charge transport is coupled to ferroelectric polarization.264 The supramolecular organization of the SubPc 87 in columnar assemblies provides long-range polar order that supports collective ferroelectric behavior of the amide side groups, as well as charge transport through the stacked semiconducting SubPc macrocycles. Simultaneous switching of polarization and conductivity measurements of devices based on LCs of SubPc 87 sandwiched between gold electrodes, indicated a polarization reversal when the externally applied electric field passed the coercive field, as well as a strong rectifying behavior at low voltages. The experiments displayed a remarkable hysteresis behavior in the current–voltage (j–V) loop which is attributed to a bulk conductivity modulation. In other words, the material switches from a high conductivity state, when polarization and conductivity are parallel, to a low conductivity state, when they are antiparallel (Fig. 28).
 Fig. 28  (A) Ferroelectric polarization and (B) current vs. applied voltage for a Au/SubPc 87/Au diode. Polarization and current are separate measurements on the same device. Arrows indicate loop sense. Device film thickness L = 1 mm, T = 130° to 135 °C. (C) Schematic representation of the self-assembled liquid crystalline ferroelectric material in which the polarization, which can be switched as a function of the direction of the array of hydrogen-bonded amide groups, is coupled to electron transport, and leads to a bulk switchable and rectifying conductivity. Adapted with permission from ref. 264. 
Very recently, Zhang et al. have revisited the mesogenic behaviour of the (SC12H25)6SubPc derivative 89, bearing this time an axial fluorine substituent. 89 has been organized in hexagonal columnar mesophases and further oriented perpendicular to electrodes at the Colh phase temperature (70 °C) under application of a DC E-field of 10 V μm−1, resulting in the development of spontaneous polarization along the columnar axes (Fig. 29).13 The orientation of the LC assemblies along the applied E-field direction was maintained for at least six months unless the samples were heated up to their isotropic melt. These columnar assemblies additionally exhibit a bulk photovoltaic effect (BPVE), which implies the direct current generation under photoirradiation. Before measurements, the samples were poled under DC E-field of 10 V μm−1, and upon light irradiation over a wide range of wavelengths up to 650 nm, a photocurrent of 0.13 μA cm−2 at zero voltage and an open-circuit voltage (Voc) of 0.18 V was observed. Furthermore, just by sandwiching this columnar assembly between two ITO electrodes, the resultant device reached a light-on/off ratio, Ilight/Idark, as high as 6.6 × 103, indicating that the polar columnar assemblies with SubPcs are promising for visible light photodetectors.
 Fig. 29  (a) Molecular structure of LC SubPc 89 and the transition temperatures determined by DSC.
