(b) J–V curves under a solar simulator (red) and in the dark (black) showing the photovoltaic properties of the device made of 89. Adapted with permission from ref. 13. Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society. 
To the best of our knowledge, only one report of liquid crystalline SubPcs bearing the requested aliphatic long chains at the axial position has been published.244 Specifically, unsubstituted SubPc 86 (R1 = H, R2 = C12H25) adopts an hexagonal columnar mesophase between 19 °C and 68 °C, driven by weak core–core interactions and strong van der Waals forces. Photophysical studies indicate a J-aggregate-like character of this SubPc-C12-based mesophase. An increase in the length of the axial alkyl chains narrows the temperature window over which liquid crystallinity exists. In fact, SubPc 86 (R1 = H, R2 = C16H33) forms a smectic liquid crystalline phase only between 44 °C and 55 °C.

2.5 Subphthalocyanine-based multicomponent systems

2.5.1 Subphthalocyanine fused systems. 
In contrast to flat polyaromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs), curved π-conjugated systems exhibit enhanced solubility and decreased aggregation by π–π stacking. Moreover, introducing heteroatoms into aromatic π-extended frameworks results in molecular building blocks that exhibit unusual properties like, for instance, metal coordination provided by the presence of nitrogen atoms.
Fused SubPc dimers (SubPc)2, that is, π-extended aromatic molecules comprising two SubPc units merged through a common benzene ring, are unique SubPc derivatives with peculiar geometry and optical properties. As mentioned, SubPcs possess a rigid tetrahedral structure that do not undergo bowl inversion, giving rise to configurationally stable cone-shaped molecules. Therefore, when two SubPc units are fused, two rigid topoisomers are formed in equal amounts, namely, the syn, with the two axial substituents at the boron atoms pointing towards the same direction, and displaying a full hemicircular geometry, and the anti topoisomer, with axial groups pointing towards opposite directions, and forming a S-shaped molecule (Fig. 30).265,266
 Fig. 30  Molecular structure of the two possible topoisomers, syn and anti, formed by benzo-fusing two SubPc units. 
This type of SubPc analogues were prepared and unequivocally characterized independently by Torres and Kobayashi in 2002,265,266 by mixed condensation of 1 equiv. of 1,2,4,5-tetracyanobenzene and 10 equiv. of tetrafluorophthalonitrile in the presence of three moles of BCl3, heating at reflux of p-xylene (Table 2, method A). After purification by column chromatography on silica gel, bright dark blue SubPc dimers 90a were obtained in 20% yield, accompanied by very small quantities of a mixture of the dark blue SubPc fused trimers, which theoretically consists of three topoisomers (syn,syn, syn,trans, and trans,trans). 1H-NMR spectra of each topoisomers 90a-anti and 90a-syn showed diagnostic singlets at 10.48 and 10.46 ppm, respectively, corresponding to the protons attached to the central benzene ring. Both topoisomers exhibit similar spectroscopic features that include a remarkable bathochromic shift of ca. 120 nm of their Q-band absorptions relative to their parent monomers, appearing at about 700 nm (Table 2).

Along the last 19 years, few other fused SubPc dimers bearing different either (a) peripheral substitution, (b) axial functionalization or (c) aromatic bridge subunit have been reported. In some cases, the corresponding trimers have been also described.
2.5.1.1 Changes at the periphery. 
The preparation of SubPc fused dimers 90 from tetrafluorophthalonitrile presents several advantages such as: (i) it provides good solubility to the final macrocycle, (ii) its symmetry allows an easier NMR characterization, and (iii) it forms stable derivatives. Very few other peripheral substituents such as halogens, thioether, and phenoxy groups have been incorporated into fused SubPc dimers, all of them with lower yields.187,267–270 The synthetic methods, summarized in Table 2, are very similar, only differing in the excess of phthalonitrile acting as peripheral unit employed, and the addition or not of o-DCB as reaction co-solvent. Most probably, either the lack of stability and/or solubility of the intermediates or final products might be the reason for the remarkable different yields, which are also summarized in Table 2. In some cases, these problems could be overcome by performing a subsequent substitution of the axial chlorine atoms by a phenoxy group on the reaction crude (Table 2, method A + D) in order to obtain stable and soluble products, amenable of being purified by column chromatography, size exclusion chromatography or recycling preparative HPLC. The latter was, for instance, the approach for preparing (SubPc)291 bearing peripheral octylthioether groups.267,268 Whereas the chlorinated axial derivates decomposed on silica gel, the corresponding tert-butylphenoxy derivatives were obtained in 16% yield after purification by column chromatography. On the other hand, the reduced symmetry of the 4-iodophthalonitrile precursor led to the formation of a SubPc dimer 92 as a complex mixture of 14 isomers, which was difficult to characterize.267,268Table 2 also indicates the cases in which the corresponding topoisomers syn and anti were separated.
Peripheral substitution showed a very strong effect on the absorption bands of dimers (see Table 2). In particular, electron-donating octylthioether substituents (as in 91), led to a Q band absorption centered at 739 nm, which is red-shifted by around 45 nm with respect to perfluoro-substituted compound 90.
2.5.1.2 Influence of the aromatic/pseudoaromatic central shared bridge. 
Apart from benzene, there are scarce examples of other aromatic units have been introduced as the fused bridge shared by the two SubPcs, the reason being the difficult preparation of the corresponding tetracyano-precursors. Thus, Kobayashi and Shimizu reported in 2016 the pyrene-bridged dimer 96 by cross-condensation of 2,7-di-tert-butyl-4,5,9,10-tetracyanopyrene and tetrafluorophthalonitrile.129 Similar to the case of benzene-bridged SubPc dimers, the two isomers syn and anti, arising from the connection manner to the two bowl-shaped SubPc units were equally formed and separated. Both showed very similar spectroscopic features, so that the final configuration assignment was performed by X-ray crystallographic analysis. In fact, the syn configuration of dimer 96 was unambiguously determined by growing co-crystals of syn-96 and C60, which is embraced by the large convex–convex π-surface of the SubPc dimer. The absorption spectrum of both topoisomers of compound 96 exhibited a Q band at 610 nm in CHCl3, that is, shifted to the red and broadened relative to the SubPc monomers. However, it is remarkably blue-shifted relative to the Q band of SubPc dimers 90b (at 690 nm), indicating a smaller electronic interaction of the SubPc unit through the pyrene bridge.
In 2015 Kobayashi reported a peculiar pseudofused SubPc dimer (97) in which two SubPc units were fused to the two benzene rings of a [2.2]paracyclophane.128 Since two sp3-hybridized ethylene bridges are linking two benzene units, they are neither linear and π-conjugated. However, owing to the small distance (less than 3 Å) between them, through-space transannular interactions have been demonstrated in this type of SubPc dimers that are coined as Subphthalocyaninophanes. As a consequence of the [2.2]paracyclophane two-layer topology, standard cyclotrimerization of 4,5,12,13-tetracyano-[2.2]paracyclophane with an excess of tetrafluoro-phthalonitrile in the presence of boron trichloride, followed by substitution of axial chlorine by phenol, afforded a mixture of three isomers that differ in the orientation of the convex and concave surfaces of the SubPc units with respect to the paracyclophane moiety, namely, the concave–concave (CC), the convex–concave (CV), and the convex–convex (VV) isomers (Fig. 31). Monomeric endo and exo cyclophaneSubPcs formed by cyclomerization of only one phthalonitrile subunit were also isolated. Interestingly, the higher yields of VV and exo derivatives, as compared to those of CC and endo, are indicative of preferential formation of the less sterically hindered compounds with the adjacent moiety residing on the exo side of the SubPc unit. In the 1H-NMR spectra, the chemical shifts of the cyclophane hydrogen atoms (sharing unit) are consistent with the orientation of each SubPc unit with respect to the cyclophane bridge in each isomer, that is, depending if they are facing the concave or the convex face. In any case they never reach the downfield shifted values of SubPc dimers sharing a benzene ring (around 10 ppm), indicating the lack of π-conjugation. These protons experience the largest upfield shift (by 3.92 ppm) with respect to those of unsubstituted [2.2]paracyclophane in isomer CC, as a consequence of a strongest diatropic ring current on the endo side. Owing to their presence on both endo and exo sides, the signals of the cyclophane hydrogen atoms appear at 6.48 and 2.38 ppm in the case of CV. In contrast with the previously reported SubPc fused dimer 90a, the Q-band of all Subphthalocyaninophane dimer isomers were centered at ca. 594 nm, experiencing very small redshift with respect to those of SubPc-cyclophane monomers (8 nm) and with respect to regular SubPcs (and 20 nm). The latter can be rationalized considering a small through-space expansion of the conjugated systems through the cyclophane bridge. Moreover, CV and VV isomers exhibited a broadening and a decrease in intensity of the Q band, and in the case of VV, the Q-band absorption split into two peaks at 617 and 593 nm.
 Fig. 31  Schematic representation of the three possible isomers of Subphthalocyaninophane 97 described by Kobayashi.128 
2.5.1.3 Influence of the axial substituent. 
Replacement of axial halogen atoms of SubPc dimers with phenols allowed the preparation of more stable and soluble derivatives. Most authors made it by refluxing the crude product from the mixed condensation reaction of phthalonitriles with phenol or 4-tert-butylphenol in toluene for 16–24 hours (Table 2, method A + D). In this way, axially functionalized dimers 90b and 90c were obtained in 13 and 19% yields, respectively.265–268 Axial substitution reaction from pure fused SubPc dimers 90a under the same conditions led to similar overall yields. Similar to what is observed in SubPcs, axial substitution by phenoxy groups causes only a very small blue-shift in the Q band absorption of SubPc fused dimers. The phenol groups in the axial position not only provide increased solubility and stability to SubPcs and SubPc fused dimers, but they can also be a tool for the assignment of the syn and anti topologies by means of 1H-NMR spectroscopy.265 The aromatic signals corresponding to the axial tert-butylphenol appear at 5.2–5.5 and 6.7–6.9 ppm in all dimers (90c, 91 and 92). The signals at 5.2–5.5 ppm, corresponding to the protons that are closer to the π-surface of the SubPc moiety, were employed as probes for the assignment of each topoisomer. While in the anti isomer the chemical shifts of the aromatic phenolic protons are very similar to those of the corresponding SubPc, in the syn isomer the proximity of the axial groups (less than 3 Å) and their resulting interaction shift significantly the signals with respect to those of the SubPc (ca. 0.2 ppm).
Interestingly, when 3-hydroxybenzaldehyde was used as axial ligand to prepare 90d, only the corresponding syn-diformyl topoisomer was obtained in overall 5% yield.271 Even more surprisingly, only decomposition products could be observed when the axial substitution was carried out from pure dichloro anti isomer 90a, while in the case of the syn topoisomer, axial substitution proceeded with 47% yield starting from pure syn dichloroSubPc 90a.
Recently, functionalization of a 1:1 mixture of syn/anti dichloro-SubPc fused dimers 90a using a mild procedure described by Nielsen87 allowed the easy introduction of ethynyl axial groups by reaction with trimethylsilylacetylene mediated by AlCl3 forming 90e (Table 2, method E).58 The small size and lack of polarity of the ethynyl axial substituents precluded the separation of the topoisomers by chromatographic work up on silica gel and moreover, they exhibited identical 1H-NMR and UV-vis features. This bis(ethynyl)SubPc fused dimer 90e could be further axially functionalized by Sonogashira coupling with a monoiodo-SubPc, allowing the preparation of an interesting SubPc-SubPc fused dimer 99 (see Fig. 32) whose ability to complex fullerene has been demonstrated (see discussion later in Section 2.5.4.2).58 Besides the preparation of this particular derivative, compound 90e seems to be a very promising starting material for introducing complex axial substituents in good yield through a convergent synthetic strategy. The 1:1 mixture of topoisomers 99, which physical separation was possible after tedious chromatography, showed two different blue-greenish spots by TLC. Also in this case the anti-99 topoisomer showed gradual decomposition in solution, contrasting with the syn-99 topoisomer, which could be spectroscopically characterized. The 1H-NMR spectra of syn-99 in CDCl3 showed the diagnostic singlets at high chemical shifts around 10.50 ppm corresponding to the protons of the central benzene ring of the SubPc fused dimer unit. The appearance of more than one singlet indicates the formation of different stereoisomers, rac and meso, depending on the mutual relative orientation of the two axial ethynyl-SubPcs linked to each boron atom of the SubPc dimer platform. Signals corresponding to these axial SubPc fragments, in particular those of the benzene units closer to the concave face of the SubPc fused dimer, are significantly upfield shifted (0.7 ppm) owing to the well-stablished influence of the anisotropic ring current of the aromatic SubPc dimer surface. UV-vis absorption spectra of SubPc-SubPc dimer hybrids syn-99 and anti-99 in chloroform are basically defined as the sum of their macrocyclic components. Both compounds feature the characteristic Q band maximum of the perfluorinated SubPc dimer unit at 696 nm, with a number of less intense shoulders at higher energy, followed by the Q band of the octysulfanyl-substituted SubPc units peaking at 596 nm. The high intensity of this band, approximately doubling that of SubPc monomer, supports the presence of two units of SubPc per unit of SubPc dimer in the final structure of anti/syn-99.
 Fig. 32  Molecular structure of (SubPc)2SubPc-fused dimer 99 reported by Torres and co-workers.58 
2.5.1.4 Unsymmetrical subphthalocyanine fused dimers. 
The preparation of SubPc fused dimers with different peripheral substituents at each SubPc half implying the cross-condensation of 1,2,4,5-tetracyanobenzene and two different phthalonitriles, would probably lead to complex reaction mixtures. Torres and co-workers have developed a stepwise synthetic method to prepare these SubPc dimers from ortho-dicyano-substituted SubPc derivatives 37–41,48 which are then subject to standard cross-condensation with another differently substituted phthalonitrile in the presence of BCl3.48 Among the possible combinations, the preparation of push–pull derivatives with potential unsymmetrical electronic distribution along their extended aromatic surface was tackled. For that, one SubPc half was decorated with electron acceptor substituents and the other half with electron-donor ones. In particular, condensation of di(tert-butyl)-substituted SubPc 41 with 4,5-dichlorophthalonitrile in the presence of BCl3 proceeded in 16% yield to provide the unsymmetrical dimer 98 using an optimized SubPc/phthalonitrile/BCl3 ratio of 1:4:6. The alternative experimental procedure that imply first the activation of 4,5-phthalonitrile and then, the addition of a solution of dicyano-SubPc 41 in p-xylene, resulted more convenient. In this case, although the starting dicyano-SubPc 41 was bearing a p-tert-butylphenoxy group as axial substituent, the final dimer was isolated as a dichloro derivative, probably due to the excess of BCl3 employed. Dimer 98a was purified by column chromatography on silica gel, and the product was found to be stable, as a syn/anti 1:1 mixture. Separation of the two topoisomers of dimer 98a was not possible during the chromatographic purification step. However, the substitution reaction of the two axial chlorine atoms by 4-tert-butylphenol allowed for the separation of both topoisomers anti-98b and syn-98b in 90% overall yield (Scheme 12). Dimer 98b could also be obtained with similar yields by reaction of SubPc 39 with 4-tert-butyphthalonitrile (Scheme 12).
 Scheme 12  Stepwise synthesis of unsymmetrically substituted SubPc fused dimers from ortho-dicyanoSubPcs developed by Torres and co-workers.48 
The 1H-NMR spectrum of dimer 98 in CDCl3 exhibits several diagnostic singlets between 10.40 and 10.35 ppm, corresponding to the two highly deshielded protons of the central benzene ring. The appearance of more than one singlet for each topoisomer evidences that they are in fact a mixture of three different regioisomers defined by the relative position of tert-butyl groups in the electron-donating SubPc moiety.
2.5.1.5 Heterodinuclear fused dimers. 
The combination of a flat Pc macrocycle and a bowl-shaped SubPc subunit which share a benzene ring result in new hybrid large π-conjugated systems with different topologies and aggregation properties. Kobayashi reported in 2014 the preparation of Pc-SubPc fused heterodimer 100 (Fig. 33) by cross-condensation of an ortho-dicyano Zn(II)Pc derivative and excess of the corresponding phthalonitrile under standard reaction conditions, in the presence of BCl3.187 In this case, electronic interactions between the SubPc and the ZnPc units have been inferred from the absorption spectrum of heterodimer 100, which is quite different to the sum of the spectra of ZnPc and SubPc constituents. While the Q bands of ZnPc and SubPc monomers are centered at 708 and 604 nm, respectively, the heterodimer 100 shows a strong Q band absorption at 800 nm in CHCl3. Besides, aggregation and spectroscopic properties of this heterodimer are intermediary between those of the homodimers of the constituent unit, i.e. ZnPc-ZnPc and SubPc-SubPc 94.
 Fig. 33  Hetereodinuclear fused dimers reported by Kobayashi, Jiang and co-workers.187,188 
Jiang and co-workers described in 2016 the preparation of the first fused heterodimer 101 made up of a porphyrin (Por) and a SubPc components sharing a quinoxaline unit (Fig. 33).188 Similar to the Pc-SubPc dimer, this molecule exhibits a peculiar planar-bowl structure confirmed by XRD analysis. The synthetic methodology employed, as for previous fused dimers, consisted first in a mixed cyclotrimerization of two phthalonitrile precursors (1 mol of 20,30-dicyano-pyrazino[2,3-b]porphyrin and 13 mol of 4,5-bis(2,6-dimethylphenoxy)phthalonitrile) in the presence of BCl3, in a refluxing aromatic solvent (in this particular case, chlorobenzene), and subsequent axial substitution with phenol. Por-SubPc-fused hybrid 101 displays two intense Soret-like absorptions at 410 and 439 nm and relatively weak Q-like bands at 500–700 nm. Compared to the absorption spectra of the Por and SubPc references, the red-shifted and distinctly altered spectral features of 101 indicate a strong conjugative interaction between the Por and SubPc moieties through the quinoxaline-fused bridge (Fig. 33).


2.5.2 Axially linked “all-SubPc” systems. 
2.5.2.1 Oxygen- and nitrogen-bridged subphthalocyanine dimers. 
Despite the first report about the preparation of μ-oxo-(SubPc)2102 based on the dehydration reaction of two SubPc-OH units 56 appeared in 1996 by Hanack,272 the yields were very low and the reaction conditions quite irreproducible. A more convenient and reliable method for the preparation of 102, based on an equimolar reaction of SubPc-OH 56 with SubPc-Br 31 in 1,2-dichlorobenzene, in the presence of K3PO4 acting as a HBr scavenger (Table 3, method A), was reported later by Bender.153 This method also gave access to unsymmetric oxygen bridged SubPc dimers constituted by two differently substituted SubPc units (Fig. 34). In particular, dimers 103–105 were prepared in 36%, 40%, and 43% yield, respectively, by treating equimolar amounts of SubPc-OH 56 with the corresponding SubPc-Halo, namely F12SubPc-Br, Cl6SubPc-Cl, or Cl12SubPc-Cl, in the presence of K3PO4 in 1,2-dichlorobenzene at 180 °C for 1 hour. One of the peculiar properties of these SubPc derivatives is their high solubility in a broad range of solvents. On the other hand, whereas the electrochemical properties of these oxygen-bridged SubPc dimers are not very different to those of related SubPc-Halo derivatives, the absorption and fluorescence properties are remarkably different. Thus, their UV-vis spectra display a Q band absorption with a noticeable blue shift and broadening in comparison with those of the corresponding SubPc monomers. Moreover, their emission spectra showed unusual red-shifted and large Stokes shifts, together with very low fluorescence quantum yields. Very recently, femtosecond transient absorption measurements have revealed the ultrafast excimer formation from the initial exciton of symmetric dimer μ-oxo-(SubPc)2102, which in polar solvents decays to form a charge separated state.273 Moreover, the performance of μ-oxo-(SubPc)2102 in planar heterojunction organic photovoltaic (OPV) devices has been tested, showing good results when applied as an electron donating material in simple bilayer structures with C70 as acceptor layer. Thereafter their HOMO/LUMO energy levels are well-placed to apply it as a bifunctional donor/acceptor interlayer material in both energy and charge cascade OPV device architectures.274

 Fig. 34  Molecular structure of axially linked SubPc dimers. 
In 2016 Torres and co-workers reported the preparation of μ-aza-(SubPc)2 derivatives using an innovative stepwise procedure via activated SubPc triflate species (method B, Scheme 13).74 The synthetic route toward unsymmetrical μ-azo SubPc dimers 107–110 involves the reaction of (dodecafluoro)SubPc(NHPh) 111 or 112 with the corresponding triflate, either 113 or 114 from hexa(octylthio)SubPc-Cl or (dodecafluoro)SubPc-Cl (method B, Table 3) in refluxing toluene, affording the desired μ-azo dimers in moderate yields. The introduction of reactive functionalities, such as iodo, in the aniline substituent opens the door to the incorporation of these μ-azo SubPc compounds in multicomponent systems of higher complexity. UV-vis absorption and emission spectra of the nitrogen-bridged SubPcs 107–110 were registered in different solvents at room temperature to evaluate any solvent polarity effects. As in the case of the oxygen-bridged SubPc dimers, they exhibit blue shifted and significantly broadened Q band absorptions (λmax (109) = 560 nm; λmax (107) = 548 nm) with respect to the corresponding monomeric SubPc-Cl. These dimers display a single fluorescence emission maximum that is strongly red shifted (λmax (109) = 610 nm, λmax (107) = 652 nm, in comparison with the monomeric species (λmax = 588 nm), as well as large Stokes shifts. Apart from particular differences, the effect of the bridging atom (i.e. N vs. O) does not seem to influence significantly the electronic properties of these SubPc derivatives.
 Scheme 13  Stepwise synthetic approach to obtain μ-aza-SubPc dimers 107–110 developed by Torres.74 
2.5.2.2 Aryloxy- and alkyloxy-bridged subphthalocyanine dimers. 
By far, reaction between SubPc-Cl species and an excess of phenols in refluxing toluene or other high-boiling aromatic solvents has been the most recurrent way to prepare multicomponent SubPc-based systems in which a SubPc is axially linked to other unit(s). As mentioned in Section 2.2.1.1, this procedure requests an excess of phenol, therefore, the preparation of dimers or trimers from benzene-diols or benzene-triols turns out more challenging than it looks at first glance. One strategy for the preparation of these derivatives consists in the one-step reaction of the hydroxylated unit with the chlorinated SubPc precursors, which directly affords the target product (Table 4, method A). An alternative approach involves the formation of a 1:1 intermediate featuring one SubPc unit linked to the nucleophile through one of the OH functionalities, and the subsequent reaction of the remaining unreacted hydroxyl group with other SubPc units (method B, Table 4).

The one-step pathway (method A, Table 4) was employed by Çoşut et al. for the synthesis of hexameric or octameric SubPc-based systems upon reaction of SubPc-Cl with hexa- or octa(p-hydroxyphenoxy)cyclotriphosphazene in refluxing toluene.95,275 The emission bands of these compounds are bathochromically shifted with respect to unsubstituted SubPc-Cl due to the close proximity of the SubPc units, which favours π–π interactions between them.
In 2015, Shibata and co-workers reported a peripherally trifluoroethoxy (TFEO)-substituted SubPc homodimer featuring a o-, m-, or p-hydroquinone linker (24o, 24m, 24p, Fig. 9) prepared through reaction of an excess of (TFEO)12SubPc-Cl precursor with o-, m- or p-dihydroxyphenol in refluxing toluene in the presence of TEA (method A, Table 4).70 Remarkably, no reaction was observed employing H12SubPc-Cl as starting material. On the other hand, the preparation of H12SubPc and F12SubPc homodimers 25 and 26, as well as the synthesis of H12SubPc-F12SubPc heterodimer 27, were performed by a two-step procedure which involved first, the preparation of a SubPc-hydroquinone (SubPc-Hq) derivative and then, a second reaction in a pressure vessel using a 1:1 mixture of SubPc-Cl and the corresponding SubPc-Hq derivative in toluene at 180 °C for three days (method B, Table 4).94
Analogous heterodimers featuring a TFEO-substituted SubPc unit and a H12SubPc or a F12SubPc unit (28–29) and (TFEO)12SubPc-H12SubPc-F12SubPc trimer 30 were also prepared through a multi-step approach (Scheme 14).70,73 In these cases, the hydroquinone- or phloroglucinol functionalized (TFEO)12SubPc was sequentially reacted with the corresponding activated triflate derivatives (method C, Table 4), first F12SubPc-OTF (54), which is less reactive, and then 14, formed from H12SubPc-Cl.
 Scheme 14  Stepwise synthesis of SubPc trimer 30 described by Shibata.73 
As a result of the diamagnetic ring currents from the two SubPc units in the dimer, the 1H-NMR resonances of the linking hydroquinone moiety, around 5 ppm, showed considerable upfield shifts, larger than the ones of axial phenoxy substituents in SubPcs. It is also noticeable the sensitivity of hydroquinone bridge resonance to the peripheral substitution of the SubPc components. In the case of the unsymmetrical dimer 27p, the hydroquinone bridge showed two different proton signals as two doublets between 4.83 and 4.73 ppm.
Concerning the absorption properties, the UV-vis spectra showed similar Q band maxima to the SubPc-phenoxy derivatives, and quite different to the corresponding μ-oxo dimers, indicating a low degree of interaction between the SubPc components. Differences caused by the ortho-, meta- and para-hydroquinone substitutions were not observed. In the UV-vis spectra of heterodimers, two absorption peaks, irrespective of the solvent and concentration, were observed in Q band region, suggesting that each SubPc unit absorbs light independently. Fluorescence quantum yields (ΦF) of 0.32 and 0.33, respectively, were calculated for homodimers 24ortho- and para-in dioxane upon excitation at 530 nm. On the other hand, meta-homodimer 24m showed a higher fluorescence quantum yield (ΦF = 0.52) in dioxane, indicating that electronic communication between the SubPc moieties of dimers linked at ortho- and para-positions is stronger than that between the dimers linked at meta positions. In the case of heterodimers 28, their fluorescence decreased significantly in dioxane. In particular, fluorescence peaks at 570 nm are more significant quenched than those around 616 nm, which implies energy/electron transfer from the H-SubPc moiety to the TFEO-SubPc moiety. ΦF values of ortho-linked SubPc dimer 28o displayed a more important decrease, probably implying that, in addition to regular through-bonding electron transfer, a through-space electron transfer process due to the closer proximity of the two electronically different SubPc components takes place.
The 1H-NMR spectra of trimer 30 showed unique peaks at 2.6, 2.8 and 2.9 ppm corresponding to three protons of the central benzene ring which are strongly shielded by the diatropic magnetic currents of the three different SubPc moieties. This suggests an independent absorption of each SubPc component, which is not influenced by their SubPc neighbour. On the other hand, the steady-state fluorescence spectra of trimer 30 show quenched peaks suggesting energy transfer from H12SubPc to F12SubPc via TFEO-substituted SubPc units, and/or directly from the H12SubPc to the F12SubPc units.
SubPc-azobenzene-SubPc 116 and SubPc-Bis(azobenzene)-SubPc 117 are interesting examples of SubPc dimers axially linked by a photoactive unit. They were prepared using method A (Table 4) by treating SubPc-Cl 20 with the corresponding bis-hydroxyazobenzenes.24,127 Single crystals of 116 show the azobenzene moiety is highly planar in a trans configuration. The UV-vis absorption spectrum of triad 117 in DMF shows the standard profile of SubPc absorption spectra: a B band at ca. 300 nm and a Q band in the 550–600 nm region. In addition, a shoulder is observed in the 357 nm region, corresponding to the azobenzene π–π* transition. More interestingly, the photoisomerization of 116 was studied in dilute DMF solutions. Conversion of the trans isomer to a cis rich photostationary state was accomplished through irradiation with a 365 nm light. The cis/trans back reaction occurred upon irradiating in the visible region with a 570 nm laser, indicating that SubPc can act as a light-harvesting unit. In the dark, the isomerization also take place in about two days. The trans/cis photoisomerization process could be followed by monitoring the decrease in the intensity of the shoulder band at 357 nm. Additional evidence for the photoisomerization was obtained by NMR spectroscopy since, in addition to the doublets for the azobenzene unit in the trans isomer at 7.23 and 5.41 ppm, two new doublets are observed at 6.15 and 5.24 ppm, assigned to the corresponding cis isomer when a CDCl3 solution of 116 is irradiated with UV region light (365 nm). As a consequence of a closer proximity of the SubPcs in this cis-isomer, the signals are considerably upfield shifted. Integration of these groups of signals indicates that 25% of triad 116 is in the cis form in the PSS. In the case of triad 117 the conversion of the E,E conformer to a photostationary state that contains the E,Z conformer was accomplished through irradiation at 385 nm.127 As in the previous triad 116, only the main π–π* bis (azobenzene) band of the E,E-isomer loses intensity upon irradiation. Photoisomerization was also inferred from the appearance of a new singlet in the 1H-NMR spectrum, assigned to the central benzene ring of the bis(azobenzene) unit. However, no changes were detected in the terminal phenoxy units, probably due to the longer distance of the bis(azobenzene) bridge. Significantly, an obvious decrease in the fluorescence emission intensity is also observed.
Other SubPc dimers linked through axial positions by bis(aryloxy) groups, prepared by treating SubPc-Cl compounds with different bisphenols, had been previously reported by Bender.276 Recently, the reaction of an axially activated SubPc-OTf (either 14 or 54) and a naphthalenediimide diol, in the presence of a naphthalene-containing crown ether, has been employed for the preparation of the first mechanically interlocked [2]rotaxane containing SubPc stoppers.79
A few SubPc dimers have been prepared by post-functionalization of the axial ligands (method D, Table 4) of more accessible SubPc starting materials bearing alkoxide groups with terminal reactive functional groups, such as alkynes and azides (Scheme 15). The synthesis of SubPc dimers 118 and 119, containing a butadiyne bridge, was based on the palladium-mediated oxidative homocoupling of terminal alkynes.72,138 Besides, SubPc dimer 120, where the two SubPc macrocycles are connected through a triazole ring, was synthesized by azide-alkyne Huisgen cycloaddition of terminally substituted azido 127 and ethynyl SubPc 126 (Scheme 15).138
 Scheme 15  Axially linked SubPc dimers 118–120 prepared by a post-reaction strategy.72,138 
2.5.2.3 Alkynyl-bridged subphthalocyanine dimers. 
The first ethynyl-bridged SubPc dimer 128 was reported by Rapenne and co-workers in 2012. It was prepared in 31% by reaction of SubPc-Cl 20 with 1.7 equiv. of ethynylmagnesium bromide in THF, with microwave heating at 100 °C (method A, Table 5).277 Spectroscopic characterization indicated that interaction between the two SubPc units is negligible. Later on, in 2016, the group of Nielsen reported a milder method allowing the axial functionalization of a SubPc with alkynyl substituents, using TMS-protected alkynyl nucleophiles in the presence of aluminium chloride, at room temperature (method B, Table 5).87 Although a great number of SubPc derivatives have been prepared in the last years through this procedure, the application of this methodology to the preparation of dimeric systems using bis(TMS)-protected dialkynes is more limited. In the original paper, only one example of dimer containing a ferrocene bridge (129) was reported, with poor (i.e., 8%) reaction yield.

More recently, the same group successfully applied this methodology to prepare dimer 130, in which two SubPcs are axially crosswise linked through the geminal positions of a tetraethynylethene (TEE) bridge unit (Scheme 16).75 Key in this synthetic strategy was to play with two slightly different alkyne-protecting groups, namely TMS- and TIPS-, in the TEE starting material, which selectively react at the trimethylsilyl (TMS) end-capped alkyne units under these conditions. SubPc-TEE-SubPc dimer 130 could be used as starting material upon convenient desilylation of its TIPS protecting groups using 10 equiv. of silver(I) fluoride, followed by in situ Sonogashira coupling reaction with different iodo-aryls, giving access to new SubPc-functionalized acetylenic scaffolds. N alternative protocol to the harsh and expensive TIPS deprotection reaction was developed,278 allowing the preparation of SubPc dimer 131 in two steps from SubPc-Cl 20. The latter consisted first, on the introduction of dibromo-enyne unit by the well-established AlCl3-mediated alkynylation using the appropriate TMS-acetylenic derivative for linking the two SubPcs by their axial positions. Then, the vinylic dibromide unit was subjected to Sonogashira coupling with trimethylsilylacetylene to obtain 131 (Scheme 16). Moreover, Glaser–Hay oxidative homocoupling could be successfully applied to the preparation of linear oligoyne-bridged SubPc dimers 133 (butadiynediyl) and 134 (octatetraynediyl), after deprotection of the TMS-protected SubPc derivative.279
 Scheme 16  Synthetic strategies developed by Nielsen for the preparation of alkynyl-bridged SubPc dimers. 
Mixing selective TMS-alkynylation, Sonogashira coupling and Glaser–Hay oxidative coupling in the appropriate sequence, SubPc dimer 132 bearing a larger (TEE-TEE) acetylene bridge was also prepared (Scheme 16).278 The electrochemistry behaviour of all these compounds have been carefully studied by cyclic voltammetry recorded in CH2Cl2 using 0.1 M Bu4NPF6 as counter electrolyte and potentials referenced vs. Fc/Fc+. In general, reductions and oxidations potentials in SubPc dimers linked by a flexible spacer such as 118, show two-electron reductions and oxidations at potentials similar to those of the monomer, suggesting a lack of interactions between the macrocycles. In the case of TEE-bridged SubPc dimer 130 some changes in the redox properties were observed, mainly, sequential oxidation of the two SubPc units at +0.54 V and +0.65 vs. Fc/Fc+, which might indicate that the SubPc units are in close proximity, and experience a Coulomb repulsion.279 On the other hand, the SubPc units of the oligoyne-bridged dimers 133 and 134 are slightly easier to reduce than that of 130 (−1.51 vs. −1.57 V), which can be attributed to the different geometries (linearly-conjugated vs. cross-conjugated bridge separating both SubPcs), but also to a larger degree of stabilization of an unpaired electron on each boron atom of the dianionic structure. In contrast, linear dimers 133 and 134 experience a single two-electron oxidation at + 0.54 V of the two SubPc units rather than two sequential one-electron oxidations observed in 130.
2.5.2.4 Subphthalocyanines incorporated in polymers by their axial position. 
Despite the widespread use of SubPcs in organic electronics as small molecule, and the well-known benefits of polymeric organic materials, only few reports deal with SubPc-containing polymers. In particular, it is noteworthy the work of Bender and Lessard in this field, who described in 2012 the first SubPc incorporated as pendant unit into a random styrene-phenoxy copolymer 135, using a post-polymerization transformation strategy that consist in the displacement of the axial bromide of SubPc-Br 31 by the phenolic side groups in a preformed polymer (Fig. 35).280 Later on, they developed a synthetic route involving the reaction with the carboxylic acid groups of a styrene (S)–acrylic acid (AA) copolymer, obtaining the corresponding SubPc-containing polymer 136281 by TEMPO-mediated polymerization; however, a significant amount of an undesired side product was formed. Fortunately, this problem could be circumvented when styrene was copolymerized with methacrylic acid (MAA), obtaining the corresponding polymer 137145 (Fig. 35) without any side product. Polymer 137 showed similar electrochemical properties, solution-state absorption and photoluminescence characteristics when compared with a SubPc monomer, although the solid-state PL emission of the polymer was excitation wavelength-dependent. Preliminary organic light-emitting diodes (OLEDs) were fabricated to assess the potential role of the SubPc polymer in organic electronic devices.145 The resulting orange OLED was characterized by having a major emission maximum peak at 592 nm and a turn on voltage of around 3.5 V, which is comparable to other SubPc small molecule-based OLEDs. These devices represent the first published report of solution processed SubPc polymer-containing organic electronic devices.
 Fig. 35  Molecular structure of the SubPc-containing polymers 135–137 developed by Bender and co-workers axially linked to the main chain as pendant groups. 
Considering the importance of thermal annealing processes in organic electronic devices, and the relatively high thermal transition (Tg) of 137, a new polymer (138) was engineered was engineered, incorporating n-butyl methacrylate (BMA) monomers in addition to styrene (S) and methacrylic acid (MAA) monomers.144 However, while the increasing composition of BMA was found to decrease the Tg of the prepolymers, differential scanning calorimetry analysis revealed that the Tg of the final SubPc-containing terpolymers varied only by an insignificant amount, from about 197 to 217 °C, with poly(MAA0.40-SubPc/BMA0.5/S0.10) displaying the lowest Tg, meaning that incorporated SubPc units mainly dictate these transition temperatures. Moreover, solid state fluorescence spectroscopy showed that annealing solid-state films above this temperature causes SubPc aggregation and unusual aggregation-induced dual emission.
Finally, SubPc has been incorporated as terminal group of either α (initiating side) (139) or ω (propagation side) (141) ends of some polymers, such as poly(styrene) and poly(n-butylacrylate), produced by nitroxide mediated polymerization (NMP).143 Whereas 139 (Scheme 17) was prepared by selective coupling of SubPc-Br to the terminal carboxylic acid group at the α-side end of the pre-polymer, 141 has been achieved through the preparation of a SubPc-TEMPO derivative 140, which subsequently reacts with the nitroxide fragment of the preformed polymer yielding 141 (Scheme 17). In both cases, the incorporation of the SubPc chromophore was confirmed using a photodiode array detector connected in line with a gel permeation chromatography (GPC) setup.
 Scheme 17  Synthetic routes towards polymers 139 and 141 in which SubPcs are incorporated as terminal groups through the axial position. 


2.5.3 Peripherally linked “all-SubPc” systems. 
2.5.3.1 Non-conjugated subphthalocyanine dimers. 
Binuclear SubPcs have been prepared by double cyclotrimerization of a bis(phthalonitrile) precursor aiming to double the absorption properties of these chromophores. In particular, a 4,4′-bis(phenoxy) bridge was used to link two phthalonitrile units by their 4-position making use of the easy ipso substitution reaction of 4-nitrophthalonitrile by phenols. Mixed cyclotrimerization of the corresponding bis(phthalonitrile) and 1,2-dicyanobenzene in the presence of BBr3, followed by substitution with different 4-substituted phenols led to a series of binuclear SubPcs 142131 (Table 6) that were tested as catalysts in lithium/thionyl chloride (Li/SOCl2) batteries, observing a significant increase of the stored energy and the capacity of the batteries whose electrolytes contain these SubPc derivatives.

2.5.3.2 Conjugated subphthalocyanine dimers. 
SubPcs have also been connected through a peripheral butadiynediyl bridge by Glaser–Hay dimerization of the corresponding trimethylsilyl-functionalized SubPc precursor. Protodesilylation with an excess of AgF followed by dimerization reaction in the presence of CuCl and TMEDA in an open flask gave 143 (Table 6) in a good yield at room temperature, and slightly higher yield when performed at 40 °C.279 SubPc dimer 143 was obtained as a diastereomeric mixture of a meso form and a pair of enantiomers, which could not be separated by column chromatography and were non-distinguishable by NMR spectroscopic analysis. As expected, dimer 143 exhibits a redshifted Q-absorption maximum at 593 nm, in line with the extended π-conjugated system.
Two SubPc units were connected to 1,2-diethynylbenzene by Sonogashira coupling with monoiodo-SubPc using the previously optimized Pd2dba3/AsPh3/CuI reaction conditions, affording SubPc dimer 144 (Table 6) in good yield.172 Since the starting chiral iodoSubPc consisted on a racemic mixture, the corresponding dimer 144 was obtained as a mixture of meso and racemic diastereomers, that could be separated by column chromatography and assigned by chiral HPLC (while the racemic gave two peaks the meso diastereomers gave only one).
2.5.3.3 Conjugated subphthalocyanine cyclic oligomers. 
Considering the sensitivity of the SubPc macrocycle towards nucleophiles and the restricted chemistry that this peculiar macrocycle allows in general, palladium-mediated coupling reactions have been the best tool that SubPc chemist handled for the preparation of multicomponent SubPc based systems. Monoiodo-SubPc derivatives are by far the most used starting materials for performing these chemical transformations. In contrast, ortho-diiodoSubPc has been scarcely employed in the preparation of multicomponent systems. Two beautiful examples of SubPcs incorporated into conjugated cyclic polyacetylenic frameworks 145 and 146 have been described so far. Such arrays aim at both increasing the absorption properties and extending the chromophore conjugation, with a consequent shifting of the visible absorption bands to the infrared or near-infrared regions.
The first report by Torres and co-workers consisted on the preparation of a dehydro[18]annulene fused SubPc trimers 145 (Table 6) by a palladium-mediated homocoupling of the corresponding ortho-diethynyl-substituted SubPcs.282 Optimization of the reaction conditions led the PdCl2(PPh3)2/CuI/DABCO/I2 catalytic system. Compounds 145 were obtained as a mixture of two topoisomers that could not be separated, one with all SubPc units facing the same direction, and another with one of the SubPc moieties facing the opposite direction. In a more recent example, a conjugated SubPc dimer 146 sharing a conjugated cyclic bridge was published by the group of Nielsen (Table 6).53 The two SubPc units were fused to the radiaannulene core with 10% yield by a four-fold Sonogashira coupling of ortho-diiodoSubPc and the bis(triisopropylsilyl)-protected tetraethynylethene (TEE) monomer, using AsPh3.
The Q band absorption maximum undergoes a progressive bathochromic shift on going from a single-unit SubPc to the fused dehydro[18]annulene trimer 145 by ca. 20 nm per SubPc unit up to 623 nm. The extinction coefficients also increased according to the increasing number of SubPc chromophores. In the case of the radialene fused SubPc dimer 146 the red-shift is even stronger (60 nm compared to that of unsubstituted SubPc up to 621 nm), and it shows a small degree of solvatochromism. Its decrease in fluorescence when increasing the solvent polarity in the heptane, CH2Cl2, MeCN progression suggests quenching via a charge-transfer state. In addition, polyacetylenic scaffolds such as radiaannulenes and dehydroannulenes are molecules with good electron acceptor properties. Indeed, trimer 145 exhibited easier oxidation and reduction processes, by 30 and 50 mV respectively, than the SubPc reference. The radialene 146 is an excellent multi-electron acceptor, exhibiting four closely spaced one-electron reductions at −1.42 V, −1.68 V, −1.92 V and −2.16 V during the negatively going scan, corresponding to the formation of the radical anion, dianion, radical trianion and tetraanion, respectively.
2.5.3.4 Covalent subphthalocyanine-based porous polymeric materials. 
The incorporation of π-conjugated chromophore units into porous organic polymers (POPs) allowed for the implementation of their applications as materials for optoelectronics by combining their porosity with strong light harvesting capability and versatile photophysical and electronic properties. Only very recently, SubPcs have been included as monomers in porous organic materials, either covalent or supramolecularly built.
Very recently, the polymerization of an axially phenoxy-substituted C3-tribromoSubPc 147 and 1,4-diethynylbenzene by a triple Sonogashira coupling in two-dimensions has been reported (Scheme 18).169 An extension of this methodology was applied to the preparation of a 3D-polymer by reaction of tribromo-SubPc 148 bearing a 4-bromophenoxy axial group, that allows extending the porous polymer along the z dimension (Scheme 18). Polymers 149 and 150 were characterized FT-IR spectroscopy, following the disappearance of the C–H terminal alkyne stretching vibrations of the starting 1,4-diethynylbenzene at around 3280 cm−1. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) indicated a great thermal stability of the SubPc-containing polymers 149 and 150, with decomposition temperatures (5% weight loss) of 314 °C and 276 °C, respectively. However, their powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) patterns showed no diffraction peaks, indicating that they were amorphous. To confirm the porosities of SubPc-polymers the nitrogen gas adsorption-desorption isotherms at 77 K were measured, suggesting a limited microporous structure in these polymers. The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area was calculated to be 434 m2 g−1 for 149 and 106 m2 g−1 for 150, respectively. The smaller specific surface area of 150 may be attributed to the congested structures around the SubPc units caused by the axial coupling. Both SubPc-polymers showed a similar pore size distribution (PSD) with dominant pore sizes at 2.8 nm and 3.4 nm, respectively, confirming their mesoporosity. Moreover, they were tested as photosensitizers for singlet oxygen generation. The better singlet oxygen generation ability upon irradiation at 690 nm of SubPc-polymer 149 may be attributed to its more red-shifted light absorption and a larger specific surface area.
 Scheme 18  Covalent SubPc-based porous polymeric materials. 


2.5.4 Supramolecular subphthalocyanine-containing assemblies. 
2.5.4.1 Metallomolecular assemblies. 
Directional and strong metal–ligand interactions had been the most popular recognition motif for the construction of well-defined supramolecular architectures based on SubPcs. In particular, pyridyl-substituted SubPcs have been employed as building blocks to build different assemblies based on Pd(II)-, Pt(II)- and Zn(II)-pyridine interactions (Fig. 36). The first report by Torres and Claessens in 2002 described the self-assembly of a hollow capsule M3L2 SubPc made of two preorganized tritopic C3-symmetric tri(pyridyl)SubPcs 151 and three Pd(II) ions that coordinate two pyridine ligands each.283 The well-defined internal concave surface of this metallo-supramolecular homodimeric cage made it an ideal vessel for the complexation of convex-shaped C60 and C70 fullerenes.
 Fig. 36  Peripheral- and axially-substituted pyridyl SubPcs employed to build metallosupramolecular assemblies. 
Indeed, upon adapting the internal volume of the SubPc capsule incorporating a rigid alkynyl bridge between the SubPc and the pyridine ligands, cage 158 showed strong interaction with C60 in acetone solution, where C60 itself is insoluble.284 In addition to the two tris(pyridyl) SubPc ligands, each Pd(II) and Pt(II) of cages 158 is coordinated to other secondary ligands, such as diamines (ethylenediamine, en, 158a284 or disphosphines (1,3-bis(diphenylphosphino)-propane, dppp, 158b,92 1,1′-bis(diphenylphosphino)-ferrocene, dppf, 158c92 or (S)-BINAP), 158d,285 which help to provide additional properties to the cages, such as solubility, redox and chirality (Fig. 37). Systematic complexation studies of C60, C70 and their soluble PCBM derivatives were performed with cages 158b and 158c using different spectroscopic techniques.92 Specifically, titration experiments by 1H and 31P NMR spectroscopy indicated a slow equilibrium on the NMR time scale, which made possible to calculate binding constants by direct integration from the spectra (see Table 7). The values obtained from the 1H-NMR spectra reveal that C70-PCBM forms more stable associates with these SubPc capsules. The lower Ka values obtained with the dppf cage 158c may be a consequence of the rigidity of both the SubPc and the ferrocene ligand, which, when combined, do not easily fulfill the required square-planar geometry of the metal. Moreover, encapsulation studies between metallosupramolecular capsule 158b (M = Pt) and different Pc–C60 fullerene conjugates have been carried out (Fig. 38),286 showing their potential of these cages in the construction of complex photoactive systems based on the combination of supramolecular and covalent binding of SubPcs and Pcs.
 Fig. 37  Metallosupramolecular SubPc dimeric capsules 158 made of different metals (Pd, Pt) and secondary ligands.

 Fig. 38  Schematic representation of SubPc-based metallosupramolecular dimeric capsules 158 showing a cavity adapted to the complexation of (a) fullerenes and (b) Pc–C60 dyads. 
All SubPc-based capsules 158a–c possess a plane of symmetry due to chiral self-discrimination during the self-assembling process, which results in the stereoselective formation of a heterochiral capsule containing one M and one P enantiomer (Fig. 39a). However, by using a chiral diphosphine ligand, namely (S)-BINAP, the coordination positions of the Pt metal could be arranged in such a way that the formation of the meso cage was prevented, and for the first time, a homochiral capsule was reported (Fig. 39b).
 Fig. 39  Partial chemical structure of (a) heterochiral meso cage 158b and (b) homochiral cage 158d formed from different secondary ligands. 
A preliminary study of multicomponent self-assembly example through both in situ dynamic covalent imine formation and subsequent coordinative pyridine-Fe(II) linkage has been reported by Torres and co-workers.174 Specifically, the three components, a C3-triamino-substituted SubPc, 2-formylpyridine and a Fe(II) salt were mixed in acetonitrile or DMF and heated at 45 °C for at least 24 h. The formation of a Fe2SubPc3 helicate in the dynamic equilibrium was inferred by mass spectrometry, where peaks corresponding to tetra-charged [Fe2SubPc3]4+ species were detected, thus indicating that only two of the three amino groups attached at each C3-SubPc ligand were involved in the formation of the metallo-organic complex.
Different axially linked SubPc-based metallosupramolecular assemblies have been published based on the coordination of the axial pyridyloxy group of SubPcs 155 and 157 to the central Zn(II) or Ru(II) ion of porphyrins and phthalocyanines.287 More recently, Kimura reported a supramolecular SubPc-Pc dyad to be used in DSSCs, based on the coordination of the peripheral pyridine substituents of SubPc 154 to the central Zn(II) cation of typically bulky Pc-COOH. 154 displayed panchromatic absorption through complementary light harvesting by the SubPc unit, which is transferred to the Pc by a FRET mechanism.113 The formation of the complexes between SubPc 154 and a model Pc compound was monitored by absorption and fluorescence spectroscopy, determining high stability constants, namely 14 × 104 M−1 in toluene.
Moreover, coordinating the 3-pyridiloxy axial substituent of SubPc 156 to the Zn metal center of a Pc–corrole conjugate allowed to self-assemble the first example of an array composed of three different porphyrinoids, which drives a cascade of energy and charge-transfer processes.81
2.5.4.2 Subphthalocyanine–fullerene complexes. 
The use of C60 derivatives as electron acceptors in most organic photovoltaic devices, and the importance of controlling the contact between donors and acceptors to facilitate the electron transfer between them, has pushed forward the search of fullerene receptors during the last years. Among them, π-conjugated curved molecules such as corannulene or SubPcs have been proposed as ideal molecules to bind complementary spherically shaped fullerene guests, as a result of concave–convex intermolecular interactions. Since fullerenes are scarcely polar molecules, the electrostatic interactions with the receptor might be negligible, therefore the contact area between host and guest should be maximized to enhance dispersion forces, in most cases exploiting π–π interactions. In order to obtain efficient OPVs, not only a good complexation has to be ensured, but also some electronic matching is needed to favour the electron transfer process. The bowl-shaped geometry of SubPc provides the best topology for noncovalent π–π complex formation with the curved surfaces of C60 and C70 fullerenes. Computational studies indicate that the closest contact between C60 and SubPc is 3.20 Å with the SubPc five-membered rings. In contrast, C70 enables two types of interactions with the SubPc receptor, namely, through its polar region, or by the equatorial region, the latter being more favorable, since C70 exposes a larger area maximizing close contacts.288 These calculations also predicted the higher stability of the 2:1 SubPc/C60 complexes. Indeed, SubPc and fullerene heterolayers have been successfully incorporated as donor–acceptor components in organic heterojunction photovoltaic devices. In the case of endohedral fullerenes, encapsulation is predicted to occur preferentially by the face without contacts with the inner cluster in the case of Sc3N@C80 and over the face of fullerene next to the scandium atoms for Sc3N@C80.289
Some X-ray crystal structures of SubPc/C60 complexes have been reported in the solid state favouring either the 1:1 or the 1:2 stoichiometry, depending on the axial and peripheral substituents (see Section 2.4.1.2). Nevertheless, interactions between SubPc receptors and C60 fullerene in solution remain unexplored to a large extent, remaining unclear if such interactions are strong enough in solution to affect spectroscopic properties of the noncovalent donor–acceptor assemblies. Experimental studies by Torres and co-workers on the complexation of different fullerenes by cone-shaped SubPc molecules bearing different type of peripheral substituents have shown that shape complementarity alone is not enough for the formation of strong complexes between SubPcs and fullerenes.290 Additionally, structural factors such as the electronic nature of the SubPc (which is modulated by the peripheral substituents) and the incorporation of peripheral medium-sized alkyl chains seem to play an important role in the binding process, influencing both the stoichiometry and strength of the complexes formed with C60 and C70 in solution. Accordingly, while perfluorinated, electron-deficient SubPc does not interact appreciably with fullerenes, unsubstituted SubPc forms weak 1:1 complexes and electron-donating hexalkylthio-substituted SubPc derivatives render jellyfish like 2:1 complexes with C60 (or C70) with binding constants as large as 105 M−1 (Fig. 40a). In line with these results, Nemykin carried out a detailed complexation study using unsubstituted SubPc, SubNc, and more interestingly, SubPc 160 bearing electron-donor α-(hexathiophenyl) groups (Fig. 40b).184 Only the electron-rich SubPc 160 showed spectroscopic changes during the UV-vis and steady-state fluorescence titration experiments, indicative of a stronger interaction with C60 and C70 fullerenes. In particular, a new weak charge-transfer band in the NIR region centered at 678 nm (C60) and 676 (C70) was observed during the titrations. More recently, preorganized tweezer SubPc-based dimeric receptors have been reported by Nielsen and Torres (Fig. 40c and d).58,172 The design of receptor 99 is quite elaborated and gather the previous experience in fullerene complexation as it contains two octaalkylsubstituted SubPcs attached through rigid ethynyl spacers to a rigid platform, which is the syn-topoisomer of a SubPc fused dimer, molding a well-defined cavity. Moreover, axial substitution at the two complexing SubPc monomers with a bulky 4-tert-butylphenoxy group was envisioned to enforce the desired face-to-face conformer suitable for fullerene complexation. Stronger interactions with C60 and C70 in both cases allowed to determine by NMR titration and Job plot analysis association constants in toluene in the order of 103 M−1 for receptor 161 and values of Ka of 105 M−1 for 99, as inferred from UV-vis absorption and fluorescence titrations.
 Fig. 40  SubPc–fullerene complexes reported in solution showing different stoichiometries. In all of them two SubPc units embrace a fullerene guest. Adapted with permission from ref. 184 and 188. Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. 
2.5.4.3 Supramolecular subphthalocyanine-based porous polymeric materials. 
McGrier et al. reported in 2017 the first SubPc-based porous organic polymeric frameworks (SubPc-POPs 162 and 163) incorporating SubPc and 1,4-diboronic acid monomers (Fig. 41). It was based on the reaction between hexahydroxy-SubPc and 1,4-benzene diboronic acid in a 1:1 (v/v) mixture of dioxane and mesitylene at 105 °C for three days.120 Both the choice of the reaction solvent, temperature and the substitution of the axial chlorine atom by a phenoxy substituent were crucial factors for the success of this process. The POP-SubPc derivatives 162 and 163 were characterized by different techniques. The FT-IR spectra of SubPc-POPs 162 display the characteristic CN stretching of the SubPc monomer at 1465–1468 cm−1, and the B–O stretching of the axial ligands at 1046–1039 cm−1. In addition, the B–O stretching modes at 1347–1352 cm−1 are indicative of the boronate ester linkages. Most importantly, the permanent porosities of the SubPc-POPs were evaluated by nitrogen gas adsorption isotherms at 77 K. Application of the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) model over the low-pressure region provided surface areas of 231 and 93 m2 g−1 for SubPc-POP 162 and 163, respectively. Nonlocal density functional theory (NLDFT) was used to estimate the pore size distributions of SubPc-POPs 162 and 163 yielding values of 1.7 and 1.5 nm, respectively, which is indicative of the microporosity of the materials. However, powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) analysis of the SubPc-POPs revealed amorphous materials with no apparent long-range. Authors claim this disorder to be related to the large size of the axial phenoxy substituents, preventing the monomers from forming the desired concave-to-ligand stacking interactions. In contrast to the monomers in solution, fluorescence of SubPc-POPs was fully quenched in the solid-state, most probably due to the formation of disordered J-aggregates.
 Fig. 41  Supramolecular SubPc-based polymers. 
The introduction of terpyridine peripheral substituents in SubPcs allowed the formation of metallo-supramolecular polymers using a transition metal as linker. Tris(terpyridyl)SubPc was treated with Fe(BF4)2 forming the Fe(II) metallo-supramolecular polymer (poly-SubPc–Fe 164) bearing a polyisobutylene at the axial position for enhanced solubility and film formation (Fig. 41).15 The polymer was designed to present electrochromic properties, therefore spectroelectrochemical measurements were performed by monitoring the in situ changes in absorption spectra of polySubPc-Fe 164 upon applying potential ranging from 0.0 to 1.6 V. A gradual decrease in the absorbance peak at 580 nm with simultaneous slight increase in the absorbance at 390 nm was observed, so that at 1.6 V, the absorbance band at 580 nm was completely quenched. The electrochromic behaviour of the poly-SubPc–Fe film was mainly attributed to the oxidation of Fe(II) to Fe(III) at 1.6 V, which prevented the MLCT from Fe to the SubPc ligand, causing discoloration. The MLCT was regained by the reduction of Fe(III) to Fe(II) at 1.0 V, recovering the blue colour of the film. The results showed that the oxidative stability of the poly-SubPc–Fe 164 film is exceptionally good for up to 1000 cycles. In addition, switching times required for transition to bleached and coloured states were relatively short, 8.5 and 4.0 s, respectively. Finally, the coloration efficiency (CE) of poly-SubPc–Fe 164, which is the important parameter representing the quality of electrochromic devices, was calculated to be as high as 325.3 cm2 C−1.
Moreover, a SubPc-based 3D covalent organic framework (COF) capable of forming cocrystals with fullerene (C60) has been simulated by plane-wave density functional theory as a promising next generation photovoltaic material. The results showed that there is a driving force of 0.27 eV for exciton charge transfer to the pocket-bound fullerenes. Charge separation could then occur at the COF–C60 interface, followed by the transfer of the free electron to the nanowire of C60 acceptors with a driving force of 0.20 eV.241

2.6 Subphthalocyanine-containing donor–acceptor systems
The need to deeply understand and control the primary events taking place in natural photosynthesis prompted the construction of a plethora of artificial, nature-mimicking photosynthetic systems with the ultimate aim of converting solar light into other forms of energy, such as chemical or electrical energy.291–297 In this context, much attention has been focused on the study and optimization of the factors that govern the efficiency of photoinduced energy and electron transfer processes as well as their mutual interplay. For this purpose, several donor–acceptor (D–A) models have been reported over the past decades. For the preparation of such multicomponent ensembles, a plethora of molecular building blocks have been employed, such as porphyrinoids and fullerenes to cite a couple.298–303
From the seminal work of Torres, Guldi and Echegoyen,304 SubPcs have proven to be outstanding candidates for the preparation of valuable energy/electron D–A synthetic models. A detailed overview of the SubPc-based D–A systems published in the first decade after this first report has been presented by some of us in 2014.1 The reason of the great suitability of SubPc chromophores as components of D–A hybrids relies on their unique structural and optoelectronic properties. As a matter of fact, SubPcs are characterized by strong absorptions in the UV-vis region, with extinction coefficients of the order of ∼50000 M−1 cm−1 and excitation energies above 2.0 eV. Furthermore, they feature intense fluorescence emission characteristics, small Stoke shifts and low reorganizational energies, as well as a rich redox chemistry. Notably, their photophysical and electrochemical properties can be easily tuned upon proper functionalization of the macrocycle, as evidenced in Sections 2.3.5, 2.3.7 and 2.3.8.62 On the other hand, their versatility in terms of chemical modification of the axial and peripheral substituents, as well as their π-conjugate bowl-shaped structure, allow to include these chromophores in a large variety of covalent and supramolecular multicomponent arrays (see Section 2.5).
SubPcs have been employed either as donor or as acceptor moieties in both energy and electron D–A systems. Their role within such multicomponent ensembles can be tailored by modulating the substitution pattern of the SubPc chromophore, the electronic properties of the molecular counterpart, the polarity of the solvent and/or the energy of the photoexciting radiation.

2.6.1 Photoinduced energy transfer in subphthalocyanine-containing donor–acceptor systems. 
By virtue of their outstanding absorption and emission features, SubPcs can act either as energy-donor antennae or energy-acceptor units in photoactive systems. As a consequence of their energetically high-lying singlet excited states, these chromophores commonly play the role of donors in excitation energy transduction events towards fullerenes, Pcs, Ps, SubPc fused dimers, properly functionalized BODIPYs, azaBODIPYs and pentacene dimers, which generally features smaller singlet excited state energies. On the other hand, they usually act as energy acceptors in combination with chromophores with higher singlet excitation energies, such as BODIPYs, polycyclic aromatics or perylenediimides (PDIs). Oligomeric SubPc-based systems in which different SubPc units act as either energy donor or energy acceptor have also been described.305
2.6.1.1 Subphthalocyanines as energy donors. 
SubPcs have been widely exploited as energy donors in large variety of D–A models. Among the molecular counterparts employed in the construction of such SubPc-based energy transfer systems, fullerenes represent the most explored. As a matter of fact, the good spectral matching between the two chromophores along with the multiple functionalization options offered by fullerene derivatives led to the preparation of a wide range of covalent and supramolecular ensembles featuring SubPcs and C60 as respectively energy donor and energy acceptor moieties.
In one of the pioneering works of Torres, Guldi and Echegoyen, energy transfer within a set of SubPc–C60 dyads (165–167, Fig. 42) covalently linked through the axial position of the SubPc macrocycle via phenoxy spacers was reported.304 In these systems, the efficiency of energy transfer toward the fullerene was found to be regulated by the ortho-, meta, or para-substitution pattern of the isomeric bridging unit.202
 Fig. 42  Chemical structure of the axially linked SubPc–C60 dyads 165–173 investigated by the groups of Torres, Guldi and Echegoyen. 
Control over energy versus electron transfer kinetics in covalently linked SubPc–C60 dyads can be reached through chemical modification of the SubPc unit, as shown some years later by the same authors.197,202 As such, the introduction of proper functionalities at the periphery of the macrocycle allow to tune the energy levels of the species involved in the decay of the SubPc singlet excited state populated upon irradiation. Studies on axially linked SubPc–C60 systems functionalized at the periphery of the macrocycle with iodine, fluorine, catechoxy and triphenylamine substituents (165–172, Fig. 42) pointed out that SubPc singlet energy transduction constitutes the dominating quenching mechanism in the presence of electron-withdrawing peripheral substituents (i.e., for 165–168 in all the investigated solvents and for 169 in non-polar environments).197,202,304 About a decade later, energy transfer to the fullerene unit was shown to be not the only deactivation pathways of photoexcited electron-deficient SubPcs in axially linked SubPc–C60 systems. In particular, in a couple of analogous hybrids (168 and 173, Fig. 42) featuring a C60 moiety connected to the axial position of the macrocycle, the competition between alternative decay mechanisms was regulated by selective population of either the first or the second singlet excited state of the SubPc moiety through control of the excitation wavelength (λex).165 Interestingly, it was observed that while excitation with a lower-energy radiation – which results in exclusive population of the SubPc-centered first singlet excited state – triggers an efficient singlet–singlet energy transfer to the C60 moiety, the generation of the energetically higher lying SubPc second singlet excited state upon irradiation with a lower λex makes electron transfer dominates the deactivation scenario (vide infra). In other words, the energy transfer mechanism is operative as long as the higher lying singlet excited state of the SubPc is not populated. Under this condition, the singlet excited state of the fullerene unit is generated, which undergoes ISC and subsequent triplet–triplet energy transfer to afford the SubPc triplet excited state as final photoproduct. Energy transfer was found to take place also in peripherally connected SubPc–C60 systems, as long as the two units are not strongly electronically coupled.306
Besides in covalently linked hybrids, exciton energy transduction to fullerenes has been studied also in SubPc-based supramolecular assemblies. In 2013, the groups of Guldi and Torres reported the first case of unidirectional energy transfer within SubPc-based non-covalent systems in a series of models featuring a fullerene unit interacting with the concave faces of jellyfish-like hexaalkylthio SubPcs (e.g.159, Fig. 40) through supramolecular interactions.290 Pump probe experiments suggested that upon formation of 2:1 complexes with either C60 or C70, energy transfer to the encapsulated fullerene unit is effective in the decay of the singlet exited states of the outer SubPcs. In the same year, a series of M3L2 SubPc-based capsules forming 1:1 adducts with C60 was reported by the same groups.92 The photophysical characterization of some of these supramolecular systems (158c, d, M = Pd, Fig. 37) revealed that upon photoexcitation of the SubPc an exciton energy transduction scenario is operative, which leads to the population of the singlet excited state of the C60 guest. A sequence of ISC and triplet–triplet energy transfer completes the deactivation cascade, affording as final photoproduct the SubPc-centered triplet excited state.
In most SubPc–fullerene systems undergoing excitation energy transduction, formation of C60 singlet excited state by singlet–singlet energy transfer from the SubPc triggers a cascade of photophysical processes, involving a rate-determining C60-centered ISC to populate the lower-lying C60 triplet excited state and a triplet–triplet excitation energy transduction in which energy is back-transferred to the SubPc. The quenching of the fullerene triplet excited state through the secondary energy transfer event prevents the use of SubPc–fullerene derivatives as triplet state sensitizers. In order to circumvent Dexter-type back energy transfer, direct covalent connection between the two chromophore should be avoided in order to minimize the orbital overlap between the donor and the acceptor, allowing at the same time through-space dipole–dipole interactions at the basis of the Förster-type singlet–singlet energy transfer. With this idea in mind, Sølling and co-workers designed a triad (174, Fig. 43) in which a SubPc and a C60 moiety are connected through a cyanoazulene bridge linked to the axial position of the macrocycle.89 Steady-state and transient spectroscopy measurements along with DFT calculations corroborated that this geometry affords highly efficient Förster-type singlet energy transduction from the SubPc to the fullerene whereas no significant back energy transfer via electron exchange was observed, as expected on the basis of the exponential dependence of the orbital overlap on D–A separation. In light of the aforementioned, a rationalization of the results previously reported by Kim and co-workers in 2008 was proposed.307 In that work, a SubPc–diazobenzene–C60 triad (175, Fig. 43) was presented in which the C60 triplet excited state was detected as final photoproduct in nanosecond transient experiments. Sølling and co-workers underlined the similarity between the role of the cyanoazulene and diazobenzene linkers in the two systems, which prevent electron exchange triplet–triplet energy transfer favouring through-space primary energy transfer.89
 Fig. 43  Chemical structure of the axially linked SubPc–C60 systems 174–175 featuring cyanoazulene and diazobenzene bridges investigated by Sølling, Kay and co-workers. 
Porphyrinoids113,126,195,308–310 have also been employed as energy-accepting counterparts in SubPc-based D–A models.302 In this regard, a supramolecular system was recently reported by Kimura and co-workers, in which excitation energy transfer from a peripherally pyridine-functionalized SubPc (154, Fig. 36) to the interacting ZnPc units was argued to take place on the basis of steady-state fluorescence measurements with an estimated efficiency of 41%.113 Singlet–singlet energy transfer through dipole–dipole interaction was also observed in a SubPc–ZnPor dyad (176, Fig. 44) linked through a phenoxy bridge.126 In this case, a back energy transfer to the SubPc after population of the Por triplet excited state by ISC was inferred from pump–probe experiments. Notably, in an analogous system featuring a free-base Por, fluorescence essays suggested the absence of any interaction between the two chromophores in the excited state.125
 Fig. 44  Chemical structure of dyad 176 investigated by Meech and co-workers. 
Interestingly, singlet–singlet excitation energy transduction was also observed to take place between monomeric SubPcs and SubPc fused dimers. As a matter of fact, the extension of the π-conjugated system in the latter results in a bathochromic shift of the absorption an emission features when compared with monomeric SubPcs. As a result of the spectral matching between the emission of monomeric SubPcs and the absorption of SubPc fused dimers, energy transfer to the extended π-system is feasible. In this connection, the groups of Guldi and Torres reported very recently an intriguing all-SubPc system featuring a SubPc fused dimer covalently linked through axial ethynyl spacers to the periphery of two monomeric SubPc units (99, Fig. 32).58 An initial evidence of excitation energy transfer from the monomer-centered singlet excited state (2.09 eV) to the dimer-centered singlet excited state (1.77 eV) upon selective excitation of the SubPc monomers emerged from steady-state fluorescence experiments, in which a dramatic quenching of the SubPc emission was observed along with an enhancement of the dimer fluorescence. In pump–probe measurements, selective population of the monomers singlet excited states (λex = 550 nm) turned out to trigger an ultrafast and unidirectional energy transfer to the dimer, which kinetic overcame the time resolution limit of the experimental setup. On the other hand, an electron transfer scenario was observed upon photoexcitation of the SubPc dimer moiety (vide infra).
Although the singlet excited state of BODIPY dyes usually lies lower in energy with respect to the singlet excited state of SubPcs, boron dipyrromethene chromophores can be converted into good energy acceptor candidates in SubPc-based D–A systems upon proper functionalization of the pyrrolic rings. As known, the introduction of suitable functionalities in the BODIPY core has the potential to tune to a large extent the excitation energies of these dyes, modulating their energy donor/acceptor behaviour.311 As a matter of fact, the functionalization of the α positions with styryl groups results in a substantial bathochromic shift of the absorption features, turning the spectral matching between SubPc emission and BODIPY absorption rather favorable.98,136,312–314 In this context, two sets of SubPc-BODIPY conjugates (177–181, Fig. 45) differing in the number and in the nature of the styryl units connected to the 3,5-position of the BODIPY dye were recently presented.98,136 In all these derivatives, energy transfer from the photoexcited SubPc was postulated on the basis of the quenching of the SubPc emission band and the increasing of the BODIPY-centered emission, although in neither of these works pump–probe experiments were reported to corroborate the steady-state experimental evidence.
 Fig. 45  Chemical structure of the SubPc-BODIPY systems 177–181 investigated by Çoşut and co-workers. 
Despite azaBODIPYs constitute a well-known class of BODIPY analogues with significantly red-shifted spectral features, no reports on SubPc-azaBODIPY systems had been published until 2014, when the group of D’Souza presented a D–A model featuring an azaBODIPY unit linked to the axial position of two SubPc moieties (Fig. 46).99 In triad 182, control over the deactivation cascade triggered upon photoexcitation was reached through selective population of the singlet excited state of one of the photoactive moieties as well as by tuning solvent polarity. In particular, when the SubPc units are photoexcited in toluene, dipole–dipole energy transfer dominates over electron-exchange interactions affording the azaBODIPY singlet excited state, which then undergoes charge separation. Upon photoexcitation of the SubPc units in a more polar solvent or selective excitation of the azaBODIPY moiety, an electron transfer scenario is operative (vide infra). A similar photophysical behaviour was observed in the azaBODIPY-based triad 183 (Fig. 46) reported one year later from the same group, where one of the SubPc moieties had been replaced by a C60 unit.109 Also in this case, the operativity of the two competitive decay mechanisms (i.e. energy versus electron transfer) upon photoexcitation of the SubPc was found to be determined by the polarity of the solvent, being singlet–singlet energy transfer to the azaBODIPY chromophore dominating in toluene.
 Fig. 46  Chemical structure of the SubPc-BODIPY systems 182–183 investigated by D’Souza and co-workers. 
Remarkably, ultrafast and quantitative singlet energy transfer from photoexcited SubPcs has been recently exploited to trigger singlet fission in meta-phenylene tethered pentacene dimers (Pnc2).
