To this end, the groups of Tykwinski, Torres and Guldi prepared a series of conjugates in which a Pnc2 unit was connected through a carboxylate functionality to the central boron atom of an hydrogenated, a triphenoxy or an hexathiooctyl SubPc (184–186, Fig. 47).78 The comprehensive spectroscopic characterization of these derivatives revealed that upon photoexcitation of the SubPc chromophore, energy is funnelled via dipole–dipole energy transfer to the Pnc2 moiety, which undergoes singlet fission to afford a pair of correlated Pnc2 triplet excited states. As an extension of this work, control over FRET and singlet fission dynamics was reached by means of rational spacer design within a second series of SubPc-Pnc2 derivatives (Fig. 47).82 As a matter of fact, upon tailoring of the length and the flexibility of the molecular bridge connecting the SubPc and the Pnc2 moieties in conjugates 187–190, is was possible to tune the rate of the energy transfer as well as the dynamics of intramolecular singlet fission by a factor of 3.2 and 1.6, respectively.
 Fig. 47  Chemical structure of the SubPc-Pnc2 systems 184–190 investigated by Tykwinski, Torres, Guldi and co-workers. 
A peculiar case of SubPc-based hybrid systems is constituted by dyads featuring a photochromic unit connected to the SubPc macrocycle (Fig. 48). Photoisomerization of a dihydroazulene moiety covalently linked to the central boron of a SubPc in 191 was found to have an impact on the fluorescence features of the macrocycle.57 In particular, an almost quantitative quenching of the SubPc emission was observed upon complete conversion of the photochromic moiety to vinylheptafulvene. Excitation energy transduction from the SubPc to the photoswitch moiety was suggested by the authors as a possible rationale on the basis of distance and spectral-matching considerations, although the occurrence of an electron transfer event was not ruled out. An analogous system had been previously reported by Shen and co-workers, featuring a dithienylethene moiety as photochromic unit (192).22 In this case, an energy or electron transfer process was proposed to rationalize the observed fluorescence quenching in steady-state measurements. Nevertheless, it is worth to mention that in both these systems an in-depth photophysical characterization would be needed in order to shed further light on the operative deactivation mechanisms.
 Fig. 48  Chemical structure of the SubPc-based systems 191–192 incorporating photochromic units investigated by Nielsen, Shen and co-workers. 
2.6.1.2 Subphthalocyanines as energy acceptors. 
Although in energy D–A systems SubPc chromophores have been mainly employed as donors, several examples are known in which these macrocycles act as energy acceptors in the presence of a suitable photoactive counterpart.
In this regard, BODIPYs represent good candidates as energy donor moieties by virtue of their absorption and emission features.139,312 The impact of the substitution pattern of the BODIPY pyrrolic rings on the energy transfer dynamics within SubPc-BODIPY systems has been studied by Çoşut and co-workers, who compared the energy transfer quantum yields and the fluorescence lifetimes for a couple of conjugates (179 and 193, Fig. 45 and 49) featuring respectively a 3,5-ethyl substituted and a 1,3,5,7-methyl substituted BODIPY unit.139 In these systems, the repulsion between the β methyl groups and the linker was deemed to induce a co-planarization of the two dyes in the tetrasubstituted derivative, resulting in a change of the orbital energies of the BODIPY moiety and, in turns, in the enhancement of the energy transfer efficiency. Anyway, it is worth to mention that no transient measurements were presented to corroborate the reported experimental parameters.
 Fig. 49  Chemical structure of SubPc-BODIPY 193 investigated by Çoşut and co-workers. 
Energy transfer to the SubPc macrocycle was inferred also in SubPc-PDI conjugates, but neither quantitative data regarding fluorescence quenching, energy transfer efficiencies or fluorescence lifetimes, nor transient measurements were presented for the investigated derivatives.124 Truxene and pyrene derivatives have also been employed as energy acceptor units in SubPc-based D–A systems.156,157 FRET from an apical tetraphenylethylene moiety has been inferred to justify the aggregation induced emission enhancement observed in SubPc 67 (Fig. 13).119
In addition, it is worth to mention the role of acceptors of SubPcs in triplet–triplet back energy transfer events taking place in several D–A hybrids, among which the most common example are SubPc–fullerene systems (vide supra).89,92,165


2.6.2 Photoinduced electron transfer in subphthalocyanine-containing donor–acceptor systems. 
In the presence of suitable molecular partners, electron transfer events can be triggered upon photoexcitation in SubPc-based D–A models. In these systems, the donor versus acceptor role of the SubPc unit is mainly determined by the electronic features of the macrocycle – which are largely dependent on the nature of the peripheral substituents – as well as by the electrochemical characteristics of the molecular counterpart. Besides the energetics of the components, other factors such as geometric parameters and solvent polarity contribute to determine the kinetics of charge separation (CS) and charge transfer (CT) processes taking place in these ensembles. In this context, a computational study on a series of molecular triads featuring SubPcs as central light harvesting unit was reported by Mikkelsen et al., which was aimed to modulate the sequential electron transfer capability of the heteroarray through optimization of the orbitals energy alignment.315
Beyond multicomponent systems featuring SubPcs in combination with different chromophores, a few all-SubPc conjugates have been presented in which the SubPc macrocycle acts either as electron donor or as electron acceptor depending on the peripheral substitution pattern. For this purpose, proper selection of the ring substituent is crucial in order to tailor the photophysical behaviour of each SubPc unit within the conjugate. In this context, Shibata and co-workers reported in 2015 a series of symmetrical and unsymmetrical SubPc dyads featuring hydrogen, fluoro or trifluoroethoxy (TFEO) substituents at the periphery (Fig. 9).70 In the case of the H12SubPc-TFEO12SubPc heterodimer 28, a substantial quenching of the SubPcs fluorescence was observed, which was attributed by the authors to an intramolecular electron transfer from the hydrogenated moiety to the TFEO-substituted SubPc. The entity of the quenching was found to be dependent on the substitution pattern of the axial hydroquinone linker and was rationalized in terms of through-bond and through-space mechanism. On the other hand, for the F12SubPc-TFEO12SubPc heterodimer 29, a decreasing of the emission intensity was detected only in the presence of an ortho substitution pattern of the bridging unit. In an analogous trimer linked though a 1,3,5-trihydroxybenzene bridge presented by the same authors (30, Fig. 9), electron transfer from the hydrogenated SubPc to the fluorinated SubPc unit directly or via a TFEO-SubPc moiety was also inferred.73 Anyway, no pump–probe measurement where reported to corroborate the occurrence of charge transfer or charge separation processes.
Turning to fused SubPc systems, a push–pull heterodimer was presented in 2015 by Zango et al. featuring an electron-rich t-butyl functionalized half and an electron-poor chlorinated half (98, Scheme 12).48 Spectral evidence of formation of a charge transfer polarized state (<1.7 eV) along with an energetically lower-lying delocalized singlet excited state (1.73 eV) came from the detection of a dual emission in steady-state fluorescence measurements, as well as from pump–probe assays in which the transient signatures of both the excited states could be distinguished.
2.6.2.1 Subphthalocyanines as electron donors. 
Fullerenes cover a central role as electron acceptor partners for SubPcs in the construction of D–A systems by virtue of their unique redox and structural properties such as their favorable reduction potentials and their characteristic low reorganization energies, which favour the formation of long lived excited states.301
The photophysical behaviour of axially linked SubPc–C60 dyads undergoing electron transfer events upon photoexcitation has been thoroughly explored by the groups of D’Souza, Echegoyen, Guldi and Torres.93,104,105,107,164,197,202 The rational choice of the ring substituents of the SubPc unit as well as the incorporation of molecular spacers connecting them to the SubPc benzenes or the exploitation of supramolecular interactions involving the peripheral functionalities have been used as strategies to modulate the dynamics of photoinduced electron transfer within such SubPc–C60 derivatives.
The electronic features of the ring substituents are known to influence to a large extent the dominating deactivation mechanism in SubPc–C60 conjugates upon photoexcitation. In particular, in the presence of electron-rich functionalities electron transfer is commonly favoured over energy transfer. As a matter of fact, electron transfer to the fullerene unit has been observed from diphenylamino-substituted SubPcs (170–172, Fig. 42) or catechoxy-substituted SubPcs (169, Fig. 42) in polar environments.197,202 In 2016, a series of novel SubPc–C60 dyads (194–198, Fig. 50) in which the electron-donor character of the SubPc had been enhanced through functionalization of either the α- or the β-positions of the macrocycle with thioaryl substituent was presented by the group of D’Souza.105 In all these derivatives, a fast CS process (kCS = 1.4–6.3 × 1010 s−1) was found to take place after population of the SubPc singlet excited state, as corroborated by pump–probe absorption studies in polar solvents, in which the transient features corresponding to the SubPc˙+ and C60˙− species where detected. As expected for C60-based electroactive systems, the kinetic constant for the subsequent charge recombination (CR) process was found to be one or two order of magnitude lower than that of CS.
 Fig. 50  Chemical structure of the axially linked SubPc–C60 systems 194–198 investigated by D’Souza and co-workers. 
In the same year, a SubPc–C60 system (199, Fig. 51) featuring 4-iodophenoxy substituent at the SubPc periphery was reported, in which electron transfer was disclosed to be the dominant quenching mechanism upon population of either the SubPc or the C60 singlet excited states.107 It is interesting to notice that this deactivation scenario is opposite to that observed when the iodo substituents are directly linked to the SubPc macrocycle (165–167, Fig. 42), as in this case a singlet–singlet energy transfer to the fullerene moiety was detected.197 As proposed by the authors, such a difference in the excited-state photophysical features can be ascribed to the moderately electron-rich character of the phenoxy group in 199, which is in contrast with the electron-withdrawing effect of the directly linked halogen atoms in 165–167.
 Fig. 51  Chemical structure of the axially linked SubPc–C60 systems 199–203 investigated by D’Souza and co-workers. 
The energetics of SubPc–C60 system (and, in turn, electron transfer kinetics) can be modulated also by taking advantage of supramolecular interactions involving the peripheral substituents of the SubPc unit, as described in 2015 by D’Souza and co-workers.164 As an example, π–π stacking interactions between graphene and the SubPc peripheral pyrene moieties in 200 and 201 (Fig. 51) result in enhanced CS and CR kinetics. The acceleration of the electron transfer events taking place in these graphene-decorated hybrids was ascribed to the tuning of the free energy of the processes, even if the contribution of geometric factors and the impact of graphene defects on the excited-state kinetics may also be taken into account.
In D–A systems, a strategy for the generation of long-lived charge separated states consist in the activation of a multistep electron transfer cascade through the rational organization of molecular units with modulated redox potentials. As a result, the generated charges are distanced and CR kinetics are slowed down. This considerations are at the basis of the design of a multicomponent SubPc–C60 system (202, Fig. 51) in which three phenothiazine (PTZ) units are appended to the macrocycle through phenoxy linkers and act as hole transporter.93 Free energy calculations suggested that in this system the primary electron transfer to form (PTZ)3–SubPc˙+–C60˙− and the subsequent hole transfer to form (PTZ)2PTZ˙+–SubPc–C60˙− either from the SubPc or the C60 singlet excited states are both exoergonic.93,104 Femtosecond and nanosecond transient experiments provided spectral proof of the formation of these radical ion species. The estimated rate constants (as large as 5.41 × 10−6 s−1 in benzonitrile and 4.00 × 10−6 s−1 in toluene) and the experimental lifetimes of the final radical ion pair (as large as 185 ns in benzonitrile and 250 ns in toluene) were found to be respectively lower and higher than the ones normally observed in the absence of a hole shifting unit, confirming the effectiveness of the electron transfer cascade for the stabilization of the charge-separated state.105,107 The presence of the phenoxy spacer between the SubPc core and the PTZ units turned out to have a crucial impact on the kinetics of the electron transfer events. In this regard, an analogous system (203, Fig. 51) was presented in which the PTZ moieties were directly connected to the SubPc macrocycle.104 Spectroscopic, electrochemical and computational studies revealed a strong intramolecular electronic interaction between SubPc π-system and PTZ unit. As a result resulting of the delocalization of the HOMO on the whole SubPc-(PTZ)3 unit, the latter act as a single entity in the electron transfer process, leading to the [(PHZ)3-SubPc]˙+–C60˙− charge separation product which subsequently decay to the SubPc-centered triplet excited through a rather fast charge recombination process (kCR = 2.1 or 1.41 × 109 s−1 in benzonitrile and toluene, respectively).
In peripherally linked SubPc–C60 dyads, the nature of the spacer was found to determine the degree of orbital overlap between the SubPc and C60 π-surfaces and, in turn, the deactivation pathway upon photoexcitation, being electron transfer the dominating scenario in shorter-spaced conjugates.306
Evidence of sequential CS and charge shift were found in a SubPc-based supramolecular system recently reported by Guldi and Torres groups featuring an electron-withdrawing SubPc fused dimer functionalized at the axial positions with two monomeric electron-rich alkylthio SubPc units (99, Fig. 32).58 Upon photoexcitation of the dimer moiety, a polarized SubPcMδ+–SubPcDδ− CT state or a ionic SubPcM˙+–SubPcD˙− charge separated state is formed, depending on the polarity of the solvent. If laser irradiation is carried out in presence of either C60 or C70, the primary CT/CS process is followed by a charge shift from the SubPc dimer to the fullerene to afford the SubPcM˙+–C60˙− charge separated state, which undergoes charge recombination to afford the SubPc monomer-centered triplet excited state. Charge migration is driven by the gain in electrostatic energy which results from the decrease of the fullerene–SubPc separation and overcompensate the lower electron-withdrawing character of C60 with respect to the SubPc fused dimer. On the other hand, excitation of the monomeric SubPc units led to an energy transfer deactivation scenario (vide supra).
Electron transfer within supramolecular complexes featuring an electron rich hexathiophenyl-substituted SubPc and C60 (160, Fig. 40) was examined in 2016 by Nemykin and co-workers.184 In this system, signature peaks related to the presence of the C60˙− species were detected in pump–probe measurements. DFT calculations provided additional support to the hypothesis of an electron transfer from the α-substituted thiophenyl SubPc to the fullerene moiety.
1,1,4,4-Tetracyanobuta-1,3-diene (TCBD) has recently emerged as an intriguing photoactive partner for SubPcs. The first report of TCBD-containing D–A systems based on SubPcs was published by the groups of Torres and Guldi in 2017.43 In this work, two SubPc derivatives were presented in which a TCBD–aniline unit was linked to the axial position of the macrocycle (10 and 11, Fig. 6). The electrochemical and photophysical properties of the conjugates were found to be deeply influenced by the nature of the peripheral substituents (i.e. hydrogen or fluorine atoms). In the case of the hydrogenated derivative 10, CT and CS features were evidenced in the ground and in the excited states, respectively. As a matter of fact, steady-state spectroscopic measurements revealed a substantial ground-state CT interaction between the donor π-surface of the macrocycle and the acceptor TCBD moiety, as suggested by the bathochromic shift of the low-energy absorption band – which broadens in polar solvents – as well as by fluorescence emission and excitation assays. On the other hand, pump–probe experiments provided conclusive evidence of electron transfer upon photoexcitation of either the SubPc or the TCBD–aniline ground CT state, which afford the H12SubPc˙+–TCBD˙−–aniline radical ion pair. In this regard, it is worth to underline the ultrafast kinetics of both CS and CR processes, which occurs in the picoseconds timescale, in comparison to the kCS and kCR values normally observed for C60-based systems. For the fluorinated derivative 11, as well as for the precursor SubPc–ethynyl-aniline conjugates, a completely different scenario was observed (vide infra).
As an extension of this work, and with the aim to investigate the effect of the anchoring position of the TCBD–aniline moieties to the SubPc on the electrochemical and photophysical characteristics of the system, two SubPc-based conjugates were studied featuring three or four TCBD–aniline units at the peripheral or axial and peripheral positions of the macrocycle (12 and 13, Fig. 6).46 The synthesis of 12 had previously been published by Nielsen and co-workers, although in this case neither the electrochemical characterization nor an in-depth photophysical study of the system was reported.55 In 12 and 13, the ground-state electronic communication between the SubPc macrocycle and the electron-withdrawing TCBD moieties gives rise to a bathocromic shift and a broadening of the Q-band absorption as well as to an additional low energy transition in the NIR region which intensity is significantly enhanced for the four-substituted derivative. On the contrary, electronic dynamics in the excited state differ from that observed for the axial derivatives 10 and 11.43 Interestingly, the deactivation cascade triggered by excitation of the SubPc moiety is determined by the substitution pattern of the SubPc chromophore. In the peripherally substituted derivative 12, a polarized state (i.e. (SubPcδ+–[(TCBD–aniline)3]δ−)* is formed through CT upon photoexcitation of the SubPc, which directly decays to the ground state. When an additional TCBD–aniline unit is linked to axial position of the macrocycle (13), evidence of generation of a fully charge separated state (i.e., SubPc˙+–[(TCBD–aniline)4]˙−) were detected. Such a radical ion pair state is formed either by deactivation of the CT *(SubPcδ+–[(TCBD–aniline)3]δ− transient species (in less polar solvents) or directly by deactivation of SubPc singlet excited state (in more polar solvents). The stabilization of the charge separated species in 13 can be ascribed to the orbital distribution of the system, which features a nodal plane passing through the boron atom that determines the decoupling between the axial ligand and the SubPc ring.
Other SubPc-based systems featuring a TCBD–Fc unit linked to either the axial or the peripheral position of the macrocycle were prepared by the group of Nielsen, and their steady-state photophysical and electrochemical properties were probed (57–58, Fig. 11 and 204, Fig. 52).56 The new optical and electronic features observed upon incorporation of the TCBD–Fc moieties were ascribed to a ground-state electronic communication between donor and acceptor moieties within the systems. Anyway, a comprehensive spectroscopic study of the electronic interactions in the excited state was not reported.
 Fig. 52  Chemical structure of the peripherally substituted SubPc–TCBD–Fc system 204 investigated by Nielsen and co-workers. 
azaBODIPYs can also act as electron acceptor in SubPc-based D–A systems, as demonstrated by D’Souza and co-workers.99,109 In triad 182 (Fig. 46), featuring two SubPc units covalently linked through the axial position to the α-phenoxy substituents of an aza-BODIPY moiety, the formation of a (SubPc)SubPc˙+–azaBODIPY˙− charge separated state was observed upon population of the azaBODIPY singlet excited state either by direct photoexcitation or by energy transfer from the photoexcited SubPc in toluene (vide supra), as well as from deactivation of the SubPc singlet excited state unit in more polar solvents. A similar dependence of the excited SubPc behaviour on solvent polarity was observed in the SubPc–azaBODIPY–C60 triad 183 (Fig. 46) reported from the same group, in which the shift from apolar solvents (i.e. toluene) to polar solvents (i.e. benzonitrile or THF) makes electron transfer to dominates over energy transfer, affording the SubPc˙+–ADP–C60˙− and SubPc˙+–ADP˙−–C60 radical ion pairs by respectively through-space and trough-bond electron transfer (vide supra).109
Electron transfer to naphthalene- or perylene-diimides has also been reported.316,317
Evidence of electron transfer was also reported for a fused SubPc–porphyrin hybrid fused through a quinoxaline bridge (101, Fig. 33).188 Theoretical calculations of the HOMO and LUMO distribution in the fused system suggested an intramolecular charge transfer from both the SubPc and the porphyrin moiety to the quinoxaline unit. The electronic interactions within the conjugate were confirmed by steady-state and transient spectroscopic measurements. As a matter of fact, a solvatochromic behaviour of the emission band was detected along with and a two-component decay profile, which was rationalized in terms of charge separation and charge recombination processes.
Finally, electron transfer in the solid state was studied by Lopez and co-workers in co-crystals of C60 and 3D covalent organic frameworks (COF) based of SubPcs, in which a driving force of 2.7 eV for CT from the SubPc-based electron donating scaffold to the fullerene at the COF–C60 interface was calculated.241
2.6.2.2 Subphthalocyanines as electron acceptors. 
A number of electron rich counterparts have been used for the construction of D–A systems in which SubPc exhibits electron acceptor features. Among them, ferrocene represents one of the most exploited building blocks, after the seminal work200 reported by the groups of Torres, Echegoyen and Guldi in 2006.318
In this contest, several SubPc–Fc dyads (22, Scheme 6 and 205–212, Fig. 53) have been reported in which the electronic communication between the interacting moieties was tailored through proper design of the linker connecting the central boron atom and the metallocene carbon.80,88,181,199,200,316 A Fc–SubPc–C60 triad has also been presented.319 The presence of an ethynyl spacer between the SubPc and the Fc units in 205 slows down both CT and CR processes in virtue of the increased distance.88 The variation of the kinetic parameters in comparison with similar structures reported earlier bearing a methoxy or an ester spacer (206, 209) was rationalized by the authors on the basis of the different conformational flexibility of the linker.181 When a phenoxy bridge is employed (211), the substitution pattern (i.e. meta or para) of the phenyl ring determines the distance between the electroactive moieties and, in turns, regulate the rate of CS.199 Interestingly, when ferrocene units are covalently linked to the peripheral positions of the macrocycle (213–214, Fig. 54), no evidence of electron transfer can be detected.111 In these systems, the short distance imposed by the peripheral substitution pattern, along with the strong perturbation of the SubPc conjugated electronic systems, turn heavy atom-induced ISC the dominating quenching mechanism over CS, as confirmed by the deactivation kinetics of the Sub singlet excited state and the lack of any charged specie feature in the transient spectra of the investigated species. Electronic communication in the ground state between chromophores was revealed by spectroscopic, electrochemical and theoretical studies also in a series of hydrogenated and fluorinated SubPcs in which the Fc was linked to the axial position through a propanoic or propenoic spacer.80 Nevertheless, no steady state fluorescence or pump–probe experiments were carried out to investigate the excited-state dynamics. The capability of a series of SubPc–Fc dyads to undergo electron transfer as a function of the peripheral substitution pattern of the macrocycle and the nature of the linker was investigated by Mikkelsen and co-workers by means of time-dependent DFT studies, showing a strong dependence of the energy of the Fc-centered HOMO on the molecular structure of the system.320
 Fig. 53  Chemical structure of the axially linked SubPc–Fc dyads 205–212 investigated by Conradie, Blank, Nemykin, El-Khouly, Fukuzumi, Torres, Echegoyen, Kay and co-workers. 
 Fig. 54  Chemical structure of the peripherally linked SubPc–Fc systems 213–214 investigated by Rodríguez-Morgade, Guldi, Torres and co-workers. 
Porphyrinoids302 have also been employed as electron-donor moieties in both covalent14,76,195,308,321 and supramoleular81,322 SubPc-based systems. In many of these systems, the electron-accepting abilities of the SubPc unit have been enhanced by functionalization of the macrocycle with peripheral electron-withdrawing substituents.14,76,195,308,321 In some of these models, the SubPc acts as final electron acceptor of a cascade of excitation energy transduction and/or electron transfer events, which leads to charge-separated states lifetimes on the orders of nanoseconds or tens of nanoseconds. By virtue of its energetically low-lying LUMO, a perfluorinated SubPc was employed as final acceptor moiety in a panchromatic tetrad (215, Fig. 55) in which a cascade of excitation energy transduction, CS and charge shift events was shown to yield a long-living R2N˙+–Por–Pc–SubPc˙− charge-separated state with a lifetime of 10 ns and 11.2 ns in anisole and benzonitrile, respectively.76 Very recently, the supramolecular interaction between the axial pyridine ligand of a perfluorinated SubPc and the central Zn atom of a Pc moiety was exploited in a couple of porphyrinoids-based triads (216–217, Fig. 56) in which the SubPc unit acts as ultimate electron acceptor of a cascade of electron transfer events triggered upon photoexcitation of the corrole unit.81 In this system, the charge shift from the Pc to the SubPc affords a Corr˙+–Pc–SubPc˙− charge separated state which lifetime is two orders of magnitude higher than the initial Corr˙+–Pc˙−–SubPc˙− radical ion pair. A μ-oxo heterooligomer featuring two SubPc units axially linked to the central phosphorus atom of a tetrabenzotriazacorrole was also reported, but the photophysical features of the triad were not probed in the excited state.69 Finally, the electron D–A characteristics of an amino-Por-SubPc conjugate have been theoretically studied by means of DFT calculations both in the ground and in the excited state.323
 Fig. 55  Chemical structure of the Ar3N–Por–Pc–SubPc tetrad 215 investigated by Torres, Guldi, de la Torre and co-workers. 
 Fig. 56  Chemical structure of the supramolecular corrole–Pc–SubPc systems 216–217 investigated by Torres, Guldi, Paolesse and co-workers. 
Turning to TCBD–aniline SubPc-based systems, electronic interactions both in the ground- and in the excited state were found to be determined by the substitution pattern of the SubPc macrocycle (namely, the anchoring position of the TCBD units) and, in the case of the axially substituted derivatives, by the nature of the peripheral substituents of the SubPc moiety.43,46 In F12SubPc–TCBD–aniline conjugate 11 (Fig. 6), the intramolecular interaction between the aniline fragment and the electron-poor SubPc macrocycle give rise upon photoexcitation in low polar solvents such as toluene to the generation of an exciplex species, namely [(F12SubPc–TCBD)δ−–anilineδ+]*. Evidence for this polarized exciplex state came, among others, from the presence of a broad low-energy emission band in the fluorescence spectrum, which is quenched and bathochromically shifted upon increasing the solvent polarity, as well as transient absorption measurements. On the other hand, in more polar solvents (i.e. benzonitrile), the deactivation of the SubPc singlet excited state leads to the population of the energetically low lying (F12SubPc–TCBD)˙−–aniline˙+ fully charge separated state. The modification of the electronic characteristics of the SubPc by replacement of the peripheral fluorine atoms with hydrogen atoms results in a dramatic change of the photophysical behaviour of the macrocycle, which acts as electron donor towards the axial TCBD unit (vide supra). Focusing on the ethynyl-aniline precursors 218 and 219 (Fig. 57), electron transfer to afford the SubPc˙−–ethynyl-aniline˙+ radical ion was revealed in both the perfluorinated and the hydrogenated derivative in benzonitrile. In toluene, photoexcitation of the SubPc moiety in 219 triggers the population of the F12SubPcδ−–ethynyl-anilineδ+ polarized state, while ISC to the triplet excited state dominates the deactivation of the SubPc singlet excited state in 218. A solvent-dependent behavior in the excited state was revealed also for derivative 220, featuring three peripheral ethynyl-aniline moieties and a phenoxy axial functionality (Fig. 57).46 In this case, no evidence for electron transfer were found in toluene, chlorobenzene and anisole, in which an ISC mechanism is operative upon excitation of the SubPc unit. On the contrary, when shifting to a more polar solvent such as benzonitrile, CS is observed, which leads to the formation of the SubPc˙−–[(ethynyl-aniline)3]˙+ radical ion pair. Turning to the (TCBD–aniline)-substituted derivatives 12 and 13 (Fig. 6), photoinduced electron transfer from the SubPc to the TCBD–aniline moieties was detected as a result of the strong electron-accepting features of the TCBD unit.
 Fig. 57  Chemical structure of the SubPc–ethynyl-aniline systems 218–220 investigated by Torres, Guldi and co-workers. 
In 2017, an axially linked SubPc–pyrene dyad (221, Fig. 58) featuring a para-phenoxy bridge was presented as novel electron D–A system.170 In this conjugate, the formation of a Py˙+–SubPc˙− charge separated state was inferred, which was deemed to decay to the SubPc singlet excited state. Electron transfer upon population of either the pyrene (in polar and apolar solvents) or the SubPc (in apolar solvents) excited states was deducted from the quenching of the dyes emission features within the dyad, the shortened fluorescence lifetime of the SubPc moiety upon linking the pyrene unit, the estimated free energy values and the detection of the SubPc triplet excited state signatures in nanosecond transient measurements. Anyway, femtosecond pump–probe experiments would be desirable to corroborate the formation of a radical ion pair species.
 Fig. 58  Chemical structure of the SubPc–pyrene dyad 221 investigated by El-Khouly, Nam, Fukuzumi, Durmuş and co-workers. 
After the early report from the groups of Torres and Guldi, in which an electron-rich phenothiazine (PTZ) was connected for the first time to the axial position of a SubPc,317 the electron-donor features of PTZ moieties were further exploited by D’Souza and co-workers in PTZ–SubPc–C60 multicomponent systems (202 and 203, Fig. 51) in which three phenothiazine units were linked to the periphery of the macrocycle.93,104 When connected through a phenoxy spacer, the PTZ serves as secondary electron donor of a sequential electron transfer cascade, enabling charge distancing by means of hole transfer from the SubPc to the electron-rich PTZ and affording, in turn, charge separated state lifetimes in the range of hundreds of nanoseconds.93 On the other hand, linking the PTZ through a direct N–C bond to the meta positions of the SubPc periphery results in the delocalization of the HOMO over the entire SubPc-(PTZ)3 entity, which acts as electron donor preventing SubPc to serve as secondary electron acceptor (vide supra).104
Interestingly, fullerenes – well-known energy donors and electron acceptors in SubPc-based conjugates- can also act as electron donors towards SubPcs upon tuning the electronic characteristics of both the chromophoric units. In this context, the groups of Torres, Guldi and Akasaka reported in 2015 the study of two couples of conjugates (168, 173 and 222, 223, Fig. 42 and 59) featuring a strong electron accepting SubPc linked through the axial position to a La2@C80 or a C60 moiety.165,166 For the endohedral metallofullerene derivatives, the energetically low-lying (La2@C80)˙+–SubPc˙− charge separate state (1.32 eV and 1.36 eV respectively for 222 and 223) can be easily accessed from the SubPc singlet excited state. In the case of the empty fullerene conjugates, population of the SubPc second singlet excited state (3.4 eV and 3.3 eV for 168 and 173, respectively) is required to generate the higher lying (2.17 eV and 1.95 eV for 168 and 173, respectively) C60˙+–SubPc˙− radical ion pair. On the other hand, upon population of the SubPc first singlet excited state in the SubPc–C60 derivatives, energy transfer to the fullerene is operative (vide supra).
 Fig. 59  Chemical structure of the axially linked SubPc–C60 dyads 222–223 investigated by Feng, Torres, Guldi Akasaka and co-workers. 
The photophysical properties of SubPcs linked to oligothiophenes,324 polymethine-cyanine325 and extended tetrathiafulvalene units326 have also been investigated, revealing electron transfer events to the SubPc chromophore.

2.7 Applications of subphthalocyanines
Throughout this review, it has been shown how the extraordinary features of SubPcs can be drastically modulated via either axial or peripheral functionalization. This in-depth understanding of the structure-property relationship of SubPc derivatives, together with the control of their organization at the nanometer scale, have enabled their use in a plethora of state-of-the-art fields that range from electronic devices to biomedicine. Thus, the aim of this section is to show the reader the different applications that SubPcs have found and are currently finding in the context of material science. In particular, this section has been divided in three main parts: (1) applications in photovoltaic devices, (2) applications in biochemistry and biology and (3) additional applications.

2.7.1 Applications in photovoltaic devices. 
Over the past few years, organic solar cells (OSCs) have emerged as a prominent alternative to the conventional silicon-based photovoltaic devices. In this connection, SubPcs offer many molecular properties that are especially useful in this field and, importantly, can be applied as both donor and acceptor materials since they present substituent-dependent redox profiles. As a result, SubPcs have been extensively studied in organic photovoltaics (OPV) and currently hold a privileged position among the most employed organic semiconductors.
In this section we will comprehensively describe the most recent and relevant achievement in the preparation of SubPc-containing solar cells. Moreover, the key aspects that govern their efficiency will be discussed. This section is in turn divided according to the architecture configuration (Fig. 60). First, planar-heterojunction (PHJ) and bulk-heterojunction (BHJ) devices will be commented. After these two subsections, which cover the most explored configurations, we will overview their incorporation in tandem architectures (TSCs). Then, some examples of SubPcs as sensitizers in dye-sensitized solar cells (DSSCs) and their employment as hole- or electron-transport material (HTM and ETM) in perovskites solar cells (PerSCs) will be commented.
 Fig. 60  Schematic representation of the architectures where SubPcs have been implemented: (a) PHJ, (b) BHJ, (c) TSCs, (d) DSSCs and PerSCs. 
2.7.1.1 Planar-heterojunction (PHJ) solar cells. 
This type of heterojunction represents the simplest device donor–acceptor architecture and has been very useful for establishing the basic principles that govern the performance of SubPcs in solar cells. According to the SubPc role, this subsection has been divided in two parts: (1) SubPcs as donor material, in the context of which the H12SubPc-Cl(20)/C60 couple is the main combination employed, and (2) SubPcs as acceptor material, when fullerene is replaced by a SubPc derivative.
2.7.1.1.1 Subphthalocyanines as donor material. 
In 2006, the groups of Thompson and Forrest showed for the first time the potential of the 20/C60 couple in the field of OSCs.327 They found that this pioneering combination exhibited a more than doubled open-circuit voltage (Voc) compared to the conventional CuPc/C60 device, and afforded a power conversion efficiency (PCE) of 2.1%. This work, together with further reports by the group of Forrest328 and those of Heremans329,330 and Lee,331 set the basis of 20/C60 PHJ-OSCs giving rise to what nowadays is the main activity of many research groups.
As a first approach to optimize these devices, several groups have been directing their investigations to refining the contact interlayers that link the electrodes and the 20/C60 junction. Firstly, the insertion of a hole transport material (HTM) to form an ohmic contact at the anode interface, and to selectively collect holes, has been explored. Among them, transition metal oxides, in particular molybdenum oxide (MoOx), have been widely used due to their deep lying Fermi level and suitable energy alignment with a variety of organic semiconductors.332–337 In addition, it has been found that MoOx also increases the device stability by suppressing undesirable interfacial reactions at SubPc/C60 junction.338 The use of other metal oxides, such as vanadium oxide (V2Ox), have also been reported by Jones and co-workers.339 Remarkably, this oxide outperformed most commonly used ITO, MoOx and PEDOT:PSS materials furnishing a PCE of 2.8%. Further improvements in this line were accomplished by Qiu et al. by employing a solution-processed ammonium heptamolybdate (AHM) layer, which also led to better results than MoOx and PEDOT:PSS affording a PCE of 3.0%.340
Along the same lines, some efforts have been directed to improve the MoOx/SubPc junction by inserting additional functional layers. In 2015 the group of Bernède reported a significant improvement by adding a CuI interlayer as morphology control material.341 AFM experiments revealed a more homogeneous surface morphology of the SubPc films after the insertion of CuI. This order gave rise to an improved hole mobility and PCEs as high as 4.66%. Other materials, such as dinaphthothienothiophene or perylene-tetracarboxylic-dianhydride, have also proven to improve the short circuit current density (Jsc).342 Another interesting example was recently reported by Ye and co-workers by introducing an hexapropyltruxene layer that reduced the exciton quenching at the MoOx/SubPc contact.343
Non-inorganic HTMs have also been documented. In 2018, Golder et al. synthetized three novel derivatives, (1,4-bis(β-cyano-p-bromostyryl)benzene, N-methyl-3-{4-[naphthalen-1-yl(phenyl)amino]phenyl}-4-phenylmaleimide and 2,6-bis(diphenylamino)anthraquinone) and tested their application as anodic buffers. Upon insertion of a 2 nm layer of such molecules, the device power conversion efficiency was improved from 3.98% to 4.92% as a result of the enhanced Jsc, which reached remarkable values as high as 7.11–7.65 mA.344 Interestingly, in this work the authors also demonstrated that a high wide band-gap material is not a requisite for the efficient exciton blocking.
Going one step further, several other investigations have been directed to the insertion of an exciton blocking material (EBM) between the cathode and the acceptor layer to avoid charge recombination losses and the penetration of metal into the active layer. Among them, Bathocuproine (BCP) represent the most studied and efficient EBM in 20/C60 devices.345,346 In the case of SubPcs, rather than employing pristine BCP, the dilution of BCP with C60 has been found to further improve the device performance and lifetime.347 Several other exciton blockings, such as N,N-dicarbazolyl-3,5-benzene,348 buffer layer of bis-(naphthylphenylaminophenyl)fumaronitrile349 or bis-(di(2-fluorophenyl)aminospiro)-fumaronitrile,350 have also proven to be effective by showing remarkable PCEs as high as 5.08, 4.77 or 5.13%, respectively. To a lesser extent, the substitution of the EBM by electron transport materials (ETM) has also been investigated. For instance, it has been shown that a 4-(5-hexylthiophene-2-yl)-2,6-bis(5-trifluoromethyl)thiophen-2-yl)pyridine layer increases the built-in potential and hence the PCE.351 Other cathodic buffers such as tris-(8-hydroxyquinolynato)aluminum (Alq3), indium chloride phthalocyanine,352 or hybrid Ca/Alq3 mixtures have been tested as well.353
More recently, the insertion of a blue-luminescent sensitizer layer has been briefly explored by Zheng and co-workers in order to harvest the short-wavelength photons. Specifically, they found that carbazole derivatives have the suitable UV-vis profile for this purpose and led to a significant PCE enhancements when inserted between MoOx and 20.354,355
Several other studies have been focused on improving the morphology of the anode and cathode, which typically are ITO, and Ag or Al, respectively.356 For instance, Wei and co-workers developed a new transparent anode, composed of nanoporous silver and MoOx, whose efficiency was comparable with that of ITO-based devices (PCE of 3.45%).357 Shortly after, an improved charge collection in 20/C60 devices was achieved by using nanostructured electrodes, instead of the usual planar morphology, which resulted in a gain in the field factor (FF) and PCE.358 Another interesting example was reported by Lo et al. demonstrating that a UV ozone treated ITO/ZnO anode can improve the performance and stability of 20/C60 OSCs.359 The authors suggested that this enhancement is due to a higher work function of such anode and a better SubPc absorption triggered by the light scattering of ITO/ZnO surface. Additional strategies, such as the use of patterned and multilayer electrodes,360,361 metallic nanocavities,362 or ITO treated with O2 plasma363 have also yielded promising results. In addition, other studies have been recently devoted to tuning the work function of ITO by physical or chemical functionalization with Langmuir-Blodgett monolayers or benzenesulfonyl chlorides, respectively.364,365
Turning to another approach, the use of three organic materials in a cascade configuration (ternary solar cells) broadens the absorption range of visible light, resulting in an increase in Jsc and hence in a higher PCE. In this configuration, the SubPc can act as ambipolar interlayer between the donor and the acceptor facilitating the exciton dissociation.366,367 Within this concept, rubrene and tetracene in combination with 20 and C60 were preliminary evaluated by Li et al. Unfortunately, in spite of improved Jsc and Voc, a PCE not higher than 1.9% was found.368 Later, the group of Arango reported an insightful study on a ClAlPc/20/C60 cascade architecture. In this work, the authors demonstrated that Voc does not correlate with the outermost HOMO–LUMO offset and, instead, it is conditioned by the lowest HOMOD–LUMOA energy difference (ΔEDA).369 More recently, the group of Bernède explored the optimal thickness of the SubPc-based ambipolar interlayer in a (E)-bis-1,2-(5,5-dimethyl-(2,2:3,2-terthiophene)vinylene (BSTV)/C60 planar-heterojunction. Thus, for a thickness of 12.5 nm, the authors reached a gratifying PCE of 5.18%. Supported by optical measurements and considering the improved Jsc, they attributed this excellent result to the efficient FRET from BSTV to SubPc.370 Shortly after, a pentacene/20/C60 combination was reported. In this case, the Jsc was found to increase as a result of the FRET from pentacene to SubPc. However, this Jsc values did not compensate the Voc and FF losses and this combination showed a PCE of 2.90%.371
The typically short exciton diffusion length (LD) in PHJ OSCs largely limits the device efficiency. In this sense, Holmes et al. claimed a 40% LD increase by diluting the SubPc donor layer into 1,4-Bis(triphenylsilyl)benzene as a wide-energy-gap host material. Remarkably, the authors achieved a PCE of 4.4%.243 On the basis of this work, the same authors reported in 2015 a PCE of 4.3% using an interesting 20:224 (Fig. 62)/C60 device configuration. In this case, the donor layer consists of a SubPc/SubNc mixture, where 20 is the host material. In contrast to UGH2, in this case the diluting agent (20) also contributes to exciton generation by exciting the donor (224) via FRET.372
Until now we have exclusively outlined the extensive research on the 20/C60 couple. Nevertheless, the donor capability of other SubPcs with different axial or peripheral substitution have been documented. For instance, in 2014 Bender and co-workers demonstrated the donor capability of 78 (Fig. 15) with C60 in a planar heterojunction. The authors reported a modest PCE of 1.49% and a large Voc of ∼1 V, which was remarkably higher than those obtained with other donor materials, such as CuPc (0.44 V) or pentacene (0.30 V).373 Shortly after, Shtein and co-workers studied the impact of axial fluorination. They found that H12SubPc-Cl 20 led to a better PCE (2.85%) in comparison with the fluorinated one (H12SubPc-F; 2.44%). Supported by DFT calculations, the authors attributed this result to the lower HOMO–LUMO band gap of 20 and the different impact of the dipole moment in the polaron pair dissociation.374 In 2015, Bender et al. studied the performance of SubPcs bearing para-substituted aryls (by hydrogen; H12SubPc–Ph, methoxy; H12SubPc–PhOMe or fluorine; H12SubPc–PhF) in a ITO/PEDOT:PSS/aryl-SubPc/C60/BCP/Ag device configuration. Although small variations in Voc and Jsc between the different derivatives were observed, H12SubPc–Ph led to a higher PCE (3.18% vs. 2.70% and 2.66% of H12SubPc–PhOMe and H12SubPc–PhF, respectively) as a result of the higher field factor. To explain these results, it was inferred that H12SubPc–Ph offers a longer exciton diffusion length.141 Later, the same authors characterized the μ-oxo-SubPc 102 in different PHJ photovoltaics devices taking 20 as ref. 274. First, they studied the 102/C60 couple in a simple double-layer device. In spite of presenting a lower HOMO energy than 20, 102 led to lower Voc (0.90 vs. 1.06), and hence lower PCE (2.1% vs. 2.4%). The authors partly attributed this drop to the different surface dipole formed between the SubPc and C60. Further improvement in PCE (2.7%) was achieved by substitution of C60 by C70 in the acceptor layer. This finding was explained considering the increase of Jsc, which arises from the strong absorption of C70 between 450 and 550 nm, and between 600 and 700 nm. Then, a 20/102/C70 cascade device structure was evaluated. Interestingly, all cascade devices showed higher FF and PCE than 20/C70 or 102/C70 devices. Regarding SubPcs with different peripheral substitution, in 2018, the potential of 224 as alternative donor material was explored.375 As will be commented in Section 4.1, 224 is synthetized in combination with alloyed mixtures of Cl-SubNcs which have at least one chlorine atom in the bay position. Thus, Bender et al. assessed the impact of the different degree of chlorination on the performance of SubNc in a ITO/PEDOT:PSS/H18−nClnSubNc-Cl/C70/BCP/Ag configuration. They concluded that the chlorination has a positive effect on the PCE by enhancing both the Voc and FF. Very recently, the same group reported a very insightful work exploring the impact of the SubPc regiochemistry on the photovoltaic performance. Importantly, they observed that β-substitution led to higher Jsc values and thus higher efficiencies than α-substitution.239
On the other hand, as will be discussed in Section 2.7.1.1.2, fullerene derivatives present major issues that avoid their practical use in large-scale OSCs. Consequently, several authors have also explored the combination of 20 with non-fullerene acceptors. For instance, in 2013 Nielsen et al. prepared a device with truxenone derivatives as acceptor material which, despite yielding lower Voc values, outperformed the PC61BM-based ref. 376. After that, Sullivan et al. reported that 20 paired with N-ethyl barbituric acid bithiophene can afford remarkably high Voc values (1.38 V). More interestingly, they found that the Jsc and FF showed a significant improvement in an inverted configuration (ITO/Zr(acac)/EBB/SubPc/NPD:MoOx/Al). In this case, the PCE (2.39%) increased up to values comparable to the corresponding C60 device (3%).377 In parallel, the group of Li and Su studied the effect of inserting a 5 nm interlayer of 20 between MoOx and a C60:TAPC bulk-heterojunction. Interestingly, as a result of the build-in field induced by the MoOx/20 Schottky junction, the PCE was increased by 35.4% compared with the reference cell without SubPc (from PCE of 2.77% to 3.75%).378
Conventional heterojunction OSCs typically involve the deposition of the electron donor layer on top of a transparent anode, with the cathode deposited last. However, inverting the configuration (i.e., acceptor deposited on the transparent electrode) offers several advantages.379 In this configuration, the C60/20 couple has been tested as well and modest PCEs around 2.4% were achieved in the first reports.380,381 More recently, Morris et al. documented an interesting example achieving a higher conversion efficiency with an inverted device (3.31%) than with the common configuration (2.50%). The authors attributed this unusual result to the formation of a C60/ITO Schottky junction and the efficient exciton-blocking at the SubPc/MoOx interface.382
A crucial factor in the commercial viability of OSCs is the outdoor lifetime. In this connection, Bender et al. studied the performance of several SubPcs paired with C60 in a rooftop ambient environment, and found that axial substitution strongly affects the longevity of the devices.383 Although a partial hydrolysis was observed over time, SubPcs were found to be robust materials. Indeed, the degradation mainly arises from the C60 decomposition, which tends to undergo photo-oxidation and photo-oligomerization reactions.384–387 Further studies of the degradation mechanism have been carried out by transient photocurrent techniques (TPC).388
To close this subsection, it should be mentioned that the 20/C60 couple have been also considered as reference heterojunction to develop different device characterization methods and computational models, including the quantification of energy levels and recombination rates by TPC,389 the determination of exciton diffusion length,390,391 the analysis of charge-transfer (CT) kinetics,392 and the characterization of CT complexes at the donor–acceptor interface.169,393–405
2.7.1.1.2 Subphthalocyanines as acceptor material. 
Since the first steps of this fast-growing field, fullerenes have been the dominant acceptor on account of their excellent electronic properties.406 However, these derivatives present intrinsic shortcomings that hinder the production of cost-effective and high-performance devices (e.g., high-cost synthesis, low absorption coefficients in the visible range, limited variability in the energy levels, or morphological instability). As a result, “fullerene-free solar cells” has become a hot topic within the field of OSCs.
In Section 2.3.8, we have seen how SubPcs can exhibit high electronic affinities if peripheral positions are functionalized with electron-withdrawing substituents. On this basis, the group of Torres and Jones took the first steps in the use of SubPcs as acceptor in OSCs by achieving modest but promising PCE of 0.96% and 2.7% using halogenated derivatives (4 and 2, respectively) in combination with several donors.160,407 Another early research on this direction was reported by Verret et al., testing a SubPc dimer 90a as acceptor component, paired with 20 as donor. The red-shifted absorption and improved acceptor properties of 90a resulted in an enhanced photocurrent and high Voc values that gave rise to an outstanding PCE of 4%.408
However, it was not until three years later when the real potential of SubPcs as acceptor materials was demonstrated. In 2014, Cheyns, Torres and co-workers achieved a remarkable PCE of 6.4% using 2 as acceptor material and 224 as donor material.409 In this work, the authors showed that the complementary absorption profiles of this couple, together with the ideal energy alignment of LUMO, increase the photocurrent density without loses in the open-circuit voltage. Moreover, it was found that the integration of diindeno[1,2,3-cd:1′,2′,3′-lm]perylene (DIP) as EBM further improved the photocurrent density. Supported by voltage dependent photoluminescence (PL) and light intensity dependent current density–voltage (J–V) measurements, the authors found that the exciton dissociation at the SubNc/SubPc junction was very effective. As a result, the optimized device displayed excellent Voc, Jsc and FF values giving rise to this outstanding efficiency. One year later, the same group could improve this PCE value to 6.9% for a device using a similar configuration. In this work, the authors systematically tested four different SubPcs as acceptors (2, 4, 225, 226; Fig. 61), in combination with four small-band-gap donor materials. Again, Cl6SubPc-Cl 2 showed the highest efficiency, presenting the optimal energy levels to meet the perfect trade-off between Voc and Jsc.49 It is important to mention that, in addition to 226, several others cyanated SubPcs with potential application as n-type semiconductors in FF-solar cells have been reported.54
 Fig. 61  Chemical structure of the SubPcs employed as acceptor by Cheyns, Torres and co-workers in ref. 49. 
 Fig. 62  Chemical structure of SubPc 20 and 224, (a) configuration and (b) exciton dissociation mechanism of the OSC investigated by Cnops and co-workers. Adapted with permission from ref. 410. 
Another tremendous step further came when Cnops et al. reported a record-breaking PCE of 8.4% using a ternary mixture of α-sexithiophene (α-6T)/224/20.410 In this milestone work, the SubPc plays a different role. Instead of acceptor material, the SubPc acts as a light-harvesting layer, transferring the excitons to the acceptor one (SubNc) via FRET (Fig. 61) Thus, the exciton dissociation takes place at the α-6T/224 junction following a two-step mechanism. As a result, the three complementary absorbing materials resulted in a quantum efficiency above 75% between 400 and 720 nm, giving rise to excellent Jsc and hence improved PCEs. This study paved the way to the use long-range FRET in SubPc/SubNc systems as a powerful strategy to prepare highly efficient fullerene-free solar cells. A deeper insight in such energy transfer resonance was later provided by Rumbles and Reid using computational calculations.411 It is noteworthy that, years later, the group of Lee demonstrated that, in addition to this energy transfer mechanism, another mechanism contributes to this high PCE. In particular, the authors found that SubPc and SubNc single layers can generate free carriers much more efficiently than typical organic semiconductors upon photoexcitation and behave as efficient ambipolar semiconductors.412,413
After this 8.4% PCE, several authors have been directing their investigations to the characterization of this efficient configuration. For instance, the energy levels of this configuration were carefully characterized by means of UPS, IPES, and XP.414 The authors claimed that the high Voc arises from the formation of different dipoles that shift the energy levels of each component. In an attempt to further improve the efficiency of this combination, Huang and co-workers reported in 2016 the use of MoOx instead of PEDOT:PSS as HTM in this α-6T/244/20 heterojunction.415 However, MoOx was proven less effective and the efficiency was much lower compared to that of Cnops et al. (4.35% vs. 8.4%). Later, Vandewal and co-workers introduced an additional interlayer between α-6T and 224 with the aim of increasing the Voc.416 Interestingly, the insertion of Rubrene, DIP or 72 led to a Voc enhancement, reaching maximum values up to 1.16 V (vs. 0.98 V of the reference device). Based on highly sensitive EQE (sEQE) measurements, the authors attributed this result to the suppression of nonradiative recombination as a consequence of the reduced contact between α-6T and 224. Nevertheless, the integration of such layers difficulted the efficient exciton dissociation giving rise to lower Jsc and PCEs. On the other hand, the stability of this ternary combination has also been evaluated. In spite of its promising efficiency, this cascade devices typically exhibit very short lifetime as a result of the rapid degradation of α-6T and necessitate more research focused on the stability issue.417
Further studies using SubPcs as acceptor have been made by Bender and co-workers. First, they evaluated the influence of the chlorination degree of 224 on the efficiency of α-6T/224 planar heterojunction devices. In accordance with the trend observed when SubPNc acts as donor (see Section 2.7.1.1.1), the more degree of chlorination, the more PCE is achieved (4.32%).418 Turning back to chlorinated SubPcs, this group have also explored the performances of 2, paired this time with tetracene and pentacene as donor materials. Unfortunately, PCEs about 1% were obtained with this combination.182 In another work, the influence of the donor deposition method (in solution or under vacuum) in the device efficiency was evaluated using 20 and 1. In this case, a PCE of 3.86% was obtained for vacuum deposited α-6T/20 heterojunction.419 Moreover, the H12SubPc–Ph and 102 derivatives described in the previous section have also been studied as acceptors, leading to PCEs of 2.89% and 0.37%, respectively.141,274
On the other hand, the deep LUMO energy levels of perchlorinated SubPcs could render these derivatives triplet harvesters of singlet fission materials. However, it should be noted that this deep LUMO energy also results in a decrease of Voc. In this regard, though reaching PCEs lower than 1.4%, Bender and co-workers have established some useful guidelines for the design of this type of solar cells.420
Several other studies using 20 as acceptor in combination with different donors, such as acenes or phthalocyanines, have been reported by the group of Li,368 Pakhomov,421 Bernède422 and Bender.423 Moreover, other less conventional SubPcs, such as thiadiazole SubPzs, have also been studied by Stuzhin and co-workers.424 Additionally, the design of other accepting sulfur-containing SubPcs have been theoretically explored.425,426
Turning to interlayer optimization, alternative cathodic buffers different from BCP have also been investigated in this configuration.427 In 2015, Hancox et al. advanced an inexpensive, low concentration, solution processed zirconium acetylacetonate electron extracting layer. Thus, employing the 20/2 couple, PCEs comparable to those of BCP were obtained both in normal and inverted configurations (2.39% and 2.20%, respectively). On other hand, as described in the preceding section, CuI can improve the surface roughness of SubPc layers. Thus, Pakhomov and co-workers studied the performance of CuI/20 heterojunction, delivering a PCE of about 2%. In this case, CuI not only acts as a morphology control layer but also as donor material.249
The outdoor stability of OSCs with SubPcs acting as acceptor material has also been investigated by the group of Bender.428 To this end, the authors firstly evaluated the PCE loss over time of α-6T/SubPc (bearing –Cl, –OPh or –Ph at the axial position) planar heterojunction devices using different ISOS protocols. It was found that 20 shows the higher stability (needing several days to lose 20% of efficiency). The authors suggested that 20 presents a higher stability against hydrolysis, in comparison with SubPcs bearing –OPh and –Ph. Interestingly, the PCE decrease mainly arises from loses in Jsc and FF, while Voc remains largely unaltered. In a second work, the same authors explored the stability of the α-6T/224/20 trilayer OSCs. Once again, the porphyrinic derivatives showed excellent stability for several days and the PCE loss was mainly due to the degradation of the electrode and/or buffer layers (i.e., ITO/PEDOT:PSS).429
2.7.1.2 Bulk-heterojunction (BHJ) solar cells. 
As an alternative to PHJ and with the aim of lowering the probability of exciton deactivation, the use of blended donor–acceptor mixtures (i.e., bulk-heterojunction) has been extensively investigated. In this configuration, SubPcs derivatives have also been widely applied and, as we will see, have led to outstanding efficiencies as both donor and acceptor material.
2.7.1.2.1 Subphthalocyanines as donor material. 
In 2012, the group of Holmes reported for the first time the use of SubPcs in bulk-heterojunction by evaluating the performance of a vacuum-processed device with an active layer comprising a 20:C70 mixture.430 In this pioneering work, the authors found that the device containing 80 wt% of C70 yielded efficiencies of 5.4% and claimed that the improved electronic characteristics of C70 with respect to C60 are crucial to achieve higher photocurrent densities. Chen and co-workers later showed that the post-annealing of this 20:C70 active layer gives rise to a significant increase of PCE.431 Using SCLC measurements, the authors attributed this enhancement to a more efficient charge collection, hence again in Jsc. To shed light on this, they studied the morphologic change as a function of temperature and observed that, upon higher temperatures, a larger grain size is observed due to the phase separation of SubPc and C70. Further work along the same line was conducted by Williams's group employing C60 instead of C70 as acceptor material.432 In this case, a lower PCE (3%) was found. Additionally, the authors explored the influence of the SubPc:C60 ratio on the performance of the device and observed that a lower donor content (which is the main light absorber) led to a reduction in Jsc and FF as a result of hole trapping and recombination effects. Worth of mention is that, in addition to the enhanced PCEs, SubPc/C70 OSCs typically exhibit higher BHJ-device lifetimes compared to those of C60 owing to its reduced tendency to photodimerization and its shorter exciton lifetime.433
More importantly, this architecture has demonstrated an excellent synergy with wet deposition techniques, which is key for cost-effective production. As explained along this review, SubPcs possess excellent solubility and low tendency to aggregate, and can form high quality thins films via solution processing. As a result, solution processed SubPc-based BHJ devices have been and are being extensively investigated. In this case, the limited solubility of C60(70) forces the use of more soluble derivatives, such as PC61BM. One of the first examples of such devices was reported by Yang and co-workers, by preparing a devices comprising a 224:PC70BM blended mixture.434 After evaluating several fabrication details, such as buffer layers, 224:C70 ratio, or the active layer thickness, the authors documented a PCE of 4% under 1 sun AMG 1.5G irradiation. They also explored the temperature-dependence of the carrier mobilities by determining the PCE at 80 °C. As expected, such mobilities increased and the PCE raised up to 5%. The authors also claimed an improved active layer morphology by device post-annealing, resulting in higher Jsc and FF. As described in Section 2.7.1.1.1, the group of Bender also studied the effect of peripheral chlorination on the performance of these SubNc:PC70BM devices.375 Once again, higher chlorination degrees led to better efficiencies. Ince and co-workers also contributed to this area by testing the performance of SubPcs peripherally functionalized with a variety of donor groups.106 However, despite presenting improved donor properties, the paring of these SubPcs with PCBM gave rise to modest PCE less than 1%. Another example by the group of Bender presented an interesting comparative study between SubPc and Pc (both axially functionalized with n-hexylsilyl oxide) as additives in ternary P3HT:PC60BM devices in order to improve the exciton dissociation.150 However, SubPc showed no improvements.
On the other hand, these SubPc:C60 blended junctions has been very useful to explain some crucial processes operating in BHJ OSCs as well as to design new methods for device characterization.
In connection with the former aspect, Holmes et al. reported an insightful work studying the role of MoOx as HTL and its impact on the different exciton dissociation mechanism.435 The authors explained that the excitons formed within the C60 material may undergo efficient bulk ionization generating photocurrent as a result of the large built-in field created by the MoOx/C60 interface. Another very relevant physical process was deeply studied by the group of Schlenker.436 In this work, the authors studied by means several spectroscopic techniques the kinetic competition between charge separation and triplet formation in SubPc:C60 BHJ, which actually represents a crucial issue since determines the photocurrent and hence the efficiency of SubPc–C60-based OSCs.
Regarding the second aspect, Holmes and co-workers demonstrated a new technique for determining the charge collection efficiency that requires no input from optical field simulations.437 In another work, Yun et al. developed a new method to characterize the energy level alignment and molecular components in a SubPc:C60 BHJ solar cell by means UPS, REELS and IPES spectroscopies. This method, in contrast with traditional techniques, allowed the energy-level determination from each combination ratio by separating each electronic structure from the BHJ spectrum.438 A very important concept related to BHJ solar cells was published by Leo and co-workers. In this pioneering work, the authors demonstrated that just varying the ratio of two SubPc donors (2 and 20) within a device composed of a 2:20:C60 BHJ, can efficiently tune the band structure of the semiconductor material and hence the PCE.439
2.7.1.2.2 Subphthalocyanines as acceptor material. 
Prompted by their success as acceptor in PHJ, SubPcs have also been explored to prepare fullerene-free BHJ-OSCs. In this case, particular attention has been paid to Cl6SubPc-Y derivatives on account of their excellent acceptor properties, energy levels,440 and their high solubility. On this line, the groups of Cooke and Samuel reported the first work by using Cl6SubPc-OPh in combination with three common donor polymers (MEH-PPV, P3HT, and PTB7).101 Thus, with PTB7, respectable PCEs about 3.5% were achieved in standard stack solution BHJ architecture. Analyzing the J–V curve and PL experiments, the authors also explained that losses mainly take place by trap-assisted and geminate recombination. Later, the group of Bender gave further insights into the acceptor properties of such Cl6SubPc-OPh by pairing it with a wide range of reported donor polymers, and compared its performance with that of PC61BM.441 In all cases, SubPc provided lower PCEs than PC61BM and the same trend was found in the electron-donating polymer selection criteria. Only very recently, the same group reported a 3.6% PCE employing 2 as single acceptor. In this work, the authors evaluated several deposition methods and found that a solution mixed PHJ–BHJ configuration led to higher efficiencies.442
In 2017, Torres, Janssen and co-workers documented a record efficiency for solution-deposited SubPc-based solar cells.110 In this work, the authors screened four Cl6SubPcs-Y (2 and 227–229; Fig. 63) bearing different substituent at the axial position in a ITO/ZnO/PTB7-Th: Cl6SubPc-Y/MoOx/Ag device architecture. Interestingly, the less soluble SubPc 2 gave rise to the best results (PCE 4%) showing an excellent photocurrent contribution. However, these devices still suffered from low FF, which is a collective result of low electron mobility, serious bimolecular recombination, and suboptimal BHJ morphology. In an attempt to address these issues, the same authors described later the synthesis and applications of novel and highly soluble SubNc derivatives as acceptor in BHJ. Unfortunately, these SubNc, with –Cl, –F or –SO2C5H12 as peripheral substituents, furnished modest efficiencies of 1%, which was attributed to the low Jsc and FF caused by the imbalanced hole/electron transport and suboptimal heterojunction morphology.443 Further work involving SubNcs as acceptor was conducted by Bender et al., by studying the performance of several arylated SubNcs paired with P3HT as donor. As a result of the poor acceptor properties of these unsubstituted (partially chlorinated) SubNc, PCEs about 0.7% were observed.444
 Fig. 63  Chemical structure of the SubPcs employed as acceptor by Martínez-Díaz, Janssen, Torres and co-workers in ref. 49. 
Apart from peripheral substitution by halogen atoms, another simple and facile strategy to prepare efficient SubPc-based acceptors relies on the covalent pre- or post-functionalization of SubPcs with others well-known acceptor derivatives (NFA). As such acceptors involves large π-conjugated systems, this approach may offer several advantages: (1) improved supramolecular organization arising from the additional π–π interactions, which leads to better charge transport properties, (2) higher electron affinities and red-shifted absorption of the SubPc, and (3) wider range cover of solar spectrum, and hence higher photocurrent densities in the device.
Aromatic diimides derivatives, such as naphthalenediimide (NDI) or perylenediimide (PDI), represent one of the most studied systems within the field of organic n-type semiconductors.445,446 These compounds possess many exciting features including high electron affinities, good charge transport properties, solubility, and excellent thermal and oxidative stability, that makes them promising candidates to solution-processed OSCs. Motivated by these advantages, the research activity of several authors has recently been devoted to the peripheral functionalization of SubPcs with this kind of diimide systems in an attempt to improve their performance in BHJ (Fig. 64).
 Fig. 64  Chemical structure of the diimide-SubPc derivatives used as non-fullerene acceptor in BHJ OSCs. 
The first example in this direction was documented by Hang et al. by reporting the synthesis and device performance of two “star-shaped” acceptors consisting of a SubPc-based core, and three PDIs linked by single bonds or acetylene bridges (230 and 231, respectively) to the peripheral positions (Fig. 64).173 As a consequence of the conjugation with PDIs, these SubPcs cover a wider absorption range since they exhibit the absorption of both a PDI (450–570 nm) and a red-shifted SubPc derivative (570–650 nm). Thus, for the inverted device structure ITO/ZnO/PBDB-T:acceptor/MoOx/Al, a remarkable PCE of 4.53% was reached using 230. In contrast, a modest PCE of 1.78% was obtained with 231. The authors attributed these results to the more balanced carrier transport and the better layer morphology of 230 resulting from the twisted molecular structure that prevent aggregation. Further studies relating to these diimide-SubPc systems were recently conducted by the groups of Jiang, He and Wang.447 In their outstanding work, the authors synthetized a PDI-fused SubPc, also called “rylene annulated SubPc” (232), and tested the photovoltaic utility in BHJ holding two different substituent at axial position (Cl- or tBuPhO). Remarkably, the fusion of PDI at the SubPc core caused red-shifts up to 115 nm in the Q-band, resulting in small band-gaps about 1.70 eV. This, together with the strong absorption of PDI moieties from 370–520 nm, result in a significant photocurrent contribution of the acceptor showing a broad EQE from 300 nm to 800 nm. Moreover, as inferred from electrochemical measurement, the acceptor properties of SubPc were also found to improve (LUMO energy of −3.78 eV). Thus, in combination with PBDBT-2F as donor, SubPc 232 with axial chlorine afforded the impressive record efficiency of 7.53%. Another efficient approach on this line was introduced by Cheng and co-workers. In this case, the authors simply functionalized the peripheral position of SubPc and SubNc with three imide groups (Fig. 64), giving rise to 233 and 234. It is important to highlight that the physical properties of these derivatives, and hence their device performance, can be easily tuned by means B-functionalization (SubPc) or N-functionalization (diimide). In the first work, the authors explored the performance of 233 within (ITO)/ZnO/donor:acceptor/MoOx/Ag configuration.448 After optimizing several aspects, such as donor material, additives, or B- and N-substituents, among others, the authors reported PCEs as high as 4.92%. These devices exhibited a broad photoresponse from 300 nm to 700 nm, which stems from the contribution of both donor and acceptor. Moreover, AFM studies revealed a good compatibility with the donor. However, the authors claimed that these triimide-SubPcs display poor stabilities. In an attempt to overcome that issue, shortly after the same authors explored the SubNc homologue. In this case, the improved stability and NIR-absorption of 234 led to an admirable PCE of 6.25% using a similar device architecture.449
In addition, SubPcs functionalized with non-imide-based acceptors have been briefly investigated. For instance, PCEs up to 4.6% and 3.17% have been reported employing SubPcs peripherally functionalized with -(3-oxo-2,3-dihydroinden-1-3-ylidene) maleonitriles116 and diketopyrrolopyrrole groups, respectively.450
2.7.1.3 Tandem solar cells. 
Tandem configuration (TSCs; Fig. 60c), in which two or more single cells (sub-cells) are stacked in series, has been accepted as a general strategy to increase Voc of OSCs, as well as to harvest a broader range of solar irradiation. Motivated by their success in PHJ and BHJ, several authors have also explored the application of SubPcs in this type of solar cells.
One of the first example of SubPc-based tandem architecture was reported by Cheyns et al. in 2010.451 These authors achieved an encouraging PCE of 5.5% by stacking two sub-cells with a high Voc, composed of 20/C60 and 224/C60 planar heterojunctions. As seen in previous sections, SubPc and SubNc, besides giving rise to high Voc values in combination with C60, present a perfect optical synergy that leads to enhanced Jsc. Thus, using this tandem setup, Voc, Jsc and FF values as high as 1.92 V, 4.3 mA cm−2, and 62%, were reached. Remarkably, the measured efficiency corresponds to a 40% increase compared to the efficiency of the optimal single cells.
A poor current matching between subcells drastically decrease the performance of the tandem device. This is due to the fact that less light is absorbed by the back sub-cell compared to the front one, creating an imbalance in the photocurrent generated. With the aim of improving the absorption of the back sub-cell, Feng and co-workers reported a very interesting work using the so-called surface plasmon polariton (SPP) induced field enhancement, which has proven successful to improve the absorption in OSCs without increasing the thickness of the active layers.452 Thus, for two identical SubPc/C60 subcells, the authors claimed a 10.4% enhancement in the photocurrent by using periodical corrugation in the metal cathode. The resulting device showed an excellent PCE of 6.1%. Related to this, the group of Kim demonstrated using numerical simulations that the use of a microcavity structure with a capping layer can also enhance the absorption, and hence the Jsc.453
Later, the groups of Jones and Sullivan claimed a better current balancing by incorporating a N,N′-bis(1-naphthyl)-N,N′-diphenyl-1,1′-biphenyl-4,4′-diamine:MoOx mixture as hole transporting optical spacer layer between 20/C60 sub-cells. Interestingly, the small amount of MoOx shift the Fermi level of the film ensuring a good energy alignment. The excellent conductivity and transparence of such interlayer led to a significant photocurrent increase, raising the efficiency by ∼80% (PCE of 4.2%). EQE measurements showed that the increase in absorption in the SubPc region was the cause for the improved photocurrent balancing.454
Several other studies have been directed to find new donor couples. Along this line, in 2014 Xue and co-workers documented a TSC with a remarkable spectral response spanning from 300 nm to 1000 nm. In this case, PbPc and 20 were the donors of the front and back sub-cells, respectively. In both sub-cells C60 is the acceptor component. After a detailed study of the individual sub-cells, the authors found that the C60 layer thickness has a critical impact on the photocurrent generation of the tandem device. With this issue optimized, the authors achieved a respectable PCE of 4.5%.455
Other important work in this area includes that of Pakhomov and Travkin, who presented the performance of all-phthalocyaninic-dye-based TSCs. By these simple architectures and optimizing each single junction sub-cell separately, the authors explored the impact of different factors (i.e., interconnecting layer composition, buffers, or layer thicknesses) on the Voc of both each sub-cell and the whole tandem device.456–458
Zhang and co-workers also studied the composition of the interconnecting layer, also referred as charge recombination zone, by connecting in a tandem mode 20/C60 and ClAlPc/C60 planar heterojunctions.459 Specifically, the authors compared the efficiency of BCP and 3,4,9,10 perylenetetracarboxylic bisbenzimidazole as electron transporting layer, and evaluated the role of Ag layer thickness on the device performance. Concerning the former, they found that, despite BCP exhibited higher Voc, PTCBI showed a greater energy level alignment and much smoother surface, giving rise to an efficient ohmic contact with Ag and hence higher FF and PCE (51% and 3.49%, respectively). On the other hand, it was found that a very thin Ag layer further improves the interface morphology, presents an excellent transparence, and enhances the absorption of C60 by SPP effects.
Alternatively, bulk-heterojunction has also been considered for the preparation of SubPc-based tandem architectures.
