Indeed, in 2014, Jin and co-workers stablished the record efficiency of this section by reporting an impressive 7.66%, obtained by simply stacking two identical 20:C70 sub-cells linked by a highly transparent MoOx/F16CuPc/Ag/BPhen layer (Fig. 65).460 This success mainly arises from this particular ICL, where the F16CuPc plays a crucial role promoting the extraction of holes and carrier recombination, and extending the recombination zone. As a consequence, the resulting device present and excellent current matching and Voc values as high as 2 V, which almost correspond to the sum of the Voc of each sub-cell, pointing out once again to an excellent current balance. Remarkably, the tandem device largely outperformed the individual cells, which reached PCE values of 5.5%.
 Fig. 65  (a) Device configuration and (b) schematic energy level diagram of the ICL of the TSC investigated by Jin and co-workers. Adapted with permission from ref. 460. Copyright 2014 Elsevier B.V. 
Apart from being used as donor, SubPc 20 has also proven to be very efficient as additional interlayer between the hole transporting material and the active bulk-heterojunction.461 In this sense, Gao et al. documented PCEs as high as 7.27% with the following configuration: ITO/MoO3/SubPc/TAPC:C70/Bphen/Ag/MoO3/SubPc/TAPC:C70/Bphen/Al. The authors attributed this efficiency to the high absorption of C70 and the band bending caused by the inclusion of SubPc that significantly improved the holes extraction. Additionally, in this work the authors proposed an insightfully carrier transport mechanism considering UPS measurements.
2.7.1.4 Dye-sensitized solar cells (DSSC). 
Since the seminal work published by O’Regan and Gräztel in 1991,462 DSSCs have been extensively investigated as a promising cost-effective alternative to the common p–n junction devices. In this configuration, particular attention has been paid to the optimization of the sensitizer. In this sense, although most efforts have been directed to the use planar porphyrinoids sensitizers (e.g., Pors or Pcs), SubPcs have also shown promising results. In 2014, the groups of Torres and Nazeeruddin synthetized five SubPcs bearing different carboxylic acids both at the axial and the peripheral positions, and tested for the first time their performances in DSSCs.47 The authors screened several electrolytes and found that those containing Li+ significantly improved the Jsc. Thus, with the optimized electrolyte in hand (mixture of 0.5 M NaI and 0.5 M LiI), a maximum PCE of 1.32% was obtained using the peripherally functionalized SubPc. In contrast, when the axially substituted SubPcs were tested, PCEs drastically decreased as a result of the poor electronic communication between SubPc orbitals and TiO2 (PCEs of 0.05–0.38%). Importantly, the addition of coadsorbant did not improve the performance owing to the low aggregation tendency of SubPcs. In spite of the low PCEs obtained and the low Jsc values, this work paved the way for further research on SubPc-based.
In an attempt to improve Jsc, the same authors described two years later another set of asymmetric SubPcs holding a carboxylic acid at the periphery.50 Such compounds were tested in DSSCs on [9+4] mm thick TiO2 film (9 mm thick active layer and an additional 4 mm thick scattering layer) and also afforded low PCEs ranging from 0.23% to 0.91%. Supported by electrochemical measurements, the authors concluded that the poor efficiencies of SubPc-based DSSCs mainly arise from the small driving forces for electron injection as a consequence the low-lying LUMO levels of these sensitizers, which significantly limits the Jsc.
Later, Gotfredsen et al. similarly evaluated the efficiency of both liquid- and solid-state DSSCs using a new push–pull SubPc sensitizer consisting of two aniline donor moieties and a benzothiadiazole acceptor moiety. In the case of liquid-state devices, it was found that dyes tend to desorb to the mesoporous TiO2. As consequence, the resulting devices led to a low efficiency of 0.46%. In contrast, the solid-state device incorporating this push–pull SubPc reached high Jsc values of nearly 4 mA cm−2 and a PCE of above 1.5%, which represent the highest value for SubPc-based DSSCs.55
Instead of using a direct linking of the SubPc to TiO2, the group of Kimura proceeded in a different way by using SubPcs as light-harvester of a ZnPc-based sensitizer. To this end, the authors synthetized two SubPcs possessing one (234) or three (235) pyridines at peripheral position which were able to form supramolecular dyads with ZnPc via Zn–N coordination (Fig. 66). These pyridine-SubPc derivatives showed an emission which nicely overlaps with the absorption of ZnPc. As a result, an efficient FRET between SubPc and ZnPc was observed in solution. In this way, the incorporation of SubPcs bound to ZnPc sensitizer in DSSCs led to panchromatic responses with additional photocurrent generations in the range of 500–600 nm. The device incorporating trisubstituted-SubPc afforded a respectable PCE of 5%.113
 Fig. 66  Chemical structure of the pyridyl SubPcs employed as light-harvester in DSSCs by Kimura and co-workers in ref. 111. 
2.7.1.5 Subphthalocyanine-containing perovskites solar cells. 
Solar cells based on organo-metal halide perovskites (PerSCs) have gained an unprecedented research interest reaching impressive PCEs above 25% in a few years. In view of the potential that SubPcs have demonstrated in the other configurations, some authors have started to explore their potential also in this type of solar cells. However, it should be noted that, since in this case the perovskite layer is the light absorber, the role of SubPc is just extracting the holes or electrons (HTM or ETM) from such layer. Accordingly, this section is divided in (1) SubPcs as HTM and (2) SubPcs as ETM.
2.7.1.5.1 Subphthalocyanines as HTM. 
In an attempt to substitute the well-known spiro-OMeTAD, the groups of Lianos and Kumar explored the use of 20 as HTM.463 In this study, the authors prepared a PerSC consisting of a CH3NH3PbClxI3−x perovskite deposited onto a mesoporous TiO2 surface by spin coating, where the SubPc layer was subsequently introduced by vacuum deposition. SEM studies revealed well-defined layer structure evidencing that this fabrication method is effective. Interestingly, when this device was stored under ambient conditions in dark, it underwent an incubation period of 9 days during which the efficiency increased, affording a maximum PCE of 6.6%. The cell stability with and without SubPc was studied by Micro-Raman techniques. With SubPc, the device started to degrade after the 9th day. In contrast, the device without SubPc rapidly degraded in a period of two days. Therefore, it was concluded that the placement of SubPc on the top slowed down the degradation process.
Further research on this SubPc-based HTMs was reported by Wan et al. describing the use of a new Me6SubPc-Cl.26 In this case the SubPc was deposited by spin coating and integrated in the following solar cell configuration: FTO/SnO2/PCBM/perovskite/Me6SubPc-Cl/Au. The layer morphology was again evaluated by SEM measurements and revealed that the SubPc formed a smooth and flat surface in contact with perovskite and Au layers. After that, the solar cell performance of this novel derivative was measured under AM 1.5G illumination and compared with that of 20 and spiro-OMeTAD. Remarkably, the Me6SubPc-Cl device outperformed that of spiro-OMeTAD and 20, displaying a high Voc of 0.986 V and a PCE of 6.96%. The authors attributed these results to the fact that the HOMO–LUMO levels of Me6SubPc-Cl are better aligned with the valence band of the perovskite material.
Xu and co-workers proceeded in a different way by incorporating 20 as a passivating agent with the aim of filling the voids and defects in the perovskite surface.464 They found that, upon addition of small SubPc concentrations (0.5–1 mg mL−1), the free charge carrier lifetime increased because of the efficient passivation of the surface defect trap-states. Thus, the Jsc and FF values dramatically raised boosting the PCE up to 13.6%. The addition of higher concentrations of SubPc led to the formation of clusters that hinder the charge transfer from perovskite to PC61BM and therefore lower device performances. Another noteworthy aspect is the mildness of the device fabrication method. The SubPc was deposited by spin-coating thus avoiding the damage of the perovskite by thermal annealing, as for example may happen with other common strategies, such as the thermal deposition of polymers.
2.7.1.5.2 Subphthalocyanines as ETM. 
In contrast to the extensive research carried out to optimize the HTM in PerSCs, ETMs are mainly monopolised by fullerene and its derivatives. Accordingly, for the same shortcomings commented in Sections 2.7.1.1.2 and 2.7.1.2.2, there is an urgent need to develop non-fullerene organic ETMs. Inspired by their success in the conventional p–n devices, several authors have just recently explored the use of SubPcs as ETM in PerSCs. The first example was reported by He, Russell, Djurišić and co-workers.465 In this pioneering work, they employed a mixed acceptor layer composed of SubPc 2 an C60 and reported an impressive PCE of 22% using the device architecture depicted in Fig. 67. More importantly, the authors demonstrated that the implementation of a SubPc in the ETM significantly increased the device stability. Based on a comprehensive device characterization, the authors explained that the SubPc chemically interacts with the perovskite halide ions in a such a way that avoid their migration toward the electrode. As a result, these devices exhibited an unprecedent long-term stability (95% of PCE retained over 1200 hour of outdoor testing and 90% of PCE retained under illumination and MPPT for over 2000 h).
 Fig. 67  (a) Device architecture and (b) J–V curves of the PerSC reported by He, Russell, Djurišić and co-workers. Adapted with permission from ref. 465. Copyright 2021 Science China Press. Published by Elsevier B.V. and Science China Press. 
In parallel to this work, Torres, Nazeeruddin and co-workers reported the use of 2 and it fluorinated analogue Cl6SubPc-F as ETM, either alone or in combination with C60, in PerSCs where the active layer were deposited by solution processing method (triple-cation mixed-halide perovskite; CsFAMAPbIBr) or by thermal evaporation (methylammonium lead iodide; MAPI).466 In all cases, the SubPc with chlorine atom outperformed the derivative with fluorine. Unfortunately, the incorporation of SubPc into the ETM did not improve the PCE (maximum PCEs of 7.7% and 10.8% in MAPI and CsFAMAPbIBr devices, respectively). As deduced from the J–V curves, EQE spectra, and PL, the low efficiencies mainly arise from the low charge extraction capability that the SubPc present in these specific set-ups. In spite of that, this work established a fundamental starting point for the further research of SubPcs as non-fullerene ETM in PerSCs. In spite of obtaining very low efficiencies, the groups of Atienza and Remiro-Buenamañana also claimed the potential of SubPcs in PerSCs by finding that the co-crystallization with SubPcs improves the photoresponse of the perovskite in the visible region.467


2.7.2 Applications in biochemistry and biology. 
In recent years, porphyrinic photosensitizers have gained increasing attention also for the detection and treatment of several diseases.468–470 In this field, SubPcs have also demonstrated a great potential. On the one hand, SubPcs present intensive absorption an emission within the biological window, which enables their use as fluorescence probes for the detection of diseases. On the other hand, with regard to the therapeutic activity, they have a longer triplet lifetime compared to other widely used porphyrinoids, such as porphyrins or Pcs, and therefore usually provide excellent quantum yields for the generation of singlet oxygen (Section 2.3.7). In addition, SubPcs can exhibit excellent water solubility due to their low tendency towards aggregation and their versatility in terms of functionalization, which is of paramount importance for their study in the living world.
In this section we will show the recent works reporting on the use of SubPcs in biomedicine and we will analyse what factors determines their efficiency as imaging or therapeutic agent. According to the role of the SubPc, this section is in turn divided in two parts, depending on whether the SubPc molecule is exclusively used for bioimaging or has a therapeutic effect. In the latter case, the SubPc derivative can also be employed as molecular probe.
2.7.2.1 Subphthalocyanines as probes for bioimaging. 
The first concern in the application of SubPcs in biochemistry is to select the best method for their introduction into the cell media. In addition to diagnosing, the in vitro visualization by microscopy is a powerful tool to assess what method is the most suitable. A priori, the synthesis of water-soluble derivatives seems to be the most efficient approach. To this end, the SubPc can be functionalized with polar groups at either the axial or the peripheral positions.1 However, several authors have also obtained encouraging results by means other strategies. For instance, Decréau and co-workers explored the use of several SubPcs as fluorescence probes for melanoma B16 cells and followed two strategies to place them into cells.97 The first one involved the nano-encapsulation of hydrophobic SubPcs in a lipidic nanoparticle (i.e., liposome). Once incorporated in cells, such liposomes are expected to fuse with the cell membranes, leaving the SubPc in a membrane (bilayer) environment. The second strategy consisted of the synthesis of water-soluble SubPcs which are axially functionalized with an alkylsulfonate derivative (Fig. 68). Remarkably, both strategies turned out to be equally efficient and did not showed cytotoxicity. Another noteworthy finding in this work was that, depending on the pH, SubPcs bearing amine-containing axial substituents can show a pH activatable fluorescence. In a subsequent work, the same group developed a new strategy to achieve water solubility consisting in the axial functionalization of SubPcs with a chelator moiety (tetraxetan; DOTA, or Pentetic acid; DPTA) complexed with medically relevant metals, such as Gd, Y or In (Fig. 69).100 These derivatives, which are bimodal contrast agents, showed sharp Q-bands in water, thus evidencing high solubility, and also provided good fluorescence images of B16 cells by means confocal/biphoton microscopy.
 Fig. 68  Top (left to right): General structure of hydrophobic/hydrophilic activatable SubPcs employed by Decréau et al. Bottom left: Incorporation of hydrophobic SubPc in the liposome structure. Adapted with permission from ref. 97. 
 Fig. 69  SubPcs appended with a chelator moiety (DOTA or DTPA) complexed with several metals: Gd, Y, In, Cu, Ga, Lu employed by Decréau et al. Adapted with permission from ref. 100. 
Further research in the application of SubPcs as probes have involved their conjugation with biomolecules to achieve site-selective bioimages. For instance, the axial position of SubPc or SubPc-containing liposomes has been functionalized with bombesin, a well-known peptide which is an over-expressed receptor at the surface of tumour cells.108 In this case, the use of liposome was found to be the most suitable approach since the direct functionalization of SubPcs led to modest water solubility and low stability, presumably due to the intrinsic sensibility of SubPc against certain nucleophiles. Unfortunately, such liposomes lost inhibitory activity with respect to the reference compound (Bombesin [1–14]), which exhibited 66-fold affinity for BBN receptors. Importantly, this work claims again the use of liposomes as promising alternative to employ non-water-soluble or unstable SubPcs. In 2020, Voskuhl and co-workers described the synthesis of axially substituted SubPcs that are able to form host-guest complexes with cyclodextrins. Interestingly, these conjugates produced excellent fluorescence images and exhibited a lower phototoxicity in comparison with the free SubPc.471 Other studies describing the use of axially functionalized SubPcs involve SubPc-BODIPY dyads which, depending on the excitation wavelength, display dual-color emission or even singlet oxygen generation.472,473
With regard to SubPcs peripherally functionalized, Trivedi and co-workers employed SubPcs and SubNc with different degrees of peripheral fluorination to visualize MDA-MB-231 breast tumor cells by epifluorescence microscopy.52 As expected, the number of fluorine atoms strongly affects the optical and physical properties of these compounds (e.g., quantum yield and solubility), as well as their cellular uptake. The same authors reported later a similar study using F3SubPc-Cl. This SubPc, which has fewer F-atoms than those of the previous work, showed similar fluorescence properties (ϕ = 0.29), improved cellular uptakes, and produced excellent fluorescence images from cells.474
It is important to mention that, although all of the above-mentioned work has been carried out in vitro, SubPcs have also proven effective for in vivo fluorescence experiments. In this regard, Yurt et al. demonstrated that tBu3SubPcs axially holding m-hydroxybenzoic acid tend to accumulate in tumoral cells and are promising imaging agents for the diagnosis of breast tumor.475
2.7.2.2 Subphthalocyanines as therapeutic agents. 
As mentioned above, SubPcs can produce 1O2 in high quantum yields and thus can also be employed for the photodynamic therapy of several diseases. Specifically, the treatment of (1) antibiotic-resistant bacteria and (2) cancer have been the main targeted diseases.
2.7.2.2.1 Therapy of bacteria. 
The potential of SubPcs in this field was firstly documented by Kim and co-workers by publishing a pioneering work reporting the use of SubPc-based nanospheres for the killing of antibiotic-resistant bacteria (Fig. 70).18 These nanospheres were prepared by olefin cross-metathesis of alkenylmercapto-SubPcs, followed by the alkylation of sulfur atoms to obtain a positively charged surface. Such positive charges improve the solubility in water and the ability to target negatively charged bacterial membranes. Using molecular mechanics models and several microscopies, it was found that these nanospheres have an average diameter of 150 nm and contain approximately 18.000 SubPc units. After the synthesis and characterization, the authors assessed the in vitro photobiological activity against several Gram-negative, -positive, and antibiotic-resistant bacterial cells. Remarkably, these nanoparticles yielded excellent result by showing a more than 99% efficiency of photoinactivation even with a dose as low as 4.2 J cm−2 and a loading concentration of 10 nm. It is important to highlight that the nanosphere largely outperformed the efficiency of the SubPc monomers, presumably due to it generates a higher local concentration of 1O2. Moreover, the higher local concentration compared to any given small molecule also enhances the interaction with the given microbe. Worth of mention is that in addition this material displayed interesting photoacoustic properties that may render it useful in photoacoustic tomography imaging (PTI).
 Fig. 70  SubPc-based nanospheres investigated by the group of Kim. Adapted with permission from ref. 18. Copyright 2015 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
An alternative strategy comprises the combination of SubPcs with species that are also able to form 1O2. For instance, the use of SubPc linked to TiO2 nanoparticles has recently been described by Ozturk et al. They demonstrated that these derivatives exhibit good inhibitory and bactericidal effects on S. aureus and E. coli strains when irradiated with different light doses.476 Remarkably, a subsequent work by the same authors demonstrated that these conjugates also have promising antifungal properties.477
The use of SubPcs in field have been further explored using simpler, non-soluble derivatives. For instance, Yurt et al. found promising bactericidal effects (almost zero CFU values upon 20 J cm−2 light doses and a cc of 64 μg mL−1) in the treatment of Gram-positive (S. aureus) and Gram-negative (E. coli) strains with a SubPc holding a phenoxy derivative in the boron atom.115
2.7.2.2.2 Therapy of cancer. 
In 2007, Xu et al. demonstrated for the first time the application of a SubPc sensitizer for the PDT of cancer. They found that water-soluble SubPc axially functionalized with ethylene glycol moieties could serve as efficient photosensitizers by exhibiting a high photocytotoxicity against HepG2 human hepatocarcinoma and HT29 human colon adenocarcinoma cells, offering in certain cases IC50 values as low as 0.02 μM.478
More than ten years later, Torres, Juarranz de la Fuente, de la Escosura and co-workers published a key article exploring the dual-role of a novel series of SubPcs as fluorescence probes and PDT sensitizers (Fig. 71).77 In order to modulate the hydrophilicity and hydrophobicity, the SubPcs were functionalized with different substituents at the axial position with (1) a pyrene-containing hydrophobic moiety (236), where the pyrene moiety could act as an additional fluorophore, (2) an amino group with two alkoxylated chains for increasing hydrophilicity (237), or (3) a hydrophobic group without the pyrene unit (238). The biological activity of these derivatives was firstly tested in vitro employing HeLa, SCC-13, and A431 cancer cells lines. These SubPcs efficiently entered into the tumoral cells and provided excellent fluorescence images. Interestingly, fluorescence microscopy revealed that the subcellular localization of SubPcs depends on the axial substituent. Then, the phototoxicity of SubPcs upon green light irradiation was addressed considering different concentrations and cellular types. As inferred from the drastic decrease in cell survival, these SubPcs showed excellent activity. To further confirm the theranostic potential of these derivatives, preliminary in vivo studies were carried out with the best SubPc. The authors found that the SubPc fluorescence can also be detected in vivo, and that a significant size reduction of the treated tumoral cells was observed after the irradiation.
 Fig. 71  Axially substituted SubPcs 236–238 investigated by Torres, Juarranz de la Fuente, de la Escosura and co-workers in ref. 76. 
Very recently, Novakova, Martínez-Díaz and Torres reported an insightfully study exploring the impact of axial and peripheral substitution on the optical properties and photodynamic activity of SubPcs.479 In this work, the authors prepared SubPcs axially functionalized with tyrosine methyl ester (Tyr) or benzo-18-crown-6 (CE), and peripherally functionalized with alkylsulfanyl groups or halogen atoms, such as fluorine or iodine. Importantly, these axial ligands were found to improve the hydrophilic properties of SubPcs. Upon light irradiation, most of the prepared SubPcs showed high in vitro photodynamic activity. In particular, the SubPc derivative holding axial CE and peripheral alkylsulfanyl groups showed the best IC50 values (i.e., 2.3 and 4.4 nM for SK-MEL-28 and HeLa cells, respectively).
Following another concept, Ng, García-Iglesias and Torres published a pioneering work reporting the use of a SubPc-based sensitizer able to self-assemble in water into spherical nanoparticles.260 To this end, the SubPc was functionalized with tetra(ethylene glycol) side-chains and an axial fluorine atom to improve the aggregation properties of SubPcs. The resulting SubPc-based aggregates readily internalised into cancer cells and localised in the lysosomes. Through gradual disassembling, the photosensitising nanosystem is activated, leading to cell death with IC50 values as low as 1.2 μM.
The application of SubPc–TiO2 conjugates have also been documented for the therapy of cancer. In particular, it has been assessed their photoactivity against several carcinoma cell lines, such as HepG2 or HT29. These derivatives have proven to be very efficient against HepG2 but showed modest activity against HT29. In the former case, concentrations as low as 1.57 μM were needed to achieve phototoxicities above 95%, whereas concentrations above 6.25 μM were required for HT29. In addition, the labelling of these SubPc–TiO2 nanoparticles with 131I and their use in nuclear bioimaging have been described.117 In a subsequent work, the same authors carried out a similar study in mammary (EMT6) and cervical (HeLa).480 Again, photocytotoxicities close to 100% were obtained at a cc. of 2.2 mg mL−1 and a light dose of 90 J cm−2. Very recently, Yurt and co-workers evaluated the photodynamic activity of SubPcs on colon carcinoma cells (HT-29). This compound displayed good PDT properties (phototoxicity above 90% at cc. of 3.13 μg mL−1 and 30 J cm−2) and, more interestingly, showed an uptake in HT-29 cells two-fold higher than the healthy cell line.481
2.7.2.2.3 Subphthalocyanines as anti-inflammatory agents. 
In addition to the treatment of cancer and bacteria, some studies have demonstrated that SubPcs can regulate or stimulate the immunomodulatory response of macrophages. For example, it has been documented that 72 and thiophene-substituted SubPcs have anti-inflammatory activity on macrophages and can be used to suppress the pro-inflammatory activities of the macrophages.112,482 In stark contrast, the introduction of three peripheral iodine atoms (i.e., I3SubPc-OPh) triggers the opposite effect and provides adjuvant and immunostimulatory functions by increasing the pro-inflammatory cytokine production levels. On the other hand, SubPcs decorated with peripheral chlorine atoms have also displayed promising anti-inflammatory properties. In this regard, it has been found that the more degree of chlorination, the higher is the activity of the SubPc. In particular, 48 efficiently inhibits the production of pro-inflammatory TNF and IL6 cytokines in mammalian macrophages after 10 min of Xenon light irradiation.483


2.7.3 Additional applications. 
The unique structural, physical, and optoelectronic properties of SubPcs render them potential active materials not only in organic photovoltaics or biology but also in a plethora of applications that range from molecular optoelectronics to catalysis. In this section we will outline the reported work on SubPcs in these additional fields which, despite being not so explored as those previously commented in Sections 2.7.1 and 2.7.2, have attracted increasing attention in material science.
2.7.3.1 Organic light-emitting diodes (OLEDs). 
Since the pioneering work carried out by the groups of Torres484 and Chen,485 SubPcs have proven to be a promising class of orange emitters with excellent electrical performance in OLEDs. In this context, noteworthy is the work of Bender et al. These authors firstly observed that the fluorination degree has a negative impact on the luminescence efficiency.209 Then, they found that SubPcs holding phthalimide at axial position can exhibit the dual function of being the (p- or n-type) charge carrier and the emitter material. Remarkably, this derivative produced good luminescence efficiencies as both hole (77 cd m−2) and electron (143 cd m−2) transport emitter.154 Furthermore, the use of organic polymers functionalized with SubPcs have been reported. This strategy, although still suffering from low luminance, represents a promising option to prepare solution-processed devices.145 Importantly, Bender et al. also found that SubPc performances in OLEDs devices are drastically improved when, instead of using neat films, the SubPc is diluted in a host material that balances the hole and electron transport.486,487 Moreover, SubPcs have been employed as charge generation layer in tandem OLEDs by Yu et al.488 They employed a blend film of 20 and C60 to connect two identical electroluminescence units based on Ir(ppy)3. This 20:C60 layer was found to be very beneficial for the OLEDs performance by displaying excellent charge generating and transport abilities.
2.7.3.2 Nonlinear optics (NLO). 
By virtue of their particular symmetry and electronic structure, these macrocycles have displayed excellent second harmonic generation (SHG) when functionalized with various donor or acceptor substituents. Indeed, quadratic hyperpolarizability β values offered by some of them are among the highest values reported for organic molecules. Moreover, the third-order harmonic generation (THG) by SubPc have also been reported and respectable cubic susceptibilities (γ) have been obtained. However, although some strategies have been recently developed to improve this nonlinearity, such as the functionalization with gold nanoparticles489 or the control of film morphology by annealing,246 this field was primarily explored during the first decade of this century.1
2.7.3.3 Organic field effect transistors (OFETs). 
Organic field effect transistors (OFETs) are the basic building blocks for many flexible integrated circuits and displays and still constitute a very active research area. Nevertheless, although a recent example has been reported,490 the application of SubPc in this field was also mainly developed at the beginning of this century and we refer the reader to our previous review.1
2.7.3.4 Organic photodetectors (OPDs). 
The donor–acceptor heterojunction of SubPcs has also found utility in the field of organic photodetectors. In this case, 20, 78, and 224 have been the derivatives of choice and very respectable EQE values and spectral sensitivities have been obtained.21,491–495 Worth of special mention in this field is the contribution of Miyajima, Araoka, and co-workers.13 They demonstrated that a SubPc with peripheral thioethers (Fig. 72) assembles into a no centrosymmetric columnar liquid crystal upon external electric fields and exhibit bulk photovoltaic effect (BPVE), a very interesting property for next generation molecular materials, such as more efficient photodetectors. When the authors deposited this columnar assembly between two ITO electrodes, the resultant device reaches a light-on/off ratio as high as 6.6 × 103, which is a value comparable to that of typical OPD utilizing a donor–acceptor bulk-heterojunction.
 Fig. 72  Molecular structure and self-assembly of the SubPc employed by Miyajima, Araoka and co-workers to prepare liquid crystal with BPVE. Reproduced with permission from ref. 13. Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society. 
2.7.3.5 Resistive memories. 
In 2017, the group of Yam successfully prepared a SubPc-containing resistive memory device by solution processing.171 To this end, the SubPcs were functionalized at both the axial (239) and the peripheral positions 240 with a push–pull fragment (Fig. 73). Remarkably, the authors found that, depending on the substitution pattern, a switching of behaviour from binary to ternary can be observed. Thus, axially substituted SubPcs exhibit ternary resistive memory behaviour with well separated current ratios of 1:106:108 between “OFF”, “ON1” and “ON2” states, while only binary logic is observed for peripherally substituted SubPc.
 Fig. 73  Molecular structures of 239 and 240 and (a) current–voltage characteristics of an ITO/239/Al device. (b) Retention time of the ITO/239/Al device in “OFF”, “ON1” and “ON2” states under a constant stress (1 V). (c) Current–voltage characteristics of an ITO/240/Al device. (d) Retention time of the ITO/240/Al device in “OFF” and “ON” states under a constant stress (1 V). Adapted with permission from ref. 171. Copyright 2017 American Chemical Society. 
2.7.3.6 Electrochromic materials. 
For this application, the SubPc core has been functionalized with three terpyridines in such a way that, in the presence of Fe(II), furnishes a metallo-supramolecular polymer (poly-SubPc–Fe). When this polymer was subjected to positive potential (from 0 to 1.6 V), excellent blue-to-transmissive transitions were observed. This discoloration is due to the oxidation of Fe(II) to (III), which avoids the MLCT from Fe to SubPc ligand. Importantly for future applications, this process was found to be highly reversible.15
2.7.3.7 Photoredox catalysis. 
Given the strong and red-shifted absorption of SubPcs, some authors have envisaged their use in the rapidly growing field of photoredox catalysis. In this case, the role of SubPc is (1) capturing the light to reach the excited state, (2) generating high-energy, SubPc-based radical ions and (3) triggering an electron transfer process while returning to the ground state (Fig. 74). On this basis, Shibata and co-workers found that trifluoroethoxylated SubPcs is very effective to catalyse the iodo-trifluoromethylation of alkenes under red LED irradiation.496 Indeed, after optimizing several parameters, this SubPc outperformed other commonly used photocatalysts by furnishing the model product in a 96% yield after 1 hour. Importantly, it was found that the reaction does not work when using 20 as catalyst, thus suggesting that the peripheral substitution of SubPc with trifluoroethoxy groups is crucial for its catalytic activity. Another example in this field includes that of García and co-workers.155 This group demonstrated that a SubPc encapsulated within a metal–organic framework can generate α-ketyl radicals from α-haloacetophenones under sunlight irradiation. Once formed, these radicals may trigger different reactions, such as the dehalogenation or the coupling with alkenes.
 Fig. 74  Action mechanism of SubPc-based photocatalysts. 
A different approach was introduced by Ma and co-workers by employing SubPcs for the photodegradation of organic pollutants. These authors functionalized the axial position of SubPcs with several ligands to incorporate them to TiO2. In this case, the role of the SubPc is to improve the catalytic activity by populating the conduction band of these materials which subsequently produce superoxide or hydroxyl radicals.497–502 This SubPc-doping of TiO2 has also found to improve the catalytic efficiency in hydrogen generation processes.503
2.7.3.8 Catalysis in lithium batteries. 
The features of lithium batteries can be improved by using a catalyst that facilitate the redox reactions operating in them. Along this line, the group of Zhang has explored the integration of several SubPcs in Li/SOCl2 batteries. Based on voltammetry experiments, the authors advanced the ability of such SubPcs to catalyse the SOCl2 reduction (SOCl2 + 2e− → 1/2S + 1/2SO2 + 2Cl−). Thus, they found that batteries whose electrolytes contained SubPcs displayed an increase up to 40.6 and 53.2% in the storage energy and capacity, respectively. Moreover, SEM images revealed that SubPcs avoid the formation of LiCl films at the electrodes, which typically leads to great efficiency losses.103,131
2.7.3.9 Molecular machines. 
Very recently, SubPcs have started to find some application in the field of molecular machines. In 2020, Shimizu, Pantos and co-workers prepared a rotaxane employing SubPcs as stoppers. The equilibrium between the slipping-on and slipping-off configurations in these rotaxanes was found to be highly dependent on the SubPc peripheral substitution. Moreover, such substitution was also found to modulate the electron transfer between the SubPc and NDI of the thread.79
2.7.3.10 Sensors. 
By virtue of their strong absorption in the visible range and their sensitivity to certain nucleophiles, SubPcs have also found utility in the field of anion sensors. In particular, they have proven to be particularly useful in the detection of fluorine504 and cyanide ions.27,505,506 In addition, it has been theoretically predicted that SubPcs could be effective for the detection of I−, Br−, and BF4− ions.507 SubPcs have also been successfully employed for the analysis of red wines,25 the determination of catechin in tea extracts,140 the assay of acetylsalicylic acid in the drug Cardiomagnyl,508 and for the sensing of pesticides.509 Interestingly, the Park's group recently reported a new strategy for the selective detection of sulfide anion, which is based on anion–π non-covalent interactions between the sulfide and the π-cloud of fluorinated SubPcs.510

3. Subporphyrazines
3.1 Synthesis and reactivity of subporphyrazines
SubPzs are curved, aromatic macrocycles, consisting of three pyrrole subunits connected through their 2,5-positions by aza-bridges.1 Their structure results from the formal removal of the three fused benzene rings in a SubPc. The first synthesis and unequivocal characterization of these compounds was reported in 2005 by Rodríguez-Morgade and Torres6 in an article that described these porphyrinoids bearing alkyl and sulfanyl substituents directly attached to the β-positions of the pyrrole subunits (Scheme 19, 241, 246–8). Soon after, the preparation of 242 was reported By Kobayashi and Durfee.511 Similar to SubPcs, SubPzs are cone-shaped, 14 π-electron aromatic compounds that so far are known exclusively as boron(III) complexes.
 Scheme 19  The synthesis of SubPzs through cyclotrimerization reaction. Note that only C3 regioisomer is represented. 250 is obtained as a mixture of the C3 and C1 regioisomers. 
The synthesis of SubPzs is carried out by cyclotrimerization of substituted maleonitriles templated by boron trichloride, usually in refluxing xylene.6,511 For SubPzs substituted with thioethers at their periphery, this procedure requires a tedious separation of the desired hexasulfanyl-SubPz and a monochlorinated SubPz arising from replacement of one thioether function by a chlorine atom under the cyclotrimerization conditions. Alternatively, the crude reaction containing the mixture of hexasulfanylated and chlorinated compounds can be treated with the corresponding 1-alkylthiol in 1,3-dimethyl-2-imidazolidinone (DMI) at 0 °C, affording the symmetric hexasulfanyl-SubPzs.512
With only one exception constituted by the unsubstituted derivative, which resulted to be unstable,513 the corresponding fumaronitriles do not afford SubPzs under the same reaction conditions. This hypothesis has recently been reinforced through the reaction of a cis/trans-mixture of the perfluorinated bisaryl dinitrile with BCl3 in p-xylene, which leads to SubPz 249 and recovery of the unreacted fumaric isomer.514
Apart from the perfluorinated SubPz 249, other hexaaryl-SubPzs (255–267) have not been prepared directly from a cyclotrimerization reaction, because the corresponding bisaryl maleonitrile precursors are difficult to prepare. In these cases, the thermodynamically more favoured fumaronitrile isomers are obtained as the main, or sometimes exclusive products. Attempts to photoisomerize these fumaronitriles into the corresponding maleonitriles lead to inseparable mixtures with phenanthrene-9,10-dicarbonitriles and their 4a,4b-dihydro derivatives, arising from photochemically driven electrocyclic reactions.515,516 1,1,2-Tricyano-2-arylethylene derivatives contain the required configuration for two of the nitrile groups, so they have been cyclotrimerized to construct push–pull SubPzs (Table 8, 250–252).517 Owing to the unsymmetric nature of the tricyano-precursors, SubPz 250 was obtained as a mixture of two regioisomers with C3 and C1 molecular symmetry, isolated in a 33:1 ratio. The discrepancy of this ratio from the statistical ratio (1:3) was explained on the basis of the instability of these push–pull SubPzs in solution. In particular, the C1 isomer is less stable than the C3 isomer, and for 251 and 252, only the C3 regioisomers were obtained.

The difficulties associated to the synthesis of substituted maleonitriles, in addition to the incompatibility of many functional groups with boron trichloride have precluded the preparation of SubPzs peripherally substituted with functional groups other than those described above. Peripheral functionalization with aryl and vinylene moieties has been achieved by modification of preformed SubPzs through Pd catalyzed, CuTC mediated cross-coupling reactions of hexasulfanylated SubPzs with a variety of arylboronic512,518 or vinylboronic512 acids, respectively (Scheme 20). This strategy has allowed to introduce functional groups of different nature attached to the SubPz periphery through arylene (255–267) or vinylene (268–270) linkers. The resulting extended π-systems showed strong electronic communication between the macrocycles and the terminal functional groups, this serving to modulate in depth the electronic character of the macrocycles.512,518
 Scheme 20  Hexaarylated and hexavinylated SubPzs prepared by cross-coupling reactions of hexasulfanylated SubPzs with boronic acids. 
Contrasting to SubPcs, for which axial substitution can be an issue,68 SubPzs readily react at the axial position.63,159 The axial chlorine atom arising from the cyclotrimerization with BCl3 has been replaced by hydroxy,6,511 phenoxy,63,159t-butylphenoxy512,518 or fluorine159,214 by simple substitution with water, phenol, t-butylphenol or treatment with BF3·Et2O, respectively (Scheme 21). All of these reactions occur with higher efficiency on SubPzs than in SubPcs.
 Scheme 21  Axial substitution of SubPz-Cl derivatives by OH, ArO or F. 
The high axial reactivity of SubPzs has been used to assemble SubPz dimers and trimers by direct reaction of the chloro-SubPzs with hydroquinone and phloroglucinol, affording 279 and 280 (Fig. 75) in 40% and 64%, respectively.519
 Fig. 75  SubPz dimer 279 and trimer 280. 
A different functionalization of SubPzs at the axial position consisted in the formation of π-complexes by treating axial phenoxy-SubPz with a Cp*Ru+ salt.63 Within SubPzs, Ru coordination takes place exclusively to the axial phenoxy group, without altering the pyrrole units. Therefore, the corresponding arrays 281 and 282, connected by a ruthenoarene unit (Scheme 22), have been prepared in 90% yield by treating 279 and 280 with an excess of [Ru(Cp*)–(MeCN)3][PF6] in dichloromethane (Scheme 22). Electrochemical and photophysical studies revealed strong coupling between the electron-accepting SubPzs and the electron-donating hydroquinone or phloroglucinol linkers in 279 and 280. Upon functionalization of these spacers with the organometallic Ru(II) subunit, the electron-donating benzene core is replaced by the electron-accepting ruthenocene unit,63 thus limiting inter-SubPz interactions in 281 and 282.
 Scheme 22  Synthesis of ruthenoarene-connected SubPz arrays. 
The SubPz axial substituent has also been subjected to classical organic reactions such as reduction and esterification reactions (Scheme 23), to link a selected SubPz (283) to a pentacene dimer, which acts primarily as energy acceptor and secondarily as a singlet fission enabler.520
 Scheme 23  Functionalization of a SubPz with a pentacene dimer at the axial position. 
3.2 Characterization of subporphyrazines

3.2.1 Single-crystal X-ray diffraction. 
The crystal structure of fluoro[1,2,6,7,11,12-hexapropylsubporphyrazinato] boron (277) exhibits a cone shape with the boron atom coordinated to three nitrogen atoms and the fluorine atom situated at the vertex of an almost regular tetrahedron (see Fig. 76 and Table 9). The degree of curvature can be characterized by the SubPz bowl-depth, defined by the distance from the mean plane of peripheral six β-pyrrole carbons to the center boron atom.159 The bowl-depth of 277 is of 1.768 Å, so the curvature is slightly more pronounced than that of SubPcs (see ref. 1 and Section 2.3.1), and definitely more pronounced than that of SubPs, as it has been predicted by DFT calculations (see Section 6.3.1).1,521,522 Peripheral triarylation517 or hexaarylation512,518 do not produce relevant changes in the SubPz bowl depth.
 Fig. 76  Atom labelling of SubPz 277.

Triarylated SubPzs exhibit relatively small dihedral angles (from 17.43° to 27.45° in C3-250) of aryl groups with respect to the plane defined by the pyrrole to which they are attached.517 Upon hexaarylation, these dihedral angles increase, ranging from 28.45° to 54.80, as a result of the higher steric congestion related to C3-250,512,518 although they are still smaller than those observed for porphyrazines (24.26° to 68.18°).523 This is related to the Cα–N–Cα angles, which are smaller for SubPzs than for porphyrazines (114° vs. 124°, respectively), providing more room for peripheral substituents in the former, and favouring π-conjugation over the entire molecule. In fact, only substitution at the meta-positions of hexaarylated SubPzs with sterically demanding tert-butyl groups forces to adopt less coplanar conformations, up to 68.12° in 267, which are comparable to those of p-tert-butylphenyl porphyrazines.523
Among hexaarylated SubPzs substituted with one methoxy group per benzene ring (compounds 256 and 262), the dihedral angles between two consecutive benzene rings are significantly larger for the sterically more congested meta-substituted compounds (54.80° to 58.30° for 262) than for the para-substituted SubPzs (38.57 to 55.96 for 256). The angles between two consecutive benzene rings are also much smaller than those observed for the corresponding octaarylated porphyrazines. As a result of all these smaller dihedral angles between the substituents and macrocycle, peripheral aryl substituents produce stronger influences on the electronic properties of SubPzs related to those of porphyrazines.
Hexavinylation produces a slight planarization of the SubPz structure, with a bowl depth of 1.600 Å observed for 269 (Table 9). Indeed, the degree of curvature of 269 is the same as that observed for the SubPz internal core of dodecafluorinated SubPc 4, which displays a SubPz bowl depth of 1.604 Å.159 The smaller volume of the vinylene moieties related to that of the phenylene rings allows for a more planar configuration and hence, a more effective π-delocalization over the whole structure. Thus, the vinylene CC bonds in 269 lie nearly in the same plane as the pyrrole rings, with small deviation angles ranging from 2.69° to 6.30°, except for one of the CC moieties, which is tilted by 18.45°. Importantly, the dihedral angles between the pyrrole and the terminal phenyl rings in 269 are also much smaller (2.92° to 32.70°) than those of the triarylated and hexaarylated series, favouring a stronger electronic communication between the macrocycle and the terminal phenyl ring in the former. Such an enlargement of the absorptive and radiative chromophore was evidenced also in UV-vis spectroscopy as we will discuss below.
In the solid state, SubPzs have a high tendency to assemble into aggregates of different dimensions, whose geometry is strongly dependent on the axial and peripheral functionalization.159,511 For example, SubPzs 277, 262, 263 and 267 stack in head-to-tail, alternating configurations, but in 277 the axial B–F bonds are perfectly aligned within a column and parallel between columns (Fig. 77),159 while for 262 the axial B–O bonds are not aligned within a column, although they are parallel. For 263 the axial B–O bonds are angled between columns, and for 267 the macrocycles are arranged in a zigzag fashion (Fig. 77).512 SubPz C3-250 also stacks in columns, but in this case, within the slipped-stack columns, solvent molecules reside between macrocycle molecules (Fig. 77).513
 Fig. 77  Examples of SubPz aggregates in the solid state. Adapted with permission from ref. 512. Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society. 
In stark contrast, SubPzs 269, 272 and 256 do not stack in columns, but can form dimers, either in a convex–convex configuration (272),511 or concave–concave arrangements that can form dimeric capsules (256) (Fig. 77),518 or with one macrocycle slipped with respect to the other (269) (Fig. 77).512 The axial and peripheral substitution also determine the SubPzs liquid crystalline behavior in more extent than for SubPcs.6,261


3.2.2 NMR spectroscopy. 
The 1H-NMR spectra of SubPzs evidences their C3-symmetry and indicates free rotation of the peripheral appendages, even for the bulkier substituents of 262, 263, 265 and 267. Their aromatic nature produces low field shifts of the protons corresponding to the peripheral substituents, accompanied by high field shifts for those of the axial functions. In particular, axial hydroxy groups appear at δ = −0.94 to −1.47 ppm6 and axial tert-butylphenoxy-substituents are seen as two doublets at 5.31–5.44 ppm and 6.82–6.90 ppm, accompanied by a singlet around 1.11–1.14 ppm.512 Boron resonances follow the same tendency as for SubPcs, appearing in 11B-NMR at δ = −14.3 to −15.8 ppm.1,159


3.2.3 Optical properties. 
The optical properties of SubPzs evidence the aromatic nature of these macrocycles. SubPzs substituted with six peripheral alkyl chains (for example, 241, 271, 272 and 277), display very similar UV-vis spectra, composed of two equally intense absorption bands at λmax = 295 (ε = 38160 l mol−1 cm−1) and 499 nm (ε = 40206 l mol−1 cm−1) for 241, corresponding to the Soret and Q-transitions, respectively (Fig. 78).6 Visible-light excitation in the 400–500 nm range generates fluorescence patterns that are virtual mirror images of the absorption, with maxima evolving at 520 nm (ΦF = 0.1 for 241).524
 Fig. 78  Comparative absorption spectra of SubPc 4 (pink line), and Pr6SubPz 241 (orange line), (H37C18S)6SubPz 248 (red line), Ph6SubPz 255 (green line) and (H7C3CHCH)6SubPz 268 (violet line). 
The removal of the three fused benzene rings from the SubPc skeleton produces a 75–80 nm blue shift of the Q-band and a slight lowering of the absorption coefficients for this band (Fig. 78). From a different viewpoint, when going from a porphyrazine to the similarly substituted SubPz, the π-conjugated system is reduced from 18 to 14 π electrons, inducing changes in the UV-vis spectra that are best exemplified by an approximately 100 nm hypsochromic shift of the Q-band.6,524–526 The corresponding MCD signal of the Q-band is a derivative-shaped Faraday A term with negative and positive envelopes at 498 and 489 nm, respectively, although the negative envelope shows a much greater intensity. Group theory suggests that SubPzs with C3V symmetry have degenerate LUMOs, and the observed spectrum is consistent with this prediction. With respect to the B-region, the corresponding MCD signal has a smaller intensity than that of the Q-band, supporting a smaller angular momentum change for the Soret band.511
The origins of the Q and Soret bands of hexaalkylSubPzs are basically similar to the SubPcs.1,214
The optical spectra of SubPzs peripherally substituted with functions containing π-electrons or heteroatoms with lone pairs display strong absorptions in a window that goes from 384 nm to 517 nm, in addition to their intense Soret and Q-bands.6,13,512,513,518 For thioether-substituted SubPzs, these features, which appear at 434 nm, have been attributed to n → π* transitions from nonbonding sulphur electrons to the macrocycle, by analogy with those exhibited by the porphyrazine tetramers.527 But in contrast with the tetramers, in SubPzs these bands are 15–25% higher than the Q-band (see Fig. 78), denoting a higher impact of peripheral thioether substituents on the electronic properties of SubPzs than in porphyrazines.6 SubPzs substituted with six peripheral aryl groups also display strong CT bands, which position and intensity correlate with the electronic character of the substituents at the aryl moieties, so that electron donors red-shift and increase CT bands, and electron acceptors blue-shift and decrease these absorptions (Fig. 80).9 In line with this, the CT band disappears when peripheral phenyl groups are perfluorinated.514
Peripheral sulfanyl and aryl substituents also shift the SubPz Q-bands to lower energies (for example, λmax = 556 and 539 for 253 and 255, respectively), which position can be modulated by further functionalizing the aryl moieties.6,512,518,524 In particular, substitution at the meta-positions with alkoxy or phenyl groups (SubPzs 262 and 265) produces little perturbation of SubPzs in the ground state, therefore, the absorption profiles are preserved. In a different way, para-substitution of aryl groups with electron-donors (SubPzs 256, 257, 258 and 263) or phenylene moieties (SubPzs 264 and 266) further red shifts the SubPz absorption bands up to 31 nm in 258 (70 nm with respect to 241),512,518 while electron acceptors produce weaker alterations (0–8 nm red shift in 259–261 with respect to 255) (Fig. 80).518 Conversely, a 11 nm blue shift of the SubPz Q-band is achieved upon decreasing the coplanar configuration of the aryl moieties with the SubPz core, through introduction of bulky alkyl rests (SubPz 267)512 (Fig. 80) or perfluorination (SubPz 249).514
The higher influence of peripheral substituents on the SubPz π-system compared to other related porphyrinoids such as Pcs, SubPcs, SubPs and porphyrazines has been ascribed to the combination of three structural features within SubPzs that do not meet in the other macrocycles, namely, their meso-aza bridges, their non-benzo-fused pyrrolic units and their electron deficient, boron(III) central atom.512 In fact, there seems to be a parallelism of these effects with those observed for phosphorus(V) octaarylporphyrazines.528
Compared to hexaarylated SubPzs, the push–pull SubPzs C3-250–252 exhibit a further red-shift of their Q bands, appearing at λmax = 633, 594 and 571 nm, respectively.517 As for the hexaarylated series, the extent of the red-shift correlates with the Hammett σp parameters of the substituents of the aryl groups. The absorption maxima of the corresponding CT bands are also red-shifted in the same order as observed for the Q band absorptions.517 Contrary to the corresponding push–pull porphyrazines for which a significant perturbation of the Q band absorption is observed going from a regioisomer to another,529C1-250 displays its Q band at 626 nm. However, significant changes featured by two broad absorptions at 499 and 435 nm are observed in the higher energy region (Fig. 79).
In the MCD spectra, a derivative-shaped Faraday A term with an inflection point close to the maximum of the Q band absorption is observed for C3-250–252, indicating degenerate excited states due to the C3 high molecular symmetry (Fig. 79). C1-250 shows the corresponding feature as a derivative-shaped pseudo Faraday A term. The latter can be observed if molecules with less than three-fold molecular symmetry accidentally possess nearly degenerate excited states.517
 Fig. 79  MCD spectra of SubPzs C3-250 (red line), 251 (green line), 252 (blue line), and C1-250 (orange line) in toluene. Reproduced with permission from ref. 517. 
 Fig. 80  Comparative absorption spectra of Ph6SubPz 255 (green line), (p-F3C–C6H4)6SubPz 260 (red line), (p-Me2N–C6H4)6SubPz 258 (light blue line), (3,5-(t-Bu)2–C6H3)6SubPz 267 (pink line) and (PhCHCH)6SubPz 269 (dark blue line). 
Peripheral hexavinylation of SubPzs produces extended π-systems owing to the coplanarity of vinylene branches with the macrocycle. This is reflected in the absorption properties of SubPz 268 by a Q-band absorption at 571 nm, similar to that a bare SubPc,1,512 in addition to a strong CT band at 431 nm denoting electronic delocalization over the vinylene moieties (Fig. 78). More importantly, in hexavinylated SubPzs the typical electronic tunability of SubPzs is preserved, and the vinylene moieties serve as linkers to electronically connect SubPzs with other π-systems or redox active chromophores. Thus, six phenyl rings connected through vinylene linkers further red shift the Q-band in SubPz 269 by 54 nm with respect to that of 268 (Fig. 80). The latter suggests a better electronic communication of the terminal phenyl rings with the macrocycle in 269 than that produced in the directly attached hexaphenyl-SubPz 255 (39 nm red shift).512
In general, the emission spectra of sulfanylated, arylated, vinylated and push–pull SubPzs mirror image the lowest energy absorptions and are bathochromically shifted with respect to those of the hexaalkylated series (see Table 10). Hexavinylation produces stronger impact (λem = 604 nm in 268) than hexaarylation (λem = 563 nm in 255). The extent of the bathochromic shift also varies with the functionalization of the aryl moieties. Thus, electron donor para-substituents produce stronger effects (36–202 nm in 256–258, 264 and 266) than meta-substituents (8–12 nm in 262, 265 and 267) and electron acceptor para-substituents (1–18 nm in 259–261).512,518 Besides, the same aryl substituents produce a higher impact when they are a part of a push–pull SubPz (for example, λem = 612 nm in 256 and λem = 662 nm in 250).512,517

3.2.4 Redox properties. 
SubPz 276 exhibits in CV two reversible redox processes, namely an oxidation at +0.83 V and a reduction at −1.72 V (Table 11). SubPzs are, therefore, more difficult to reduce and oxidize electrochemically than SubPcs. The effect of axial ligation has only a small impact on the redox properties of SubPzs. For instance, replacing the phenoxy group in 276 by a fluoride anion in 277 shifts the oxidation potential anodically by only 10 mV. As an exception, π-coordination of 276 to RuCp* to give 286 shifts the first reduction and oxidation peaks anodically by 200 mV and 140 mV, respectively.63
