Peripheral functionalization produces important changes in the electrochemistry of SubPzs. Firstly, all the hexasulfanylated, hexaarylated and hexavinylated SubPzs exhibit three or four reversible reduction peaks in addition to one or two oxidation processes that are irreversible in most of the cases. Moreover, all the hexasulfanylated, hexaarylated and hexavinylated SubPzs are by 160–870 mV easier to reduce, even when functionalized with electron donor functions, such as 258 (E1/2 = −1.56 V).512,518 Significantly, the latter potential value is very close to that exhibited by the RuCp* π-complex 286. As a general trend, the effect of para-substitution in the first reduction potentials of hexaaryl-SubPzs correlates with the Hammett constants (σp) corresponding to the substituents, so that the potentials are shifted cathodically in the order: NO2 < CF3 < CO2Me < H ≈ MeS < MeO < NMe2. The most positive reduction potential corresponds to the nitro-substituted SubPz 259, with E1/2 = −0.85 V for the first reduction potential.518 In addition, the effect of the substituents can be different depending on the position to which they are attached. Thus, methoxy substituents in SubPzs 256 and 262 produce opposite effects, with E1/2 being by 90 mV cathodically shifted in 256518 with respect to 255 and by 50 mV anodically shifted in 262.512
Likewise, the oxidation trend correlates quite well with the corresponding Hammett constants for each substituent at the para-position. Again, the position of the substituents determines opposite effects. For example, 256 is 170 mV easier to oxidize than 255, while 262 is 50 mV more difficult.512


3.2.5 Theoretical calculations. 
The effect of fusing benzene rings to the SubPz backbone has been studied by the DFT method and reviewed in terms of molecular structures, molecular orbitals, atomic charges, electronic absorption, CD spectra and dipolar/octupolar second-order NLO contribution ratio.1 In addition, the computed spectral features of alkyl and thioalkyl-substituted SubPzs by means of TDDFT calculations have been confronted with the available experimental data.214 In a parallel study, the structures of beryllium SubPc and SubPz complexes have been explored using the BPW91/6-31+G(d,p)//BPW91/6-31G(d) level of theory and the TD-DFT approach.225 Both Be complexes were found to be thermodynamically stable structures in the gas phase, although NBO and AIM analyses revealed a slightly different nature of bonding in Be complexes with respect to the boron SubPcs and SubPzs. Thus, the natural bond orbital (NBO) analysis did not find bonding orbitals between SubPc, Be, and axial chloride moieties, although lone pairs of N atoms and empty Be p orbitals strongly interact. Contrasting, in the boron complexes a proper bonding orbital between B and N, as well as a bonding orbital between B and the axial substituent, were found. The predicted UV-vis spectra resulted similar to those of the corresponding boron complexes. Thus, for example, the Q-band for SubPz 277, which had been predicted in vacuo at 430 nm,214 was found at 462 nm for the corresponding beryllium complex.225
The self-assembly of beryllium SubPc and the corresponding complexes with water and pyridine has been computed at the wB97XD/6-311+G(d,p) level of theory. BeSubPc units may form face-to-face or face-to-tail dimers connected through Be–N non-covalent intermolecular interactions.226 Besides, hydrated BeSubPcs in which water is ligated at the axial positions were predicted to arrange stable face-to-face Be-SubPc dimers that are brought together through hydrogen bonds. Given its simpler structure, the corresponding hydrated BeSubPz has been used as a model to study the formation of one to three-unit columnar aggregates.226 Similar to the CH⋯F bonds previously described for BSubPc arrays,265 in this case water is responsible for the hydrogen bond interactions (O–H⋯N, O–H⋯C) between monomers that result crucial to stabilize columnar, helicoidal aggregates.226
The structural, magnetic, and optical properties of the four subporphyrinoids, namely, SubP, SubPz, BzSubP and SubPcs have also been computed using a theoretical approach based on PCM-TD-DFT with the PBE0 exchange–correlation functional.215 Using this level of theory, the 0–0 energies for SubPzs were determined with an absolute deviation of 0.2 eV with respect to the experimental results, slightly smaller than those predicted before (0.4 eV).214 In addition, the NICS value of −20.7 calculated at the center of the SubPz ring indicated a strongly aromatic character, similar to that of SubP (−20.6). Alike their tetrapyrrolic analogues, the absolute NICS values are slightly larger in the smaller SubP and SubPz compounds than in their benzo-counterparts.215

3.3 Subporphyrazines with annulated rings
Other type of SubPzs bearing heterocycles such as pyrazine, thiophene, dibenzoquinoxaline, thiadiazole, selenadiazole, and 1,3-dithiole-2-one, directly fused at the β-positions of their pyrrole units, have been the object of recent reports. As we will see, fusing aromatic heterocycles to the SubPz core leads to planarization of the structure in a higher degree than fusing benzene rings in SubPcs.
Several pyrazine-fused SubPzs have been reported (Fig. 81).64,530–535 All of these Subpyrazinoporphyrazines have been prepared by cyclotrimerization reaction of the corresponding dinitrile derivatives, in the presence of BCl3, under classical conditions. The yields range from 0.3% for 289 to 35% for 291.530,531 In SubPz 290,530,535 the SubPc bowl depth, determined as the distance of the boron atom to the mean plane formed by external carbon atoms of pyrazine rings is of ∼2.47 Å, similar to that of a SubPc, although the SubPz core is slightly more planar than a regular SubPz (SubPz bowl depth of 1.513 Å). Very recently, the C1 and C3 positional isomers of tert-butyl substituted tripyrazinoSubPzs were first obtained and efficiently separated by column chromatography.536
 Fig. 81  Structures of some subpyrazinoporphyrazines. 
Subpyrazinoporphyrazines are isoelectronic with SubPcs, in the sense that they both contain 28 π-electrons delocalized over the SubPz macrocycle and the fused six membered rings. However, the lower delocalization of the π-system in the pyrazine series with respect to the benzo series is evidenced by their electronic spectra. Thus, SubPz 289 shows its Q-band at λmax = 530 nm, more than 30 nm blue shifted related to SubPc.530 Electrochemical and DFT calculations indicate that fusing the three pyrazine rings to the SubPz structure leads to stabilization of the frontier π-molecular orbitals, and enlarging the HOMO–LUMO gap by ∼0.2 eV related to SubPcs. This endows the subpyrazinoporphyrazine dye with enhanced electron deficiency. The latter is reflected in the cyclovoltammetric study of 290, which shows its first reduction potential at E1/2 = −1.248 V vs. Fc/Fc+, that is, 472 mV easier to reduce than SubPz 276.530 The introduction of six chlorine atoms at the pyrazine rings shift this value still more anodic at −0.168 V.534
Compounds 292, 293532 and 294, 295533 contain dibenzoquinoxaline and phenanthroquinoxaline moieties, respectively, fused to the SubPz pyrroles. As expected, SubPzs 292–295 display bathochromically shifted Q-bands with respect to SubpyrazinoPzs 289–291. But surprisingly, SubPz 295, containing three additional fused rings, displays its Q band at higher energy (λmax = 588 nm) than 292 (λmax = 592 nm). DFT calculations and electrochemical studies revealed further stabilization of the frontier molecular orbitals upon fusing benzene rings to the Subpyrazinoporphyrazine structure. Indeed, dibenzoquinoxaline and phenantroquinoxaline SubPzs are very strong electron acceptors, showing their first reduction potentials anodically shifted to −1.09 V in 292 and − 0.94 V in 295.532,533
SubPzs bearing annulated 1,2,5-thiadiazole-424,537 and 1,2,5-selenadiazole-rings538 (Fig. 82) have also been prepared by BCl3 assisted cyclotrimerization of the corresponding dicyano derivatives. The effect of fusing the thiadiazoles to the SubPz core produces a planarization of the tripyrrolic moiety that is reflected by an even shorter SubPz bowl depth of 1.363 Å.538
 Fig. 82  Structures of thiadiazole- and selenadiathole SubPzs 296 and 297. 
The effects in the frontier orbitals of 296 and 297 upon annulating the sulphur and selenium heterocycles have been predicted by DFT calculations. Thus, substitution of the three HCCH fragments in SubpyrazinoPz 289 by sulphur atoms to give 296, leads to additional stabilization of both frontier orbitals, with net decrease of the HOMO–LUMO gap.538 But when the HCCH fragments are replaced by the less electronegative Se atom, the HOMO and the LUMO orbitals destabilize, the latter in lesser extent, leading to a higher decrease of the HOMO–LUMO gap, with respect to 289. Hence, the Q-band of 296 is shifted to lower energies (λmax = 538 nm), with respect to 289,537 while selenadiazole rings produce a higher displacement of both Soret and Q-bands appearing at 342 and 574 nm, respectively for 297 (Fig. 83).538 Compounds 289, 296 and 297 display emission bands at λem = 542 nm (ϕF = 0.15), 564 nm (ϕF = 0.11) and 608 nm (ϕF = 0.03), respectively and generate singlet oxygen with high quantum yields of ϕΔ = 0.7–1).538
 Fig. 83  Comparison between optical spectra of H12SubPc-Cl 20, 289, 296 and 297. Reproduced with permission from ref. 538. Copyright 2019 Elsevier Ltd. 
Dichlorophenylboron(III), with lower Lewis acidity, has also been used in the synthesis of unsymmetric thiadiazole fused SubPzs through crossover cyclotrimetrization of 1,2,5-thiadiazole-3,4-dicarbanitrile with 3,4,5,6-tetrafluorophthalonitrile (Scheme 24).539 The latter affords compounds 298 and 299 bearing a phenyl group as the axial substituent. The sequential replacement of the benzene rings in a SubPc structure by these nitrogenated heterocycles reduces the degree of delocalization progressively, with the consequent blue shift of the Q-bands. In addition, the reduced symmetry of SubPzs 298 and 299 gives rise to absorption spectra with split Q-bands, exhibiting Qx and Qy components lying at 579 and 554 nm for the former, and 569 and 541 nm for the latter, respectively.
 Scheme 24  Synthesis of low-symmetry thiadiazoleSubPzs. 
Related crossover cyclotrimerization reactions have been performed using 1,2,5-selenadiazole-3,4-dicarbonitrile and phthalonitrile derivatives, namely, unsubstituted, tetrachloro- and tetrafluorophthalonitrile, in the presence of BCl3, leading to the corresponding low-symmetry selenadiazole-SubPzs.540
The use of 3,4-dicyanothiophene in similar crossover cyclotrimerization reactions afforded a mixture of the corresponding SubPc and the two thieno-fused SubPzs 300 and 301, which were obtained after axial substitution (Fig. 84). Here, the chlorine atoms arise from chlorination under the reaction conditions.541 Other attempts to obtain non-chlorinated compounds were unsuccessful or afforded unstable compounds.542
 Fig. 84  Structures of thieno-SubPzs 300 and 301. 
The effect of only one thiophene moiety slightly reduces the curvature of 300, which shows a SubPz bowl depth of 1.546 Å.541 On the other hand, removing the nitrogen atoms on going from the thiadiazole-SubPzs 298 and 299 to the thieno-SubPzs 300 and 301 enhances the electronic delocalization over the whole structure, an effect that is evidenced by the bathochromic shift of the Q-bands (Fig. 85), which Qx components now appear at λmax = 605 nm for 300 (26 nm red shift upon removal of two nitrogen atoms) and 636 nm for 301 (67 nm red shift upon removal of four nitrogen atoms).541
 Fig. 85  Comparative absorption and emission spectra of SubPc-(t-BuC6H4–O) (pink line), SubPz 300 (purple line) and SubPz 301 (blue line). Reproduced with permission from ref. 541. Copyright 2017 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Tri-(benzo[b]thiopheno)subporphyrazines 302 (Fig. 86) have been prepared by cyclotrimerization reaction of the benzo[b]thiopheno)-2,3-dicarbonitrile in the presence of BCl3 followed by axial substitution.543 Chiral HPLC rendered two pairs of optically resolved regioisomers, namely R/S-C3 and R/S-C1 in a 1:2 ratio, close to the statistical distribution. In addition, both regioisomers presented a higher ratio of the S-enantiomer. Upon fusing the benzo[b]thiophene moiety to the SubPz core, the sulphur atoms are directly attached to the β-pyrrole carbons, similar to SubPzs 246 and 253 (Table 10) and different to compounds 300 and 301, in which the sulphur atoms are separated by carbon atoms. Accordingly, the UV-vis spectra of both regioisomers of 302 show features that remind the electronic spectra of the hexasulfanyl series. Thus, C3-302 shows its Q-band at 558 nm, in addition to a band at 394 nm that could be attributed to a n → π* transition from the sulphur lone pair to the macrocycle, in a parallelism with porphyrazines and SubPzs. As for C1-250, C1-302 shows a splitting of the absorption bands arising from the reduced symmetry.
 Fig. 86  Structures of tri-(benzo[b]thiopheno)subporphyrazines 302. 
1,3-Dithiole-2-one constitutes another type of heterocycle that has been directly annulated to the SubPz, by performing a BCl3 templated cyclotrimerization reaction of the corresponding 2-oxo-1,3-dithiole-4,5-dicarbonitrile.544 The latter afforded SubPz 303, which was subsequently substituted at the axial position to give 304 and 305. The 1,3-dithiole-2-one moiety is not a conjugated ring and therefore, it does not produce the typical SubPz planarization stimulated by aromatic rings like pyrazine, thiophene, thiadiazole or even benzene, discussed before in Section 3.2.1. In fact, the SubPz bowl depth of 305, with a value of 1.716 Å, falls within the range of a regular SubPz (see Table 9).
Interestingly, the electronic spectrum of 304 shows the typical envelope of a hexasulfanyl-SubPz, but somehow attenuated, displaying three absorption bands at 291, 393 and 530 nm, corresponding to B, n → π* and Q-transitions, respectively. The 50 and 30 nm blue shift observed for the CT and Q-bands, respectively, related to other hexasulfanyl-SubPzs, could be rationalized in terms of the diminished donor ability of the sulphur atoms towards the SubPz macrocycle, owing to the electron accepting nature of their neighbouring carbonyl group.
Unsymmetric SubPzs 306–308 (Fig. 87) have been prepared by crossover cyclotrimerization of tetrafluorophthalonitrile and oxo-1,3-dithiole-4,5-dicarbonitrile, followed by axial substitution under conventional reaction conditions.545
 Fig. 87  Structures of dithiole-2-one fused SubPzs 303–308. 
3.4 Subporphyrazine analogues
Triazasubporphyrinoids in which one or more of the pyrrole rings is replaced by other type of heterocycles, or contain other modifications in the triazaporphyrin core, will be referred to as SubPz analogues and discussed in this section. Macrocycles that preserve the SubPc isoindolic core and contain additional rings annulated to the SubPc benzene moieties will be discussed in Sections 4 and 5.
Cross-conjugated bis(1,3-dithiol-2-ylidene)-substituted subtriazachlorins 309 and 310 were serendipitously discovered when treating 308 with triethylphosphite. Whereas the goal was carrying out a coupling reaction to furnish a SubPz with a TTF moiety directly attached to the periphery (Scheme 25), the crystal structure showed that 309 displays double-bond attributes for the bonds attaching the 1,3-dithiol-2-ylidene to the β-pyrrole, and single bond nature between the β-pyrrolic carbon atoms, indicating the Subchlorin nature of this SubPz derivative,513 as well as a small contribution of the cross-conjugation to the system.545 The TTFV moiety exhibited up and down orientations of the 1,3-dithiole rings, with a small torsion angle of 45° and a close contact between the sulphur atoms, which are substantially congested. The rest of the structure retains a SubPc-like curved geometry, with a decreased SubPz bowl depth of 1.444 Å.
 Scheme 25  Synthesis of TTFV-SubPzs 309 and 310. 
The UV-vis absorption spectra of 309 and 310 reflects the presence of a deeply disturbed π-system, exhibiting a significantly split Q-band absorption at 622 and 832 nm, associated to a coupled Faraday B term in the MCD spectrum (Fig. 88). The tetrathiafulvalene vinylogue (TTFV) exhibits better electron-donating properties than TTF and hence, produces sizeable effects in the oxidation window of 309 and 310, which show a first reversible oxidation process at 0.15 V vs. Fc+/Fc. The latter can be ascribed to a TTFV centered oxidation, while a first reduction wave at −1.10 was assigned to a macrocycle-centred process.
 Fig. 88  UV-vis-NIR absorption (bottom) and MCD (top) spectra of 309. Reproduced with permission from ref. 545. Copyright 2019 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Although containing a 14π-electron circuit, compounds 309 and 310 can be considered as SubPz analogues, because one of the pyrrole rings of the SubPz skeleton has been replaced by a 3,4-dihydropyrrole. Compounds 311 fall into the same category, and have been previously reviewed,1 as it was Subazaphenalenephthalocyanine 312 (Fig. 89), in which one of the pyrrole rings have been replaced by a benzoisoquinoline. Density functional theory (DFT) and time dependent DFT have been recently used to predict the effects of substitution with electron donating and electron withdrawing groups on the physical and optical properties of 312.546 Remarkably, Subtriazachlorin 311 constitutes the only reported stable SubPz derivative prepared using a fumaronitrile as the starting material. In Subbiliazine 313 (Fig. 89), a boron atom is used to contract a bis(pyrazolylimino)isoindoline, to form a hydrogen bond as a meso-linkage. These compounds have a bowl shape structure similar to that of SubPzs and SubPcs.547
 Fig. 89  Structures of SubPz analogues 313–313. 
More recently, one of the pyrrole rings has been replaced by azepine (314, 315 and 316)548,549 and carbazole rings (317–320).550314 and 315 contain 14 π-electron aromatic cores, which have been assembled in crossover cyclotrimerization reactions of tetrafluorophthalonitrile with 2,2′-biphenyldicarbonitrile and 4,5-phenanthrenedicarbonitrile, respectively, in the presence of BCl3 at 180 °C in 1-chloronaphthalene (Scheme 26).548 Under these conditions, an unprecedented replacement of the axial chloride by fluoride took place, presumably sourced from tetrafluorophthalonitrile.
 Scheme 26  Synthesis of azepine SubPzs 314 and 315. 
Compound 314 exhibits the typical SubPz conical structure. The biphenyl moiety is tilted by 43° from the mean plane of the three Np atoms. P and M enantiomers of 314, based on the helicity of the dibenzoazepine unit, stack alternately to form one-dimensional columnar packing assembled by π–π stacking of the macrocycles, which is enhanced by the small fluorine atom at the axial position.159 The crystal structure of 314 revealed a considerable planarization of the concave structure, characterized by a SubPz bowl depth of 1.248 Å (we have considered as bowl depth the distance between the boron atom and the mean plane containing the pyrrole β-carbons and the azepine 3 and 6 carbons). The shallower coordination geometry of the boron may cause changes in its Lewis acidity resulting in the preferential formation of the axially fluoro-substituted compounds. The absorption spectra of 314 and 315 is dominated by split Q-bands (denoted as Q1- and Q2-bands, respectively) appearing at 682 and 510 nm for 314 and 693 and 513 nm for 315, and attributed to the distorted molecular structure arising from the biphenyl moiety. The corresponding Faraday B terms in the MCD spectra, indicate nondegenerate excited states. Azepine SubPzs also exhibited fluorescence at 710 and 706 nm with fluorescence quantum yields of 0.08 and 0.006, respectively.
Another reported azepineSubPz 316 contains a chiral binaphthyl fragment fused to the azepine ring, which provides inherent and induced chirality to the macrocycle.549 The synthesis of each R or S enantiomer of 316 has been performed separately, starting from the corresponding enantiopure R or S [1,1′-binaphthalene]-2,2′-dicarbonitrile, respectively, as indicated in Scheme 27. Its absorption spectrum (Fig. 90) showed their Q1- and Q2-bands at 692 and 528 nm, respectively.
 Scheme 27  Synthesis of 316 containing a dinaphthoazepine moiety. 
 Fig. 90  MCD spectrum (top line), CD spectra (middle line) and absorption spectrum (bottom line) of compound 316. Reproduced with permission from ref. 549. 
In the MCD spectrum of 316 (Fig. 90), two Faraday-B terms were observed in the Q-band region, a negative at 690, and a positive 529 nm, indicating their homogeneous π–π* transition nature, with corresponding transitions localized on the main conjugated chromophore. In the Soret region, other two Faraday-B terms, a positive at 443 nm and a negative at 343, were detected as well. The CD spectra of (R) and (S)-316 (Fig. 90) are perfect mirror images, confirming their enantiomeric nature. Both enantiomers of 316 yielded two negative CD signals at 698 and 530 nm corresponding to the Q-band. The ratio of the CD intensity for the Q2-band was calculated to be very close (∼0.8) to that obtained for the Q1-band, confirming that both Q-bands follow the mechanism of the induced optical activity from peripheral binaphthyl group.548 On the other hand, the ratio of the CD signal at 348 nm corresponding to the Soret band is only 0.07 of that obtained for the Q1-band, indicating the inherent chirality characteristics for this Soret band.548
Carbazosubporphyrazines 321–324 (Scheme 28) contain a 16 π-electron, antiaromatic core,550 which has been assembled in two steps. The first step consists in a condensation reaction in ethanol of a diiminoisoindoline derivative and a carbazole-1,8-diamine, in a 2:1 ratio, to afford a three-unit intermediate 317–320. This type of three-unit compounds are common synthetic intermediates for the synthesis of mixed hemiporphyrazines,551 three-quarter phthalocyanines552 and porphyrazines,553 or some or their expanded analogues.554 The formation of this intermediate is followed by a boron template, ring closure to afford the SubPz analogues 321–324.
 Scheme 28  Synthesis of the antiaromatic carbazoSubPzs 321–324. 
Analysis of the crystal structure of 322 revealed a planar π skeleton, with a SubPz bowl depth of 0.473 Å. In addition, the C–N distances along the inner core do not indicate significant bond-length alternation. Evidence for the antiaromaticity of the compounds came from NMR data, absorption spectra and theoretical calculations. Specifically, the carbazoSubPz bearing a methoxy group at the axial position exhibited the corresponding resonance at δ = 3.73 nm. Significantly, the 11B-NMR spectra of 321–324 showed signals at δ = 9.10–9.23 ppm, downfield shifted related to those of fluoro-SubPzs (δ = −15.0 ppm),1 while the 19F-NMR signals were detected at δ = −91.0 to −93.7 ppm, more deshielded than the corresponding resonance of SubPzs (δ = −157.45 ppm).159 All these data indicate the presence of a paratropic ring current, with an estimated NICS(0) value of 6.35. In agreement with the NMR data, the absorption spectra (Fig. 91) showed very weak absorption envelopes over 600–900 nm, accompanying intense peaks with maxima at ∼416 nm.
 Fig. 91  Absorption spectrum of 324. Adapted with permission from ref. 550. Copyright 2019 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
A different modification of the SubPz core consists in the replacement of one or more of the meso-nitrogens by another atom. Subtribenzodiazaporphyrin 326 has been prepared by a cross-condensation reaction of the aminoisoindolene dimeric intermediate 325 with phthalonitrile in the presence of BCl3, followed by axial substitution under conventional conditions. The hybrid 326 was obtained in 15% yield, together with the corresponding symmetric SubPc and the aza-BODIPY compound 327 (Scheme 29).555 Alternatively, the cyclotrimerization reaction can be done in the presence of different boronate esters B(OR)3, in diglyme at 200 °C, affording 326 as the sole product, bearing an axial substituent derived from the type of used boronate. Although the yield was lower by the latter procedure (10%), the workup is simpler, and the procedure has the indisputable advantage of being versatile.
 Scheme 29  Synthesis of subtribenzodiazaporphyrin 326. 
The structure of 326 is considerably more planar than that of the corresponding SubPc, as evidenced by single crystal X-ray diffraction. The SubPc and SubPz bowl depths of 326 (R = Ph) are of 2.185 Å and 1.428 Å, respectively, in the range of that of the SubPc borenium cation.1 The crystal structure also shows the meso-phenyl ring lying orthogonal to the macrocyclic plane. Its broad 1H-NMR signals indicate slow rotation of this group at room temperature. Lowering the temperature leads to sharpening and evidences the magnetic non-equivalence of the concave and convex macrocycle faces.
The optical spectra of 326 are represented in Fig. 92. At first glance, the changes are not as drastic as it could be expected. Both absorbance and fluorescence maxima are blue-shifted by 10–20 nm related to of SubPc, while the Q/B-band ratio decreases, reflecting a tendency towards a porphyrin character. Moreover, the reduced symmetry is manifested through splitting of the absorption bands.
 Fig. 92  Absorption and emission spectra of SubPc (blue line) and 326 (R = Ph) red line. Reproduced with permission from ref. 555. Copyright 2019 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
4. Subnaphthalocyanines
4.1 Synthesis and reactivity of subnaphthalocyanines
Subnaphthalocyanines (SubNcs) are curved, aromatic macrocycles, consisting of three benzoisoindole subunits connected through their 1,3-positions by aza-bridges. The formal addition of three fused benzene rings to a SubPc structure can result in two types of SubNcs, namely, 2,3-SubNcs and 1,2-SubNcs (Fig. 93). The latter exists as two C3 and C1 regioisomers, each one consisting in a pair of enantiomers.
 Fig. 93  Structures of 2,3-subnapthalocyanines and 1,2-subnaphthalocyanines. 
The first example of subnaphthalocyanine is a 2,3-isomer and dates back to 1995.556 Hanack described the cyclotrimerization reaction of naphthalene-2,3-dicarbonitrile, as well as that of its tert-substituted derivative, in the presence of dichloro(phenyl)borane in boiling naphthalene, affording analytical amounts of SubNcs 328 and 330 (Table 12). Both compounds were reported to show absorption bands at 650 and 654 nm, respectively, matching the absorption profile that is currently well-known for these derivatives. The number of publications on SubNcs is roughly one-tenth of that of SubPcs. Therefore, compared to the list of available SubPcs, the number of SubNcs that has been reported is quite limited, although in the last ten years there is an increasing interest in the study of these extended-conjugated analogues for their application in photovoltaics.

The most usual method to prepare 2,3-SubNcs consists in the cyclotrimerization reaction of the corresponding naphthalene-dicarbonitrile derivative in different aromatic solvents (Scheme 30 and Table 12), in the presence of a boron template, which is usually BCl3. The reported yields range from ca. 1% to 80%, depending on the substitution pattern and the reaction conditions. For unsubstituted SubNc 224, multichlorination processes of the aromatic rings occur, usually at the naphthalene bay positions (C4, C9, C16 and their symmetric sites), giving rise to a mixed alloy mixture of SubNc derivatives with different degrees of chlorination (Cl–ClnSubNc), which complete separation is not feasible. Bender has dedicated much work to search for reaction conditions that could inhibit the chlorination processes, without much success.418 In fact, a total occupancy of 0.99–1.13 peripheral chlorines per SubNc molecule,418,444 with an average molecular formula of C36H16.87BCl2.13N6, was determined for 224.418 In general, the use of an electron-poor solvent (nitrobenzene) for the synthesis of Cl–ClnSubNc resulted in the highest degree of bay position chlorination. More recently, bay position bromination has also been detected when attempting to increase the yield of 329 by using a modified version of the Kennedy method, namely, o-dichlorobenzene as reaction solvent.557 Under optimized conditions, 329 was obtained in 32% yield as a Br–BrnSubNc alloy. It is worth noting that it is possible to avoid bay position bromination by employing low temperature synthetic methods, although only analytical amounts of the macrocycle are produced.
 Scheme 30  Synthesis of 2,3-SubNcs. 
Due to the cyclotrimerization conditions, most of SubNcs have been prepared axially substituted with chloride. As for SubPcs, axial substitution by aryloxy groups has been done by treatment with phenol,444,449 pentafluorophenol,444p-tert-butylphenol562 and p-nitrophenol114 in toluene either directly,114,444,449 or by intermediation of the corresponding axial triflate derivative, which is obtained by treatment with silver trifluoromethanesulfonate.562 Moreover, substitution by fluoride is carried out by treatment with AgBF4 in toluene.449,561,563–565
1,2-Subnaphthalocyanines were prepared for the first time by Kobayashi and Nyokong by cyclotrimerization reaction of 1,2-naphthalenedicarbonitrile under standard conditions, giving rise to a 1:3–1:6 mixture of the C3 and C1 regioisomers of 342, which were identify by X-ray diffraction analysis. The four stereoisomers (Scheme 31) could be separated by column chromatography, followed by chiral HPLC.36 A negative CD sign in the Q-band region was found to correspond to a clockwise arrangement of the naphthalene moieties, whereas a positive signal was attributed to an anticlockwise organization. The smaller rotational strengths of the enantiomers of C1-342 compared to those of C3-342 was ascribed by the authors to the reduction of transition magnetic dipole moments of C1-342 due to the presence of an oppositely arranged naphthalene moiety.
The examples of peripheral functionalization of SubNcs by reactions on preformed macrocycles are also very limited. Sonogashira coupling of alkynes with iodo565 or bromo-substituted563 SubNcs has been carried out (Scheme 32).
 Scheme 31  Synthesis of 1,2-SubNcs. 
 Scheme 32  Hexaalkynyl SubNcs prepared by Sonogashira coupling. 
4.2 Characterization of subnaphthalocyanines

4.2.1 Single-crystal X-ray diffraction. 
The crystal structure of 2,3-SubNcs exhibit the typical cone shape of the SubPc analogues. The depth of the cavity can be characterized by the SubNc bowl-depth, defined by the distance from the mean plane of peripheral six Cf carbons (see Fig. 94) to the center boron atom. The bowl-depth of 224 is of 3.250 Å, deeper than that of SubPcs, owing to the three additional benzene rings that enlarge the π-surface (Table 13). However, the curvature is slightly less pronounced than that of SubPcs, and even less pronounced than that of SubPzs. This is in line with that observed upon annulating peripheral rings to the SubPz structure. Specifically, the average distance between the boron atom and the plane defined by the 3 Np atoms (P1) decreases in the order SubPz (0.62 Å) > SubPc (0.61 Å) > SubNc (0.60 Å). The drop is more evident when compared the SubPz bowl-depth (P2), which decreases in the order SubPz (1.70 Å) > SubPc (1.60 Å) > SubNc (1.50 Å). A similar effect is observed for SubPc bowl-depth (P3): SubPc (2.55 Å) > SubNc (2.40 Å).
 Fig. 94  Atom labelling of 224 and chemical structure of SubNc 359.

The crystal structure of 1,2-SubNc C3-342 exhibits P1, P2 and P3 that are comparable with those of its 2,3-isomers, reflecting a slight planarization of the structure with respect to SubPcs. In contrast, the degree of curvature of C1-342 is slightly higher than the average observed for SubPcs (see Section 2.3.1).


4.2.2 Optical properties. 
When benzene rings are fused to the SubPc structure, there is a narrowing of the HOMO–LUMO gap due to destabilization of the 1a2 HOMO of SubNc, relative to the 1e LUMO and 1a1 MO.566 The effect is smaller in magnitude than that observed on going from SubPz to SubPc, since in SubNc the additional benzene rings lie further from the inner perimeter where the largest MO coefficients are anticipated for the HOMO and LUMO orbitals.566 The effect on the 1a2 MO is originated from an antibonding interaction at the point of attachment of the C4H4 bridges that are added upon annulation of benzene rings. The latter accounts for the absorption features of SubNcs, which show Q-bands at lower energies than those of SubPcs (Fig. 95).
 Fig. 95  Comparison between UV-vis spectra of (p-NO2–C6H4)-a SubPc (red line) and SubNc 361 (blue line). Adapted with permission from ref. 114. Copyright 2018 Elsevier Ltd. 
Peripherally unsubstituted SubNcs 224, 359 and 360 display very similar UV-vis spectra, composed for 224 of Soret bands at λmax = 293 and 328 (ε = 47200 and 45000 l mol−1 cm−1, respectively) and a Q-transition at 656 nm (ε = 44700 l mol−1 cm−1), with a shoulder at 594 nm (Fig. 96).184 Additional weaker bands in the 350–500 nm region constitute a significant difference from peripherally unsubstituted SubPcs. The MCD spectrum of 224 (Fig. 96) is dominated by a derivative shaped x/y-polarized A1 term.184 Moreover, peripherally unsubstituted SubNcs fluoresce upon excitation at their Q-band with small Stokes shifts of 30 eV.418
 Fig. 96  UV-vis absorption spectrum (top) and MCD spectrum (bottom) of SubNc 224. Reproduced with permission from ref. 184. Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. 
As for SubPcs, axial substitution produces little variation in the optical properties of SubNcs (see Table 14 for comparison). As a general trend, replacement of axial chloride by axial fluoride slightly blue shifts both the Q-band and the λem by 4–6 nm. A similar effect is observed upon substitution by phenoxy or tert-butylphenoxy groups. Other axial aryloxy groups with electron-deficient character, like pentafluorophenoxy or p-nitrophenoxy, do not produce such blue shifts.

Unlike SubPcs, for which the introduction of electron donating or withdrawing groups always produce bathochromic shifts of the Q-bands, the effect of peripheral substitution in SubNcs can produce either red or blue shifts, depending on the nature of the substituent (Table 14). Given the limited catalogue of peripherally substituted SubNcs, building a general trend is not easy, but the available data seem to indicate that substituents functioning essentially as electron acceptors, such as halogens (except fluorine), imides, sulfones, or triple bonds, produce small bathochromic shifts of the SubNc Q-bands. Fluorine, with strong π-donor ability, produce small hypsochromic shifts. Thus, for example, dodecafluorination (SubNcs 333 and 363) produces a 10 nm hypsochromic shift of the SubNc Q-band (contrasting with the 10 nm bathochromic shift produced in SubPcs) and 0–5 nm hypsochromic shift of the SubNc λem. However, dodecachlorination (SubNcs 334 and 362) does not have any noticeable effect on the Q band of SubNc, while it shifts λem 8 nm bathochromically.562 Hexafluorination (SubNc 332) shifts 11 nm both Q and emission bands to higher energies, while the other halogens (SubNcs 336, 338 and 339) do it to lower energies, this effect increasing in the order Cl < Br < I (Fig. 97). Substitution with twelve sulfones produces 21 nm and 36 nm red shift of Q and emission bands, respectively, in SubNc 335, while the effect upon imidation is of 6 and 1 nm, respectively, and 19 nm upon ethynylation (SubNc 353).
 Fig. 97  Comparative absorption spectra of hexahalogenated SubNcs: 332 (orange line), 338 (blue line), 336 (green line) and 339 (red line). Reproduced with permission from ref. 563. Copyright 2018 Elsevier Ltd. 
Compared to 2,3-SubNcs, 1,2 SubNcs 342 (Fig. 93, Scheme 31) exhibit much smaller red-shifts of 12 nm, related to the corresponding SubPc,36,85 although similar to their 2,3-isomers, they show weak bands in the 350–500 window, at around 390 and 340 nm.


4.2.3 Redox properties. 
SubNc 224 exhibits in DPV three main redox processes, namely two oxidations at +0.26 V and +0.73 V and a reduction at −1.35 V (Table 15). They are, therefore, easier to oxidize and more difficult to reduce electrochemically than the corresponding SubPc. Compared to peripheral substitution, axial ligation has a small effect on the redox properties of SubNcs. For instance, replacing the chloride group in 224 by phenoxy in 359, shifts exclusively the reduction potential cathodic by 180 mV, while substitution with the electron-deficient pentafluorophenoxy moiety makes the reduction of 360 slightly easier than in 359 (50 mV) and the oxidation somewhat more difficult (40 mV). A similar trend is observed upon axial substitution of dodecahalogenated SubNcs with a tert-butylphenoxy group, resulting in moderate cathodic shifts of 110 and 130 mV for the first reduction potential of 362 and 363, related to the corresponding chloro-substituted SubNcs 334 and 333, respectively.

Peripheral functionalization produces more important changes. Dodecachlorination and dodecafluorination makes the oxidation of 334 and 333 by 380 and 360 mV more difficult, respectively, while the reduction is facilitated by 200 and 140 mV, respectively. The highest effect is observed upon dodeca-substitution with sulfones in 335, with the first reduction process 240 mV anodically shifted and no oxidation process detected. Finally, the electron-deficient character of imides serves to make the first reduction process of 340 and 341 by 110–150 mV easier.

5. Subphthalocyanines with annulated rings
There are a few examples of SubPcs containing heterocycles or carbocycles other than benzene, directly fused to the isoindole rings. SubPcs linearly (compounds 368 and 369Fig. 98) or angularly (compounds 370 and 371Fig. 98) annulated with thiazole rings have been prepared by Fülöpova in 19–33% yields under standard cyclotrimerization conditions from the corresponding dinitrile precursors.567 Both types of macrocycles have been obtained as mixtures of the C1 and C3 regioisomers in a nearly statistical 1:3 ratio, except 369, for which a 1:5 ratio was obtained. The resolution of all the C3 enantiomers of 368–371 was accomplished by chiral HPLC, while only the angular C1-370 and 371 could be resolved.
 Fig. 98  Structures of thiazole-SubPcs 368–371. 
All thiazole-SubPcs show red shifted absorption Q-bands indicating extension of the conjugation through the fused heterocycle, and all of them fluoresce when excited at their Q-bands, with small Stokes shifts of 36 meV, and negligible differences between the C1 and C3 isomers. Judging from their higher red shifts, linear compounds (λQ = 603 and 615 nm for C3-368 and C3-369, respectively) seem to have a somewhat more efficient extended-delocalization than their angular isomers (λQ = 589 and 593 nm for C3-370 and C3-371, respectively), although all of them in lower degrees than SubNcs (Fig. 99). Besides, all thiazole-SubPcs show very high singlet oxygen quantum yields. The configuration of the enantiomers has been assigned based in their CD spectra, which show perfect mirror images over the entire absorption window for each pair of enantiomers (Fig. 99).
 Fig. 99  (a) Absorption spectra of the C3 regioisomers of thiazole-SubPc 368–371. (b) Experimental and simulated CD spectra for the enantiomers of C3-368. Adapted with permission from ref. 567. Copyright 2016 Elsevier Ltd. 
Angular thiazole-SubPcs 370 and 371 show their electrochemical oxidation process very similar to that of SubPc-Cl 20, ranging from 0.73 to 0.83 V. Linear compounds 368 and 369 are easier to oxidize, with values of 0.50–0.60 V for the first oxidation potential. On the other hand, all linear and angular thiazole-SubPcs are more difficult to reduce, displaying values for their first reduction process of 1.15–1.43 V.
1,3-Dithiole-2-one,544 imide,448 TTF544 and trithiole568 moieties have also been annulated to the SubPc isoindole rings with 14%, 46–55% and 0.7% and 17% yields, respectively, by cyclotrimerization reactions of the corresponding dinitrile derivatives. Annulating imides to the SubPc isoindole rings produces stronger alteration of the optical properties than fusing the same heterocycles to the SubNc skeleton. Thus, for example, the Q-band and emission bands are 26 and 33 nm bathochromically shifted, respectively, upon imidation to SubPc (SubPc 375, Fig. 100), while the effect on SubNc is only of 4 and 10 nm, respectively (SubNc 234, Table 14).
 Fig. 100  SubPc triimides 372–376. 
Likewise, annulating 1,3-dithiole-2-one moieties to the SubPz backbone produces stronger impact (30 nm bathochromic shift of the Q-band and fluorescence quenching in SubPz 304, Fig. 87) than fusing the same moieties to SubPc isoindoles (23 and 25 nm red shift of λQ and λem in 378, respectively, Scheme 33).
 Scheme 33  Synthesis of 1,3-dithiole-2-one and TTF annulated SubPcs. 
As an alternative to cyclotrimerization, Furuta and Shimizu have fused TTF moieties to SubPcs (SubPc 380) in 7% overall yield by condensation reaction of tris-(1,3-dithiole-2-one)-SubPc 377 with 4,5-dithiohexyl-1,3-dithiole-2-thione in the presence of P(OEt)3 (Scheme 33).544
The same TTF- and trithiole functionalized phthalonitriles have served to prepare unsymmetric and push–pull SubPcs, respectively, by crossover cyclotrimerization reactions with tetrafluorophthalonitrile.568,569
A series of SubPcs bearing an annulated fluorenone subunit have been prepared in 3–41% by crossover cyclotrimerization reaction of ethyl 2′,3′-dicyano-[1,1′-biphenyl]-2-carboxylate with different phthalonitriles (Scheme 34).570 These compounds show a SubPc bowl-depth of 2.56 Å, similar to that of SubPc. The P and M enantiomers of SubPc 382 were resolved by chiral HPLC. Table 16 summarizes optical and redox properties of SubPcs containing annulated rings.
 Scheme 34  Synthesis of fluorenone–SubPcs 381–386.

6. Subporphyrins
6.1 Synthesis of subporphyrins
Discovered in 2007 by Osuka2 and Kobayashi in two independent reports,3,571 Subporphyrins (SubPs) constitute the youngest members of the Subporphyrinoid family. SubPs have shown extraordinary structural versatility, which is clearly indicated by the prolific number of reports on SubPs in a relatively short period of time, much higher than those on SubPzs and SubNcs, reported one, two and twelve years before, respectively. A large part of their structural versatility arises from the presence of meso bridges that are an additional functionalization site. Smaller Cpyrr–Cmeso–Cpyrr angles of the trimeric SubPs (ca. 117°) with respect to porphyrins (ca. 127°) provides larger explementary angles and consequently, larger distance between the meso substituents and the adjacent pyrrole rings. As a result, sterically non-congested meso-aryl SubP substituents can rotate freely, allowing their strong electronic coupling to the SubP core and causing large substituent effect.2 The synthesis and properties of SubPs published during the first six years after their discovery have been reviewed by some of us and others in previous works.1,4,5,572 Here, a strong focus is given on the synthesis of SubP. Besides, the present review intends to offer a general picture of the chemistry and properties of SubPs, in particular, the effects of substitution in the structure and properties of these macrocycles. Therefore, we will emphasize new results that have appeared in the last eight years, but will also include some relevant aspects already discussed in the previous reviews.
The photophysical and photochemical properties, as well as some applications of SubPs, have been studied in detail by Kim and Osuka,573–576 among others.45,577–579
Tribenzosubporphyrins (BzSubP) axially substituted by a hydroxy group are prepared by cyclotrimerization of different isoindolinone precursors a–c (Scheme 35).7 Unsubstituted BzSubP 387, which constituted the first example of Subporphyrin, was reported by Osuka and co-workers in ∼1.4% yield through a modified Gouterman's protocol,580 consisting of treatment of the isoindolinone a with a boron template species, such as boric acid, or phenylboronic acid, or 4-methoxyphenylboronic acid, under an inert atmosphere, at 350 °C or under microwave irradiation (Scheme 35). The meso-phenyl-substituted derivatives such as 388 were reported soon after by Kobayashi, Luk’yanets and co-workers in ∼8% yields by boron templated cyclotrimerization of the isoindolinone b, or by condensation of phthalimide c with phenylacetic581 or 2,6-dichlorophenylacetic acids.582
 Scheme 35  The synthesis of BzSubPs 387 and 388. 
The axial hydroxy groups can be replaced with alkoxy, carbonyloxy or silyloxy moieties, upon treatment with the corresponding alcohol,581,583 carboxylic acid,7,583 or triphenylsilyl chloride,581 respectively.
Contrary to the methods to prepare all triaza-subporphyrinoids (i.e. SubPcs, SubPzs, SubNcs and most of their analogues) and BzSubPs, which are all based on boron template cyclizations of three monomeric units, namely, dicyano- and isoindolinone-derivatives, the synthetic procedures for the assembly of the SubP backbone consist in the ring closure of different trimeric intermediates d–f (Scheme 36).
 Scheme 36  Synthetic procedures for the preparation of SubPs. 
Symmetric SubPs with C3 symmetry, bearing identical pyrrole and meso-substituents (A3 type SubPs, Ar1 = Ar2 = Ar3 in Scheme 36) are usually prepared through methods developed independently by the groups of Kobayashi and Osuka, consisting of Adler reaction of preformed tripyrrolylborane derivatives d or e (Scheme 36).2,3,571 A less conventional method is based on the metathesis-type transformation of a [32]heptaphyrin derivative.584 For the synthesis of unsymmetrically substituted SubPs of A2B (Ar1 = Ar2 ≠ Ar3 in Scheme 36) or ABC-types (Ar1 ≠ Ar2 ≠ Ar3 in Scheme 36), the in situ aroylation of a tri-N-tripyrromethene precursor generated from tripyrrane f, followed by intramolecular cyclization (Scheme 36) is the most convenient procedure.585

6.1.1 Synthesis of symmetric A3-type subporphyrins. 
As stated above, the general procedures for the preparation of A3-SubPs involve Adler reactions of a tripyrrolylborane derivative of d and e types, with arylaldehydes. By using these methods, meso-aryl substituted SubPs are obtained in yields ranging from 1 to 10%.1,5Table 17 shows selected examples of SubPs that have been prepared by Adler reactions. For a more detailed list see ref. 1 and 5. Derivatives 389, 391–394 have been prepared by method A, which involves treatment of tri(1H-pyrrol-1-yl)borane (d) with the corresponding aryl aldehyde in refluxing propionic acid.3,571 Method B uses N-pyridine-tri(1H-pyrrol-1-yl)borane (e) for the Adler reaction. The optimized conditions consist of condensation of e with an aryl aldehyde in 1,2-dichlorobenzene at 0 °C, in the presence of trifluoroacetic acid under nitrogen, followed by TFA quenching and oxidation at reflux temperature.2 Examples of SubPs prepared by method B are 395–398, 405, 406 and 408. Method C consists of a modification of method B that includes an additional oxidation step with MnO2, which results in increased yields via smooth oxidation of subchlorin formed as a byproduct in the cyclotrimerization step to the corresponding SubP.586–589 A, B and C procedures provide SubPs in their axially substituted hydroxo forms (R = H in Scheme 36), which are usually converted into the corresponding methoxo SubPs by treatment with MeOH (Scheme 37). By applying the methods A, B or C, a variety of meso-triaryl substituted SubPs have been prepared. The nature of the aryl substituents is versatile, including substituted benzene rings,2,3,571,587,588 heterocycles like pyridine,3,571 thiophene,586 benzo[b]thiophene,586 mono-,579 oligo-590 or dendritic591 carbazole, or ferrocene moieties592 (Table 17 and ref. 1 and 5).
Table 17 Selected examples of A3 type SubPs obtained by Adler reaction of tripyrrolylborane derivatives d or e

 Scheme 37  Axial substitution of hydroxo-SubPs with alcohols. 
Syntheses of β-substituted SubPs are mostly carried out by initial β-halogenation followed by nucleophilic substitution reactions. So far, there has been only one report on direct cyclotrimerization reactions of a β-substituted tripyrrolylborane derivative 410 (Scheme 38) by Panda that afford β-hexaethylated SubPs 411 and 412 in 8 and 5% yield, respectively.594 SubP 411, containing phenyl-substituted meso-positions, has been obtained from 410 by applying general method B. In addition, pyridine-tri-N-(3,4-diethylpyrrolyl)borane (410) has been condensed with trioxane, in the presence of catalytic methanesulfonic acid, affording the meso-unsubstituted SubP 412.594
 Scheme 38  Synthesis of meso-phenyl substituted (411) and unsubstituted (412) β-hexaethyl-SubPs by direct Adler reaction from pyridine-tri-N-(3,4-diethylpyrrolyl)borane (410). 
The other example of meso-free A3 SubP 415 has been prepared from the f type tripyrrane 413, by reaction with triethylamine borane, to afford the corresponding tri-N-tripyrromethene complex 414, which is in situ condensed with boiling triethyl orthoformate (Scheme 39).595
 Scheme 39  Synthesis of meso-unsubstituted SubP 415. 
A less common procedure to prepare A3-SubPs consists of a transannular metathesis-type transformation, in which a SubP is extruded from a [32]heptaphyrin derivative upon cooperative metalation with Cu(II) and B(III), affording a copper(II) porphyrin as a by-product (Scheme 40).584 The method has allowed to prepare meso-perfluorinated SubPs 418584 and 419596 in 36 and 6% yields, respectively. Both SubPs are hitherto not accessible through the tripyrrolylborane-based protocols. However, the scope of the procedure is very limited by the scarce diversity available in the substitution of the [32]heptaphyrin precursors, which is reduced to 2,6-dichlorophenyl597 and 3,5-bis(trifluoromethyl)phenyl-meso-moieties,598 besides the above-mentioned pentafluorophenyl and trifluoromethyl groups.
 Scheme 40  Preparation of SubPs 418 and 419 by metathesis of [32]heptaphyrins. 


6.1.2 Synthesis of unsymmetric A2B-type and ABC-type subporphyrins. 
A2B-type meso-aryl-SubPs have been prepared by cross Adler reaction of the pyridine-tri-N-pyrrolylborane precursor e (Scheme 36) with a stoichiometric mixture of benzaldehyde and p-bromobenzaldehyde under the conditions of method C. This afforded four SubPs, namely AAA, AAB, ABB and BBB, as a non-separable mixture.599 Subsequent derivatizations of the crude by Sonogashira ethynylation,599 Ni-catalyzed homocoupling,583 or Buchwald–Hartwig amination,600 afforded the corresponding mixture of compounds, from which different A3, B3, A2B and AB2-SubPs were separated by the standard chromatographic techniques. Method B using 4-(diphenylamino)benzaldehyde and 4-formylbenzoic acid was also the choice to prepare a push–pull SubP that has been designed as photosensitizer for photoelectrochemical water oxidation.578
A more rational approach to prepare unsymmetrically substituted SubPs involves the preparation of a triethylamino tripyrromethene borane intermediate (g, Scheme 41) by reaction of a f-type tripyrrane with BH3·NEt3, followed by in situ aroylation with aroyl chloride with concomitant ring closure. This method has been demonstrated to be very effective for the synthesis of versatile symmetric and unsymmetric SubPs, since the access to various f-type intermediates is relatively simple and SubPs can be constructed in a controlled manner. However, the Adler reaction from tri(1H-pyrrol-1-yl)borane precursors still constitutes the most usual access to symmetric SubPs. Table 18 compiles A3, A2B and ABC SubPs that have been directly prepared by following the reactions shown in Scheme 41. R1 and R2meso-substituents arising from tripyrrane f can be the same or different and comprise H, ester functions and a variety of phenyl rings with different substitution patterns. Except for compounds with R3 = H, which are prepared by reaction with trimethyl or triethyl orthoformate (Table 18), the reaction of g with different aroyl chloride derivatives functionalized with substituted benzene rings, as well as thiophene or naphthalene rings, has provided a wide assortment of SubPs, which can be further manipulated by conventional organic chemistry for different purposes, as we will discuss next sections.
 Scheme 41  General procedure for the synthesis of SubPs from tripyrrane intermediates f.

6.2 Reactivity of subporphyrins
Stepwise approaches enable the synthesis of SubPs containing hydrogen, carboxylic esters, some heterocycles, or aryl groups at the meso-positions, while peripheral β-modifications of SubPs have been somewhat left behind, except for two examples of β-hexaethylSubPs.594 The axial position can be another candidate for the fabrication of SubPs. In the beginning of SubP studies an axial methoxy substituent had been often used, since it facilitates the isolation and purification of the compounds owing to the chemical stabilities of methoxo-SubPs. We will discuss the chemistry of SubPs bearing axial substituents other than methoxy group in Section 6.2.2.

6.2.1 Reactivity at the peripheral and meso positions. 
Peripheral functionalization of SubPs has been started by halogenation reactions, namely bromination, chlorination or iodination with usual halogenation reagents such as NBS, Br2, and NCS. Then, nucleophilic aromatic substitution and Pd catalyzed coupling reactions of β-halogenated SubPs have given versatile β-halogenated SubPs.608 Actually, hexabrominated-609,610h and hexachlorinated-SubPs i611 have been used as intermediates of a variety of peripherally substituted SubPs (Scheme 42).
 Scheme 42  Peripheral hexafunctionalization of SubPs. 
Hexabrominated SubPs h were subjected to Suzuki–Miyaura and Stille coupling to provide compounds 454–459 and trifluoromethylation to give 464–466 in good to very good yields, while non-catalyzed nucleophilic aromatic substitutions (SNAr) were performed on SubP i affording hexasulfanylated compounds 460–463 (Table 19) in 74–95% yields. Furthermore, controlled halogenation followed by sulfanylation, or palladium catalyzed amination reactions gave to SubPs bearing one peripheral sulfanyl (467),611 amine (469–472) or amide functions (473) (Fig. 101).612 In addition, oxidation of the peripheral thioether of 467 with MCPBA provided a diastereomeric mixture of the corresponding sulfoxides 468 (Fig. 101).613 A hydroxy group,614 as well as a trifluoromethyl and other perfluorinated functions, have also been introduced through monobromo-SubPs.610

 Fig. 101  Examples of peripherally monofunctionalized SubPs. 
A procedure to peripherally nitrated SubPs consists of treatment of 409 with Cu(NO3)2, a milder nitration reagent, to give β-nitrated SubP 475 (Scheme 43), and subsequent hydrogenation afforded β-aminated SubP 476.612
 Scheme 43  Peripheral nitration and amination of SubPs. 
Ir-catalyzed β-selective borylations of meso-free porphyrins have been extensively studied to create various β-substituted porphyrins and β–β linked cyclic porphyrin rings.615,616 This reaction was found to be similarly effective for meso-free SubP. Under the similar reaction conditions, meso-free SubP 438 was converted to diborylated SubPs j in 85–94% yield, which have been shown to be valuable synthetic precursors of β-functionalized macrocycles through Suzuki–Miyaura coupling (Scheme 44 and Table 20).617,618 In addition, Cu-mediated iodine exchange reaction results in the diiodo SubP 478, which has in turn been converted into the corresponding β,β′-dideuterated (487), diformylated (488) and diiminylated SubPs (489).
 Scheme 44  β,β′-Diborylated SubPs as synthetic intermediates of unsymmetrical β-functionalized macrocycles.

The β-borylation reaction of 438 can be carried out in a controlled manner, by treatment with 0.8 equivalents of (Bpin)2 in the presence of a catalytic amount of [Ir(cod)OMe]2 and dtbpy.619 The resulting monoborylated SubP has been used as synthetic precursor of meso-to-meso SubP dimers, which will be discussed in Section 6.4.2.
Introduction of specific substituents at the meso-positions is beneficial in creating new functional SubPs, since the electronic nature of the SubP is largely perturbed by the strong substituent effects.620 This is due to the smaller rotational barrier of the meso-aryl substituents in SubPs as compared with that of porphyrins. Therefore, meso-functionalization can be used in SubPs, not only for structural purposes, but also for designing systems with specific redox and optical properties.
The functionalization at the SubP meso-positions can be performed by chemical manipulations of preformed meso-substituted SubPs. This strategy has been used to functionalize macrocycles with alkyl groups, amines, or conjugated systems containing oligothiophene, oligophenylene and oligo(1,4-phenyleneethynylene) branches. For example, meso-alkyl SubPs have been prepared in 43–50% yield by desulfurization of meso-thienyl SubPs, like for example 400 and 401 (Table 17), using RANEY® nickel to lead the corresponding alkyl-substituted SubPs (Scheme 45).1,586
 Scheme 45  Synthesis of meso-trialkylSubPs 490 and 491. 

meso-Aminophenyl-substituted SubPs 492 and 493 (Fig. 102) have been prepared in quantitative yields by reduction with SnCl2 in HCl of the corresponding nitrophenyl-SubPs,573,588 or by Pd-catalyzed reduction with sodium borohydride.595 The primary amine can be further removed by diazotization reaction followed by treatment with aqueous hypophosphorous acid.595 Functionalization with amines can also be done by palladium catalyzed Buchwald–Hartwig reaction of the corresponding brominated meso-aryl SubPs with amines. Some examples are constituted by meso-(4-(N,N-dibenzylamino)phenyl)- (494–496), and aza-crown substituted (497) SubPs (Fig. 102).600 SubP 493 can also be obtained by Buchwald–Hartwig amidation of the corresponding brominated SubP with benzamide, followed by hydrolysis.588
 Fig. 102  SubPs containing meso-aminophenyl substituents. 
Thiophene derived SubP 400 (Table 17) has been brominated at the thiophene rings with NBS,621 and the resulting SubP 256, together with the bromophenyl-SubP 395 (Scheme 46), have been used as precursors of extended-conjugated systems by means of Sonogashira or Suzuki couplings (Scheme 46). Some examples include 5,10,15-tris-oligo(1,4-phenyleneethynylene) substituted SubPs (498–502) for two-photon-absorption applications,622 tris-oligo-(1,4-phenyl) macrocycles 503 and 504,623 as well as tris-oligo-2,5-thienylene-substituted SubPs 505–507 (Table 21).621 These types of palladium catalyzed coupling reactions are also very useful to assemble multimacrocyclic and multichromophore systems, which will be discussed in Section 6.4.
 Scheme 46  Synthesis of meso-substituted SubPs extended-conjugated functions by Sonogashira and Suzuki coupling.

Other modifications of meso-substituents include oxidation of alkyl substituents at the benzylic position to give a meso-carbonyl SubP,586 quaternization of pyridyl substituents in 391 and 393 (Table 17) by alkylation with MeI,577,624 hydrolysis of esters,576 and Knoevenagel condensation.576
SubPs bearing a halogen atom, namely, bromine (508) or chlorine (509), directly attached to the meso-position constitute versatile platforms for introducing a variety of functional groups by C–C, C–heteroatom or C–metal bond formation. Both A2B halogenated intermediates have been prepared by bromination or chlorination with NBS or NCS, respectively, of the meso-free SubP 429 (Scheme 47).
 Scheme 47  Synthesis of halogenated A2B-type SubP intermediates (see Table 22). 
Bromo-SubP 508 has been employed in palladium catalyzed coupling reactions including Negishi, Suzuki–Miyaura and Sonogashira couplings,576,601 amination (i.e. Zhang protocol),625,626 alkoxylation, sulfanylation or phosphorylation reactions.627 SubP 508 has been also borylated with Bpin and subsequently used in Suzuki–Miyaura coupling reactions.593 Besides, treatments of 508 with nitrogen-, oxygen- and sulphur-based nucleophiles serve to prepare the corresponding meso-substituted amine-, ether or thioether SubPs, respectively, through Pd-free SNAr-type reactions.608,628 Except for electron-deficient amines, the catalyst-free protocol is more efficient for amination and aryloxylation reactions, while the Pd-catalyzed sulfanylations usually work better. SNAr reaction is also the method used to introduce diarylamines and heteroarenes. Likewise, carbon-nucleophiles such as the malononitrile anion have been used in SNAr reactions on 508.629 Chlorinated intermediate 509 can also be used SNAr-amination reactions, being typically more reactive in amination processes implying electron-deficient amines.628,630Table 22 lists all the SubP derivatives obtained by these procedures.

The reaction of SubPs with electrophilic reagents is another route to functionalize the meso-positions. meso-LithioSubPs can be generated by bromine-lithium exchange of bromo-SubP derivatives such as 508 through treatment with n-Buli at −98 °C. The resulting organolithium species can be trapped with different electrophiles, such as benzophenone, DMF, CO2, chlorotrimethylsilane, N-fluorobenzenesulfonimide and mesitylboryl fluoride, giving rise to the corresponding meso-functionalized SubPs 546–552 (Table 22).631

meso-Nitro SubPs 555 and 556 were prepared by nitration reaction of meso-free SubPs 429 and 445 with AgNO2 and I2 and were reduced to meso-amino SubPs 557 and 558 with NaBH4 and Pd/C, respectively (Scheme 48).606,632
 Scheme 48  
meso-Amino SubPs 557 and 558. 
Some meso- and peripheral substituents may be cyclized at the adjacent position to afford fused SubPs. For example, SubP 560 bearing a fused thiochromane has been prepared from meso-2-bromophenylsulfanyl-substituted SubP 559 by intramolecular Pd-catalyzed C–H activation reaction (Scheme 49).628 A related SubP 564 (Scheme 49) bearing a fused pyran ring has been synthesized in 14% overall yield by cleavage of the methoxy group of SubP 431 with BBr3, giving a hydroxy group, followed by perbromination and intramolecular ether formation in the presence of potassium carbonate.633 Similarly, SubP 562, containing two fused dihydroquinoline rings, has been obtained by a double cyclization of the aryl groups in the β,β-diiodo-meso-diarylamino subporphyrin SubP 561. The latter entails a radical-nucleophilic substitution reaction (SRN1) involving stable radical intermediates that are generated by single electron transfer from tert-butoxide or diarylamide anions to the SubP, with concomitant loss of iodide ions of 561.634
 Scheme 49  SubPs with fused heterocycles at the meso,β-positions. 
A few cyclometalated subporphyrin complexes wherein the metals are coordinated either to the meso- or the β-pyrrole positions have been reported. The former are constituted by the NCN-type pincer complexes 565 and 566 (Fig. 103) that have been prepared by metalation of the meso-free 486 with palladium and platinum salts, respectively,618 as well as the earring complex 567,635 which has been obtained by treatment of the corresponding meso-free ligand with Pd(OAc)2.
 Fig. 103  Structures of pincer (565 and 566) and earring (567) meso-metallated SubPs, and β-pyrrole metalated SubPs 568 and 569. 
When the meso-position is substituted with a 2-pyridine ligand such as SubP 545, iridium complexation takes place by treatment with [Cp*IrCl2]2, producing C–H activation of the adjacent pyrrole β-positions to give a mixture of regioisomers 568-exo and 568-endo in a 72/28 ratio.593 Likewise, Ir(III) metalation of the corresponding trispyridyl-SubP 408 proceeds quite cleanly, producing exclusively C3-symmetric SubP 569.593


6.2.2 Reactivity at the axial position. 
Most of SubPs are reported as methoxo-compounds that are obtained by treatment with MeOH, as one of the last steps in the synthesis of SubPs. This contrasts with axial ligand exchange reactions of SubPcs, which typically require more forcing conditions.68 The axial hydroxo- or methoxo-groups can easily be replaced by any other alkoxy or carbonyloxy groups, for different purposes, for example, to obtain single crystals that can be studied by XR diffraction,586 or to endow the molecule with additional functionalities, such as a thiol group.636 This process can be reverted to the axial hydroxy SubP by simply washing the B-alkoxy and B-carbonyloxy subporphyrin with aqueous HCl. Interestingly, bowl-inversion has been observed in some SubPs containing a chiral sulfinyl substituent at one of the β-positions, upon treatment with alcohols at different temperatures. The latter strongly suggests the formation of a borenium cation intermediated SN1-type mechanism for axial exchange reactions in protic solvents.613
As for subazaporphyrins,159 fluoro-substituted SubPs are prepared by treatment of the hydroxo-form with BF3·OEt2 (Scheme 50).610,637 Both alkoxylation and fluorination reactions occur in quantitative yields and are reversible under acidic conditions. Fluoro-substituted SubPs are quite less polar than the corresponding hydroxo-SubPs, providing an alternative tool, complementary to the methoxo-substitution, for their isolation.
 Scheme 50  Fluoride substitution of SuPs at their axial positions. 
SubPs bearing a B–C bond are prepared in 60–90% yields by reaction of the corresponding methoxo-SubPs with excess Grignard reagents (Scheme 51 and Table 23, methods A and B),606,638 or in 50–80% yields by treatment with the less nucleophilic arylzinc reagents (method C).639 The latter allows the axial substitution with functional groups that are not compatible with conditions of methods A and B, such as nitrile, ketone, ester, or halogens. The axial substituents may be further manipulated by standard organic chemistry, constituting an excellent platform, complementary to meso and peripheral substitution, to endow the SubPs with additional properties, such as in compounds 592–596 (Fig. 104), obtained in one step from compounds 588, 589 and 590, respectively.626,639 Methods A–C constitute an interesting and versatile alternative to the previously reported method D (Table 23) involving the intermediation of a borenium cation.640
 Scheme 51  Axial functionalization with B–C bonds.

SubP B-hydrides 597–599 have been prepared in moderate to very good yields by reaction of methoxo-SubPs with DIBAL-H.641 The hydride 597 can be hydrolyzed to give hydroxo-SubP 389, or used in hydroboration reactions of aldehydes and imines, affording the corresponding alkoxo- and amino-SubPs 600 and 601 (Scheme 52).
 Scheme 52  Generation, hydrolysis and hydroboration reactions of SubP-hydrides. 
Axial peroxides 602–604 have been synthesized by treatment of methoxo-SubP 390 with hydrogen peroxide, tert-butyl peroxide, and MCPBA, respectively, in fairly good yields (Scheme 53). Subsequent heating of the peroxide 602 gave peroxo-bridged subporphyrin dimer 605 in 97% yield.642 This reactivity of 602 contrasts with that of 389 and may be ascribed to higher nucleophilic character of the B-hydroperoxide moiety. In addition, dimer 605 was stable towards hydrolysis, contrasting with the corresponding SubP μ-oxodimer that was quickly hydrolyzed in the presence of water. Peroxides 603 and 604 did not undergo similar thermal dimerization, suggesting that the formation of 605 may involve nucleophilic trapping of a borenium cation intermediate.
 Scheme 53  Formation of SubP peroxides. 

6.3 Characterization of subporphyrins

6.3.1 Single-crystal X-ray diffraction. 
Almost all the structures of key SubPs have been revealed by single crystal X-ray diffraction analysis. SubP 415 shows a curved and conical structure, similar to those of all the other subporphyrinoids, with B–N and B–O distances of 1.50–1.51 and 1.44 Å, respectively, and a bowl-depth of 1.51 Å (Fig. 105).595 The latter characterizes the degree of curvature of SubPs, which is defined by the distance from the mean plane of the peripheral six β-pyrrolic carbons to the central boron atom. Compared to SubPzs (1.76 Å bowl-depth) and SubPcs (2.55 Å bowl-depth),76 the conical structure of SubPs is considerably shallower.159
 Fig. 105  X-ray crystal structures of SubPs 415 and 390. (a) Top view and (b) side view of 415. (c) Top view and (d) side view of 390. Thermal ellipsoids are drawn at the 50% probability level. Adapted with permission from ref. 2 and 595. Copyright 2018 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. Copyright 2007 American Chemical Society. (e) Substituent labelling for SubPs Table 24. 
As a general trend, the curvatures for substituted SubPs are slightly smaller than that of unsubstituted SubP 415, and depend on the axial, meso- and β-substituents, as well as crystal packing manners. Thus, for example, introducing one (SubP 606), or two (SubP 430) phenyl substituents at one or two of the meso-positions slightly flattens the molecule to bowl depth values of 1.42 and 1.46 Å, respectively, while the curvature of the corresponding triphenyl-substituted SubP 390 is of 1.29 Å (Table 24 and Fig. 105). Comparable effects are observed upon trialkyl (SubP 491)586 or bis ethoxycarbonyl (SubP 437) meso-substitution.603 Functionalizing at the six β-pyrrole positions with ethyl groups (SubP 412) or fusing benzene rings at the same positions (BzSubP 387) also reduces the SubP bowl depths (1.43 Å and 1.32 Å, respectively). The corresponding meso-triphenyl-substituted SubPs 411 and 388 are still shallower (1.16 and 1.18 Å, respectively), reflecting the smaller curvatures of SubPs fully substituted at both meso- and β-positions (see Table 24 SubPs 411, h, 457, 459, 455, 460, 461, 462, 463 and 388). In this respect, compound 461, containing three meso-phenyl substituents and six tert-butylsulfanyl groups at the β-positions, shows a curvature of 1.04 Å,611 which represents the smallest bowl-depth among all the reported neutral SubPs.

Differently, SubPs contain fused rings involving both meso-and β-positions, such as 562 (Scheme 49), or the corresponding metallacycles 568 and 569 (Fig. 103), are forced to take large bowl depths of around 1.63, 1.52 and 1.56 Å, respectively.593,634
A distinctive feature of SubPs with respect to their triaza-SubPc and SubPz congeners, and even to the corresponding porphyrins, is the strong interaction produced by the meso-aryl substituents with the aromatic SubP core and hence, stronger influence of these substituents on the macrocyclic electronic properties. meso-Phenyl SubPs 606, 430, 390, 508, 585, 597, 570, 637, 602, 603 and 604 lacking β-substituents (Table 24) display relatively small dihedral angles that range only between 38.1–52.0°, contrasting with those of 5,10,15,20-tetraarylporphyrins, which usually exhibit larger dihedral angles (>60°). Substitution of the meso-rings at para- or meta-positions (SubPs 397 and 407, respectively) produce SubPs with similar dihedral angles (Table 24). However, meso-aryl groups containing ortho-substituents, like in 399 and 418 (Table 24), show larger dihedral angles (61.0–79.0°) due to the steric hindrance of the ortho-substituents,2,584 these effects being even larger (78–82°) for the corresponding porphyrins.643 Other aromatic meso-rings such as thienyl- (SubP 401) or pyridyl (SubP 166) also display small dihedral angles allowing their strong electronic conjugation with the SubP core. On the opposite side are fully substituted SubPs such as 411, h, 459, 454, 460, 461, 462, 463 and BzSubP 388, which are sterically congested and so, accommodate the meso-aryl substituents almost perpendicular to the SubP core, exhibiting larger dihedral angles of 64.60–84.5°. Remarkably, the dihedral angles between the SubPs and the terminal aryl groups with the arylalkynyl- or the arylvinyl-groups (compounds 513, 512 and 454; Table 24) are considerably small, running from 14.6° to 31.6°, and indicating extended conjugation through the vinyl and ethynyl linkers.601,609
The aromaticity of SubPs is manifested in their crystal structure by the little bond length alternation in the macrocycle, although the situation for their aromatic substituents may be different. meso-4-Aminophenyl-substituted SubPs 493 and 496 (Fig. 102), for example, show a quinonoid-type bond alternation,1,573,599 in addition to low dihedral angles (41.2–58.6° for 493 and 42.7–48.6° for 496), indicating strong interactions between the meso-(4-aminophenyl) substituent and the SubP core.573,600
SubPs 523, 524 and 543 (Table 22), bearing one primary arylamino group directly attached to one of the meso-positions, show strong electronic interaction between the arylamino substituent and the SubP core.625,630 In these cases, π-donation from the amine to the SubP produces a resonance structure in which the Cmeso–N distances become shorter. This is reflected in the crystal structures of these compounds as seen by the unusually short Cmeso–N distances of 1.393, 1.374 and 1.365 Å, respectively (1.402–1.417 Å for usual C–N bonds). Likewise, SubPs 536, 537 and 538 (Table 22) containing a secondary diarylamino moiety exhibit Cmeso–N bond lengths that are shorter than Cipso–N bond lengths, suggesting a double bond character as well, which increases in the order 536 < 537 < 538 in accordance with the donor character of the diarylamino group.628 The shortest Cmeso–N distance (1.347 Å) is observed for SubP 557 bearing a primary amine directly attached to the meso-position.632 Conversely, for 4-nitrophenylamino-substituted compound 525 (Table 22) the Cmeso–N distance is of 1.405 Å, while Cipso–N distance is of 1.379 Å. The latter is thought to reflect a higher contribution of a resonance structure in which the meso-nitrogen is π-donating to the benzene ring owing to the presence of the nitro electron-attracting substituent.625
Similar effects are observed in meso-alkoxy substituted SubP 527, wherein Cmeso–O distance is of 1.455 Å, while Cipso–O distance is of 1.265 Å.627 The corresponding thioether 531, however, exhibits almost identical Cmeso–S and Cipso–S distances (1.761 and 1.764 Å, respectively), reflecting the significantly lower donor character of the sulfur atom.627 On the other hand, when the nitrogenated meso-substituents are pyrrole, indole or carbazole derivatives (SubPs 540–542) the Cmeso–N bond lengths significantly increase to 1.421–1.430 Å, suggesting much weaker or negligible electron-pair donating interactions of the meso-substituents.628meso-Nitrated SubPs 555 and 556, on the other hand, exhibits an even longer Cmeso–N distance of 1.431–1.436 Å.606,632
With respect to β-substitution, nitro and amino-SubPs 475 and 476 (Scheme 43), display short Cβ–N bond lengths of 1.399 and 1.336 Å, respectively, indicating strong interaction with the SubP ring.612 Besides, the Cβ–S distances in β-substituted hexasulfanyl-SubPs 461 and 462 show slightly shorter values of 1.750–1.759 Å related to the corresponding Cipso–S distances (1.772–1.790 Å).611,613


6.3.2 Rotational dynamics of meso-aryl-substituents. 
The rotational dynamics of SubP meso-substituents has been examined2,609 and previously reviewed.1meso-Aryl substituents of SubPs have lower rotational barriers. Since the SubP convex and concave faces are nonequivalent, the rotation of meso-phenyl groups can be studied by 1H-NMR spectroscopy. Thus, for example, meso-(3,4,5-trimethoxyphenyl)-SubP 398 displays the same chemical shift for Hexo and Hendo protons, as diagnostic of fast rotation of the meso-aryl substituents in the 1H-NMR timescale. In contrast, even at 130 °C, meso-(2,4,6-trimethoxyphenyl)-SubP 399 exhibits different signals for its corresponding Hexo and Hendo protons, indicating a larger rotational barrier of the meso-aryl groups. The coalescence temperatures and (ΔGROT) of the meso-aryl substituents for SubPs h, 454–459 have been previously determined.609
More recently, the rotational dynamics of SubPs 431, 432, 433, 434, and 435 have been studied by VT 1H-NMR spectroscopy. In order to lower the rapid rotational barrier, the corresponding axially substituted SubP-Ph derivatives were prepared using phenylmagnesium bromide.
In the 1H-NMR spectrum of 431 at 20 °C, the signals of the 2-methoxy protons are observed as two separate broad signals that sharpen at −40 °C, appearing at 4.04 and 3.53 ppm with a 56:44 ratio. These peaks have been assigned to the Hexo- and Hendo-protons of the corresponding atropisomers, respectively,602 which were found to coalesce at 35 °C. The rotation rate constant of the 2-methoxyphenyl group at each temperature has been estimated to be of 13, 340, and 4000 s−1 at −20, 20, and 60 °C, respectively. From these rate constants, the activation parameters of the rotation of the 2-methoxyphenyl group in 431 have been determined and the same method has been applied for the other SubPs. A comparison of the rotation activation parameters of 431–435 revealed variable contributions of ΔH‡ and ΔS‡ to ΔG‡ with the following consequences:
(1) The rotational barriers of SubP-Ph derivatives are smaller than those of SubP-OMe species.
(2) Ethoxy (190) and isopropoxy groups (433) exert similar ΔG‡298 values, which are larger than that of a methoxy group (431); among 432 and 433, the rotation of the 2-isopropoxy aryl group occurs via a pre-ordered transition state with less enthalpic cost.
(3) Alkyl group in 435 restricts the rotation of the meso-aryl groups much more than alkoxy groups.
(4) The chloro group at the 4-position in SubP-Ph 434 does not influence the rotational dynamics. However, good π-electron donor groups such as amino groups tend to lower ΔG‡298, probably due to electronic stabilization of the coplanar rotation transition state.
The rotational dynamics of meso-substituents in SubPs β-substituted with thioethers has also been studied.6111H-NMR studies indicate that the meso-phenyl substituents undergo restricted rotation for 460–463. Besides, the β-sulfanyl substituents are conformationally flexible in arylsulfanyl 460 and 461, as well as benzodithio-SubP 463, while are strictly regulated to an anti-conformation in the tert-butylsulfanyl-SubP 462.


6.3.3 Optical properties. 
SubP 415 displays a Soret band at 341 nm arising from a transition that is analogous to the B transition of porphyrins within Gouterman's four-orbital model. In addition, a vibronic structure appears as two absorption bands at 432 and 455 nm with an order of magnitude less intense than the B band (Fig. 106), which correspond to Q-like transitions within Gouterman's four-orbital model.644 The Soret-like band of SubPs is progressively red shifted upon substitution at the meso-positions with phenyl rings, and the same occurs for the Q-absorptions. Thus, SubPs 606, 430 and 390 (Table 25) exhibit their corresponding B-bands at 352, 362 and 373 nm, respectively, while the Q-bands appear at, 440 and 461, 450 and 461, and 484 nm, respectively, indicating electronic interaction of the meso-phenyl groups with the SubP core. The latter has been attributed to smaller steric hindrance around the meso-phenyl groups in SubPs related to the corresponding tetraphenylporphyrins,595 which leads to a substantial electronic conjugation of the aromatic meso-rings to the SubP core in meso-phenyl and thienyl-SubPs 390 and 421, contrasting with porphyrins, for which this conjugation is only marginal.
