The normalized fluorescence spectra of 415, 606, 430, and 390 are shown in Fig. 106b, revealing emission bands between 450 and 600 nm that have been assigned to S1 fluorescence. Upon sequential addition of meso-phenyl rings the fluorescence spectra show red-shift, with the fluorescence bands mirroring the respective Q-like bands, and Stokes shifts gradually higher of 150, 550, 780, and 880 cm−1, respectively.595 When going from 415 to 390 the fluorescence quantum yields (ϕF) decrease, while the fluorescence lifetimes (τF) become elongated in the order 415 = 390 < 606 < 430.
Interestingly, a different fluorescence profile has been observed for 390 at 380–450 nm upon excitation at the B-band at about 350–410 nm (Fig. 107). This fluorescence has been assigned to S2-to-S0 (S2) fluorescence because it is a mirror image of the S2–S0 absorption.575 The observation of the dual fluorescence for SubP 390 indicates that these molecules do not obey Kasha's, rule, maybe due to a large energy gap between the S1 and S2 states of SubPs. β-Hexaethyl SubP 411 does not show any discernible S2 emission, while the corresponding meso-unsubstituted SubP 412 does it upon excitation at its Soret band.594
 Fig. 107  Ground-state absorption and static fluorescence spectra of SubP 390 in toluene. λex = 370 nm. The asterisk (*) indicate Raman peak of the solvent. — Absorption; •••• S2 emission; •–•– S1 emission. Adapted with permission from ref. 575. Copyright 2013 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
As a general trend, any functionalization of the SubP skeleton at the meso- and/or β-positions cause red shifts both at B and Q absorption bands with respect to the unsubstituted 415 (Fig. 109a). The only exceptions to this rule are constituted by meso-trifluoromethyl-substituted SubP 419 and β-ethyl-substituted SubP 412, as specified below. Typically, SubP derivatives display their B-band in the 340–380 nm region and their Q-band in the 400–540 nm region (Table 25). Therefore, absorptions of SubPs lie at considerably shorter wavelengths (ca. 30–50 and 70–100 nm, respectively) than the corresponding bands of the metallated tetraphenylP (ca. 410–420 and 500–610 nm). This blue shift arises from the smaller π-electronic circuit of SubPs (14π-electrons) related to Zn(II)tetraphenylP (18π-electrons). Besides, the absorption coefficient of the SubP Soret band is ca. 25% that of Zn(II)tetraphenylP in the same solvent.2,3 The smeared vibronic structure in Q-like absorption and broad fluorescence bands seem to arise from the bowl-shaped SubP core in contrast to the comparatively flat structure of Zn(II)tetraphenylP (Fig. 108).3
 Fig. 108  Steady-state absorption (solid lines) and fluorescence (dashed lines) spectra of Zn(II)tetraphenylP (black) and 390 (red) in toluene. Reproduced with permission from ref. 2. Copyright 2007 American Chemical Society. 
SubPs usually exhibit green fluorescence in a range of 470–670 nm with the main peak observed in the 508–543 nm region (Table 25). Stokes shifts are in a range of 700–2950 cm−1 and fluorescence quantum yields are larger than those of Zn(II) tetraphenyl porphyrins (Fig. 109).
 Fig. 109  UV/Vis absorption (black) and fluorescence (red) spectra in dichloromethane (solid line) or methanol (dashed line), and calculated transition (blue bar) by TD-DFT method at the B3LYP/6-31G* level of (a) 409, (b) 399 and (c) 397. Adapted with permission from ref. 2. Copyright 2007 American Chemical Society. 

meso-Alkyl substituents also shift the absorption bands to the red (360, 450, 472 nm) in 491, although in lesser extent than triphenylation. The Stokes shift value is also small (489 cm−1), probably reflecting a smaller radiative chromophore size. A similar effect is observed upon substitution with two carboxylic esters at the meso-positions in SubP 437 (362, 476, 505 nm).603 However, an electron withdrawing alkyl chain such as the trifluoromethyl-derivative 419 causes the opposite effect, with a Soret band at 341 nm, similar to that of unsubstituted SubP. In this case, however, Q-bands are red-shifted to 443 and 473 nm. The fluorescence spectrum of 419 is also a mirror image of the Q bands, exhibiting the most intense band at 482 nm, while the fluorescence quantum yield is quite small.596
SubP 399, bearing hindered 2,4,6-trimethoxyphenyl meso-substituents, represents the second group. This SubP with restricted rotation of their meso-aryl substituents exhibits the Soret band at 372 nm and only one band at 454 nm in the Q-region,1,609 while the fluorescence band appears at 524 nm with a shoulder at 500 nm (Fig. 109). Other SubPs with only one Q-band, or with a very weak Q00 component, bear non-hindered meso groups such as 2-, 3- or 4-pyridyl (408, 393 and 391, respectively),2,593 and p-trifluoromethylphenyl-substituents (394) (Fig. 110).571 It has been suggested that electron-donating groups can give rise to more intense Q00 components than electron-withdrawing groups such as 2-, 3-, or 4-pyridyl, pentafluorophenyl, or trifluoromethylphenyl.2,571,584,593 In agreement with this consideration, 391 displays the weakest Q00 component, while the Q-band of 493, bearing strong π-electron donor amines, appears as a broad absorption at 510 nm, showing the largest increase of the Q00/Q01 ratio (Fig. 110).573 However, the complete rationalization of the optical spectra is more complex, as p-nitrophenyl-meso substituent shows completely different behavior in this respect,2 and axial substituents produce notable alterations in the energy and intensity of the two transitions (see for example Table 25 and ref. 1).593
 Fig. 110  (a) Q-band region of SubPs bearing meso-aryl groups of different nature in CHCl3. Adapted with permission from ref. 571. Copyright 2007, American Chemical Society. (b) UV-vis absorption and emission spectra of 493. Adapted with permission from ref. 573. Copyright 2009 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
4-Nitrophenyl-substituted SubP 397 represents the third type, displaying a bathochromically shifted Soret-band at 400 nm and a Q-band in which the Q00 and Q01 components have comparable intensities (Fig. 109).2
As seen for the absorption spectra of meta-substituted SubPs 402 and 492 that are almost identical to those of 390,8 the electronic influences of the meta-nitrophenyl and meta-aminophenyl substituents are almost negligible. Differently, para-nitrophenyl and para-aminophenyl meso-substituents exert remarkable red shifts both in the absorption and fluorescence spectra. While the meso–para-nitrophenyl substituent reduces the fluorescence quantum yield, the meso–para-aminophenyl substituent intensifies the quantum yield.573 As evident in Table 25, these tendencies can be considered as a general behavior of SubPs containing conjugated nitrophenyl and aminophenyl substituents at the meso-positions.
The replacement of meso-phenyl substituents by thienyl substituents, for example in SubPs 401 (Table 25), shifts both Q- and B-bands to red (400 and 534 nm, respectively), indicating again large electronic influences of the meso-thienyl substituents on the SubP core. The fluorescence is observed at 632 nm with a high fluorescence quantum yield of 0.45.586
The π-conjugation of SubPs can be further extended by elongation of three meso-aryl chains by substitution with 1,4-oligophenylene branches (compounds 503 and 504).623 Besides, conjugated systems can be anchored to the SubP meso-positions by using vinylene and ethyne linkers, shifting both B- and Q-bands to lower energies. Because of their lesser steric demand, a sole alkene (compound 512) or alkyne (SubPs 513–520) unit produces considerably stronger effects than the three phenylene units in compounds 503 and 504.
In general, the fluorescence quantum yields also increase in the order 390 ≈ 510 < 503 ≈ 512 ≈ 513 < 504 < 516 ≈ 517, probably as a reflection of the increasing size of the effective radiative chromophore. Comparative effects between non-conjugated (SubPs 510 and 511) and conjugated (SubPs 512–520) meso-substituents are illustrated in Table 25. Other conjugated substituents such as aldehyde and carboxylic acid produce comparable effects in the absorption spectra of SubPs 306 and 307, respectively.631
Diverse heteroatoms directly attached to the meso-positions also cause significant alterations in the absorption and emission spectra of SubP chromophores. In particular, nitro (SubP 555) and amino substituents (SubPs 556, 523–526) cause red shifts in B and Q-absorption bands and fluorescence bands in different extents (Table 25). The nitro substituent leads to quenching the fluorescence quantum yields, as observed for other derivatives. The effects of bromo (508), ether (527, 503), thioether (531, 534) and phosphoryl (535) substituents are much weaker (Table 25), and the absorption spectra of these compounds are comparable to that of 390.627 When a sulfanyl (531 and 534) or phosphoryl (535) group is directly attached at the meso-position, red-shifted absorption and fluorescence spectra are observed with concurrent substantial decreases in the fluorescence quantum yields due to the heavy atom effect.
Ethylation at the six β-pyrrole positions results in blue shifted Soret bands with respect to their related β-free compounds as seen for SubPs 411 and 412 whose Soret bands are observed at 360 and 339 nm, respectively. Curiously, the Q-bands appear blue-shifted to 448 and 471 nm for 411 and red-shifted to 437 and 463 nm for the meso-unsubstituted 412 (Fig. 111). The blue shift in the Soret bands of 411 and 412 has been attributed to the electron-rich ethyl substituents.594 In line with this, the electron-poor β-trifluoromethyl-substituted SubP 464 displays absorption bands at 396, 488, and 518 nm,610 which are considerably red shifted with respect to the unsubstituted SubP 570 (Scheme 50) (369, 456, 480 nm).637 It is probable that the steric congestion between the meso-phenyl rings and β-alkyl substituents reduces effective conjugation of the meso-phenyl substituent, hence causing the observed blue shift of the absorption bands of 411. Contrary to this consideration, the more congested SubPs 456 and 457 containing meso-aryl and β-phenyl substituents display red-shifted B-bands (382 nm) with respect to the β-unsubstituted 401 (373 nm) (Fig. 112).
 Fig. 111  Absorption (red line) and emission (blue line) spectra in CH2Cl2 and simulated absorption bands (black) of (a) 411 and (b) and 412. Reproduced with permission from ref. 594. 
 Fig. 112  Absorption (solid lines) and fluorescence broken lines) spectra of BzSubPs; (a) 387 and (b) 388 in CH2Cl2, and calculated absorption bands. Adapted with permission from ref. 7 and 581. Copyright 2006 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Steady-state fluorescence spectra of 411 and 412 display clear vibronic structures with peaks at 477 and 507 nm, and 469, 481 and 496 nm, respectively (Fig. 111). The fluorescence spectra are the mirror images of their corresponding Q-bands in the absorption spectra. Small Stokes shifts indicate the structural rigidity of the excited states of these SubPs. The fluorescence quantum yields of 411 and 412 were determined to be 0.20 and 0.12, respectively, which are considerably higher than those of the corresponding meso-alkylated SubPs (ca. 0.07).
Naturally, tris-β-benzene-fused SubPs 387 and 388 show red-shifted absorption and fluorescence spectra. Observed roughly similar spectra of these two SubPs suggested that the influence of the meso-phenyl substituents in 388 was only marginal, probably because of the restricted rotation of the meso-phenyl groups due to the steric congestion between the fused rings and meso-phenyl groups (Fig. 112).
The absorption spectrum of β-hexaethynyl substituted SubP 458 shows considerably red-shifted Soret bands (51 nm red shift with respect to 390). The effect is even stronger for β-hexaphenylethynylated SubP 454 (70 nm red shift). These examples indicate effective π-conjugation of the β-ethynyl substituents with the macrocyclic core.609 The corresponding fluorescence spectra show vibronic structure with similar fluorescence quantum yields to 390.
β-Substitution with heteroatoms such as Br and Cl in SubPs h, iodine in compound 477, and sulfur in 460–463, leads to effective fluorescence quenching as a consequence of the heavy-atom effect.609,611,617 Besides, the absorption spectra of hexaarylsulfanyl SubPs 460 and 461 are significantly perturbed, featuring red-shifted and split Soret-like bands at 376, 415 nm and 388, 423 nm, respectively, in addition to red-shifted Q-like bands at 507, 536 nm and 501, 528 nm, respectively. The absorption spectra of 462 and 463 show non-split red-shifted Soret-like bands and red-shifted Q-like bands.611
SubP 475 (Scheme 43) bearing one nitro group at one of the β-positions shows a split Soret-like band at 341 and 395 nm and a red-shifted, broad Q-type band at 513 nm. The emission band is solvent-polarity dependent, appearing in CH2Cl2 significantly red-shifted at 656 nm, with low quantum yield of 0.08.612 β-Aminosubporphyrin 476 also displays a broad Soret-like band at 366 nm and a slightly red-shifted, Q-type band at 474 nm, following similar tendencies as meso-substitution. β-Arylamino-SubPs 469–472 show red shifted absorptions as well, although the spectra are considerably different from those of meso-arylamino-SubPs.612 The fluorescence quantum yields of 469 and 470 in CH2Cl2 are lower (ϕF = 0.12 and 0.05, respectively) than that of SubP 523, while the corresponding Stokes shifts are 2410 and 3200 cm−1, respectively, being much larger than that of 523 (1380 cm−1), suggesting charge-transfer character for emitting species. meso-Substituted SubPs 471 and 472 are almost non-fluorescent in CH2Cl2, thereby plausibly indicating reductive and oxidative intramolecular electron-transfer quenching in their S1 states.
For SubPcs and SubPzs, the axial fluorine substituents do not alter the optical spectra significantly.159 Similarly, SubP 570 displays very similar UV-vis, MCD and fluorescence spectra to the hydroxo- and methoxo-derivatives.637 The Soret-like and Q-like bands of hydride SubP 597 are observed at 387 and 512 nm, with the fluorescence profile peaking at 560 nm with an absolute fluorescence quantum yield of 0.13.641 The absorption and fluorescence spectra of peroxo-substituted SubPs 602–604 are also quite similar to those of 390.642
The effects of the axial carbon ligands on the absorption spectra of SubPs are very weak. Thus, compounds with B–C bonds 574–583 show Soret-like bands in the 382–385 nm range and Q-like bands in the 501–508 nm range. B-bands of 584 and 585, on the other hand, appear comparatively blue and red shifted, respectively, at 379 and 389 nm.638

Fig. 113 shows the absorption and fluorescence spectra of 390, 570, 574, 597, 585 and 584.
 Fig. 113  UV-vis absorption (solid lines) and fluorescence (dashed lines) spectra of SuPs 390, 570, 574, 597, 584 and 585 in CH2Cl2. Fluorescence spectra were recorded upon excitation at the peak maxima of each Soret-like bands. Adapted with permission from ref. 641. Copyright 2015 American Chemical Society. 
Both the Soret- and Q-like bands are red-shifted in the order 570 < 390 < 584 < 574 < 597 < 585, reflecting the increasing electron-donating abilities of the axial substituents. The emission bands red-shifted exactly in the same order in good agreement with the respective Hammett inductive constants of the axial substituents. The corresponding fluorescence quantum yields are variable. Many of B–C SubPs exhibit fluorescence quantum yields (ϕF = 0.10–0.16) similar to that of 390, while strong fluorescence quenching is observed for donors in SubPs 577 and 579 that bear a strong donating axial group and in SubP 585 that bears an alkyl group.638


6.3.4 Photoexcited state dynamics. 
The photoexcited state dynamics of nitrophenyl-SubPs 440–443, with different rotational freedom because of the lack of methyl group (440), or different positions for the methyl group, namely, 3- (441), 3,5- (442) and 2-position (443), were compared to those of SubP 390. 443 has a severe rotational barrier of the nitrophenyl group, while the barrier for 441 and 442 are different.605 Transient absorption measurements showed different times for CS and CR in these SubPs, indicating that weak charge recombination-associated emission is only allowed for SubPs with freely rotating aryl groups. In particular, 390 displayed very rapid formation and decay of the CS state, showing broad absorbance in the range of 500–630 nm consisting of contributions from the [SubP+˙] and the nitrobenzene anion radical, with a CS rate of (3.7 ps)−1 and a CR rate of (330 ps)−1. The corresponding spectra of 441 and 442 also displayed quantitative CS and CR, with kCS = (3.0 ps)−1 and kCR = (530 ps)−1 for 441, and kCS = (2 ps)−1 and kCR = (210 ps)−1 for 442. The fluorescence lifetimes of 390, 441 and 442 (340, 510, and 200 ps, respectively) matched with the lifetimes for CR processes, indicating that their red-shifted fluorescence in CH2Cl2 is originating from the CS state. The TA spectra of 443, with an orthogonal conformation, indicated the rise and decay of a broad absorbance in the 480–660 nm range, arising from the CS state, with kCS = (5.0 ps)−1 and kCR = (40 ps)−1. The lack of red-shifted fluorescence can be explained by the very fast CR, producing a short-lived CS state.
Photoinduced electron transfer processes have also been studied on meso-arylethynyl SubPs 513, 515, 516 and 516H+.574
After photoexcitation into the Franck–Condon state, amino-subporphyrin 516 followed an adiabatic pathway to the CS state, with a time constant of 0.4 ps, whereas the CS state underwent CR, with a time constant of ca. 700 ps. In contrast, the TA of the nitro-SubP 515 in acetonitrile showed that the CS took place with a time constant of 2.8 ps, while CR occurred with a time constant of 20 ps. The latter is consistent with the negligible CS state emission in 515. The energy levels of the charge separated states for 513, 515 and 516 were estimated to be 2.35, 1.95 and 2.08 eV. Protonated SubP 516, on the other hand, recovered an increased fluorescence quantum yield, indicating the absence of CS state upon irradiation.
The excited state dynamics of meso-arylamino-SubPs 523, 524 and 525 were also studied, revealing these SubPs as good platforms of twisted intramolecular charge transfer (TICT). Thus, photoexcitation of 523 leads to the population of the locally excited state (LE) that has a slow transient absorption decay and high fluorescence quantum yield. In contrast, photoexcitation of 524 produces rise and decay profiles with time constants of 3.5 and 44 ps, respectively. The time constant of 3.5 ps indicates that the formation time of the CS state involves the structural twisting motion at the Cmeso–N bond, to trigger the intramolecular charge transfer, giving subporphyrin anion radical and 1,4-diaminobenzene cation radical.
Similarly, photoexcitation of 525 produces decay and rise times of excited state absorption signals of 9 ps. The population of LE is transferred to the CS state, undergoing twisting of the N–Cipso bond to trigger the opposite charge separated state, to give a subporphyrin cation radical and a nitrobenzene anion radical.645
The effect of bulky groups in the TICT processes have been studied using diarylamino-SubPs 536, 538 and 539.646 Due to rotation restriction, the electronic structures of these SubPs are more perturbed than those of 523–525. As a result, the TICT process is less efficient in 539 than in 525. As a proof of the TICT mechanism, the fused diarylamino SubP 562 (R = NMe2), with completely restricted rotation of the diarylamino moiety, exhibits fluorescence, contrasting with the fluorescence quenching observed for 524 and 538 due to TICT.623


6.3.5 Redox properties. 
Owing to their superb spin-delocalization abilities over almost the entire molecule, SubPs are able to stabilize radical species. Thus, oxidation of meso-hydroxy-,647 amino630 or dicyanomethyl-SubPs629 with PbO2 provides the corresponding meso-oxy (607), meso-aminyl (608) and meso-dicyanomethyl (609) radicals (Fig. 114).
 Fig. 114  Subporphyrin-based stable radicals. 
With respect to their electrochemical features, meso-unsubstituted BzSubP 387 is by 0.75 V easier to oxidize than the corresponding SubPc.189 On the other hand, β-hexaethyl SubP 412 is 0.21 V easier to oxidize that the parallel β-hexapropyl SubPz 276.63 Both comparisons illustrate the more electron-deficient nature of the meso-triaza-Subporphyrinoids with respect to meso-carbon macrocycles. Electrochemical studies the other meso-unsubstituted SubPs 415, 430 and 358 have not been reported.
Comparing BzSubPs and SubPs, meso-triphenylSubP 390 is by 0.51 V more difficult to oxidize and 0.40 V easier to reduce than the corresponding benzoannulated compound 388. For meso-arylated SubPs such as 390, 395–398, 407 and 409, the first one-electron oxidations are reversible waves in the range of 0.64–1.02 V versus the ferrocene/ferrocenium ion couple, while the second oxidation steps are irreversible. A plot of the first oxidation potentials of these SubPs versus the Hammett σ values constitutes a straight line. Compounds 390, 395–398, 407 and 409 show their first reduction potential in the −1.30 to −1.83 range. Besides, except for the nitro-derivative 397, a plot of the first reduction potentials versus the Hammett σ values also constitutes a straight line, suggesting that the first reductions of these SubPs take place at the SubP π-system (LUMO orbital).2
Both oxidation and reduction couples shift depending on the donating or withdrawing nature of the substituents, as well as the position, i.e. meso-, β- and axial, at which they are attached. Alkylation at the meso-positions has a little impact (0.01 V cathodic shift) on the oxidation potential of SubP 491 (Table 26), while the reduction wave is cathodically shifted by 0.34 V with respect to the triphenylated 390, denoting the electron richer character of meso-alkyl SubPs, related to the meso-phenyl compounds.586 Alkylation at the β-positions produces a stronger effect. In fact, neither 411 or 412 show any reduction process up to −2.0 V.594 In addition, 411 shows a quasi-reversible first oxidation potential at 0.59 V and a second irreversible one at 1.24 V, whereas 412 shows the first reversible oxidation potential at 0.62 V and the second irreversible at 1.19 V. Anyway, the strongest effect is observed upon alkylation at the axial position in 585, which shows its first oxidation and reduction processes at 0.52 and −2.14 V, respectively.

As a general trend, axial coordination with carbon-based electron-donating groups produces the highest effects in the redox properties of SubPs, related to the meso- (compare, for example, 576 with 392 and 579 with 405 in Table 26) or β-substitution (compare, for example, 457 with 574 and 455 with 338 in Table 26). In this respect, SubPs 574–585 show more negative oxidation (−0.17 to 0.68 V) and reduction potentials (−1.93 to −2.14 V) than 390, with both processes shifted by the same extent. The latter results in similar electrochemical HOMO–LUMO gaps for 574–585, lying in the of 2.57–2.66 eV range. Exceptions are aminophenyl- (577) and ferrocene (579) substituted SubPs, with electrochemical HOMO–LUMO gaps of 2.36 and 1.84 eV, respectively. For the latter compounds the first oxidation processes appear at 0.32 and −0.17 V, corresponding to the oxidation of the 4-dimethylaminophenyl and ferrocene moieties, respectively. The equivalent oxidation of meso-triferrocene-SubP 405 has been observed as a split wave at −0.028 and 0.14 V, a fact that has been interpreted as a diagnostic of effective electronic communication among the appended ferrocene units.592 The HOMO–LUMO gap of 405 is also considerably reduced to 2.05 eV, and the same occurs for other SubPs substituted with electron-donating groups as seen in thiophene-substituted SubP 401 (2.16 eV).586
The redox couples of compounds endowed with electron withdrawing groups such as 394–397 (Table 26) appear at more positive potentials. SubPs 396 and 397, containing conjugated electron-withdrawing substituents at the para-positions, exhibit different multireduction processes. In particular, 4-cyanophenyl-substituted SubP 396 displays three single-electron reduction waves at −1.53, −1.73, and −1.90 V, whereas and 4-nitrophenyl-substituted SubP 397 shows a three-electron reduction wave at −1.30 V. For 397, the deviation from linearity of the plot of the first reduction potential versus the nitro Hammett σ parameter suggests that the first reduction process might occur at the 4-nitrophenyl groups.2
Comparison of the effects of electron-withdrawing groups at the meso-, β- and axial positions suggests a different trend with respect to the above discussed effect of electron-donating groups. Thus, compared to 390, meso-4-cyanophenyl- (396) and meso-4-bromophenyl- (393) substituents shift the first oxidation potentials anodically by 0.24 and 0.06 V, respectively, while reduction processes are also anodically shifted by 0.29 and 0.12 V, correspondingly.2 However, the same substitution patterns produce weaker effects when they are at the axial site. Thus, for example, the oxidation potentials of the axial cyanophenyl-SubP 588 and bromophenyl-SubP 590 appear 0.14 and 0.08 V anodically shifted, respectively, compared to the axial phenyl-SubP 574, whereas the reduction processes are 0.07 V more difficult for 588 and 0.05 V easier for 590.639 Other electron-withdrawing substituents such as aldehyde, carboxylic acid (SubP 548 and SubP 549 in Table 22)631 and ester (Table 26)603 or trifluoromethyl (Table 26) shift the redox potentials towards more positive values. For example, 4-trifluoromethyl-moieties anodically shift both oxidation and reduction processes around 0.35 V when they are attached at the meso-phenyl rings (compare compounds 570 and 572 in Table 26) and around 1.00 V when they are directly attached to the β-positions (compare SubPs 572 and 466 in Table 26). Likewise, the two-meso-esters of 437 anodically shift to 1.00 V the first oxidation potential and to −1.6 V the first reduction wave.603
SubPs 389 and 390, bearing axial hydroxo- and methoxo-substituents, respectively show similar values for the first oxidation and reduction potentials.3 The replacement of the oxo-substituent by fluorine in 570 leads both the oxidation (+0.13 V) and reduction waves (+0.04 V) to more positive potentials.641 Axial hydride (SubP 597) dramatically shifts the oxidation peak to 0.36 V, while axial peroxides produce weaker influence. In particular, the first one-electron reductions of 602, 603 and 604 are quasi-irreversible, suggesting that the one-electron reduction induces O–O bond cleavage. Besides, the first reduction potentials are positively shifted in the order 603 (−1.94 V) < 602 (−1.81 V) < 604 (−1.70 V), reflecting the electron-donating abilities of the peroxy substituents.642
The effect of heteroatoms at the pyrrole β-positions of SubPs varies according to the electronic nature of the substituents. HexabromoSubPs (h) present anodic potential shift of both oxidation and reduction waves (Table 26) indicating that inductive effects of bromine are dominant for modulating the electrochemical potentials.609 β-Sulfanyl-SubPs 460–462 (Table 26) show one irreversible oxidation process at 0.65, 0.77, and 0.67 V, respectively, roughly similar to 390. More different, the corresponding oxidation wave of SubP 463 appears at 0.55 V.611 The cathodic window, however, shows one reversible reduction wave for each compound at −1.63, −1.58, −1.79, and −1.79 V, respectively, significantly more positive than that of 390. Monofunctionalized β-sulfanylSubP 467 (Fig. 101) shows redox features more similar to those of 390, namely at 0.79 and −1.95 V, corresponding to the oxidation and reduction waves, respectively.611
A single nitro- or amino-substituent attached at the β-position produces much stronger impact in SubPs 475 and 476 (Scheme 43), respectively or in arylamino-SubPs 469–472 (Fig. 101).612 For example, the first one-electron oxidation and reduction potentials of SubPs 475 and 476 appear at 0.82 and −1.39 V and 0.28 and −2.08 V, that is, more positive (ΔE = +19 and +60 V) and more negative (ΔE = −35 and −0.09 V), respectively, than those of the corresponding SubP 409 (Table 26 and Fig. 115).612 The electrochemical HOMO–LUMO gaps for 475 (2.21 eV), arylamines 469–472 (2.10–2.46 eV) and 476 (2.36 eV) are calculated to be smaller than that of compound 409 (2.62 eV).
 Fig. 115  Cyclic voltammograms (black) and a differential pulse voltammogram (grey) of SubPs 409, 475 and 476. Supporting electrolyte: 0.10 M tetrabutylammonium hexafluorophosphate in CH2Cl2. Adapted with permission from ref. 612. Copyright 2013 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
The corresponding potentials of the meso-substituted nitro-SubP 555 and amino-SubP 557 (Scheme 48) are much more influenced. For 555, the redox features appear still more positively shifted appearing at 1.23 (oxidation, ΔE = +0.52 V) and −1.20 V (reduction, ΔE = +0.77 V), while for 557, they are negatively shifted to 0.11 V (oxidation, ΔE = −0.60) and −2.19 V (reduction, ΔE = −0.22 V).632 The effect of the amino group is comparable to that produced by another aliphatic amines such as 526 (Table 26). Together with axially substituted hydride- (597) and aminophenyl-SubPs (577), as well as meso-tris-ferrocene-SubP (405) and tris-thienyl-SubP 401, SubPs bearing amino groups directly attached to the meso positions (523–526) are among the easiest to electrochemically oxidize, with oxidation potentials ranging from −0.18 V for the p-dimethylaminoanilino-substituted compound 524 to 0.42 V for the p-nitroanilino-derivative 525.
The electrochemical potentials of SubP radical species 607–609 appear in the 0.20–0.47 V range for oxidation waves and −0.29 to −0.40 V range for reduction processes. On the basis of these values, the corresponding excitation energy gaps to the S1 state has been estimated to be in the 0.75–0.85 eV range.629,630,647


6.3.6 Theoretical calculations. 
DFT studies on the electronic structures and IR spectra of SubPs were reported with the exchange–correlation (XC) functional of Perdew, Burke and Ernzerhof (PBEPBE) coupled to the DGDZVP basis set.648 Besides, the electronic structure, absorption spectra, and 1H and 13C-NMR spectra of the hydroxyboron(III) SubP complex, as well as the free-base SubP, had been performed at the B3LYP/6-311G(2d,p)//B3LYP/6-31G(d) theory level.521 Both reports have been previously reviewed by some of us.1
Trends in the electronic structures and optical properties of ring contracted porphyrins have been analyzed by comparing the properties of SubPs with those of triphyrins and vacataporphyrins, using cyclic polyene perimeter-model approaches, such as Gouterman's four-orbital model and Michl's perimeter model.649
More recently, exploration of the ground and excited states to determine absorption and emission properties of SubPs and BzSubPs, including the 0–0 energies and the vibronic couplings, has been carried out using PCM-TD-DFT calculations. Comparison of the aromaticity of SubPs with that of other porphyrinoids, as well as the impact of meso-substitution on the SubP core, has been rationalized.215 The NICS values computed at the centers of the rings have been estimated to be of −20.6 for SubP and −19.1 for BzSubP, in line with those calculated for the corresponding triaza-analogues (−20.7 for SubPzs and −18.7 for SubPcs). With respect to the optical properties, the Q-bands have been predicted to involve changes in the electronic density upon photon absorption, to be mainly localized in the center of the macrocycles, with the pyrrolic nitrogens and the meso-atoms gaining electronic density, while the α-carbon atoms lose it (Fig. 116). The Soret band involves more delocalized excited states. For the benzo-analogues BzSubP and SubPc, the analysis of the vibrational modes signals two families of modes playing a key role: the first one at 500–750 cm−1, accounting for shoulder at ca. 15 nm from the 0–0 band, and the second one at 1400–1500 cm−1 being responsible for the presence of a secondary maximum in BzSubP, or the enlargement of the band in SubPc (Fig. 116).
 Fig. 116  Density difference plots (isovalue = 0.0008 a.u.) corresponding to the absorption of light of the first excited states involved in the Q (top) and Soret (bottom) bands of subporphyrinoid systems. Red (blue) regions indicate a gain (loss) of electronic density upon photon absorption. Reproduced with permission from ref. 215. Copyright 2017 American Chemical Society. 

6.4 Synthesis and properties of subporphyrin oligomers and complex systems

6.4.1 Subporphyrin oligomers connected at the meso positions. 
SubP dimers connected with biphenylene,583 bithiophene621 and butadiyne585 bridges, as well as complex dendritic carbazole-functionalized,590,591 or capped588 SubPs, had been previously reported and reviewed by some of us.1
More recently, a meso-to-meso directly linked SubP dimer 610 (Fig. 117) has been assembled in 72% yield by reductive coupling of bromo-SubP 508 in the presence of [Ni(cod)2] and 1,5-cyclooctadiene.604 Bis-SubP 610 shows slow rotation through the meso–meso bridge, with ΔH‡, ΔS‡ and ΔG‡298 values of the rotation barrier of 14.7 kcal mol−1, 1.28 cal mol−1 K−1, and 14.3 kcal mol−1, respectively. The SubP units show a mean bowl depth of 1.33 Å, and B–B distance of 7.21 Å, with a dihedral angle of 55.58° between the two subporphyrin cores. In accordance, the absorption spectrum of 610 shows a Soret-like band at 403 nm with a broad shoulder around 388 nm, and broadened Q-like bands with two peaks at 462 and 516 nm and a tail extending over 550 nm. Besides, 610 shows emission at 593 nm with a large Stokes shift (2520 cm−1). These optical features suggest conjugative interactions of the two SubP units due to the small rotational barrier of the meso–meso bond, in addition to excitonic interaction between the chromophores.
 Fig. 117  Structures of meso–meso-linked SubPs. 
SubP dimers connected with trans-ethylene (611) and acetylene (612) linkers (Fig. 117) have been prepared by Stille and Sonogashira coupling reactions, respectively, using bromo-SubP like 508 as the precursor. Specifically, treatment of 508 with trans-1,2-bis(tributylstannyl)ethene afforded 611 in 36% yield. Dimer 612 was prepared in 61% overall yield by using a three-step procedure consisting of Sonogashira reaction of 508 with ethynyltrimethylsilane, followed by deprotection and Sonogashira coupling of the triple bond with 508.650 The crystal structures of 611 and 612 show anti arrangements of the macrocycles favorable for electronic communication, with a slightly tilted (26.6°) ethylene linker, whereas the acetylene bridge is almost coplanar to the SubP rings. Indeed, both dimers show significantly red shifted and intensified Q-bands at 660 and 630 nm, respectively, with a tail reaching around 700–750 nm. The fluorescence bands are also bathochromically shifted and enhanced, indicating large structural changes in the S1-state, as a result of the effective conjugation between the two chromophores.

meso-Lithium SubP, obtained by bromine-lithium exchange of bromo-SubP derivatives such as 508, has been employed to synthesize SubP dimers 613, 614, and 615 (Fig. 117), by reaction with 1,2-dichlorotetramethylsilane, dimethyl carbonate, and formyl-SubP 548, respectively.631 The dihedral angles between the carbonyl group and the two SubP rings are small (33.7 and 23.6°, respectively) for the ketone 614, suitable for extended conjugation over the entire molecule. In fact, the corresponding absorption spectrum was found to be largely redshifted, owing to the effective electronic communication of the two SubP units through the carbonyl linker. On the other hand, the Si–Si bond takes a nearly perpendicular orientation in dimer 613 (87.9° with respect to the SubP core), favorable for σ–π conjugation, which contributes to slightly red-shifted Q-bands.
Other meso-to-meso linked SubP dimers have been connected through azo- (616), amino- (617), sulfide- (618) and disulfide- (619) bridges (Fig. 117). Oxidation of amino-SubP 557 with PbO2 afforded the meso-to-meso azo-SubP dimer 616 in 83% yield, presumably intermediated by the corresponding aminyl radical of 608 type.632 Dimer 616 shows fairly coplanar stacking arrangement favorable for electronic conjugative interaction. This results in largely perturbed and red-shifted absorption spectra, with split Soret (339, 423 nm) and Q-bands (654, 704 nm), accompanied by quenched fluorescence.
Bis-SubP amine 617 has been obtained in 46% yield by SNAr reaction of chloro-SubP 509 with amino-SubP 557.630 Due to its electron rich nature, dimer 617 is unstable under aerobic conditions, and tends to generate the corresponding aminyl radical as a fairly stable species.
S-bridged dimer 618 has been assembled in 43% yield by treating chloro-SubP 509 with 0.55 equiv. of Na2S·9H2O in the presence of catalytic amount of n-Bu4NBr. When 1.5 equiv. of Na2S·9H2O were employed for this reaction, the S–S-linked dimer 619 was obtained in 35% yield, presumably arising from aerobic oxidation of the corresponding monomeric SubP-thiol during the workup.651 The average bowl depth for SubP units in dimer 618 is of 1.38 Å, with a dihedral angle between the two macrocycles of 86.7°. This orthogonal arrangement precludes electronic interaction between the two chromophores, giving rise to red shifted fluorescence, as well as split Soret band, together with broad Q-bands; the optical features have been rationalized in terms of exciton coupling between the two SubP entities. A large Stokes shift of 2670 cm−1 is indicative of substantial structural changes in the excited state. The SubP components of disulfide 619 experience less electronic perturbation than the sulfide counterpart and quenched fluorescence, owing to the very short lifetime of its S1 excited state.


6.4.2 Subporphyrin dimers connected at the β-positions. 

syn- and anti-β-to-β 1,3-butadiyne-bridged SubP dimers 620 (Scheme 54) were prepared in around 30% yields by deprotection reaction of 2,13-bis-(tert-butyldimethylsilylethynyl)-SubP 485, followed by oxidative dimerization by treatment with Cu(OAc)2.617 Both syn- and anti-isomers show in their crystal structures non-equivalent CC units and distortions of the triple bonds, with no substantial differences in their butadiyne C–C bond lengths. The average SubP bowl depths are of 1.46 Å for the syn- and of 1.50 Å for the anti-isomers, with B–B distances of 11.92 and 11.97 Å, respectively. Both syn- and anti-dimers exhibit split Soret bands at 409 and 453 nm and 406 and 441 nm, respectively, accompanied by bathochromically shifted Q-bands at 557, 583, and 554 nm, correspondingly, as diagnostic of large electronic coupling between the two macrocycles. The differences in the absorption profiles of the two isomers have been explained in terms of exciton coupling using dipole–dipole approximation developed by Kasha.652
 Scheme 54  Synthesis of β-to-β 1,3-butadiyne-bridged SubP dimers 620. 
Directly β-to-β-connected dimer 623 was synthesized by reductive coupling of β-iodo SubP 622, which was in turn prepared by controlled borylation of SubP 621 and subsequent iodination (Scheme 55).619 In order to accomplish the peripheral borylation, the axial group must be changed to a nonpolar tolyl. Dimer 623 was obtained as an inseparable 1:1 mixture of DL and meso-diastereomers, from which preferential crystallization of the DL-diastereomer was achieved. In the solid state, DL to meso isomerization was not observed. However, pure DL-diastereomer was isomerized in methanol at 50 °C, giving a diastereomeric 2:1 mixture of DL- and meso-623, thus suggesting the occurrence of SubP bowl inversion. Because of the lesser steric congestion around the β-positions, the average Cβ–Cβ bond length in DL-623 is of 1.44 Å, and the dihedral angle between the two macrocycles is of 29.3°, much smaller than that observed for the meso-to-meso dimer 610. DL-623 shows a red-shifted Soret band that is rationalized in terms of exciton coupling between the two SubPs, in addition to sharp Q-bands at 464 and 509 nm. Observed smaller Stokes shift (1160 cm−1) related to that of other SubP dimers indicates less structural relaxation of DL-623 in the excited state.
 Scheme 55  Synthesis of the β-to-β-dimers 623. 


6.4.3 Subporphyrin systems connected through the axial positions. 
Some SubP arrays connected at the axial position20,36 and μ-oxodimers637 have been reviewed by some of us.1 As explained above, the peroxo-bridged subporphyrin dimer 605 has been prepared by heating hydroxo-peroxide 602.641

meso-Axially attached SubP trimer 625 has been obtained serendipitously by SNAr reaction of chloro-SubP 509 with potassium hydroxide.647 The formation of the triangular trimer has been rationalized by considering a spontaneous self-assembly of the SubP alcohol 624 (Scheme 56).
 Scheme 56  Synthesis of meso-to-axial cyclic SubP trimer 625. 
In 625, the SubP bowl depths are of 1.267, 1.354 and 1.356 Å. Its UV-vis spectrum displays a split Soret band, suggestive of strong through-space exciton coupling between the neighboring SubP units, enabled by the short interchromophoric separation distance of 7 Å. The trimer was monomerized to the corresponding meso-hydroxy-SubP 624 upon treatment with methanol.
More recently, a related meso-axially attached SubP dimer 626 has been assembled by Shimizu and co-workers in 9% yield, through an ether cleavage reaction of the A2B-type subporphyrin bearing an ortho-anisyl substituent 431 (Scheme 57). Despite the spatial proximity between the two subporphyrin chromophores in 626, the dimer exhibited similar UV-vis absorption, MCD and fluorescence spectra than the corresponding monomer, indicating minor interchromophore interactions in 626.653
 Scheme 57  Synthesis of meso-to-axial cyclic SubP dimer 626. 


6.4.4 Fused subporphyrin dimers. 

meso-to-meso′ and β-to-β′-doubly linked SubP dimer 627 (Fig. 118) was prepared as a single anti-isomer by selective dibromination of DL-623 at the two free-meso-positions followed by coupling in the presence of [Ni(cod)2].619 The average bowl-depth for the SubPs is of 1.42 Å in 627, and the dihedral angles between the two macrocycles are of 22.9° at the meso-position and 17.3° at the β-position, that is, smaller than that of DL-623 and favorable for extended π-conjugation. In fact, the elongation of the conjugation produces an absorption spectrum for 627 displaying broad and red-shifted B and Q-bands at 444 and 641 nm, respectively, the latter with a tail reaching around 850 nm (Fig. 119), and almost completely quenched fluorescence.654
 Fig. 118  Structures of 627–631. 
 Fig. 119  UV-vis-NIR absorption spectra of 438 and 627–630 in CH2Cl2. Adapted with permission from ref. 655. Copyright 2017 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Doubly Pt-bridged SubP dimer 628 was synthesized in 64% yield as a sole syn-isomer by treating β,β′-diboryl-SubP j (Scheme 44) with Pt(cod)Cl2 in the presence of CsF and COD. 628 was oxidized with magic blue to give 629, which was subsequently refluxed in toluene, in the presence of 1,3-bis(diphenylphosphino)propane, affording triply linked SubP dimer 630 as a result of reductive elimination.655 The crystal structure of dimer 628 revealed a square planar Pt ion coordinated in a cis-manner and holding a face-to-face SubP arrangement. The triply fused dimer 630 takes a domed structure with a positive Gaussian curvature. The calculated bowl-depth of the SubP halves is of 1.65 Å, much larger than that of SubP monomers, and indicating that the triple linkage forces additional structural strain. 630 displays absorption bands at 315, 403, 500, 810, and 942 nm (Fig. 119). A narrow optical HOMO–LUMO gap clearly suggests the effective conjugation extending over the whole molecule. The absorption bands at 315, 403, and 500 nm have been assigned as split Soret-like bands. The absorption spectrum of 629 is clearly different from that of 627, but similar to that of 630, suggesting substantial interaction through the Pt-linkage.

syn- and anti-β,β-Dithiino-SubP dimers 631 were prepared through a seven-step synthetic procedure and separated by chromatography at the end. The sequence started from arylsulfanyl-Sub 467 (Fig. 101), and had a key step consisting in the SNAr reaction between a SubP β,β′-thiol and a SubP β-Br,β-sulfone.656 Dimers 631 are conformationally very flexible, as indicated by analysis of different crystals. Hence, while the crystals of the anti-isomer showed a completely planar configuration, distinct crystals of the syn-isomer showed various conformations, going from a bent-towards-the-convex-sides structure to an almost planar conformation. The UV-vis spectra exhibit similar features for both isomers, characterized by split Soret bands arising from exciton coupling between the two SubP macrocycles, accompanying a Q-band around 490 nm. Both syn- and anti-631 formed 1:1 cocrystallates upon treatment with C60 and C70. In addition, the syn-isomer was able to capture both fullerenes in toluene, with high association constants that exceed 106 M−1.


6.4.5 Subporphyrin-based hybrid systems. 
The preparation of some SubP hybrids containing bodipy,623 1,4-quinone,589 ferrocene592 and porphyrin657 have been previously reviewed by some of us.1 For the latter, fast excitation energy transfer processes from the SubP to the porphyrin units have been reported to occur. These photoinduced events are enabled by the π-conjugative elongation of the SubP onto the phenylene linkers, as if SubP and ZnP were directly attached.658

meso- and axially appended SubP-based electron donor–acceptor (D–A) conjugates, have been assembled using 1,1,4,4-tetracyanobuta-1,3-diene (TCBD)–aniline (SubPs 632 and 633) and naphthalenediimide (SubPs 634) as the strong electron-acceptor counterparts. SubPs 632 and 633 were prepared by CA–RE reactions of meso-alkynyl-SubP 516 and axial alkynyl 584-like SubP, respectively, with TCNE.45 The crystal structures show highly nonplanar conformations for the TCBC units, leading to two enantiomers that are obtained as racemates in 632 and 633. The two R and S enantiomers of 633 were separated by chiral HPLC, while the corresponding meso-isomers 632 displayed structural flexibility that precluded the corresponding enantiomeric separation. The electronic interaction between the SubP and the TCBD–aniline unit is also influenced by the position to which the acceptor unit is attached. In particular, the attachment at the axial position in 633 produces less perturbation, since a large HOMO–LUMO gap of 2.12 eV disables any emissive CT state, and enforces an emissive exciplex deactivation in apolar environments next to the SubP-centered fluorescence; in polar environments, a CS state is generated.
Contrasting, the meso-substituted SubP 632 showed strong electronic coupling between the donor and acceptor units, owing to the very reduced HOMO–LUMO gap of 1.77 eV, enabling an emissive CT state in virtually all environments, although it is only in polar environments, where it links a local excitation with an indirect CS.
Five dyads, namely, 634, 6342Me, 6343Me, 6342,6Me, 6343,6Me (see Fig. 120) were synthesized by Suzuki–Miyaura coupling with bromo-SubP 508 with the corresponding NDI-Bpin derivatives.659 The absorption and emission spectra of all the dyads were very similar to the sum of their components, with the SubP chromophore dominating the spectra because of its much larger molar absorptivity with respect to the NDI unit. Measurements on the fluorescence decays for the five dyads show that there is a systematic variation of the singlet excited state lifetime with the substitution pattern ranging from 5–15 ps, which corresponds to near quantitative CS. TA measurements revealed that both CS and CR times vary systematically with steric bulk, reflecting that the electronic coupling between the SubP and the NDI becomes smaller as the dihedral angles between the two subunits are increased.
 Fig. 120  Structures of hybrids 632–634. The number 2 in 6342Me indicates the position of the methyl substituent(s) on the phenyl spacer. 634 alone indicates no methyl group. 

6.5 Applications of subporphyrin and subporphyrin hybrids
Compared to SubPcs, studies on the applications of SubPs to different technological fields still remain in their infancy. Axial functionalization of BzSubP 387 with a 5-mercaptopentyloxy unit to give 635 (Fig. 121) allowed to anchor the dye to the Au(111) surface via reaction with 1-heptanethiol linkers that were preorganized as a self-assembled monolayer (C7S SAM) on the Au(111) substrate, so that the SubP was electronically decoupled from the metal surface. STM differential conductance (dI/dV − V) measurements with the bare W tip exhibited a broad gap region of low conductance and three distinct responses at 2.4, −1.3, and −2.1 V.636
 Fig. 121  Structures of BzSubP 635 and SubPs 636–640. 
SubP-saponite organoclays based on quaternized SubPs 391 and 393 have been studied in terms of their luminescence properties.624 In addition, a photochemical reaction system, using a metalloporphyrin as a photocatalyst and quaternized SubP 393 as a photoantenna, has been constructed on a clay nanosheet as an artificial photosynthetic model. Efficient energy transfer from the SubP to the metalloporphyrin of up to 98% was achieved. Such dye–clay complexes have proven to photocatalyze the conversion of cyclohexene using a wide range of visible region without any energy loss.577
In another example, a dye-sensitised photoanode for visible light-driven water oxidation to O2 has been constructed using the push–pull SubP 636 (Fig. 121). The dye has been integrated as the photosensitizer in a photoelectrochemical cell with a TiO2 electrode, together with [RuII(bda)(pic)2] (bda = 2,2′-bipyridine-6,6′-dicarboxylic acid, pic = 4-picoline), which works as the water oxidation catalyst.578 Structurally referable push–pull SubPs 637–640 were investigated as photosensitizers in DSSCs achieving power conversion efficiencies (PCE) of 6.0–10.1.576
6.6 Subporphyrin analogues

6.6.1 Borenium cations. 
Borenium cations (SubP)B+ (Fig. 122) are always detected in positive ion mass spectrometry of boron SubPs and constitute feasible intermediates in axial ligand exchange reactions under acidic conditions.640 Their spectroscopic properties have been reviewed by some of us.1 The SubP borenium cations were isolated by Osuka and co-workers as stable carborane salts (see for example 641 in Fig. 122) in 58–91% yield by treating the corresponding SubP derivatives with Et3Si[CH6B11Br6].640 The crystal structure of the borenium cations is almost planar (bowl-depth of 0.114 Å) with B–N bond lengths significantly shorter than for the neutral species, reflecting the positive charge and the hybridization change of the central boron from sp3 to sp2.
 Fig. 122  Structures of borenium cation (SubP)B+ as carborane anion salt and bacteriochlorines. 


6.6.2 Modifications on the subporphyrin pyrrole rings. 
A3-type subchlorins 642–644 (Scheme 58) have been prepared by Osuka and co-workers by reduction of the “quasi-isolated” pyrrolic double bond of the parallel SubPs 390, 399, 407 with p-tosylhydrazide (method A) (Table 27).587 The use of Ni–Ra, affords the corresponding subbacteriochlorins 649–652 (Fig. 122), which are obtained with comparable yields of 21–47%.660 Panda and co-workers have prepared A3 (648) and A2B-subchlorins (645–647) in 6–11% yields directly from the complexation of the tripyrrane f wherein R1 constitute ester groups, followed by reaction with triethyl orthoformate603,661 or acyl chloride derivatives661 under standard conditions for SubP synthesis (Scheme 58 method B) (Table 27).
 Scheme 58  Synthesis of chlorins 642–648.

Subbacteriochlorins can be oxidized with Ag2O to the corresponding Subchlorins, with higher overall yields than those obtained in the one step procedure with the hydrazide.660 In turn, Subchlorins can also be oxidized to the corresponding SubPs using DDQ.
As SubPs, Subchlorins and Subbacteriochlorins exhibit a 14π-electron aromatic circuit and conical geometry, with bowl-depths of 1.26 Å, although the degree of aromaticity of the three compounds is different, as inferred from their NMR data. Accordingly, the nuclear independent chemical shift (NICS) values calculated at B3LYP/6-311G(d) level are of −10.9 ppm for 649 < −14.9 ppm for 642 < −18.9 ppm for 390.659 The different aromatic characters of the three compounds are also reflected by their optical properties, with decrease and blue shift of the Soret bands in 642 and 649, related to that of 390, while the fluorescence quantum yields follow the order 642 < 390 < 649 (Fig. 123). Comparatively, the oxidation potentials of Subbacteriochlorins are lower than those of the corresponding Subchlorins, and these are in turn lower than those of the parallel SubP. For example, the potential values for the first oxidation peaks are of 0.00 V for 649, 0.42 V for 642 and 0.78 V for 390.587,660
 Fig. 123  Comparative spectrum of SubP, subchlorin and subbacteriochlorin. Adapted with permission from ref. 660. Copyright 2011 American Chemical Society. 
Pyrrole cleaving modifications have been performed in SubPs giving rise to Subporphyrin analogues with different structural, chemical and electronic properties. Thus, the Subseco-chlorins 656 and 657 have been prepared through controlled monobromination of 574, followed by substitution to give the alcohol 654, which was oxidized to dioxoSubchlorin 655. The latter was transformed into the Subseco-chlorin 656, and subsequently decarboxylated to 657.614 Most importantly, the dioxo- and Subseco-chlorins have been used in a variety of chemical transformations to give different core modified SubP analogues,614 such as subporpholactone 664, subporpholactama 665, imidazoloSubP 666,662 dithiazoloSubchlorin 667, dithiazinoSubchlorin 668, oxodithiazinoSubchlorin 669, dioxodithiazinoSubchlorin 670,663 and quinoxalino-fused SubPs 671–674,664 as described in Scheme 59 and Table 28.

6.6.3 Subpyriporphyrins and [3]cyclo-1,8-carbazolylene. 
Subpyridoporphyrin 675 represents another type of core-modified SubPs that contains a pyridine ring replacing one of the pyrrole moieties.1,665 Reported by Latos-Grażyński before SubPs, it was prepared in 6% yield through monobenzoylation, reduction and intramolecular condensation reactions, starting from a pyrido tripyrrane k (Scheme 60). As a distinctive structural feature, Subpyridoporphyrin is obtained as a free base triphyrin, which can react with PhBCl2 to yield the metal complex 676 where the macrocycle acts as monoanionic tridentate ligand. The coordination leads to aromatization of the macrocycle because of structural changes allowing delocalization of 14π electrons. The electronic spectrum of 675 displays two bands at 346 and 730 nm, respectively, that are parallel to the B and Q-porphyrin absorption bands.666 The changes induced upon metalation are not great, apart from a slight bathochromic shift of the broad Q-band with respect to the free base.
 Scheme 60  Synthesis of subpyriporphyrins 675 and 676. 
[3]Cyclo-1,8-carbazolylenes 677 and 678 constitute even more exotic tripyrrole-based porphyrinoids that have been prepared in 11–16% yields by Higashibayashi and co-workers. The synthetic procedure involves Ni(COD)2-mediated cyclo-oligomerization of 1,8-dibromocarbazole (Scheme 61).667 X-ray analysis revealed a distorted, flake-shaped C2 structure with three chiral axes that possesses axial chirality, the relative configuration of which is expressed as (S*,R*,R*). Free base 678 represents a trivalent tridentate ligand that hosts boron, phosphorus, and silicon atoms (Scheme 61, compounds 679–681). The introduction of the different heteroatoms has been achieved by treatment with BBr3, PCl3 or PhSiCl3, respectively, in the presence of a base. In addition, the phosphorus atom of 681 has been oxidized with m-CPBA, leading to the phosphine oxide 682.
 Scheme 61  Preparation of [3]cyclo-1,8-carbazolylenes. 
X-ray diffraction analysis of the complexes 679, 681 and 682, as well as DFT calculations on 680, revealed a location for the central heteroatoms above the curved surface. The absorption profile of 677–682 is dominated by a strong band (ε = 30000–40000 M−1 cm−1) at 301–317 nm, accompanied by weaker bands (ε = 5000–17000 M−1 cm−1) at 356–415 nm, while their emission bands appear at 421–440 nm, with fluorescence quantum yields ranging from 0.06 (681) to 0.34 (680).


6.6.4 [14]Triphyrins(2.1.1). 

meso-Aryl-substituted [14]triphyrin(2.1.1) and [14]benzotriphyrin(2.1.1) derivatives reported by Shen, Yamada and Kobayashi, represent SubP analogues containing one additional meso-carbon in its structure. The insertion of the second sp2-hybridized carbon atom between one of the three neighboring pairs of pyrroles produces nearly planar, metal-free, contracted porphyrinoids, containing 14 π-electron aromatic circuits.668 Synthetic aspects and spectroscopic properties of [14]triphyrins(2.1.1) have been previously reviewed by some of us,1 and others.5,649,669,670
The synthesis of [14]triphyrins(2.1.1) can be performed in one step with 10–40% yields, by condensation reaction between a pyrrole n (Scheme 62, method A) and an aryl aldehyde in the presence of high concentration (1.2 equivalents) of BF3·OEt2.668 An azafulvene or an azafulvenium cation have been proposed as feasible intermediates of the cyclization reaction to assemble the triphyrin(2.1.1) ring.671 When 3,4-diethylpyrrole is used as the starting material, the corresponding hexaethyl-substituted [14]triphyrin(2.1.1) 683 is obtained (Table 29).672 4,7-Dihydro-4,7-ethano-2H-isoindole (BCOD-pyrrole) provides macrocycle 684 (Table 29), which has been quantitatively transformed into the corresponding [14]tribenzotriphyrin(2.1.1) 685 by retro Diels–Alder reaction. Triphyrins 686–689 (Table 29) were prepared by a similar Rothemund–Diels–Alder sequence, as well as the analogous [14]trinaphthotriphyrin(2.1.1) 691. Compounds 685, 686, and 688 have been used as efficient catalysts for direct C–H arylation of benzene with aryl halides,673 while 687 catalyses the two-electron reduction of O2 by Me8Fc, to produce H2O2.674
 Scheme 62  Methods for the synthesis of [14]triphyrin(2.1.1).

Three stepwise procedures have been reported for the synthesis of [14]tribenzotriphyrins(2.1.1). Method B (Scheme 62) consists in the McMurry coupling of the diformyltripyrrane intermediate o, followed by oxidation. The latter has been used to prepare the unsubstituted triphyrin at the meso-positions 692 (Table 29).675 Moreover, the reaction of dipyrroethane-derivative p with an aromatic aldehyde in the presence of TFA (method C, Scheme 62) allowed to prepare the [14]triphyrin(2.1.1) 693 (Table 29) bearing different substitution at the meso-positions.676 The mechanism for method C should involve partial cleavage of the dipyrrole intermediate p or an intermediate evolved from it, and formation of a free pyrrole.676 A most recent [2+1] procedure that allowed to prepare meso-arylated [14]triphyrins(2.1.1) 694–700 in 15–18% yields (method D) consists in the reduction of acylated dipyrroethene intermediates q (Scheme 62) with NaBH4, leading to the corresponding dipyrroethene-dicarbinol, which is in situ condensed with pyrrole in the presence of trifluoroacetic acid, followed by oxidation with DDQ in open air.677,678 Owing to the availability of the precursors n and q, Methods A and D have general applicability to the synthesis of various symmetric and non-symmetric [14]triphyrins(2.1.1), respectively, while the scope of methods B and C is very much restricted to compounds 691 and 692 so far.
There are very scarce examples on the pyrrole or meso-fabrication of triphyrins. Two ethynyl-ferrocene substituents have been introduced at the two-meso-methylene bridges by Sonogashira coupling reaction.678 Likewise, one of the β-pyrrole positions of the tilted pyrrole ring (see crystal structure below) has been selectively monobrominated, and subsequently subjected to Sonogashira coupling with ethynyl-ferrocene.678
The crystal structure of triphyrin 696 reveals the presence of one 1H-pyrrole and two 2H-pyrrole rings, the former attached to the meso-carbon of the ethene bridge and with strong hydrogen-bonding interactions with the two 2H-pyrrole nitrogen atoms. As a consequence, the triphyrin core shows an almost planar arrangement, except for the 2H-pyrrole ring opposite to the ethene bridge, which is slightly tilted (16.6°) from the mean plane defined by the four meso-carbons.677 Two of the meso-phenyl substituents display a dihedral angle of ∼77° each, with respect to the plane of the triphyrin, whereas the corresponding dihedral angles of the other two are of 46.7° and 36.9°, respectively. The strong triple hydrogen bonding presumably compensates the macrocycle's diamagnetic ring current effect, therefore, the resonances of the [14]triphyrin(2.1.1) inner NH protons lie at remarkably low field despite their calculated NICS(0) values ranging from −9.15 to −15.6 ppm.668,672,677 Similar structures have been reported for triphyrins 692 and 693.675,676 The crystal structure of the benzo-fused triphyrin 685 also shows 16.3° tilting for the corresponding isoindole ring situated opposite to the ethene bridge. But conversely to the pyrrole series, a rapid exchange between the two NH tautomers is suggested in the solid state.668
The optical spectra of the [14]triphyrin (2.1.1) free bases are dominated by an intense band between 350 and 450 nm and weaker bands in the 500–650 nm region (Fig. 124), which have been assigned to allowed and forbidden B and Q bands, respectively, by analogy with Gouterman's four-orbital model for tetrapyrrole porphyrinoids. Thus, for example, tetraphenyl-substituted triphyrin 696 exhibits a B-band at 380 (ε = 5012) nm, in addition to three Q-absorptions at 478 (631), 528 (794) and 564 (794) nm.677 Fusing benzene and naphthalene rings to the [14]triphyrin(2.1.1) skeleton produces red shifts both of the B and Q bands, with concomitant increase of the latter (Fig. 124a), as a diagnostic for extended conjugation.672,679 Similar effects are observed in the MCD spectra of the same compounds (Fig. 124b).672
 Fig. 124  (a) Comparative electronic absorption spectra of a [14]triphyrin(2.1.1) (690), benzo-fused (687) and naphtho-fused (691) derivatives in toluene. (b) MCD spectra of a [14]triphyrin(2.1.1) (690), benzo-fused (687) and naphtho-fused (691) derivatives in toluene. Adapted with permission from ref. 672. Copyright 2011 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Some [14]triphyrins(2.1.1) are weakly fluorescent, showing an emission band around 560 nm (695–698), or 580 nm (700).676–678 The tribenzo- and trinaphtho-series display their emission bands bathochromically shifted at 596 and 628 nm, respectively, with very low quantum yields of 0.01 and 0.04, respectively. Time-resolved fluorescence and fluorescence anisotropy measurements point to a considerable influence of the overall planarity, which is enhanced by the peripheral fused moieties, as the main the factor affecting the excited-state lifetimes,679 excluding any role of internal proton transfer (IPT) in the relaxation of the excited states.680 In fact, molecular distortion, which is produced by meso-substituents and reduced by the inner hydrogen bond, plays a major role in suppressing the non-radiative relaxation.681
The [14]triphyrin (2.1.1) free bases are electrochemically easier to reduce and more difficult to oxidize than the corresponding SubPs. Thus, for comparison, tetrabenzo-triphyrin 696 shows two reversible reductions at −0.84 and −1.25 V (1.13 V more positive than SubP 390) and one irreversible oxidation at 1.32 V (0.61 V anodically shifted with respect to SubP (390).677 As described below, the difference in the redox potentials of the two types of subporphyrinoids triphyrin and SubP arises from the additional meso carbon, rather than from the lack of central boron atom.
[14]Triphyrin(2.1.1) can function as a cyclic, monoanionic, tridentate ligand,669 and coordinate metal ions such as rhenium(I),672,675,677,682 manganese(I),675,683 ruthenium(II),672 or platinum(IV),684 leading to octahedral complexes (Fig. 126b), while rhodium(III) produces square pyramidal conformations,685 and B(III) provides tetrahedral structures (Fig. 125 and Table 30).686 In addition, η5-cyclopentadienyliron(II) and η1,η3-C8H12 iridium(III) triphyrin sandwich complexes, in which Fe(II) and Ir(III) are inserted between Cp and COD ligands, respectively, and one triphyrin macrocycle, have been reported.687,688 With the exception of the Pt(IV) complexes, which are obtained upon oxidation of the corresponding Pt(II) complexes, all of the other triphyrin complexes can be prepared by heating a free base with the appropriate metal salt. The resulting complexes are bowl-shaped, with the metal ions lying above the plane containing the three inner nitrogens. The bowl-depth for the rhenium(I) complexes has been reported to range from 2.2–2.5 Å, about 1 Å larger than those of boron(III) SubPs (see Tables 24 and 30). Similar curvature has been observed for Mn(I) complexes, although the reported bowl-depth values are about 0.2–0.3 Å shorter than those reported for the Re(I) complexes, and in the same order as that observed for the Rh(III), Ir(III) and Pt(IV) complexes bases (Fig. 126).684,685,688 On the other hand, Fe(II) complexes show a less pronounced curvature (∼1.8 Å),687 and B(III) complexes result to be much flatter, with bowl depths of 0.93–1.03 Å, even shorter than that of the parallel SubP 574 (Tables 24 and 30).686

[14]Triphyrin (2.1.1) can also function as a neutral bidentate ligand with Pt(II)684 and Pd(II) ions,689 leading to structures like 723–725 (Fig. 125 and Table 30) with deep, saddle conformation and Cs symmetry bases (Fig. 126a). The coordination mode for these complexes is square-planar, with cis-conformation for the Pt(II) and Pd(II) ions. Here, the non-coordinated pyrrole rings tilt downward from the mean plane defined by the four meso-carbon atoms, while the two coordinated pyrrole rings are tilted upward (Fig. 126a).
In general, the optical spectra of triphyrin complexes are very similar, with absorption bands in the Q-region that are more extended to the red and infrared regions than those of the corresponding free bases (Fig. 126). The intense bands in the 450–550 nm region of the spectra of the metal complexes and the weaker envelopes of absorbance intensity to the red of the Q bands (450–800 nm) have been assigned to metal to ligand charge transfer (MLCT) bands on the basis of TD-DFT calculations.672,675,677,687,688 The absorption properties of the boron complexes 718 and 719 are exceptionally more similar to that of the free bases than those of the other metal derivatives.686 Besides, the insertion of the boron atom in 718 and 719 does not bring the corresponding redox potentials near to those of SubPs. On the contrary, the oxidation and reduction features of the boron complexes appear relatively similar to those of the parallel free bases 692 and 696, at −1.38 and −0.83 V for 718 and −1.16, −0.78 and +1.27 V for 719,686 pointing to a little effect of the central boron atom in the electrochemical differences between SubPs and [14]triphyrin (2.1.1). Other central ions such as rhenium(I), produce further anodic shifts on the corresponding oxidation and reduction peaks (for example, −1.12, −0.65 and +1.35 V for 706).677


6.6.5 Other triphyrins. 
Triphyrin analogues comprise a variety of macrocycles in which one or more pyrrole units of the triphyrin structure have been replaced by carbocycles, like in carbatriphyrins(3.1.1),690 carbatriphyrins(2.1.1) (726Fig. 126),691 azulitriphyrins(1.2.1) (727),692,693 or other types of carbatriphyrins,694 including the triphyrin(2.1.1)-tetraphyrin(1.1.1.1) hybrid 737.695 Substitution of one pyrrole ring by other heterocycles also produces triphyrin analogues. Many of these works have been performed in the laboratories of H. Yamada, M. Pawlicki or Latos-Grażyński. For example, replacement by a thiophene ring leads to thiatriphyrins(2.1.1) (728 and 729).696,697 The oxatriphyrin appellative refers to an assortment of structures containing a furan unit, of which [14]oxatriphyrin(2.1.1) (730) constitutes the simpler representative.698 Other oxatriphyrins contain additional rings, like an ortho-phenylene motif (731),699 or a fused benzene ring,697 or a thiophene ring (732, 733),700,701 benzofuran (735) or benzothiophene rings (734),702 as well as dibenzofuran or dibenzothiophene rings.703 An additional furan moiety (736) leads to oxatriphyrin(3.1.1) (Fig. 127).704,705
 Fig. 127  Some examples of triphyrin analogues containing carbocycle or heterocycle subunits. 
Other modifications consist in the insertion of meso-bridges of different nature between the three triphyrin pyrrole rings. For example, the so-called vacataphyrins 738 (Fig. 128) consist of a [18]triphyrin(6.1.1) structure,706 while triphyrin 739 consists of a [15]triphyrin(1.1.3) derivative.707 [22]Triphyrin(6.6.0) (740) and [22]Triphyrin(6.5.0) (741) represent examples of what has been called expanded porphyrin contraction.708,709 In 742 one of the meso-units consists of an aminoethoxy moiety,710 while [18]triphyrins(4.1.1) containing ethyne (743 and 744)711–713 or butadiene714 bridges have been reported. All of these structures contain completely different macrocyclic cores and hence, new and diverse coordination chemistry.669
 Fig. 128  Some examples of triphyrin analogues containing different motifs as meso-bridges. 

7. Conclusions
Since the serendipitous synthesis of the first subporphyrinoid in 1972, the chemistry of tripyrrole and triisoindole porphyrin analogues has expanded considerably. This review, which comprehensively covers the last decade of discoveries, documents the exponential growth in the number of publications related to subporphyrinoids and highlights the scientific interest in this class of compounds for many application areas ranging from optoelectronics to biomedicine.
On one hand, big steps forward have been made during the last years in the fundamentals of subporphyrinoids chemistry. The development and optimization of novel methodologies for the synthesis and post-functionalization of these contracted macrocycles keep drawing attention, leading to the possibility of finely tuning the optoelectronic properties of these derivatives. Importantly, tremendous progress has been made in understanding the supramolecular organization of these cone-shaped cores. On the other hand, the application of subporphyrinoids is gaining more and more attention from the scientific community due to the outstanding physicochemical characteristics that these macrocycles offer. The field of subporphyrinoid-base systems for solar energy conversion has blossomed during the last decade, paving the way for the effective implementation of these derivatives in photovoltaics. Moreover, significant advances have been made in the exploitation of this contracted porphyrinoids for therapy and imaging in bio-focused application.
Nevertheless, this upward trend is still at an early stage, and many challenging issues at both the fundamental and application levels remain unexplored. The preparation and exploitation of chiral subporphyrinoids constitutes one of the hottest topics in the frame of the optoelectronic applications of these derivatives. Although initial studies on the application of SubPcs in solar energy conversion schemes have shown the great potential of this class of compound, future efforts are needed to optimize the architecture and the performances of SubPc-containing photovoltaic devices. On the other hand, more comprehensive studies are needed in the frame of the biological applications of SubPcs. Certainly, the development of subporphyrinoid-based applications requires further efforts and fundamental progresses in the chemistry of these macrocycles.
We hope that this review, in which the most recent advances in subporphyrinoids are presented and discussed, will inspire and boost further research in this area, contributing to unveil the fundamentals of this fascinating class of compounds, and leading to innovative developments in their known implementations in optoelectronics and biomedicine as well as in yet unexplored applications.

Table Content:

Giulia Lavarda
	Giulia Lavarda studied Chemistry at the Università degli Studi di Padova (Italy), where she obtained her BSc in 2012 and her MSc in 2014. Then she moved to the Universidad Autónoma de Madrid (Spain), where she completed her PhD in Organic Chemistry under the supervision of Prof. T. Torres focusing on the synthesis and study of novel subphthalocyanine-based systems. During this period, she realized predoctoral stays in the laboratory of Prof. A. Osuka at Kyoto University (Japan), working on the preparation of metalated subporphyrin complexes, and in the laboratory of Prof. D. M. Guldi at Friedrich Alexander University Erlangen-Nürnberg (Germany), where she studied the photophysics of porphyrinoid-containing multicomponent systems. Currently, she is Marie Skłodowska-Curie postdoctoral researcher in Prof. E. W. Meijer's group at the Eindhoven University of Technology (The Netherlands), where she investigates the fundamentals and applications of supramolecular polymers.

Jorge Labella
	Jorge Labella studied Chemistry at the Universidad de Alcalá, where he obtained his BSc with honours in 2017. Afterwards, he moved to the Universidad Autónoma de Madrid to complete his MSc in organic chemistry (honours) in 2018. He is currently working on his PhD in Organic Chemistry under the supervision of Prof. Tomás Torres, funded by a FPU scholarship. During this period, he has realized a predoctoral stay in the laboratory of Prof. Md. K. Nazeeruddin at École Polytechnique Fédérale de Lausanne (Switzerland) to work on the fabrication of perovskite solar cells. Currently, he is realizing a second predoctoral stay in the laboratory of Prof. Jonathan Sessler at the University of Texas at Austin (United States), where he is working on expanded porphyrins. His research is mainly focused on the preparation of novel SubPcs, and their application in photovoltaics, chirality, and supramolecular chemistry.

M. Victoria Martínez-Díaz
	M. Victoria Martínez-Díaz, Full Professor of Organic Chemistry at Autónoma University of Madrid, obtained her PhD in Chemistry from this university under the supervision of Prof. Tomás Torres working on Supramolecular Chemistry, on the selective recognition of chiral ammonium ions mediated by heterocycle-containing receptors. After a year postdoc at CEA Saclay (France), addressing the organization of amphiphilic functional molecules at the air–water interface using the Langmuir–Blodgett (LB) technique, she joined for two years the group of Sir Prof. Fraser J Stoddart at the School of Chemistry of the University of Birmingham (UK). Her current research interest is focused on the synthesis and organization of phthalocyanines and subphthalocyanines-based systems with potential application on organic photovoltaics and nanomedicine.

M. Salomé Rodríguez-Morgade
	M. Salomé Rodríguez-Morgade is a Full Professor at the Universidad Autónoma de Madrid and a Senior Researcher at the Institute for Advanced Research in Chemical Sciences (IAdChem) at UAM/Spain. Since 1999, she is integrated in the Nanoscience and Molecular Materials' group at UAM. Her scientific activity is concerned with the synthesis and study of molecular materials based on phthalocyanines and other azaporphyrins. Her current research interests are focused on the design and development of phthalocyanines and new porphyrinoids such as subporphyrazines and analogues for their application as photosensitizers in photodynamic therapy (PDT) and molecular photovoltaics.

Atsuhiro Osuka
	Atsuhiro Osuka started an academic career at the Department of Chemistry of Ehime University as an assistant professor. In 1984, he moved to the Department of Chemistry of Kyoto University, where he became a professor of chemistry in 1996. In 2022, he moved to Hunan Normal University as a distinguished professor. His research interests cover development of porphyrin-related compounds with novel structures, electronic systems, and functions.

Tomás Torres
	Tomás Torres is Head of the Institute for Advanced Research in Chemical Sciences (IAdChem) and Full Professor of Organic Chemistry at the Autónoma University of Madrid (UAM) and Associated Senior Scientist at the IMDEA Nanoscience. In addition to various aspects of synthetic and supramolecular chemistry, his current research interests include the preparation and study of optical properties of organic functional materials. His group, that presently consists of twenty-five researchers, is currently exploring several areas of basic research and applications of phthalocyanines, porphyrins and carbon nanostructures (fullerenes, carbon nanotubes, graphene), including organic and hybrid solar cells, and photodynamic therapy, with a focus on nanotechnology.
